CASE 01 — Acute Follicular Conjunctivitis
=========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Photophobia, discomfort, and headache centered around the right eye for 2 weeks
DURATION: 2 weeks
LATERALITY: right
PATIENT QUOTES: "I denied any sexual contact."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 37-year-old female office worker presents with a 2-week history of photophobia, discomfort, and headache centered around her right eye. One week into this course she developed sudden onset redness and watering of the right eye on the first day back at work after a vacation in Hawaii. She noted swelling in front of her right ear. She was seen by an eye-care practitioner and treated with a 2-week course of topical tobramycin–dexamethasone; the preauricular node became slightly smaller, but 2 days after stopping the drops she developed corneal epithelial infiltrates and persistent follicles. She reports no systemic or genital symptoms and denies sexual contact; social history notable for hotel stays and use of swimming pool facilities during travel. Visual acuity 20/25 OD and 20/20 OS.
History Narrative: A 37-year-old female office worker with a 2-week history of photophobia, discomfort, and headache centered around her right eye. A week earlier she developed sudden onset redness and watering of the right eye on her first day back at work after a vacation in Hawaii. She noted swelling in front of her right ear. She saw her eye-care practitioner, who found unilateral follicular conjunctivitis with a mildly tender preauricular node and treated her with a 2-week course of topical tobramycin–dexamethasone ophthalmic suspension; the node became slightly smaller, but 2 days after cessation of the drops she developed epithelial infiltrates in the cornea and persisting follicles. She denies sexual contact; stayed in hotel rooms and used swimming pool facilities during vacation. No antecedent or concurrent systemic symptoms (fever, pharyngitis, upper respiratory symptoms) were reported. Visual acuity is 20/25 OD and 20/20 OS. On exam there is a right-sided nontender preauricular node, 1–2 mm ptosis of the right upper lid, mild bulbar conjunctival injection OD, moderate number of medium-sized pretarsal follicles and papillae OD with fewer follicles inferiorly OS, and the right cornea shows diffuse midperipheral and a few central epithelial infiltrates without stromal involvement; left cornea and anterior segment are normal.
Symptom Onset: 2 weeks prior
Symptom Duration: 2 weeks
Progression: Improved after cessation of steroids with development of epithelial then subepithelial infiltrates; subsequently gradual resolution expected with observation and symptomatic care (follow-up 4–6 weeks)
Characteristics: Acute follicular conjunctivitis, likely adenoviral; unilateral right-eye onset with photophobia, watering, mild injection, pretarsal follicles and papillae, nontender preauricular lymphadenopathy, corneal epithelial and midperipheral/subepithelial infiltrates after steroid cessation, no systemic symptoms.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Exposure to swimming pools and fomites (hand–eye contact, contaminated hotel surfaces)
Alleviating Factors: cool compresses and unpreserved lubricants
Associated Symptoms: Photophobia, discomfort, headache centered around the right eye, redness, watering (tearing), swelling in front of right ear (preauricular node)
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes reported
Prior Treatments: topical tobramycin–dexamethasone ophthalmic suspension drops (2-week course)
Impact on Daily Activities: Mild — some photophobia, discomfort, and headache affecting work/activities but vision largely preserved (20/25 OD, 20/20 OS); able to continue with precautions and symptomatic treatment.
Systemic Symptoms: None
Recent Travel: Vacation in Hawaii (returned several days before onset)
Risk Factors: Recent travel (vacation in hotel in Hawaii), use of swimming pool facilities, possible fomite exposure, prior topical corticosteroid use.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: 1- to 2-mm right upper lid ptosis; mild lid swelling/erythema on right; left lid normal.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Mild injection of the bulbar conjunctiva on the right; moderate number of medium-sized pretarsal follicles and papillae in the right eye; left conjunctiva quiet with substantially fewer inferior fornix follicles
External Exam – Lacrimal System: No epiphora; lacrimal system not specifically abnormal or obstructed (no tearing or punctal findings noted).
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Right cornea: diffuse midperipheral and a few central epithelial infiltrates; no stromal involvement. Left cornea: normal.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear natural lens (no cataract)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils equal and reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive to light bilaterally
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Normal
Pupils – RAPD: No RAPD detected
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Moderate number of medium-sized pretarsal follicles and papillae in the right eye; mild bulbar conjunctival injection OD; left eye quiet with fewer inferior fornix follicles; right cornea with diffuse midperipheral and a few central epithelial infiltrates (no stromal involvement); right upper lid 1–2 mm ptosis; right-sided nontender preauricular node.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Acute follicular conjunctivitis, most consistent with adenoviral keratoconjunctivitis given unilateral follicular conjunctivitis, preauricular lymphadenopathy, recent fomite/swimming exposure, and development of corneal epithelial/subepithelial infiltrates after cessation of topical steroid; viral (adenovirus), HSV, and chlamydial testing were negative. Observation and supportive care recommended; consider topical steroid (e.g., loteprednol) only if visually significant subepithelial infiltrates persist, and follow-up in 4–6 weeks to monitor resolution and corneal opacities.
Primary Diagnosis: Acute follicular conjunctivitis, likely adenoviral
Differential Diagnosis: Adenoviral keratoconjunctivitis; Adult chlamydial (inclusion) conjunctivitis; Herpes simplex/Epstein–Barr keratoconjunctivitis
Severity: Mild-to-moderate
Laterality: Right eye
Complications: Epithelial and subepithelial corneal infiltrates leading to potential corneal scarring and persistent visual disturbance (reduced visual acuity, contrast sensitivity, or photophobia); conjunctival membranes/pseudomembranes with risk of symblepharon; possible spread/contagion to others.


Plan
All Medications: tobramycin–dexamethasone ophthalmic suspension drops; topical tobramycin–dexamethasone; unpreserved lubricants; cool compresses (supportive); loteprednol (topical corticosteroid, possible); 0.6% povidone iodine/0.1% dexamethasone ophthalmic suspension (FST-100); povidone iodine; trifluridine; vidarabine; ganciclovir ophthalmic gel; topical cidofovir; 2′3′-dideoxycytidine; interferon β; interferon γ; immunoglobulin; N-chlorotaurine; topical trifluridine therapy; systemic acyclovir 400 mg five times daily; oral acyclovir; valacyclovir; tetracycline 250 mg orally four times daily; doxycycline 100 mg twice daily; erythromycin 500 mg four times daily; sulfisoxazole 0.5–1 g four times daily; azithromycin 1 g single dose; mitomycin C (low-dose, adjunct to PTK)
All Procedures: Conjunctival scrapings for direct fluorescent antibody (DFA) testing and McCoy cell culture; cultures for adenovirus and herpes simplex; diagnostic testing (PCR/ELISA options discussed) — overall: conjunctival swab/culture and DFA testing.
Follow-Up Instructions: Re-examine in 4–6 weeks to confirm symptom improvement, follicle regression, and fading of corneal opacities; earlier/more frequent visits if visually threatening corneal opacities or conjunctival membranes/pseudomembranes develop.
Patient Education: Observation and symptomatic care are appropriate; use cool compresses and unpreserved lubricating drops. The condition may be contagious for one to two weeks (or longer)—practice strict personal hygiene: frequent handwashing, keep used tissues, washcloths, and pillowcases separate from others. Report worsening symptoms (increasing pain, vision loss, membranes, or severe redness). If membranes or pseudomembranes develop, removal may be performed to prevent complications. Discuss that no FDA‑approved antiviral for adenovirus exists; topical steroids (e.g., loteprednol) may be considered for visually significant subepithelial infiltrates but can increase viral shedding. Follow up in 4–6 weeks (sooner if vision threatened) to ensure resolution; contact provider sooner if symptoms worsen.


CASE 02 — Chronic Follicular Conjunctivitis
===========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Routine follow-up visit 15 months after penetrating keratoplasty; mild right-sided preauricular discomfort and swelling.
DURATION: 15 months
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "I had no ocular symptoms at that time." "She denied using any eye drops other than the prescription medications already listed." "She denied close contact with animals, including cats and birds." "She was monogamous, and she denied any gynecologic complaints."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 38-year-old female, 15 months post right penetrating keratoplasty, with 6-week history of febrile illness and persistent right preauricular lymphadenopathy; developed mild right-sided preauricular discomfort and swelling, now with unilateral right ocular itching/irritation, mild eyelid puffiness, prominent inferior and superior tarsal follicles and papillae, mild diffuse epithelial keratoplasty epitheliopathy; chronic topical right-eye medications include loteprednol 0.5% BID, brimonidine 0.2% BID, timolol 0.5% gel BID and bedtime methylcellulose; symptoms began after systemic febrile illness treated with oral penicillin, no other systemic complaints, denies other eye drop use, animal contact, or travel.
History Narrative: A 38-year-old woman presents for routine follow-up 15 months after penetrating keratoplasty and anterior segment reconstruction of the right eye for trauma. Her eyes are comfortable but she reports mild right-sided preauricular discomfort and swelling. Six weeks earlier she developed a febrile illness with swollen glands, particularly on the right side of her face; her internist suspected “strep throat” or mononucleosis. She had no ocular symptoms at that time. Throat culture and Epstein–Barr virus serology were negative. She was treated with oral penicillin for 14 days and recovered symptomatically except for persistent right preauricular lymphadenopathy. She is otherwise healthy with a negative review of systems. Past ocular history is significant for medically treated traumatic angle recession glaucoma of the right eye; the left eye is normal. Current right-eye medications: loteprednol etabonate 0.5% twice daily, brimonidine 0.2% twice daily, timolol gel-forming solution 0.5% twice daily, and methylcellulose at bedtime. She was last seen 3 months prior with no ocular inflammation. On exam her right visual acuity is 20/60 improving to 20/40 with pinhole, IOP 28 mm Hg OD and 14 mm Hg OS. She has a slightly tender right preauricular lymph node and slight lid puffiness (right worse than left). Biomicroscopy of the right eye shows mild lid scurf; conjunctiva with prominent follicles and papillae in the inferior fornix and superior pretarsal area; no granulomas. The keratoplasty has mild diffuse epitheliopathy (hurricane epitheliopathy). Left eye shows mild pretarsal papillae without follicles and is otherwise normal. She denies use of any eye drops other than the listed prescription medications, denies close contact with animals, and has not traveled to endemic areas. She is monogamous and denies gynecologic complaints.
Symptom Onset: 6 weeks prior
Symptom Duration: >16 days
Progression: Resolved by 2 months after stopping brimonidine
Characteristics: Unilateral chronic follicular conjunctivitis with prominent superior and inferior tarsal follicles, mild lid scurf, slight lid puffiness, preauricular lymphadenopathy, mild diffuse keratoplasty epitheliopathy (hurricane pattern), and erythema/irritation that developed while cultures pending.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Use of topical preserved ocular medications (especially brimonidine and timolol) and exposure to preservatives (benzalkonium chloride/benzododecinium bromide)
Alleviating Factors: Discontinuation of the offending topical agent (brimonidine) and use of preservative‑free lubrication (cold compresses, preservative‑free artificial tears/ointment)
Associated Symptoms: mild right-sided preauricular discomfort and swelling; fever and swollen glands 6 weeks prior; erythema and irritation of the right eye; mild lid puffiness; ocular surface epitheliopathy (hurricane epitheliopathy); irritation and increased symptoms while cultures pending
Previous Episodes: Yes — first episode
Prior Treatments: loteprednol etabonate 0.5% twice daily; brimonidine 0.2% twice daily; timolol gel-forming solution 0.5% twice daily; methylcellulose at bedtime; oral penicillin x14 days; azithromycin/doxycycline/erythromycin mentioned as possible (not given)
Impact on Daily Activities: Mild — no significant limitation; eyes comfortable with only mild irritation that improved after stopping brimonidine.
Systemic Symptoms: Fever and swollen glands (preauricular lymphadenopathy) 6 weeks prior; otherwise negative review of systems.
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: No known contacts with similar symptoms
Risk Factors: Chronic topical preserved ocular medications (brimonidine, timolol with benzalkonium chloride), prior viral prodrome with preauricular lymphadenopathy, prior penetrating keratoplasty/ocular surface surgery, traumatic angle-recession glaucoma, prolonged topical corticosteroid use (loteprednol).


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 118/76 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/60+
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/60+ (pinhole 20/40)
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N5
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 28 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: slight puffiness of the lids, right worse than left
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: prominent follicles and papillae in the inferior fornix and superior pretarsal area of the right eye; mild pretarsal papillae in the left eye; no granulomas; mild diffuse keratoplasty epitheliopathy in right eye
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Slight puffiness of the lids, right worse than left
Anterior Segment – Cornea: mild diffuse epitheliopathy with punctate keratitis (hurricane epitheliopathy) within the keratoplasty wound margin
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open, traumatic angle recession (right eye)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk and symmetric to light in the right eye (responses preserved)
Pupils – Reaction to Near: brisk
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact without restriction
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Mild lid scurf; conjunctiva with prominent follicles and papillae in inferior fornix and superior pretarsal area; no granulomas; mild diffuse keratoplasty epitheliopathy (hurricane epitheliopathy); anterior segment otherwise noncontributory.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Unilateral toxic chronic follicular conjunctivitis likely secondary to topical brimonidine use, with associated hurricane epitheliopathy of the right corneal graft. Chronic follicular response persisted despite negative viral cultures and Chlamydia testing; symptoms and follicles improved and resolved after discontinuation of brimonidine, supporting a toxic medicamentosa etiology. Close follow-up recommended to monitor for resolution and potential sequelae (punctal stenosis, conjunctival scarring).
Primary Diagnosis: Unilateral toxic chronic follicular conjunctivitis
Secondary Diagnoses: Chronic follicular conjunctivitis (toxic, due to brimonidine); medically treated traumatic angle recession glaucoma (right eye); status post penetrating keratoplasty and anterior segment reconstruction (right eye)
Differential Diagnosis: Viral conjunctivitis (adenovirus/EBV/HSV); Chlamydial (adult inclusion) conjunctivitis; Toxic (medicamentosa) follicular conjunctivitis; Conjunctival lymphoma (masquerade syndrome)
Severity: Mild
Laterality: Right
Complications: superficial punctate keratopathy, corneal/conjunctival scarring, symblepharon, punctal stenosis


Plan
All Medications: loteprednol etabonate 0.5% twice daily; brimonidine 0.2% twice daily; timolol gel-forming solution 0.5% twice daily; methylcellulose at bedtime; oral penicillin (14 days); azithromycin 1000 mg orally once; doxycycline 100 mg orally twice daily (7–10 days); erythromycin 500 mg orally four times daily (7–10 days); preservative-free artificial tears/ointment; topical corticosteroids; topical cyclosporine A 1%; topical tacrolimus ointment; trifluridine; idoxuridine; gentamicin; tobramycin; pilocarpine; apraclonidine; epinephrine; dipivefrin
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow up every 2–3 weeks until improvement is noted; monitor for resolution of follicles and ocular surface disease; discontinue offending agent (brimonidine) and use preservative‑free lubrication for symptom relief.
Patient Education: Discontinue the offending eye drop (brimonidine) and avoid preserved topical medications when possible. Use preservative-free artificial tears or ointment for comfort. Apply cold compresses for irritation. If a glaucoma medication is still needed, switch to a preservative-free alternative. Monitor closely: return in 2–3 weeks for follow-up until improvement is noted. Seek prompt care if you develop worsening pain, vision loss, or signs of infection.


CASE 03 — Acute Bacterial Conjunctivitis
========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Decreased vision, irritation, and discharge in the right eye for 3 days
DURATION: 3 days
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "my eye has been stuck shut for the past 2 days" "I don't have any fevers, chills, malaise, or any other ill symptoms" "no allergies to medications"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 60-year-old woman with 3 days of decreased vision, irritation, and mucopurulent discharge in the right eye; eyelids stuck shut for 2 days requiring warm compresses to open. No trauma or surgery, no sick contacts, no systemic symptoms (fever, chills, malaise). Symptoms began in one eye. Denies medication allergies; takes metoprolol for hypertension.
History Narrative: A 60-year-old woman presents with complaints of decreased vision, irritation, and discharge in her right eye over the past 3 days. She reports that her eye has been stuck shut for the past 2 days, requiring a warm, wet washcloth to open the eye. She denies any trauma or surgery. She denies any contacts with ill individuals and does not have any fevers, chills, malaise, or any other ill symptoms. Her past medical history includes hypertension and a hysterectomy 10 years ago. She reports no allergies to medications and uses metoprolol for hypertension. She denies any family history of eye disease. The patient is healthy with a negative review of systems other than her ocular complaints. She is wearing glasses.
Symptom Onset: 3 days
Symptom Duration: 3 days
Progression: Acute (hours to days)
Characteristics: Acute conjunctival injection with chemosis, 2+ papillary reaction, mucopurulent discharge forming crusts and a fibrin–mucus pseudomembrane; cornea clear, anterior chamber quiet; unilateral onset with eyelids stuck shut in morning; decreased vision OD (20/40 corrected to 20/30)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Touching the eye and sharing towels or washcloths (facilitates spread/ worsening); waking with eyelids stuck shut from purulent discharge leading to irritation with opening.
Alleviating Factors: Warm, wet compresses; topical antibiotic drops (trimethoprim–polymyxin B); artificial tears; avoiding touching eyes/sharing towels.
Associated Symptoms: Decreased vision, irritation, discharge, eyelids stuck shut (matting)
Previous Episodes: No prior history of conjunctivitis
Impact on Daily Activities: Decreased — symptoms improved after 2 days with treatment (initially eyelid stuck shut, decreased vision OD, irritation and discharge limiting normal activities)
Systemic Symptoms: None — denies fevers, chills, malaise or other systemic symptoms
Recent Travel: No recent travel reported
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: Denies any contacts with ill individuals
Risk Factors: - Use of contact lenses: No - Recent ocular trauma or surgery: No - Neonate or immunocompromised status: No - Close contact with infected individuals: No - Age (older adult): Yes (60 years) - Hypertension: Yes (on metoprolol) - Wearing glasses: Yes - Presence of mucopurulent discharge, papillary reaction, and pseudomembrane: Yes (clinical signs indicating bacterial infection) - Copious purulent discharge: Yes


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N5
Visual Acuity – Method: manifest refraction with correction
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 18 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Mucopurulent discharge coating lashes; inferior fornix pseudomembrane; 2+ papillary reaction of palpebral conjunctiva; lids edematous (right > left)
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Right conjunctival injection and edema with mucopurulent discharge; inferior fornix covered with fibrin–mucus pseudomembrane; 2+ palpebral papillary reaction.
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis, adnexa normal, lids with mucopurulent crusting OD; right inferior fornix pseudomembrane; no periorbital swelling or erythema.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear, no evidence of scarring or inflammation
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Right: mild cataractous changes; Left: clear


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, and reactive; no relative afferent pupillary defect noted
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Briskly reactive (no afferent pupillary defect)
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: No afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact in both eyes
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Right conjunctiva injected and edematous with mucopurulent discharge; inferior fornix covered with a fibrin–mucus pseudomembrane; inferior and superior palpebral conjunctiva with 2+ papillary reaction; corneas clear without scarring or inflammation; anterior chambers quiet; right lens with mild cataractous changes, left lens clear.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: cup-to-disc ratio 0.4 in both eyes
Fundus Exam – Macula: normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Streptococcus pneumoniae bacterial conjunctivitis of the right eye with inflammatory pseudomembrane formation. Presentation consistent with acute bacterial conjunctivitis: 3-day history of decreased vision, irritation, mucopurulent discharge with lids stuck, marked conjunctival injection and chemosis, 2+ papillary reaction, inferior fornix pseudomembrane, clear cornea and quiet anterior chamber. Gram stain with numerous gram-positive cocci in pairs and PMNs; culture grew S. pneumoniae sensitive to penicillin, ciprofloxacin, vancomycin, and trimethoprim/sulfamethoxazole. Managed with topical trimethoprim–polymyxin B four times daily for 7 days with significant improvement by 2 days.
Primary Diagnosis: Streptococcal pneumoniae bacterial conjunctivitis with inflammatory pseudomembranes
Secondary Diagnoses: Hypertension
Differential Diagnosis: Bacterial conjunctivitis (streptococcal); Viral conjunctivitis; Allergic conjunctivitis; Gonococcal (hyperacute) conjunctivitis
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right
Complications: corneal involvement including ulceration and perforation, spread of infection (orbital cellulitis), pseudomembrane formation, decreased vision, secondary keratitis, and transmission to the fellow eye or contacts


Plan
All Medications: metoprolol trimethoprim sulfate–polymyxin B eye drops penicillin ciprofloxacin vancomycin trimethoprim/sulfamethoxazole bacitracin gentamicin besifloxacin ceftriaxone artificial tears
All Procedures: Gram stain of conjunctival mucus; conjunctival cultures plated on blood and chocolate agar; peeling/debridement not performed (pseudomembrane noted, no surgical removal documented).
Follow-Up Instructions: Return to clinic if symptoms worsen, vision decreases, pain, or no improvement within 48–72 hours. Avoid touching eyes or sharing towels; use warm compresses and artificial tears for comfort. Complete prescribed topical antibiotic course (trimethoprim–polymyxin B four times daily for 7 days).
Patient Education: Use warm compresses and artificial tears for comfort. Avoid touching your eyes and do not share towels or washcloths while symptomatic to prevent spread. Instill prescribed trimethoprim–polymyxin B drops four times daily for 7 days; symptoms should improve within a few days—if they worsen, vision decreases, pain increases, or you develop fever, return for re-evaluation.
Work Restrictions: Avoid touching eyes; avoid sharing towels or washcloths while symptomatic; return to work/school once symptoms improve (after starting treatment / when discharge resolves).
Emergency Instructions: Seek urgent care if you develop severe eye pain, marked worsening vision, intense redness or swelling, fever, a rapidly increasing amount of purulent discharge, signs of corneal involvement (e.g., sensitivity to light, foreign-body sensation, white spot on the cornea), inability to open the eye, or if symptoms rapidly worsen or fail to improve within 48–72 hours despite treatment. If hyperacute symptoms suggestive of gonococcal infection occur (very rapid onset, copious purulent discharge), go to the emergency department immediately.


CASE 04 — Pterygium
===================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: intermittent bilateral red eye, dryness, irritation, and foreign body sensation
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "N/A"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 32-year-old lifeguard with intermittent bilateral red eye, dryness, irritation, and foreign body sensation; visual acuity 20/20 OU; exam shows fibrovascular overgrowth of bulbar conjunctiva extending onto the cornea of both eyes (bilateral pterygia).
History Narrative: A 32-year-old lifeguard presents with intermittent symptoms of bilateral red eye, dryness, irritation, and foreign body sensation. Visual acuity is 20/20 in both eyes. Examination reveals a fibrovascular overgrowth of the bulbar conjunctiva extending onto the cornea of both eyes.
Symptom Onset: Intermittent (present at least prior to presentation; no specific onset date provided)
Symptom Duration: Intermittent (chronic), present for an unstated duration — inferred chronic/recurrent symptoms (likely months to years)
Progression: May grow very slowly or enter a quiescent phase; occasionally become actively inflamed or grow rapidly and progressively.
Characteristics: Fibrovascular overgrowth of bulbar conjunctiva extending onto the cornea (nasal interpalpebral fissure), bilateral, associated with redness, dryness, irritation, foreign body sensation; disrupts Bowman’s layer with subepithelial fibrovascular tissue, increased fibroblasts, elastoid degeneration; may show Stocker’s iron line; often UV exposure–related, occurs in young adults (20–40), more common in males, slow-growing or quiescent but can be inflamed/actively progressive causing induced astigmatism and visual axis encroachment.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Exposure to ultraviolet light and chronic dry eye (wind/UV exposure)
Alleviating Factors: Use of preservative-free artificial tears; topical nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory medications; short course of mild topical steroid (with taper); ultraviolet-blocking sunglasses; protection from UV light; continued lubrication; surgical removal if indicated.
Associated Symptoms: bilateral red eye, dryness, irritation, and foreign body sensation
Impact on Daily Activities: Intermittent redness, dryness, irritation, and foreign body sensation; vision 20/20 bilaterally — minimal to no impact on daily activities.
Systemic Symptoms: None
Risk Factors: - Exposure to ultraviolet (UV) light/sunlight - Chronic dry eye disease - Outdoor/wind exposure (e.g., lifeguard; occupations with sun/wind exposure) - Living closer to the equator (geographic/latitude) - Male sex (more common in males) - Age 20–40 years (common in this age group)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 76 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+ (Jaeger 1+)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Fibrovascular overgrowth of bulbar conjunctiva extending onto the cornea bilaterally (pterygium), nasal interpalpebral fissures.
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Fibrovascular conjunctival overgrowth (pterygium) extending onto cornea bilaterally with disruption of Bowman's layer, epithelial irregularity, and possible induced astigmatism.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal.
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear, no lens abnormality noted (phakic)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils equal, round, reactive to light (no anisocoria)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk and equal bilaterally
Pupils – Reaction to Near: briskly constrict to accommodation (intact)
Pupils – RAPD: No relative afferent pupillary defect (RAPD)
Pupils – Other: Pupils equal and reactive to light, no afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions of gaze (no restriction)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Fibrovascular overgrowth of the bulbar conjunctiva extending onto the cornea of both eyes (bilateral nasal interpalpebral pterygia) with conjunctival injection; visual acuity 20/20 OU.
Fundus Exam – Macula: Within normal limits
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Deferred to outpatient follow-up; peripheral retina flat without lesions


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 32-year-old lifeguard with bilateral pterygia presenting with intermittent redness, dryness, irritation, and foreign body sensation. Visual acuity 20/20 OU. Exam shows bilateral fibrovascular overgrowth of bulbar conjunctiva extending onto the cornea consistent with pterygium, likely UV-related; no signs suggesting malignancy or pseudopterygium. Manage conservatively with preservative-free artificial tears, UV protection; consider short course topical steroid for symptomatic inflammation. Monitor for progression, induced astigmatism (corneal topography as needed). Surgical excision indicated if progressive growth toward visual axis, induced astigmatism, severe symptoms, or cosmetic concern; discuss recurrence risk and adjuncts (conjunctival autograft, mitomycin C, amniotic membrane) and potential complications.
Primary Diagnosis: Pterygium, bilateral
Differential Diagnosis: Pinguecula; Pseudopterygium (conjunctival scar from trauma/chemical injury/infection); Conjunctival intraepithelial neoplasia/squamous cell carcinoma
Severity: Mild
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: persistent epithelial defects; scleral necrosis


Plan
All Medications: preservative-free artificial tears; topical nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory medications; topical steroid (e.g., mild topical steroid dosed four times daily, tapered over 2–3 weeks); ultraviolet‑blocking sunglasses (protective measure, not a medication); mitomycin C (antimetabolite, 0.02% concentration example); topical antibiotic (postoperative, four times daily); topical corticosteroid (postoperative, tapered)
All Procedures: Observation/medical management (artificial tears, topical NSAIDs, short course topical steroid tapered over 2–3 weeks, UV‑blocking sunglasses) Corneal topography Pterygium excision (under local subconjunctival anesthesia) with removal of Tenon’s capsule Conjunctival autograft (secured with sutures or fibrin glue) Amniotic membrane transplantation (secured with sutures or fibrin glue) Application of antimetabolite (mitomycin C, titrated; subconjunctival use) Irradiation Primary conjunctival anastomosis Rotational conjunctival flap Histopathologic examination of excised tissue Topical postoperative antibiotic therapy and tapered topical corticosteroid
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up dictated by symptom severity and proximity to visual axis; earlier follow-up if topical steroids are used (to assess response and intraocular pressure)
Patient Education: Avoid excessive sun and wind exposure; wear ultraviolet‑blocking sunglasses and hats. Use preservative‑free artificial tears regularly for lubrication. If inflamed or symptomatic, short course of topical steroid may be prescribed (typically four times daily, tapered over 2–3 weeks) — return early if symptoms recur. If surgery is recommended, understand recurrence risk (may be high without adjuncts; adjunctive measures like conjunctival autograft, amniotic membrane, or mitomycin C can lower recurrence but have risks). Seek prompt re‑evaluation for progressive growth affecting vision, recurrent inflammation, or any new atypical appearance.


CASE 05 — Recurrent Erosion/Epithelial Basement Membrane Dystrophy
==================================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Sudden onset of unilateral eye pain with foreign body sensation, tearing, photophobia, and blurred vision upon awakening
DURATION: Sudden onset upon awakening (acute)
LATERALITY: Unilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "I awoke with sudden pain, foreign body sensation, tearing, light sensitivity, and blurred vision when I opened my eyes."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 35-year-old woman awakened with sudden onset of unilateral severe eye pain, foreign body sensation, tearing, photophobia, and blurred vision immediately upon opening her eyes. She reports multiple prior similar episodes occurring in either eye, no history of ocular trauma, and a positive family history (mother) of similar episodes. Visual acuity in the involved eye was 20/400.
History Narrative: A 35-year-old woman awoke from sleep with sudden onset of unilateral pain, foreign body sensation, lacrimation, photophobia, and blurred vision immediately upon opening her eyes. The patient had a previous history of multiple similar episodes occurring in either eye. The patient’s mother has also been affected with similar episodes. She had no history of prior ocular trauma. Visual acuity is 20/400 in the involved eye and 20/20 in the fellow eye. Slit-lamp examination of the involved eye revealed a large, discrete area of epithelial sloughing. The edges of the epithelial defect were remarkable for a “heaped-up” appearance. No infiltrate was apparent. Fluorescein dye revealed pooling over the epithelial defect. Examination of the fellow eye was remarkable for diffuse, superficial gray-white opacities in a “map” and “dot” configuration.
Symptom Onset: Upon awakening / immediately upon opening her eyes
Symptom Duration: Upon awakening (sudden onset at time of awakening)
Progression: Recurrent
Characteristics: Map lines, fingerprint lines, and dots (diffuse superficial gray-white opacities; “heaped-up” epithelial edges with large sheet-like epithelial sloughing; spontaneous awakening onset; often bilateral; autosomal dominant with incomplete penetrance)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Awakening/opening the eye after sleep (shear/traction from eyelid on loosely adherent epithelium)
Alleviating Factors: Eyelid closure during sleep (removing eyelid-induced shear), topical lubrication (nonpreserved artificial tears and ointment), hypertonic 5% sodium chloride drops/ointment, bandage contact lens, pressure patch, prophylactic topical antibiotic ointment, cycloplegia, self-retained amniotic membrane grafts.
Associated Symptoms: pain, foreign body sensation, lacrimation, photophobia, blurred vision
Previous Episodes: multiple similar episodes occurring in either eye
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant — acute episodes cause severe pain, photophobia, tearing and blurred vision (vision 20/400 in affected eye) that markedly impair activities (especially morning awakening, reading/driving) and may require treatment/patching, thus substantially limiting daily function during attacks.
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: Mother
Risk Factors: Recurrent corneal epithelial erosions; epithelial basement membrane (map‑dot‑fingerprint) dystrophy (autosomal dominant family history); female sex; prior episodes in either eye; underlying corneal dystrophy (diffuse abnormal basement membrane)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: 15 mm Hg — applanation


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear, no injection or conjunctival scarring noted
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Recurrent epithelial erosion with map‑dot‑fingerprint (epithelial basement membrane) dystrophy
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lens (no cataract)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal and reactive, normal size and round
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive to light
Pupils – RAPD: 0 (no RAPD)
Pupils – Other: brisk, symmetric
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion OU, no restriction or diplopia reported
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Involved eye: large discrete area of epithelial sloughing with “heaped‑up” edges, fluorescein pooling over epithelial defect, no infiltrate; Fellow eye: diffuse superficial gray‑white opacities in map and dot (map‑dot‑fingerprint) configuration.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Recurrent epithelial erosion syndrome secondary to epithelial basement membrane (map‑dot‑fingerprint) dystrophy of the cornea. Left (involved) eye with acute large epithelial sheet defect with heaped-up edges, fluorescein pooling, no infiltrate; visual acuity 20/400 OD? (involved eye) and fellow eye 20/20 with diffuse map‑dot‑fingerprint changes consistent with bilateral dystrophy. History of recurrent episodes in either eye and positive family history. Recommend acute management with topical antibiotic ointment and pressure patch or bandage contact lens (±cycloplegia for pain), consider self‑retained amniotic membrane if needed; for recurrent/recalcitrant disease consider total epithelial debridement, anterior stromal micropuncture, or PTK. Maintain lubrication long‑term with frequent preservative‑free artificial tears and nighttime ointment; consider 5% sodium chloride drops/ointment for several months to promote epithelial adherence. Follow closely until epithelial healing and monitor for infectious keratitis; evaluate corneal topography if vision remains irregular.
Primary Diagnosis: Recurrent epithelial erosion syndrome, basement membrane dystrophy type
Secondary Diagnoses: Epithelial basement membrane dystrophy (Map‑dot‑fingerprint dystrophy)
Differential Diagnosis: Recurrent epithelial erosion syndrome due to epithelial basement membrane (map‑dot‑fingerprint) dystrophy; Post‑traumatic recurrent corneal erosion; Other epithelial basement membrane dystrophies (Meesmann’s, Reis–Bücklers)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right
Complications: delayed corneal wound healing and refractive changes


Plan
All Medications: topical antibiotic ointment cycloplegia (topical cycloplegic) soft bandage contact lens (device) self-retained amniotic membrane grafts (Prokera, AmbioDisk) (biologic devices) topical antibiotic ointment, cycloplegia, and pressure patch (combination) topical antibiotic ointment, patch, bandage contact lens 5% sodium chloride drops 5% sodium chloride ointment nonpreserved artificial tears artificial tear ointment
All Procedures: antibiotic prophylaxis (topical antibiotic ointment), cycloplegia, pressure patching, bandage soft contact lens, self-retained amniotic membrane graft (cryopreserved Prokera or dehydrated AmbioDisk), epithelial debridement/scraping (manual removal of epithelium), anterior stromal micropuncture (25-gauge needle micropunctures), excimer laser phototherapeutic keratectomy (PTK), corneal topography
Follow-Up Instructions: Careful follow‑up until the epithelial defect has fully resolved; monitor for infectious keratitis. After healing, maintain frequent lubrication with nonpreserved artificial tears 4–8×/day and artificial tear ointment at bedtime. Consider hypertonic 5% sodium chloride drops during the day and 5% ointment at night for ~3+ months to promote epithelial adherence. Obtain corneal topography if vision is affected (irregular astigmatism or haze).
Patient Education: You have recurrent corneal erosions due to epithelial basement membrane (map‑dot‑fingerprint) dystrophy. This causes the corneal surface to be loosely adherent and can lead to painful episodes—often upon awakening—causing pain, tearing, light sensitivity, and blurry vision. Key points and home care: - It is usually a chronic, often inherited condition; examine both eyes because changes can be bilateral. - During an acute episode, treatment promotes healing and prevents infection: topical antibiotic ointment, pressure patch or bandage contact lens, and sometimes cycloplegic drops for pain; amniotic membrane grafts may be used in clinic. Seek care promptly for sudden pain, worsening vision, increasing redness, or discharge. - Long‑term measures to reduce recurrences: frequent nonpreserved artificial tears during the day (4–8×/day) and ointment at bedtime; consider hypertonic 5% sodium chloride drops by day and ointment at night for several months to help the epithelium adhere. - If episodes are frequent or vision is affected, procedures (debridement, anterior stromal micropuncture, or PTK) can produce longer symptom‑free periods. Discuss risks and benefits with your ophthalmologist. - Watch for signs of infection and follow up until the epithelial defect heals.
Emergency Instructions: Seek urgent ophthalmic care if severe pain, worsening vision, increasing redness, discharge, or fever occur; until evaluation, apply preservative-free artificial tears or antibiotic ointment if prescribed, avoid rubbing the eye, wear an eye shield or patch, and use topical cycloplegic for pain if prescribed.


CASE 06 — Fuchs’ Corneal Dystrophy
==================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Slowly progressive loss of vision, worse in the left eye, initially worse in the morning with diurnal fluctuation but more recently poor throughout the day
LATERALITY: OU
PATIENT QUOTES: "Initially, her vision was worse upon awakening and gradually cleared as the day went on; more recently, however, her vision remained poor throughout the day."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 60-year-old woman with slowly progressive vision loss greater in the left than right eye. Initially vision worse upon awakening and gradually cleared during the day (diurnal fluctuation), but more recently vision remained poor throughout the day. Current corrected visual acuities 20/200 OD and counting fingers at 4 ft OS. Left greater than right central microcystic epithelial edema and stromal edema with Descemet membrane folds; corneal guttatae and endothelial pigmentation centrally OU. Moderate nuclear sclerotic cataracts OU. IOP 14 mm Hg OU.
History Narrative: A 60-year-old woman presented with a history of slowly progressive loss of vision in the left greater than the right eye. Initially, her vision was worse upon awakening and gradually cleared as the day went on; more recently, however, her vision remained poor throughout the day. Examination revealed corrected visual acuities of 20/200 in the right eye and counting fingers at 4 feet in the left eye. Intraocular pressure was 14mm Hg in each eye. Slit-lamp examination of the cornea revealed central microcystic epithelial edema and stromal edema with folds in Descemet’s membrane in the left greater than the right eye. Corneal guttatae and endothelial pigmentation extended over the central portion of the cornea in both eyes. The anterior chamber was deep and quiet, and the iris was normal in both eyes. Moderate nuclear sclerotic cataracts were present bilaterally, and dilated fundus examination revealed normal posterior poles through a hazy view. B-scan ultrasonography of the posterior poles was unremarkable.
Symptom Onset: Slowly progressive; initially worse in morning and cleared during day, more recently vision remained poor throughout the day (chronic progressive over months to years).
Symptom Duration: Slowly progressive (present for years)
Progression: Slowly progressive
Characteristics: Slowly progressive central corneal endothelial dysfunction with corneal guttata, central stromal and microcystic epithelial edema (worse in the morning), decreased endothelial cell density, pleomorphism/polymegathism, potential endothelial pigmentation, increased central corneal thickness on pachymetry, diurnal vision fluctuation, eventual bullous keratopathy and possible corneal scarring in advanced disease.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Worse vision in the morning due to eyelid closure/surface evaporation decrease (diurnal variability), activities that increase corneal hydration (sleeping/overnight eye closure)
Alleviating Factors: Vision gradually cleared as the day went on; hypertonic NaCl drops/ointment and corneal dehydration (hair dryer) reduce morning blur.
Associated Symptoms: Slowly progressive loss of vision (worse in left eye), morning blur/diurnal vision fluctuation with worse vision on awakening, episodes of sharp pain from ruptured epithelial bullae (in advanced disease)
Prior Treatments: Hypertonic sodium chloride drops (NaCl 5%) and hypertonic sodium chloride ointment at bedtime; supportive measures including hair-dryer corneal dehydration.
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant — vision markedly reduced (worse in morning, progressed to persistent impairment) impairing daily activities, more in left eye (CF at 4 ft)
Systemic Symptoms: None
Risk Factors: Female sex, increasing age (typically >50 years), family history/genetic predisposition, and cataract surgery or other endothelial trauma


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/76 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: CF at 4 ft
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: Counting fingers at 4 feet
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: JND (finger counting at 4 ft)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen acuity (manifest refraction)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal appearing conjunctiva and sclera (no injection or hemorrhage)
External Exam – Lacrimal System: normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Central microcystic epithelial edema and stromal edema with Descemet membrane folds, central corneal guttata and endothelial pigmentation OU (L>R), consistent with Fuchs’ endothelial dystrophy; increased central corneal thickness (pachymetry likely >600 µm in affected eye).
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open (wide)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Moderate nuclear sclerotic cataract OU


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, and reactive to light (brisk)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk and equal in both eyes
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: Pupils are equal and reactive to light and accommodation
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions without restriction
Slit Lamp Exam Description: central microcystic epithelial edema and stromal edema with folds in Descemet’s membrane (L>R); corneal guttatae and endothelial pigmentation centrally in both eyes; anterior chamber deep and quiet; iris normal; moderate nuclear sclerotic cataracts bilaterally
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Hazy view but posterior poles normal through a hazy view
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Flat periphery without retinal tears or detachments, unremarkable (no peripheral pathology detected)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 1. Fuchs’ corneal dystrophy OU with more advanced disease OS than OD, manifesting as central microcystic epithelial edema, stromal edema with Descemet folds, central corneal guttata and endothelial pigmentation, and markedly reduced vision OS > OD (CF 4 ft OS, 20/200 OD). 2. Bilateral moderate nuclear sclerotic cataracts. 3. IOP 14 mm Hg OU, deep quiet anterior chambers, normal-appearing irides, hazy but otherwise unremarkable posterior poles on dilated exam and B-scan. 4. Corneal decompensation OS greater than OD with symptomatic visual decline and morning worsening consistent with endothelial failure; consider pachymetry/specular microscopy for staging and surgical planning. 5. Management options discussed: medical therapy (hypertonic NaCl drops/ointment, hair-dryer dehydration) for early symptomatic relief; surgical options for vision rehabilitation include endothelial transplantation (DSAEK or DMEK) or PKP if stromal scarring present; consider combined cataract extraction with endothelial procedure if central pachymetry >600 µm or based on surgical assessment.
Primary Diagnosis: Fuchs’ corneal dystrophy OU
Secondary Diagnoses: Cataract OU
Differential Diagnosis: Pseudophakic/aphakic bullous keratopathy; Angle-closure glaucoma with endothelial decompensation; Herpes simplex/varicella-zoster stromal keratitis; Posterior polymorphous dystrophy
Severity: Advanced
Laterality: OU
Complications: - Corneal decompensation with stromal and epithelial edema - Bullous keratopathy with painful epithelial bullae and potential epithelial rupture - Corneal scarring limiting visual outcome - Need for corneal transplantation (DSAEK, DMEK, or PKP) - Graft-related complications: rejection, graft failure, dislocation (after endothelial keratoplasty) - Postoperative astigmatism requiring suture management or further surgery - Accelerated cataract progression after PKP or cataract surgery–related endothelial trauma reducing graft survival - Persistent decreased visual acuity despite treatment


Plan
All Medications: hypertonic sodium chloride (NaCl 5%) ophthalmic solution; hypertonic sodium chloride ointment at bedtime; balanced salt solution (BSS) plus irrigation fluid; topical antibiotic drops (postoperative); topical corticosteroid drops (postoperative); nonpreserved artificial tears
All Procedures: hypertonic sodium chloride (NaCl 5%) drops; hypertonic sodium chloride ointment at bedtime; BSS plus irrigation during cataract surgery; use of dispersive or viscoadaptive viscoelastic for endothelial protection during phacoemulsification; endocapsular and chop phacoemulsification techniques; Descemet’s stripping automated endothelial keratoplasty (DSAEK); Descemet’s membrane endothelial keratoplasty (DMEK); penetrating keratoplasty (PKP); simultaneous cataract extraction and IOL placement with/without endothelial transplant; selective suture lysis and/or running suture adjustment; rigid gas permeable contact lens fitting; astigmatic keratotomy; compression sutures; wedge resection; repeat penetrating keratoplasty; LASIK (after suture removal and secure wound)
Follow-Up Instructions: - Postoperative topical antibiotic and corticosteroid drops (antibiotics until surface re-epithelializes; steroids tapered slowly over 6–12 months) - Use ointments and/or nonpreserved artificial tears as needed for ocular surface rehabilitation - Minimize number and frequency of preserved topical drops to avoid surface toxicity - After DSAEK/DMEK: remain supine until the air bubble is absorbed to allow graft attachment - After PKP: manage postoperative astigmatism with selective suture lysis and/or running suture adjustment guided by topography; prescribe glasses or RGP lenses when appropriate - Monitor for graft attachment, corneal clarity, and signs of rejection or infection; follow routine postoperative visits per surgeon protocol
Patient Education: Fuchs’ endothelial dystrophy is a slowly progressive condition in which the innermost layer of the cornea (the endothelium) thins and develops guttae, leading to fluid buildup in the cornea that causes blurred vision—often worse in the morning. Early disease may cause only intermittent “morning blur” and can often be managed with conservative measures: hypertonic saline drops (NaCl 5%) during the day, hypertonic ointment at bedtime, and simple dehydration (e.g., directing a hair dryer on low from arm’s length for a few minutes). As the disease progresses, epithelial bullae can form and cause pain and persistent vision loss. Definitive treatment for visually significant corneal edema is endothelial transplantation (DSAEK or DMEK) or, when scarring is present, penetrating keratoplasty (PKP). If cataract surgery is needed, corneal thickness and endothelial status help determine whether to combine cataract extraction with an endothelial transplant. After corneal transplant, expect topical antibiotics and steroids, a prolonged steroid taper (6–12 months), and positioning (supine) after DSAEK/DMEK until the air bubble resolves. Regular follow-up with pachymetry/specular microscopy and your cornea specialist is important to monitor progression and plan surgery when vision or symptoms warrant. If you develop sudden severe pain, decreased vision, or signs of infection, seek prompt ophthalmic care.


CASE 07 — Keratoconus
=====================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: gradual decreased vision in his right eye over the past year
DURATION: 1 year
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "I don't see clearly."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 19-year-old man with one-year history of gradual decreased vision in the right eye; long history of glasses/contacts (9 years) with recent prescription change and new contacts that do not improve vision; reports vision was previously correctable to 20/20 until last year; denies trauma, surgery, or recent eye infection.
History Narrative: A 19-year-old man with a nine-year history of glasses and contact lens wear presents with gradual decreased vision in his right eye over the past year. He reports his vision had been correctable to 20/20 until last year and despite a recent prescription change and new contact lenses he does not see clearly. He denies trauma, surgery, or recent eye infection. Past medical history is significant for seasonal allergies; he is allergic to penicillin and uses acetaminophen occasionally. He denies family history of eye disease and has a negative review of systems. He is currently wearing contact lenses.
Symptom Onset: 1 year
Symptom Duration: 1 year
Characteristics: Noninflammatory ectasia of the central cornea with progressive central/paracentral thinning, irregular astigmatism and steepening (cone-shaped protrusion), Vogt’s striae, Fleischer ring (epithelial iron deposition), usually bilateral but asymmetric, associated with corneal scarring and hydrops in advanced disease.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Eye rubbing and atopic seasonal allergies
Alleviating Factors: Rigid gas permeable contact lens use improving vision to 20/30
Associated Symptoms: Gradual decreased vision in the right eye over the past year, difficulty seeing clearly with glasses/soft contacts, high irregular astigmatism (blurred vision), no pain, no trauma, no photophobia, seasonal allergies (atopy)
Prior Treatments: Wearing glasses and soft contact lenses for 9 years; recent change in prescription and new contact lenses; trial and continued use of rigid gas permeable contact lens (improves OD to 20/30)
Impact on Daily Activities: Mildly impaired — difficulty achieving best vision with glasses/soft contacts; improved to 20/30 with rigid gas-permeable lens, so some functional limitation but manageable with specialty contact use.
Systemic Symptoms: Negative/none
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Atopy (seasonal allergies), eye rubbing, and possible contact lens wear (long-term); associations include Down syndrome, sleep apnea, and floppy eyelids (not present in this patient).


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/50
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 12
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear.
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: mild central corneal thinning with Vogt’s striae and partial Fleischer ring; corneas clear without scarring; pachymetry OD 450 µm, OS 480 µm; inferior steepening on topography (right > left)
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: clear


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, and reactive to light (no afferent pupillary defect)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk and equal bilaterally
Pupils – Reaction to Near: normal
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: No afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion OU without restriction
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Lids and lashes normal; tear lake adequate with normal tear breakup time; corneas clear without scarring or inflammation but with mild central thinning, Vogt’s striae on Descemet’s membrane, and partial Fleischer’s ring more prominent with cobalt blue illumination; anterior chamber quiet; lens clear; posterior pole normal with healthy foveal light reflex.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Cup-to-disc ratio 0.3 bilaterally
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal with a healthy foveal light reflex
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: peripheral retina flat and without any peripheral lesions; no retinal tears, holes, or detachment noted


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 19-year-old man with keratoconus of the right eye. Progressive decreased vision OD over one year with asymmetric high irregular astigmatism (manifest refraction OD −2.00 −7.50 ×60, corrects to 20/100 with glasses, RGP over-refraction improves to 20/30), slit-lamp signs of keratoconus including central corneal thinning, Vogt’s striae, and partial Fleischer ring; ultrasound pachymetry 450 μm OD and 480 μm OS; topography with inferior steepening OD and mild thinning OS. No evidence of scarring, inflammation, or posterior segment pathology. IOPs normal; otherwise healthy. Diagnosis: keratoconus OD (bilateral disease likely asymmetric). Management: continued rigid gas-permeable contact lens rehabilitation for vision; monitor for progression with pachymetry and topography; consider corneal collagen cross-linking if progression occurs and surgical options (INTACS, DALK, PKP) if contact lens intolerance, progressive thinning, or scarring. Follow both eyes regularly for progression.
Primary Diagnosis: Keratoconus in the right eye
Secondary Diagnoses: Seasonal allergies
Differential Diagnosis: Keratoconus; Pellucid marginal degeneration; Keratoglobus; Terrien’s marginal degeneration
Severity: Mild
Laterality: Right eye
Complications: residual astigmatism, myopia, glaucoma, cataract, mydriasis, graft rejection


Plan
All Medications: acetaminophen; topical corticosteroids; riboflavin (with ultraviolet light for corneal cross-linking); rigid gas-permeable contact lenses; scleral or specialty hybrid contact lenses (as options)
All Procedures: rigid gas permeable contact lens fitting; corneal topography (computerized videokeratoscopy/Placido-disc topography); ultrasound pachymetry; corneal collagen cross-linking (UV light/riboflavin); intracorneal ring segments (INTACS) implantation; deep anterior lamellar keratoplasty (DALK); penetrating keratoplasty (PKP)
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow corneal status with regular exams while wearing rigid gas-permeable contact lens; monitor the left eye for development of keratoconus changes; consider corneal topography/pachymetry surveillance and contact lens reassessment; pursue surgical options (cross-linking, INTACS, DALK, or PKP) if progression, contact lens intolerance, or central scarring occur.
Patient Education: Keratoconus is a noninflammatory thinning and bulging of the central cornea that causes irregular astigmatism and decreased vision. It is usually bilateral but often asymmetric. Typical signs include decreased vision not fully correctable with glasses, corneal thinning, Vogt’s striae, and Fleischer ring; diagnosis is supported by corneal topography and pachymetry. Management depends on severity: early disease is managed with glasses or specialty contact lenses (rigid gas-permeable, scleral, or hybrid) to improve vision; progressive disease may be treated with corneal collagen cross-linking (riboflavin + UV) to slow progression. Advanced or scarring cases may require surgery (DALK or penetrating keratoplasty; intracorneal ring segments can help in selected cases). Avoid eye rubbing, control allergies, and use prescribed contact-lens hygiene. Regular follow-up with corneal topography and pachymetry is recommended to monitor progression; report any sudden vision decrease, pain, or redness promptly.


CASE 08 — Microbial Keratitis
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Decreased vision, pain, photophobia, redness, and discharge in left eye for 48 hours
DURATION: 48 hours
LATERALITY: OS
PATIENT QUOTES: "I have decreased vision, pain, photophobia, redness, and discharge in my left eye for the previous 48 hours."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 39-year-old woman with a history of contact lens wear presenting with 48 hours of decreased vision, pain, photophobia, redness, and purulent discharge in the left eye; left vision initially CF at 3′ with central dense gray-white necrotic-appearing corneal stromal infiltrate with epithelial loss, indistinct edges, 2+ anterior chamber cell/flare and 1-mm hypopyon.
History Narrative: A 39-year-old woman with a history of contact lens wear presents with 48 hours of decreased vision, pain, photophobia, redness, and discharge in her left eye. Visual acuity was 20/20 OD and count fingers at 3 feet OS. Right eye exam was unremarkable. Left external exam showed mild lid and conjunctival edema, a papillary conjunctival reaction, and a purulent discharge. The corneal stroma had a central, dense, gray‑white, necrotic-appearing infiltrate with loss of the overlying corneal epithelium; the edges of the infiltrate were indistinct and extended beyond the stromal opacity. The anterior chamber showed 2+ cell and flare and a 1‑mm hypopyon. The iris, lens, and retinal examinations were unremarkable. Subsequent exams: at 4 days vision 20/20 OD and 20/400 OS with minimal lid and conjunctival edema, scant purulent discharge, a condensing gray‑white corneal opacity with defined borders, and epithelial ingrowth at the edges; at 11 days vision 20/20 OD and 20/400 OS with further condensation of the stromal opacity and continued re‑epithelialization; at 1 month vision 20/20 OD and 20/50 OS with a central corneal stromal opacity.
Symptom Onset: 48 hours
Symptom Duration: 48 hours
Progression: Central dense necrotic infiltrate with epithelial defect → condensing gray-white stromal opacity with defined borders and partial re-epithelialization by day 4 → further condensation of stromal opacity and continued re-epithelialization by day 11 → central corneal stromal opacity with improved vision to 20/50 at 1 month
Characteristics: Central, dense, gray-white, necrotic-appearing stromal infiltrate with loss of overlying epithelium; indistinct, spreading edges; purulent discharge; 2+ AC cell/flare with 1 mm hypopyon; condensing gray-white stromal opacity with re-epithelialization over time


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Contact lens wear (including overnight wear and poor lens hygiene), ocular trauma, compromised ocular surface (lid dysfunction, ocular surface disease, conjunctival dysfunction, lacrimal dysfunction)
Associated Symptoms: decreased vision, pain, photophobia, redness, purulent discharge
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes reported
Prior Treatments: Contact lens wear
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant short-term impairment of left-eye activities (reduced vision to CF progressing to 20/400 then 20/50 over month), causing pain, photophobia, redness, and discharge that likely limited reading, driving, and contact‑lens use; gradual improvement with residual central corneal scarring that may cause persistent visual disturbance affecting tasks requiring fine visual acuity.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: Previous contact lens wear; history of trauma; compromised ocular surface (ocular surface disease); lid dysfunction; conjunctival dysfunction; lacrimal dysfunction; overnight contact lens wear and poor contact lens hygiene


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: CF 3'
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: CF 3'
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: mild lid edema
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: mild conjunctival edema and a papillary conjunctival reaction
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Within normal limits
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Central dense gray-white necrotic-appearing stromal infiltrate with overlying epithelial defect, indistinct edges extending beyond stromal opacity; later condensing central gray-white stromal opacity with progressive re-epithelialization leaving a central corneal stromal scar.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: 2+ cell and flare; 1-mm hypopyon
Anterior Segment – Iris: unremarkable
Anterior Segment – Lens: unremarkable


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Round, reactive to light, no afferent pupillary defect, pupils equal in size (no anisocoria)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk and symmetric
Pupils – RAPD: No RAPD
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Left eye: mild lid and conjunctival edema, papillary conjunctival reaction, purulent discharge; central dense gray-white necrotic-appearing stromal infiltrate with loss of overlying epithelium, indistinct edges extending beyond stromal opacity; anterior chamber 2+ cell and flare with 1-mm hypopyon. Right eye unremarkable.
Fundus Exam – Macula: Unremarkable
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Unable to examine (media clear; posterior segment unremarkable)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Streptococcal corneal ulcer, left eye, in a 39-year-old contact lens wearer presenting with 48 hours of decreased vision, pain, photophobia, redness, and purulent discharge. Exam OS: central dense gray-white necrotic-appearing stromal infiltrate with epithelial defect, indistinct margins, 2+ AC cell/flare and 1 mm hypopyon; VA CF 3′ initially, improving to 20/400 by day 4 and 20/50 by one month with condensation of stromal opacity and re-epithelialization. Cultures grew a Streptococcus species. Plan: aggressive topical broad-spectrum fortified antibiotics tailored to gram-positive organism with frequent dosing, possible substitution with a fluoroquinolone, careful consideration of topical steroids only after clinical improvement and culture guidance, adjunctive cycloplegia and enzyme inhibitors as indicated, and surgical intervention (glue or PK) reserved for impending or actual perforation. Follow-up for re-epithelialization, scar management, and modification of contact lens use to reduce recurrence risk.
Primary Diagnosis: Streptococcal corneal ulcer, left eye
Differential Diagnosis: Bacterial keratitis (contact lens–associated bacterial corneal ulcer); Fungal keratitis; Acanthamoeba keratitis
Severity: Severe
Laterality: OS
Complications: Corneal scarring; potential corneal perforation requiring glue or penetrating keratoplasty; reduced graft survival if keratoplasty performed in infected/inflamed cornea; decreased visual acuity.


Plan
All Medications: moxifloxacin 0.5% topical, prednisolone sodium phosphate 1% topical, fortified aminoglycosides (e.g., tobramycin/amikacin) topical, fortified cephalosporins (e.g., cefazolin/ceftazidime) topical, fluoroquinolones topical, penicillins (topical/systemic), synthetic penicillins topical, erythromycin topical, bacitracin topical, polymyxin topical, chloramphenicol topical, vancomycin topical, tetracycline (doxycycline oral for enzyme inhibition), sulfonamides topical/systemic, rifampin topical/systemic, topical corticosteroid drops (general), subconjunctival antibiotics (general)
All Procedures: scraping/corneal ulcer culture (corneal scrapings with plating onto media) topical fortified antibiotic therapy (e.g., hourly fortified agents and/or fluoroquinolones) subconjunctival antibiotic injections intravenous antibiotics (hospitalization for severe cases) topical corticosteroid drops (adjunctive, started after initial antibiotics per SCUT) cycloplegic agents oral doxycycline (enzyme inhibitor adjunct) collagen shield placement impregnated with antibiotics bandage contact lens placement self-retained amniotic membrane graft corneal glue application with bandage contact lens penetrating keratoplasty (therapeutic PK) deep anterior lamellar keratoplasty (DALK) conjunctival flap (not indicated for perforation)
Follow-Up Instructions: Once infection is controlled, evaluate and correct risk factors for recurrence (stop or modify contact lens wear and hygiene, treat lid/lacrimal/conjunctival dysfunction). Consider contact lens options (rigid gas-permeable or scleral) for visual rehabilitation of irregular cornea. For central or deep stromal scarring, discuss phototherapeutic keratectomy, deep anterior lamellar keratoplasty, or penetrating keratoplasty as indicated.
Patient Education: Educate the patient that her condition is a bacterial corneal ulcer likely related to contact lens wear; emphasize immediate, aggressive topical antibiotic therapy is necessary to prevent vision loss and that strict adherence to the prescribed hour‑by‑hour drops (initially very frequent) is critical. Explain the importance of returning for follow‑up examinations to monitor healing, and that cultures helped identify Streptococcus to guide treatment. Advise to stop contact lens wear until fully healed and to review proper lens hygiene and avoid overnight wear in the future. Warn about potential complications: corneal scarring that can decrease vision, possible need for surgical interventions (glue or corneal transplant) if perforation or medical failure occurs, and that steroid drops may be considered later only under close supervision. Instruct to seek urgent care for worsening pain, vision loss, increasing redness, or new discharge.
Work Restrictions: Avoid contact with work environments that risk ocular contamination or require contact lens wear; avoid heavy manual labor or activities risking eye trauma until corneal healing confirmed; follow up for return-to-work clearance once vision stabilizes and ocular surface healed.
Emergency Instructions: Seek immediate ophthalmic care for sudden worsening—increasing pain, vision loss, new flashes/floaters, or if eye becomes increasingly red, swollen, or discharging; cover the eye with a shield, avoid topical drops not prescribed, do not wear contact lenses, and go to the emergency department or clinic right away.


CASE 09 — Keratoconjunctivitis Sicca—Dry Eye
============================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: ocular irritation in both eyes
LATERALITY: bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "burning, foreign body sensation, sandy-gritty feeling, and redness, more in his right eye." "These symptoms were worse in the morning and also in the later part of the day, and made him unable to read or drive comfortably." "He still has preserved emotional lacrimation." "He stated that he slept on his stomach, preferring his right side."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 56-year-old man with diabetes mellitus referred for second opinion for unstable ocular surface/dry eye, complaining of bilateral ocular irritation—burning, foreign body sensation, sandy-gritty feeling, and redness worse in the right eye. Symptoms worse in the morning and later in the day, interfering with reading and driving; preserved emotional lacrimation. Sleeps on stomach favoring right side. Noted reduced blink rate; right worse than left.
History Narrative: A 56-year-old man with a history of diabetes mellitus and a chief complaint of ocular irritation in both eyes was referred by an ophthalmologist for a second opinion under the impression of an unstable ocular surface due to dry eye in his right eye. He complained of burning, foreign body sensation, sandy-gritty feeling, and redness, more in his right eye. These symptoms were worse in the morning and also in the later part of the day, and made him unable to read or drive comfortably. He still has preserved emotional lacrimation. He stated that he slept on his stomach, preferring his right side. While taking the history, it was noted that his blink rate was reduced in both eyes. His best-corrected visual acuity was 20/40 in the right eye and 20/20 in the left eye. External examination did not reveal features suggestive of rosacea. There were no palpable preauricular nodes. The lid position and relationship to the globe were normal, while the lid tension was loose and floppy. Lid tension was graded as 2+ in the right eye and 1+ in the left eye. On slit-lamp examination, the meibomian secretion was normal as its fluid appeared clear and was easily expressible. The height of the tear meniscus was low in the upper and lower lids of both eyes. Both tarsal and bulbar conjunctivae were diffusely injected with the tarsal conjunctiva, showing a mixed papillary and follicular response. Ocular sensitivity was markedly reduced as measured by a Cochet–Bonnet esthesiometer. The tear breakup time in each eye was less than 2 seconds. Fluorescein showed superficial punctate staining and rose bengal staining was positive over the exposure zone, more prominent in the right eye. Intraocular pressure was 15 and 16 mm Hg, in the right and left eye, respectively. Fluorescein clearance test (FCT) revealed marked delayed dye clearance and decreased tear secretion in both eyes. The lens and the fundus were unremarkable in both eyes.
Progression: Chronic
Characteristics: Keratoconjunctivitis sicca (dry eye) due to aqueous tear deficiency from diabetes-induced neurotrophic keratopathy, with delayed tear clearance from reduced blink rate and floppy eyelids; bilateral, right worse than left, symptoms: burning, foreign body sensation, gritty, redness, worse morning and late day; low tear meniscus (<0.2 mm), TBUT <2 s, positive fluorescein and rose bengal staining, markedly reduced corneal sensitivity, Schirmer (near 0 mm), preserved emotional tearing.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: - Diabetes mellitus–induced neurotrophic keratopathy (reduced corneal sensation) - Reduced blink rate - Floppy eyelids (side-sleeping on right) - Delayed tear clearance - Low tear meniscus/aqueous tear deficiency
Associated Symptoms: burning; foreign body sensation; sandy‑gritty feeling; redness; worse in morning and later in day; inability to read or drive comfortably; reduced blink rate; preserved emotional lacrimation
Impact on Daily Activities: Unable to read or drive comfortably; difficulty reading and driving due to symptoms.
Systemic Symptoms: diabetes mellitus
Risk Factors: Diabetes mellitus; reduced blink rate; floppy eyelids; aging (56 years); sleep position (right-side sleeping causing exposure); neurotrophic corneal hypoesthesia; possible contact lens use N/A


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+
Visual Acuity – Method: Best-corrected visual acuity (Snellen)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mm Hg
