Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: No external evidence of rosacea; lid position and relationship to globe normal; lid tension loose and floppy (R 2+, L 1+)
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Both tarsal and bulbar conjunctivae diffusely injected with mixed papillary and follicular response
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Superficial punctate keratitis with positive rose bengal staining over the exposure zone; tear breakup time <2 seconds; decreased corneal sensitivity (neurotrophic keratopathy).
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: unremarkable


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, and reactive to light (no abnormality noted)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk and equal bilaterally
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: Pupils equal and reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Meibomian secretions clear and easily expressible; low tear meniscus height in upper and lower lids OU; tarsal and bulbar conjunctivae diffusely injected with mixed papillary and follicular response; tear breakup time <2 seconds OU; fluorescein positive for superficial punctate staining; rose bengal positive over exposure zone, greater OD; lids floppy with decreased lid tension (R 2+, L 1+).
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: cup-to-disc ratio 0.3 (no optic nerve abnormality)
Fundus Exam – Macula: unremarkable
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Keratoconjunctivitis sicca due to aqueous tear deficiency secondary to diabetes mellitus–induced neurotrophic keratopathy, associated with delayed tear clearance from reduced blink rate and floppy eyelids; bilateral but worse OD, with decreased corneal sensitivity, low tear meniscus (<0.2 mm), TBUT <2 s, positive fluorescein and rose bengal staining in exposure zones, and markedly delayed fluorescein clearance.
Primary Diagnosis: Keratoconjunctivitis sicca due to aqueous tear deficiency secondary to diabetes mellitus–induced neurotrophic keratitis, associated with delayed tear clearance from reduced blink rate and floppy eyelids
Secondary Diagnoses: Diabetes mellitus; Floppy eyelid syndrome; Decreased blink rate (exposure-related dry eye)/neurotrophic keratopathy-related aqueous tear deficiency
Differential Diagnosis: Aqueous tear deficiency (neurotrophic keratopathy secondary to diabetes); Delayed tear clearance from reduced blink rate and floppy eyelids; Meibomian gland dysfunction/evaporative dry eye
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: Exposure keratitis, punctate keratitis, corneal epithelial damage (superficial punctate keratitis), delayed wound healing, increased risk of infection, vision disturbance, conjunctival inflammation, ocular surface scarring, possible progression to neurotrophic keratopathy


Plan
All Medications: proparacaine; Fluress (fluorescein 0.25%); artificial tears (preservative-free); ophthalmic gels and ointments; Lacrisert; autologous serum eye drops; pilocarpine; cevimeline; topical cyclosporine; topical corticosteroids (prednisolone, fluorometholone, loteprednol etabonate); oral tetracyclines; oral macrolides; topical macrolides; punctal plugs (device, not a medication)
All Procedures: Schirmer test; Fluorescein clearance test; Fluorescein tear breakup time testing; Cochet–Bonnet esthesiometry (ocular sensitivity testing); Instillation of topical anesthetic (proparacaine) and fluorescein; Schirmer strip testing with nasal stimulation; Reflective meniscometry; Anterior-segment OCT tear meniscus measurement; Videokeratography for tear-film analysis; Interferometry (e.g., LipiView) for lipid layer; Tear osmolarity testing (e.g., TearLab); Tear-protein/inflammatory biomarker testing (e.g., InflammaDry); Ocular surface thermography; Application of preservative-free artificial tears, gels, ointments; Lacrisert insertion; Autologous serum eye drops; Topical cyclosporine; Topical corticosteroid drops/ointments; Oral/topical tetracyclines and macrolides; Pilocarpine/cevimeline (systemic parasympathomimetics); Punctal plugs insertion; Thermal cauterization of puncta (punctal occlusion); Surgical punctal occlusion (thermal cautery) with consideration of permanent occlusion; Tarsorrhaphy; Botulinum toxin–induced ptosis; Conjunctival flap surgery; Repair of eyelid malposition (e.g., ectropion repair); Scleral or bandage contact lens placement; Self-retained amniotic membrane (ProKera or similar) placement; Warm compresses and lid hygiene; Thermal pulsation (e.g., LipiFlow); Intense pulsed light therapy; Lid massage
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow up with the ophthalmologist in 4–6 weeks to reassess symptoms, tear breakup time, staining, and fluorescein clearance; sooner if worsening pain, vision loss, marked redness, or signs of infection. Continue prescribed preservative‑free artificial tears frequently; use ointment/gel at night. Begin recommended adjuncts (warm compresses, lid hygiene, punctal plugs or other treatments as discussed) and report intolerance or side effects of medications (e.g., topical cyclosporine or steroids). Consider rheumatology referral/testing if systemic symptoms suggest Sjögren’s or autoimmune disease.
Patient Education: Explain that your symptoms are from dry eyes (keratoconjunctivitis sicca) caused by reduced tear production from diabetes-related corneal nerve damage and poor tear clearance from reduced blinking and floppy lids. Recommend frequent preservative‑free artificial tears (gels/ointments at night), warm compresses and lid hygiene to support oils, and strategies to increase blink rate (conscious blinking during reading/driving). Avoid preservative-containing drops when used frequently. Use punctal plugs or thermal punctal occlusion if drops are insufficient (after controlling inflammation). For significant inflammation short courses of topical steroid or topical cyclosporine may be used under supervision. Sleep position modification (avoid sleeping on the right side) can reduce morning worsening. Report worsening pain, vision loss, or signs of infection immediately. Follow up regularly; consider referral for specialty therapies (autologous serum, scleral lens, amniotic membrane) if symptoms/signs do not improve.


CASE 10 — Postsurgical Corneal Edema
====================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Decreased vision (especially at night) in both eyes for 1 year
DURATION: 1 week
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "decreased vision (especially at night) in both eyes for 1 year"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 55-year-old man with 1 year of progressively decreased vision in both eyes, worse at night, who underwent uncomplicated left cataract phacoemulsification and presented on postoperative day 1 with persistent blurred vision and diffuse central and temporal corneal edema in the left eye; prior history notable for 10 years of well-controlled diabetes, otherwise unremarkable ocular history.
History Narrative: A 55-year-old man with a 10-year history of well-controlled diabetes mellitus presented with a 1-year history of decreased vision (worse at night) in both eyes. Past ocular and family histories were unremarkable. Best corrected visual acuity was 20/50 OD and 20/100 OS. Pupils and ocular motility were normal. IOPs were 12 mm Hg OD and 13 mm Hg OS. Confrontation fields were full. Slit-lamp exam showed moderate nuclear sclerosis OU, worse in the left eye; fundus exam showed no diabetic retinopathy. Potential acuity testing showed 20/25 in each eye. A clinical diagnosis of bilateral cataract was made and left eye cataract surgery was recommended. The patient underwent uncomplicated temporal clear corneal phacoemulsification with posterior chamber acrylic IOL implantation in the left eye. On postoperative day 1 vision was 20/100 and IOP 18 mm Hg; slit-lamp exam revealed diffuse central and incision-site corneal edema and the patient complained of persistent blurred vision without major discomfort.
Symptom Onset: 1 year
Symptom Duration: 1 year
Progression: Resolved
Characteristics: Postsurgical corneal edema (diffuse central and temporal incision–site corneal edema)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Diabetes mellitus (10 years), moderate nuclear sclerosis/dense cataract (left worse), temporal clear corneal incision/proximity of phaco tip to endothelium
Alleviating Factors: Topical corticosteroids and hypertonic saline solution (4× daily for 1 week)
Associated Symptoms: decreased vision (especially at night), persistent blurred vision without major discomfort
Previous Episodes: None
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate transient impairment (reduced vision—especially at night—until edema resolved; ultimately returned to normal)
Systemic Symptoms: None
Risk Factors: preexisting low endothelial cell density; Fuchs’ endothelial dystrophy (corneal guttata); complicated surgery


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/76 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/50
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/50
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/100
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Best corrected visual acuity (BCVA)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 13 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Diffuse central corneal edema with temporal incision‑site edema (postsurgical corneal edema)
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Unremarkable
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open and normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: moderate nuclear sclerosis in both eyes (left worse than right)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Round and reactive, no afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Reaction to Light: unremarkable
Pupils – Reaction to Near: briskly reactive to near
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: unremarkable
Extraocular Movements: unremarkable
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Diffuse central corneal edema with involvement around the temporal incision site; otherwise unremarkable slit‑lamp exam (moderate nuclear sclerosis of the crystalline lens left > right).
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: cup-to-disc ratio 0.3, optic discs normal without pallor or swelling
Fundus Exam – Macula: No evidence of diabetic retinopathy
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 55-year-old man status post uncomplicated phacoemulsification with postop diffuse central and temporal corneal edema of the left eye causing decreased vision. Exam: postoperative day 1 VA 20/100 OS, IOP 18 mm Hg, diffuse corneal edema on slit-lamp. Fellow-eye specular microscopy showed low endothelial cell density without guttata, indicating preexisting low endothelial reserve as etiology. Diagnosis: postoperative corneal edema due to preexisting low endothelial cell density exacerbated by surgery. Plan: medical management with frequent topical corticosteroids, topical hypertonic saline, control IOP/inflammation as needed; monitor pachymetry/specular counts and consider endothelial keratoplasty (DSAEK/DMEK) or penetrating keratoplasty if edema persists or decompensation occurs.
Primary Diagnosis: Postsurgical corneal edema
Secondary Diagnoses: Diabetes mellitus (well controlled)
Differential Diagnosis: Preexisting endothelial disease/dysfunction (e.g., Fuchs’ dystrophy or low endothelial cell density); surgical trauma to the endothelium or Descemet’s detachment; postoperative toxic endothelial injury (medication/contaminant exposure); elevated postoperative IOP (including pupillary block)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Left


Plan
All Medications: prednisolone acetate 1% topical (hourly acute then topical corticosteroids qid for 1 week) 5% sodium chloride topical solution/ointment (hypertonic saline) qid for 1 week topical ophthalmic aqueous suppressants (if IOP elevation) topical antiglaucoma medications (if IOP elevation) systemic carbonic anhydrase inhibitors (if IOP elevation) intracameral epinephrine (mentioned as potential toxicant; preservative‑free recommended) intracameral preservative‑free lidocaine (recommended) subconjunctival corticosteroid injection (considered for severe inflammation) cyclosporin A (systemic immunotherapy, selective patients)
All Procedures: phacoemulsification via temporal clear corneal incision with implantation of a posterior chamber acrylic intraocular lens; specular microscopy; corneal pachymetry; topical corticosteroid therapy (prednisolone acetate 1%); topical hypertonic saline (5% sodium chloride) solution/ointment; therapeutic hydrophilic contact lens fitting; penetrating keratoplasty (PK); Descemet’s stripping automated endothelial keratoplasty (DSAEK); Descemet’s membrane endothelial keratoplasty (DMEK); subconjunctival corticosteroid injection; topical and systemic carbonic anhydrase inhibitors (for IOP control); topical antiglaucoma medications
Follow-Up Instructions: Topical corticosteroids and hypertonic saline 4 times daily for 1 week; follow-up visit in 1 week to assess corneal clarity and visual acuity (earlier if worsening symptoms or pain)
Patient Education: Corneal edema after cataract surgery is usually temporary. It results from reduced function of the corneal endothelial cells that normally pump fluid out of the cornea. Treatment aims to reduce inflammation, lower any elevated eye pressure, and draw fluid out of the cornea so vision can recover. What happened to you - After your cataract operation the cornea became swollen (cloudy), causing blurred vision. This can be due to preexisting low endothelial cell counts or temporary surgical trauma. What we are doing - Strong topical steroid drops to reduce inflammation. - Hypertonic saline (5% sodium chloride) drops/ointment to help remove fluid from the cornea. - If eye pressure is high, glaucoma medicines or oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitors may be used. - In mild cases a therapeutic soft contact lens can improve comfort and vision. - If edema does not improve over weeks to months, surgical options that replace only the damaged inner corneal layer (DSAEK or DMEK) or, less commonly, full-thickness corneal transplant (PK) can restore vision. What you can expect - Many cases improve gradually over days to weeks with medical treatment; vision often returns as the edema clears. - Follow-up visits with measurements of corneal thickness and endothelial cell assessment may be done to monitor recovery. - Return sooner if you have increased pain, worsening vision, redness, or sensitivity to light. Self-care and precautions - Use prescribed drops exactly as directed and finish the course unless advised otherwise. - Avoid rubbing the operated eye. - Keep follow-up appointments; notify your doctor if symptoms worsen. - Ask before using any other eye drops, especially over-the-counter products. When surgery may be needed - If corneal edema is persistent and significantly limits vision despite medical therapy, endothelial keratoplasty (DSAEK or DMEK) is an effective option to replace damaged endothelial cells and improve vision.


CASE 11 — Dellen
================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Peripheral corneal thinning adjacent to filtering bleb (dellen) with fluorescein pooling and epithelial cell loss in right eye.
DURATION: 2 weeks
LATERALITY: Right
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 55-year-old man with glaucoma status post multiple prior filtration procedures who developed elevated IOP on maximal medical therapy and underwent repeat trabeculectomy with a new flap at the 7 o’clock position to avoid prior site; two weeks postoperatively he was found to have a moderately elevated filtering bleb from 5 to 8 o’clock and developed peripheral corneal thinning adjacent to the bleb with fluorescein pooling and a small area of epithelial cell loss.
History Narrative: A 55-year-old man with a history of glaucoma that required multiple filtration procedures in the right eye was found to have elevated intraocular pressure on maximal medical therapy. A repeat trabeculectomy procedure was performed. In order to avoid the previous surgical site, the trabeculectomy flap was established at the 7 o’clock position, which resulted in a moderately elevated filtering bleb extending from the 5 to 8 o’clock positions. Two weeks after surgery, peripheral corneal thinning was noted adjacent to the filtering bleb. Fluorescein instillation revealed pooling with a small area of epithelial cell loss.
Symptom Onset: 2 weeks after surgery
Symptom Duration: 2 weeks
Progression: Two weeks after surgery; peripheral corneal thinning noted adjacent to the bleb
Characteristics: - Associated with underlying dry eye or meibomian gland dysfunction - Well-circumscribed, “saucerlike” area of peripheral corneal thinning with sloping edges - Often adjacent to an obvious limbal elevation disrupting tear distribution (e.g., filtering bleb, dermoid, conjunctival elevation) - Tear film disrupted over the area with blinking; fluorescein may pool in the depression - Epithelium usually intact at the base (unless chronic desiccation leads to epithelial breakdown, scarring, or true tissue loss) - Area rehydrates and thickens rapidly with generous viscous lubrication and patching, confirming diagnosis


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Dry eye / inadequate tear distribution (including meibomian gland dysfunction, blepharitis/meibomitis) and adjacent limbal elevation from a filtering bleb (or other conjunctival elevation)
Alleviating Factors: Rehydration with generous viscous lubricating agents (e.g., 1% methylcellulose or ophthalmic ointment) and patching for 15–30 minutes; preservative‑free artificial tears, bland lubricating ointment, punctal occlusion, bandage contact lens or self‑retained amniotic membrane graft; oral doxycycline and vitamin C (if extreme thinning); lid hygiene (warm compresses and meibomian gland massage)
Associated Symptoms: Dry eye symptoms (ocular irritation/irritation), pooling of fluorescein indicating localized epithelial cell loss (possible epithelial breakdown), signs of meibomian gland dysfunction/blepharitis (lid disease)
Prior Treatments: multiple filtration procedures in the right eye; repeat trabeculectomy (trabeculectomy flap at 7 o’clock)
Impact on Daily Activities: Mild to moderate temporary impact due to ocular irritation and visual disturbance from peripheral corneal thinning, manageable with lubrication and usually not causing long‑term functional disability.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: - Elevated filtering bleb (limbal/conjunctival elevation) - Dry eye disease - Meibomian gland dysfunction / blepharitis - Prior filtration/conjunctival surgery - Poor tear distribution due to adjacent limbal elevation


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 76 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+ (Jaeger)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen — 20/40 (right eye)
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: No ptosis; lids without erythema or edema; lashes normal, no trichiasis or madarosis.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Filtering bleb extending from 5 to 8 o'clock with adjacent conjunctival elevation; otherwise unremarkable conjunctiva/sclera.
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Peripheral corneal thinning (dellen) adjacent to an elevated filtering bleb with localized epithelial loss/pooling of fluorescein
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Phakic


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Brisk
Pupils – RAPD: Equal, no RAPD
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion OU without restriction
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Moderately elevated filtering bleb from 5 to 8 o’clock with adjacent well‑circumscribed peripheral “saucer‑like” corneal thinning (delle) at the temporal periphery of the right cornea with fluorescein pooling in the excavation; epithelium largely intact on base after removal of excess dye; no significant conjunctival injection or corneal infiltrate noted.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Delle in right eye due to dry eye in the setting of a filtering bleb.
Primary Diagnosis: Delle in right eye due to dry eye in the setting of a filtering bleb
Secondary Diagnoses: Glaucoma (history of multiple filtration procedures); Dry eye; Meibomian gland dysfunction / blepharitis
Differential Diagnosis: Dellen; Peripheral ulcerative keratitis (inflammatory autoimmune causes e.g., rheumatoid arthritis, Mooren’s ulcer); Degenerative peripheral thinning (Terrien’s marginal degeneration, pellucid marginal degeneration)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right eye
Complications: Corneal epithelial breakdown progressing to true noninfectious corneal ulceration with stromal tissue loss, scarring, and potential progressive thinning (keratolysis)


Plan
All Medications: doxycycline, vitamin C, preservative-free artificial tears (lubricating drops), ophthalmic lubricating ointment, methylcellulose 1% solution, oral doxycycline
All Procedures: trabeculectomy; fluorescein instillation; punctal occlusion; application of preservative‑free artificial tears and ophthalmic lubricating ointment; oral doxycycline; oral vitamin C; bandage contact lens placement; self‑retained amniotic membrane graft; secured amniotic membrane grafting; limited tarsorrhaphy; surgical removal/excision of conjunctival elevation/pyogenic granuloma/dermoid; meibomian gland treatments (warm compresses and gland massage); LipiFlow thermal pulsation system
Follow-Up Instructions: Observe and treat long term for signs and symptoms of dry eye contributing to dellen formation; monitor healing and watch for progression or recurrent thinning, with escalation to bandage contact lens, amniotic membrane graft, oral doxycycline/vitamin C, punctal occlusion, or surgical intervention (amniotic membrane grafting and/or limited tarsorrhaphy) if nonresponsive.
Patient Education: Dellen are a localized area of peripheral corneal thinning caused by poor tear coverage, often next to a raised area on the eye (e.g., a filtering bleb). They can usually be treated successfully with frequent preservative-free artificial tears, a bland lubricating ointment at night, and measures to improve eyelid oil (warm compresses and lid massage). Punctal occlusion may be used to conserve tears. If thinning persists, oral doxycycline and vitamin C, a bandage contact lens, or an in‑office amniotic membrane graft can be recommended; surgery (amniotic membrane with or without tarsorrhaphy) is reserved for refractory cases. Avoid rubbing the eye, use prescribed drops as directed, and return promptly for follow-up if pain, increased redness, vision change, or growing epithelial defect occurs.


CASE 12 — Graft Rejection Following Penetrating Keratoplasty
============================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: redness, photophobia, and blurred vision in the right eye for 1 week
DURATION: 1 week
LATERALITY: OD
PATIENT QUOTES: "redness, photophobia, and blurred vision in his right eye for 1 week"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 30-year-old man with 1 week of redness, photophobia, and blurred vision in the right eye. Past ocular history of keratoconus with penetrating keratoplasty OD 5 months prior; VA 1 month earlier was 20/60 (20/30 pinhole). On presentation VA OD 20/400 no pinhole improvement; IOP 17 mm Hg OU. Symptoms accompanied by ciliary flush, broken exposed interrupted suture with surrounding infiltrate at 11 o’clock on the graft, marked stromal edema extending 2 mm into the graft from the broken suture, a line of keratic precipitates at the central edge of stromal edema, and 1+ cell and flare in the anterior chamber. Left eye stable with RGP contact lens.
History Narrative: A 30-year-old man presented with 1 week of redness, photophobia, and blurred vision in his right eye. Past ocular history was keratoconus with a penetrating keratoplasty in the right eye 5 months earlier. One month prior, right eye visual acuity was 20/60 improving to 20/30 with pinhole. At presentation, visual acuity was 20/400 in the right eye (no pinhole improvement) and 20/20 in the left eye with a rigid gas-permeable contact lens. Intraocular pressure was 17 mm Hg in both eyes. He had 2+ ciliary flush in the right eye, a broken exposed interrupted suture with a surrounding infiltrate at the graft 11 o’clock meridian, marked stromal edema extending 2 mm into the graft from the broken suture, a line of keratic precipitates demarcating the central edge of the stromal edema, and a 1+ cell and flare in the anterior chamber. The left eye was quiet with a well-positioned rigid gas-permeable contact lens, central corneal thinning and mild protrusion, and a Fleischer ring.
Symptom Onset: 1 week
Symptom Duration: 1 week
Progression: Worsening
Characteristics: Redness, photophobia, and blurred vision in the right eye for 1 week; 2+ ciliary flush, broken exposed interrupted suture with surrounding infiltrate at 11 o’clock, marked stromal edema extending 2 mm into graft from suture, line of keratic precipitates at edge of edema, 1+ anterior chamber cell and flare, visual acuity OD 20/400 (no pinhole improvement)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Broken suture with surrounding infiltrate (suture abscess)/suture-related inflammation, stromal vascularization, prior inflammation (iritis), epithelial or subepithelial rejection, trauma, large or eccentric grafts, repeat grafts
Alleviating Factors: Timely intervention with aggressive topical corticosteroids (± systemic or subconjunctival steroids), removal of broken suture and treatment of suture abscess, cycloplegics for comfort, and prompt follow-up to monitor response
Associated Symptoms: redness; photophobia; blurred vision; foreign body sensation
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes of graft rejection reported
Prior Treatments: Penetrating keratoplasty in right eye 5 months earlier
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant—markedly reduced right-eye vision (20/400), photophobia, redness, and discomfort limiting normal activities until treated
Systemic Symptoms: None
Risk Factors: stromal vascularization; large-diameter grafts; eccentric grafts; repeat grafts; any postoperative inflammation (iritis, mild trauma, epithelial or subepithelial rejection, broken sutures)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/76 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 76 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen eye chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 17 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 17 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal appearing lids and lashes without blepharitis or malposition
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: 2+ ciliary flush in the right eye; left eye quiet
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Right corneal graft with broken exposed interrupted suture and suture abscess at 11 o’clock, marked stromal edema extending 2 mm into graft from suture with central-edge keratic precipitates (endothelial rejection); 2+ ciliary flush; prior penetrating keratoplasty (5 months) for keratoconus.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: 1+ cell and flare
Anterior Segment – Iris: 1+ cell and flare
Anterior Segment – Lens: Phakic


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal and reactive to light in both eyes, normal size and round
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk bilaterally
Pupils – RAPD: 0 (no RAPD)
Extraocular Movements: Full extraocular movements intact without restriction in both eyes
Slit Lamp Exam Description: 2+ ciliary flush OD; broken, exposed interrupted suture with surrounding infiltrate at 11 o’clock; marked stromal edema extending 2 mm into graft from broken suture with a line of keratic precipitates demarcating central edge of stromal edema; anterior chamber with 1+ cell and flare; OS quiet with well‑positioned RGP contact lens, central corneal thinning and mild protrusion, Fleischer ring.
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 30-year-old man status post penetrating keratoplasty OD (5 months) presenting with 1 week of redness, photophobia, and decreased vision. Exam OD: VA 20/400 no pinhole improvement, 2+ ciliary injection, broken exposed interrupted suture with surrounding infiltrate at 11 o'clock, marked stromal edema extending 2 mm into graft from suture site, line of keratic precipitates at central edge of edema, AC 1+ cell and flare, IOP 17 mm Hg. Diagnosis: broken suture with suture abscess and endothelial corneal graft rejection OD. OS: keratoconus with rigid gas permeable lens, VA 20/20. Management plan: remove broken suture at slit lamp, culture and treat suture abscess with topical fortified fluoroquinolone, reduce/discontinue topical steroid initially if abscess present then resume/increase topical prednisolone acetate 1% frequently (up to hourly) for endothelial rejection with taper over weeks as response permits; consider cycloplegic for comfort; monitor closely with follow-up in 2–4 days; consider systemic or subconjunctival steroids if severe or recalcitrant; if no improvement over weeks, consider repeat PK or DSAEK depending on corneal status.
Primary Diagnosis: Endothelial corneal graft rejection
Secondary Diagnoses: Broken suture with suture abscess; Keratoconus (left eye)
Differential Diagnosis: Epithelial graft rejection; Subepithelial graft rejection; Endothelial graft rejection; Suture abscess/infectious keratitis
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Right
Complications: Broken suture with suture abscess; endothelial corneal graft rejection; corneal stromal edema extending into graft; keratic precipitates; anterior chamber inflammation (1+ cell and flare); potential graft vascularization leading to graft failure


Plan
All Medications: Polytrim (trimethoprim/polymyxin) drops; bacitracin ointment; topical fluoroquinolone (e.g., ciprofloxacin) drops; prednisolone acetate 1% drops; scopolamine 0.25% drops; oral prednisone; subconjunctival/transseptal triamcinolone; systemic cyclosporin A
All Procedures: penetrating keratoplasty (PK); suture removal at the slit lamp; corneal suture lysis; topical antibiotic application (e.g., Polytrim, bacitracin, fluoroquinolone); corneal culture for suture abscess; topical corticosteroid therapy (prednisolone acetate 1%); cycloplegic agent use (e.g., scopolamine 0.25%); systemic corticosteroids (e.g., oral prednisone); subconjunctival/transseptal steroid injection (e.g., triamcinolone); repeat penetrating keratoplasty (repeat PK); Descemet’s stripping automated endothelial keratoplasty (DSAEK); rigid gas permeable contact lens fitting
Follow-Up Instructions: Typically follow up every 2–4 days for more severe rejection or ~1 week for less severe cases; decrease visit frequency as rejection is controlled and topical steroids are tapered, then return to visual rehabilitation (suture lysis, glasses, or rigid contact lens fitting as appropriate).
Patient Education: Seek evaluation within 24 hours for any new photophobia, foreign body sensation, redness, or decreased vision; understand that timely presentation is critical to prevent graft failure. Remove broken sutures promptly; follow prescribed topical antibiotics for prophylaxis or treatment of suture abscesses. Be aware that epithelial/subepithelial rejections can trigger endothelial rejection and to report symptoms early. Expect frequent follow-up (every 2–4 days for severe episodes or weekly for milder cases) while undergoing treatment and gradual tapering of topical steroids as inflammation resolves.
Emergency Instructions: Seek evaluation within 24 hours of any new symptoms (photophobia, foreign body sensation, red eye, or decreased vision).


CASE 13 — Blepharitis
=====================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: bilateral itching, burning, foreign body sensation, and crusting eyelids in the morning
DURATION: 2 years
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "I have bilateral itching, burning, a foreign body sensation, and crusting eyelids in the morning."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 42-year-old woman with a 2‑year history of intermittent bilateral eyelid itching, burning, foreign body sensation, and morning crusting of the lashes, recently worsened. Symptoms are bilateral, unrelated to systemic illness, allergy, rosacea, or flu-like symptoms, and she is not using ocular medications.
History Narrative: A 42-year-old woman with no past ocular history presents with complaints of bilateral itching, burning, foreign body sensation, and crusting eyelids in the morning. Although she has experienced these symptoms intermittently over the last 2 years, her symptoms have recently become worse. She denies taking any ocular medications. Her symptoms are unrelated to any systemic illness including allergy, rosacea, or flulike symptoms.
Symptom Onset: Over the last 2 years
Symptom Duration: 2 years
Progression: Chronic, relapsing condition with intermittent symptoms worsening recently.
Characteristics: Staphylococcal anterior blepharitis with bilateral eyelid margin erythema, scaling/crusting at lash bases, trichiasis, madarosis, poliosis, lid ulceration, mild conjunctival hyperemia with inferior papillary reaction, diminished tear lake, inferior superficial punctate epithelial erosions, and mild aqueous tear deficiency.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Intermittent worsening over 2 years, poor eyelid hygiene (crusting and scales), warm weather/allergens not reported; mechanical irritation from frequent/rough handling of eyelids, meibomian gland dysfunction, dry eye/aqueous tear deficiency, chronic relapsing course
Alleviating Factors: Eyelid hygiene (mechanical debridement/lid scrubs, warm compresses), gentle eyelid massage to express meibomian secretions, preservative‑free artificial tears 4–8×/day, topical antibiotics/antibiotic ointment at bedtime, short-course topical steroids (for symptomatic relief), omega‑3 fatty acid supplementation, LipiFlow/thermal pulsation (for refractory MGD)
Associated Symptoms: itching, burning, foreign body sensation, morning crusting of eyelids, mild conjunctival hyperemia, diminished tear lake, inferior punctate epithelial erosions, symptoms of dry eye
Previous Episodes: Intermittent episodes over the last 2 years with recent worsening
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — intermittent symptoms (itching, burning, foreign body sensation, morning crusting) causing discomfort and requiring regular eyelid hygiene and frequent artificial tear use, but visual acuity and IOP normal so activities not severely limited.
Systemic Symptoms: Denies systemic illness including allergy, rosacea, or flulike symptoms
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Staphylococcal eyelid colonization (Staphylococcus aureus/epidermidis), seborrheic dermatitis/seborrhea, meibomian gland dysfunction, dry eye disease/aqueous tear deficiency, rosacea (ocular acne rosacea), dermatologic conditions (eczema), contact lens wear, recurrent chalazia, poor eyelid hygiene, prior ocular surface inflammation, age-related meibomian gland changes, possible exposure to infected individuals, immunosuppression (inferred risk for atypical/refractory cases)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+ (20/20)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Mild erythema of the eyelid margins with scaling and crusting at the base of the eyelashes; evidence of trichiasis, madarosis, poliosis, and lid ulceration.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Mild conjunctival hyperemia with mild inferior tarsal papillary reaction.
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis; orbits normocephalic and non-tender (no palpable masses)
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Inferior superficial punctate epithelial erosions
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open (normal)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lens without cataract changes


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils equal, round, and reactive to light (normal)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: PERRL
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Pupils – Other: Pupils equal, round, reactive to light and accommodation
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Mild erythema of the eyelid margin with scaling and crusting at the base of the eyelashes; evidence of trichiasis, madarosis, poliosis, and eyelid ulceration; mild conjunctival hyperemia with mild inferior tarsal papillary reaction; diminished tear lake bilaterally; inferior superficial punctate epithelial erosions on rose bengal staining; otherwise normal cornea and IOP; visual acuity 20/20 OU.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Disc normal, no cupping or pallor observed
Fundus Exam – Macula: Within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Anterior blepharitis, staphylococcal type with associated mild bilateral aqueous tear deficiency and inferior punctate epithelial erosions; chronic, relapsing course with eyelid margin erythema, scaling/crusting, madarosis, and mild conjunctival papillary reaction. Patient educated on eyelid hygiene and lubrication; follow-up in 3–6 weeks or sooner if treated with topical steroids.
Primary Diagnosis: Anterior blepharitis, staphylococcal type.
Secondary Diagnoses: Dry eye disease (aqueous tear deficiency)
Differential Diagnosis: Allergic conjunctivitis; Dry eye syndrome (aqueous tear deficiency/meibomian gland dysfunction); Giant papillary conjunctivitis; Pediculosis
Severity: Mild to moderate
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: structural damage (trichiasis, madarosis, poliosis, eyelid ulceration, scarring, eyelash/eyelid malposition), corneal disease (inferior punctate epithelial erosions, marginal infiltrates, phlyctenules, neovascularization, thinning, keratitis), recurrent chalazia, potential for malignancy masking (sebaceous cell carcinoma, basal cell carcinoma, squamous cell carcinoma), chronic relapsing ocular surface disease with dry eye and possible vision impact


Plan
All Medications: erythromycin ointment; sulfacetamide ointment; bacitracin ointment; topical steroids (short-pulse); preservative-free artificial tears (lubricants) four to eight times daily; hypochlorous acid 0.01% lid cleanser; topical azithromycin ophthalmic solution; oral minocycline; doxycycline 50–100 mg orally twice daily; tetracycline 250 mg orally four times daily; oral omega-3 fatty acid (DHA/EPA) 1–3 g/day
All Procedures: Lid hygiene and cleaning (lid scrubs, cotton‑tip debridement) Application of topical antibiotic ointment (eg, erythromycin, bacitracin, sulfacetamide) Warm compresses and eyelid massage (meibomian gland expression) Topical steroid short‑pulse therapy Oral antibiotics for meibomian disease (doxycycline, minocycline, tetracycline) Omega‑3 fatty acid supplementation Eyelid bacterial culture and sensitivity testing Eyelid biopsy (for suspected malignancy) Surgical management of eyelid/eyelash malposition and scarring LipiFlow thermal pulsation system Intense pulsed light (IPL) therapy
Follow-Up Instructions: Initial follow-up in 3–6 weeks (earlier if on topical steroids to monitor response and IOP); thereafter as needed or at routine visits. Continue maintenance lid hygiene, warm compresses, and artificial tears; reinforce chronic, relapsing nature and tailor regimen to symptom severity.
Patient Education: Blepharitis is a chronic, relapsing eyelid inflammation that requires regular eyelid hygiene and maintenance. Perform daily gentle lid cleaning (cotton‑tip or lid scrubs with mild shampoo or a commercial lid cleanser such as hypochlorous 0.01%), use warm compresses to soften debris, and gently massage lids to express oils as needed. Use preservative‑free artificial tears 4–8×/day for dry eye symptoms. Apply topical antibiotic ointment (e.g., erythromycin) at bedtime for more severe staphylococcal flares as directed. Short courses of topical steroids may be used for severe inflammation under supervision. Consider oral omega‑3 supplementation (1–3 g/day DHA/EPA) for meibomian dysfunction. Avoid frequent/rough handling of the lids. Return for follow‑up in 3–6 weeks if severe or sooner if using steroids or if symptoms are worsening or unilateral/asymmetric (to evaluate for possible malignancy).


CASE 14 — A Nearly Mature Cataract in a Patient with Glaucoma
=============================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Nearly mature cataract in the right eye (with glaucoma)
DURATION: 2 years
LATERALITY: Right
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 34-year-old Caucasian woman referred for evaluation of a nearly mature cataract in the right eye; complex ocular history notable for congenital glaucoma with multiple prior right‑eye surgeries (goniotomies, peripheral iridectomies, sphincterotomies, trabeculectomy), phthisical left eye, progressive vision loss in the right eye to counting fingers, inability to dilate pupil due to 360° posterior synechiae, and concern for zonular compromise.
History Narrative: A 34-year-old Caucasian woman with a complex ocular history including congenital glaucoma and a phthisical left eye was referred for evaluation of a nearly mature cataract in her right eye. She had undergone numerous prior right-eye glaucoma surgeries (goniotomies, peripheral iridectomies, sphincterotomies, and a trabeculectomy) and intermittently used glaucoma medications and bleb massage. Visual acuity was finger counting in the right eye and no light perception in the left. She had a functioning superior-nasal peripheral iridotomy used as an entrance pupil and a relatively thick-walled but functioning superior-nasal filtering bleb. IOP was 20 mm Hg off medications. The cornea showed a few guttae; the anterior chamber was deep and quiet. The iris was highly atrophic with very little sphincter tissue, a 3 mm pupil that did not dilate due to 360° posterior synechiae, multiple sphincterotomies at the pupillary margin, and a large radial cut at 12 o’clock. There was a dense, nearly mature cataract with absence of visible zonules through the large superior-nasal iridectomy and questionable zonular status in other quadrants. No view of the right fundus was obtainable.
Progression: Progressive


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: - Congenital glaucoma with prior multiple intraocular surgeries (goniotomies, peripheral iridectomies, sphincterotomies, trabeculectomy) leading to zonular compromise - Monocular status with phthisical fellow eye - Functioning superior nasal filtering bleb limiting surgical access - Atrophic iris with 360° posterior synechiae and radial sphincterotomies (small, non-dilating pupil) - Large axial myopia (axial length 30 mm) and large white-to-white (>14 mm) - Corneal endothelial compromise from prior surgeries (few guttae) increasing risk of decompensation - Unreliable preoperative measurements (keratometry/topography discordance)
Prior Treatments: Goniotomies; peripheral iridectomies; sphincterotomies; trabeculectomy
Impact on Daily Activities: She is able to use a computer at work, grow flowers, and is very aware of colors and brightness of objects — markedly improved functional vision enabling daily activities.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: - Monocular status (phthisical fellow eye) - Congenital glaucoma with multiple prior glaucoma surgeries (goniotomies, peripheral iridectomies, sphincterotomies, trabeculectomy) with functioning filtering bleb - Zonular weakness/compromise - Nearly mature/dense cataract - Atrophic iris with 360° posterior synechiae and radial sphincterotomies (small non-dilating pupil/corectopia) - Large axial myopia (axial length 30 mm) - Prior intraocular surgeries (increased risk for corneal endothelial loss) - Corneal guttae (endothelial compromise) - High astigmatism (6–7 D) - Elevated preop IOP (20 mm Hg)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 122/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 76
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: Counting fingers
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: NLP
Visual Acuity – Method: finger counting
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 20 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: A few corneal guttae noted; conjunctiva/sclera with a relatively thick-walled but functioning superior nasal filtering bleb.
External Exam – Orbit: Normal appearing orbit.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: A few corneal guttae
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Highly atrophic throughout entire periphery with multiple sphincterotomies, corectopic entrance via superior-nasal peripheral iridectomy, 3 mm pupil with 360° posterior synechiae, large radial cut at 12 o'clock, uses superior-nasal PI as entrance pupil.
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open (deep and quiet anterior chamber; functioning trabeculectomy; no specifics of synechial angle closure mentioned)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Dense, nearly mature cataract with zonular compromise; capsular tension ring and 6‑D foldable silicone IOL implanted in the capsular bag.


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 3 mm, entrance pupil via superior nasal peripheral iridectomy with corectopia (irregular/atrophic pupil with radial sphincterotomies and large 12 o'clock radial cut)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: sluggish/reactive
Pupils – RAPD: Positive (afferent pupillary defect)
Pupils – Other: 360 degrees of posterior synechiae; multiple sphincterotomies and a large radial cut at 12 o’clock; superior nasal peripheral iridotomy used as entrance pupil
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: A few corneal guttae; anterior chamber deep and quiet; iris highly atrophic throughout with very little sphincter tissue, multiple sphincterotomies at the pupillary margin and a large radial cut at 12 o’clock; pupil 3 mm with 360° posterior synechiae (did not dilate); dense nearly mature cataract; absence of visible zonules through the large superior nasal iridectomy; relatively thick‑walled but functioning superior‑nasal filtering bleb.
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Retina flat (no view of fundus preop); peripheral exam N/A


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Mature cataract of the right eye with zonular compromise in a monocular patient with congenital glaucoma status post multiple prior glaucoma surgeries (goniotomies, peripheral iridectomies, sphincterotomies, trabeculectomy) with a functioning superior-nasal filtering bleb, atrophic iris with radial sphincterotomies and 360° posterior synechiae resulting in a fixed 3 mm pupil, questionable zonules and absent zonules superior nasally, deep quiet anterior chamber, few corneal guttae and prior endothelial compromise, axial myopia (axial length 30 mm), flat retina on B-scan, and blind phthisical left eye — high surgical risk for zonular dialysis, vitreous prolapse, corneal decompensation, and postoperative IOP elevation; surgical plan: cataract extraction with iris-expander ring, capsular tension ring placement, careful phacoemulsification (choo-choo chop/flip technique) with endothelial protection and preparedness for anterior vitrectomy.
Primary Diagnosis: Mature cataract with zonular compromise, advanced glaucoma status post multiple surgeries including a trabeculectomy with a functioning filtering bleb, atrophic iris with radial sphincterotomies, and blind phthisical fellow eye
Secondary Diagnoses: congenital glaucoma; phthisical left eye; atrophic iris with radial sphincterotomies and corectopia; zonular compromise; functioning filtering bleb (post‑trabeculectomy); corneal guttae; prior multiple intraocular surgeries
Differential Diagnosis: Mature cataract with zonular compromise; Postsurgical/traumatic iris atrophy with sphincterotomy-related pupillary abnormality; Chronic glaucoma-related optic/visual impairment (status post trabeculectomy)
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Right
Complications: Vitreous prolapse through the superior nasal iridectomy (requiring anterior vitrectomy); risk of corneal endothelial cell loss/decompensation; potential postoperative IOP spike (managed with topical meds).


Plan
All Medications: Viscoat, Provisc, Healon 5, prednisolone acetate (topical), ofloxacin (topical), diclofenac (topical), timolol, brimonidine, prostaglandin analog (avoided), carbonic anhydrase inhibitor (avoided)
All Procedures: - Paracentesis and clear corneal incision with 16-mm Fine/Thornton ring fixation - Side-port incision - Viscoelastic (Viscoat, Provisc, Healon 5) administration (soft-shell technique) - Lysis of posterior synechiae (blunt and viscodissection) - Insertion of Morcher iris-expander ring - Continuous curvilinear capsulorrhexis - Insertion of Morcher capsular tension ring - Cortical cleaving hydrodissection and hydrodelineation - Phacoemulsification using chopper ("choo choo chop and flip") with reverse Kelman/30° bevel-down tip - Irrigation and aspiration of cortex (tangential stripping) - Injection and implantation of 6-diopter foldable silicone intraocular lens into capsular bag - Main wound closure with suture - Split-port anterior vitrectomy (anterior vitrectomy via separate paracentesis) - Removal of residual viscoelastic with vitrector - Stromal hydration to seal incisions
Follow-Up Instructions: Start topical prednisolone acetate, ofloxacin, and diclofenac TID. Monitor IOP closely — examine twice daily for 3 days. Start prophylactic timolol and brimonidine to control IOP; avoid prostaglandin analogs and oral/topical carbonic anhydrase inhibitors. Follow routine postoperative visits (week 1 and as indicated).
Patient Education: Education about risks and postoperative care: explanation of complex surgery due to congenital glaucoma, atrophic iris, zonular weakness, and prior bleb; need for iris-expander and capsular tension ring to protect zonules and cornea; possibility of vitreous prolapse and anterior vitrectomy; importance of close IOP monitoring (twice daily for first 3 days) and prophylactic glaucoma drops (timolol, brimonidine) with avoidance of prostaglandin analogs and carbonic anhydrase inhibitors early post-op; strict postoperative topical regimen (prednisolone acetate, ofloxacin, diclofenac three times daily); signs to report immediately (pain, sudden vision loss, redness, discharge, or rapidly increased IOP); activity and wound-care instructions (protect eye, avoid heavy lifting/straining, no eye rubbing) and follow-up schedule with early visits and long-term monitoring for IOP and corneal/endothelial status.


CASE 15 — Retained Lens Material after Cataract Extraction
==========================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Decreased vision, redness, and pain in the left eye for 2 months
DURATION: 2 months
LATERALITY: OS
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 47-year-old man with prior bungee cord trauma to the left eye underwent complicated cataract surgery 9 months earlier and was lost to follow‑up; he presented with a 2‑month history of decreased vision, redness, and pain in the left eye.
History Narrative: A 47-year-old man with a history of bungee cord trauma to the left eye had undergone a complicated cataract surgery 9 months earlier. He was lost to follow-up and presented to the retina clinic with a 2-month history of decreased vision, redness, and pain in the left eye. His visual acuity was 20/20 OD and 20/400 OS. Intraocular pressures were 12 mm Hg OD and 38 mm Hg OS. His pupils were reactive to light with no relative afferent pupillary defect. Examination of the right eye was unremarkable aside from a mild cataract. Slit-lamp biomicroscopy of the left eye revealed mild conjunctival injection, 2+ anterior chamber cell and flare, iridodonesis, ectopia lentis, and prolapsed vitreous. Gonioscopy of the left eye demonstrated a wide open angle with lens fragments noted inferiorly. Dilated fundus examination of the left eye revealed a posterior vitreous detachment with his crystalline lens subluxated inferiorly in the anterior vitreous; macula, retinal vessels, and optic nerve were unremarkable.
Symptom Onset: 2 months
Symptom Duration: 2 months
Progression: Resolved
Characteristics: Retained lens fragments in anterior chamber angle with associated inflammation (2+ AC cell/flare), prolapsed vitreous, ectopia lentis and subluxated crystalline lens in anterior vitreous causing elevated IOP (lens particle glaucoma).


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: History of bungee cord trauma to the left eye
Associated Symptoms: decreased vision, redness, and pain
Previous Episodes: 1
Prior Treatments: timolol, dorzolamide, brimonidine, topical atropine, prednisolone acetate
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — significant transient impairment (decreased vision to 20/400 OS, pain/redness) improving to mild persistent limitation (final UCVA 20/30 OS)
Risk Factors: History of ocular trauma (bungee cord), complicated cataract surgery, prior vitreous prolapse/zonular instability (ectopia lentis, iridodonesis), delayed follow-up and retained lens fragments


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N5 (OS 20/30 equivalent)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 38 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Mild conjunctival injection
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: mild corneal edema
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: 2+ cell and flare
Anterior Segment – Iris: iridodonesis, ectopia lentis, and prolapsed vitreous
Anterior Segment – Angle: Wide open angle
Anterior Segment – Lens: Subluxated crystalline lens with ectopia lentis and retained lens fragments (dislocated inferiorly into anterior vitreous)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Reactive to light with no relative afferent pupillary defect, pupils reactive and no abnormal size noted (normal size and reactive).
Pupils – Reaction to Light: reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Reactive to light
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: no relative afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full without restriction
Slit Lamp Exam Description: mild conjunctival injection, 2+ anterior chamber cell and flare, iridodonesis, ectopia lentis, and prolapsed vitreous
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: unremarkable
Fundus Exam – Macula: Unremarkable
Fundus Exam – Vessels: unremarkable
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Peripheral retina: no retinal breaks; peripheral retina unremarkable.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Lens particle glaucoma of the left eye secondary to retained lens fragments after complicated cataract surgery, with elevated IOP (38 mm Hg), anterior chamber inflammation (2+ cell and flare), iridodonesis, ectopia lentis and prolapsed vitreous; retinal exam without macular/optic nerve pathology. Hypothesis supported by gonioscopy showing fluffy lens particles in the angle. Acute IOP elevation and inflammation requiring aggressive medical therapy and prompt pars plana vitrectomy, lensectomy, and IOL placement. Postoperative course: IOP normalized to 12 mm Hg without medications and final uncorrected VA improved to 20/30 OS.
Primary Diagnosis: Lens particle glaucoma
Differential Diagnosis: Lens particle glaucoma; Phacolytic glaucoma; Phacoantigenic (phacoantigenic) glaucoma; Angle-recession glaucoma
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Left
Complications: glaucoma, inflammation, retinal detachment, chronic inflammation, disabling visual floaters, refractory cystoid macular edema


Plan
All Medications: timolol, dorzolamide, brimonidine, atropine, prednisolone acetate, topical beta-blocker, alpha-2-adrenergic agonist, topical carbonic anhydrase inhibitor, systemic carbonic anhydrase inhibitor, glycerin (mentioned as to avoid in diabetics), isosorbide, mannitol, frequent topical corticosteroids, periocular or systemic corticosteroids, prostaglandin analogs (not used in inflamed eye)
All Procedures: Pars plana vitrectomy; lensectomy; intraocular lens (IOL) placement; gonioscopy; slit‑lamp biomicroscopy; dilated fundus examination; pars plana vitrectomy and lensectomy with phacofragmatome (as described)
Patient Education: You have lens fragments left in your eye from a previous cataract surgery, which can cause inflammation, high eye pressure, pain, redness, and vision loss. Immediate steps: - Take prescribed eye‑pressure lowering drops (timolol, dorzolamide, brimonidine) exactly as directed. - Use frequent steroid drops (prednisolone acetate) to reduce inflammation and atropine to prevent painful spasms and synechiae. - Avoid rubbing the eye and heavy lifting; protect the eye from further trauma. Why surgery may be needed: - If pressure remains high or vision is poor, a pars plana vitrectomy and lensectomy (removal of lens fragments) with IOL placement may be recommended to remove the debris, relieve inflammation, and prevent optic nerve damage. - Surgery is often done urgently if there is uncontrolled pressure, vitreous prolapse, significant inflammation, or retinal concerns; timing may be within days to weeks depending on your exam. What to expect after treatment: - Pressure often improves after surgery; you may be able to stop some glaucoma drops. - Vision can improve significantly (example case improved to 20/30), but recovery may take weeks and depends on retinal and optic nerve health. - You will need frequent follow‑up visits for IOP checks, inflammation monitoring, and retinal exams. When to contact the clinic urgently: - Sudden worsening of vision, severe eye pain, new flashes/floaters, increasing redness, or nausea/vomiting with eye pain. Medications and follow‑up summary: - Continue all prescribed drops; return for scheduled visits and IOP checks; bring a list of medications and any history of diabetes or prior eye surgeries to each visit.
Emergency Instructions: Seek immediate ophthalmic evaluation for acute eye pain, redness, decreased vision, or markedly elevated intraocular pressure; while awaiting care, use prescribed IOP-lowering drops, frequent topical corticosteroids and cycloplegic/mydriatic as directed, and avoid miotics.


CASE 16 — Fibrin Deposition on Intraocular Lenses
=================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Photophobia
DURATION: 5 days
LATERALITY: OD
PATIENT QUOTES: "photophobia"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 59-year-old Caucasian woman presenting 5 days after combined right-eye cataract extraction with posterior chamber IOL implantation and trabeculectomy with mitomycin C for photophobia and decreased vision. She reports symptoms beginning in the early postoperative period (within 2–14 days) with photophobia; denies hypopyon or severe pain. Current ocular meds: prednisolone acetate 1% every 2 hours and ofloxacin qid in both eyes. Past ocular history notable for identical left-eye surgery 1 month earlier; medical history notable only for hypertension. Current VA 20/60 OD, slit-lamp showing mild conjunctival injection, low bleb, clear cornea, moderate AC cells and flare, and a meshwork of fibrin deposition on the IOL OD.
History Narrative: A 59-year-old Caucasian woman presented to the eye clinic five days after combined cataract extraction with posterior chamber intraocular lens implantation and trabeculectomy with mitomycin C in the right eye, complaining of photophobia. She had undergone the same procedures in the left eye one month earlier. Past medical history significant for hypertension. Current ocular medications: prednisolone acetate 1% every 2 hours and ofloxacin four times daily in both eyes. Vision reported as 20/60 OD and 20/200 OS. On exam the right eye had mild conjunctival injection with a low bleb, clear cornea, moderate anterior chamber cells and flare without hypopyon, and a meshwork of fibrin deposition on the IOL. The left eye had a low bleb, clear cornea, rare anterior chamber cells, and the IOL in good position. Dilated fundus exam showed moderate glaucomatous cupping OD and severe cupping OS; poor vision OS attributed to advanced glaucoma.
Symptom Onset: 5 days after surgery
Symptom Duration: 5 days
Progression: Resolved with medical treatment and follow-up monitoring
Characteristics: Fibrin deposition on IOL occurring 2–14 days postoperatively causing decreased vision, redness, pain, photophobia; slit‑lamp shows cellular AC reaction without hypopyon and meshwork/strands or dense membrane of fibrin on IOL/pupil; immune‑mediated from blood–aqueous barrier breakdown; risk of posterior synechiae, IOL membrane formation, secondary glaucoma and cystoid macular edema.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: surgical manipulation of the iris; incomplete removal of lens cortex/epithelial cells; intraoperative use of long‑acting miotic agents; prior or recent intraocular surgery; diabetes; hypertension; pseudoexfoliation syndrome; uveitis; prolonged surgery
Associated Symptoms: photophobia, decreased vision, redness (red eye), and pain
Prior Treatments: prednisolone acetate 1% (topical) every 2 hours and ofloxacin (topical) four times a day in both eyes
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — decreased vision (20/60 OD, 20/200 OS) with photophobia and pain limiting visual tasks; at least some interference with reading and daily activities.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: diabetes; hypertension; pseudoexfoliation syndrome; uveitis; prolonged surgery; previous intraocular surgery (including recent fellow-eye surgery); surgical manipulation of the iris; incomplete removal of lens cortex/epithelial cells; intraoperative use of long‑acting miotic agent; prior history of intraocular surgery


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/80 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart (distance)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 18 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: mild conjunctival injection
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Moderate cells and flare; meshwork of fibrin deposition on the IOL; no hypopyon
Anterior Segment – Iris: meshwork of fibrin on the IOL with no mention of iris nodules or synechiae; iris otherwise without visible abnormalities (no pupillary membrane noted)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Fibrin deposition on posterior chamber intraocular lens (meshwork of fibrin covering the IOL)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils equal, round, reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk equal to light OD and OS
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Brisk
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Right eye: mild conjunctival injection with a low bleb; clear cornea; anterior chamber with moderate cells and flare without hypopyon; meshwork of fibrin deposition on the IOL. Left eye: low bleb; clear cornea; rare anterior chamber cells; IOL in good position.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Moderate glaucomatous cupping OD; severe glaucomatous cupping OS
Fundus Exam – Macula: unremarkable
Fundus Exam – Vessels: normal caliber and no hemorrhages
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No peripheral retinal pathology noted; peripheral retina within normal limits.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Right eye: Postoperative fibrinous anterior chamber reaction with meshwork of fibrin deposition over the intraocular lens following combined cataract extraction with posterior chamber IOL implantation and trabeculectomy (5 days post‑op). Moderate AC cells and flare, no hypopyon. Left eye: quiet postoperative course with rare AC cells and well‑positioned IOL; poor vision from advanced glaucoma. Plan: intensify topical steroid (prednisolone acetate 1% frequently, up to hourly as needed), consider intracameral tPA if fibrin persists or is vision‑threatening, monitor for endophthalmitis, IOP, and cystoid macular edema; follow closely.
Primary Diagnosis: Right eye: Postoperative fibrin deposition on the intraocular lens
Secondary Diagnoses: Hypertension
Differential Diagnosis: Fibrinous postoperative anterior uveitis (fibrin deposition on IOL); Acute postoperative endophthalmitis; Persistent severe postoperative inflammation with pupillary membrane formation (severe iridocyclitis)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: OD
Complications: AC turbidity; corneal edema; elevated intraocular pressure; bleeding; corneal toxicity; band keratopathy; endophthalmitis; posterior synechiae; loss of iris function; membrane formation on the IOL; dislocation of the IOL; glaucoma; cystoid macular edema; posterior capsular opacification; infectious keratitis


Plan
All Medications: prednisolone acetate 1% (one drop every 2 hours initially, listed dosing range 4–6×/day up to hourly) and ofloxacin (four times a day)
All Procedures: Combined cataract extraction with posterior chamber intraocular lens implantation and trabeculectomy with mitomycin C (right eye); same procedures performed in left eye one month earlier.
Follow-Up Instructions: Regular eye examinations at regular intervals to monitor visual acuity, intraocular pressure, and for signs of infection or recurrent inflammation; adjust visits based on severity (e.g., within days to weekly initially) and after resolution continue routine postoperative follow-up.
Patient Education: Fibrin deposition on the intraocular lens is a common inflammatory reaction after eye surgery that usually appears 2–14 days afterward and can cause decreased vision, redness, pain, and light sensitivity. It is not the same as infection (endophthalmitis), but if you have severe pain, markedly worse vision, or increasing redness/chemosis, contact the clinic urgently. Treatment and what to expect: - Main treatment is intensive topical steroid drops (e.g., prednisolone acetate). Use exactly as prescribed and do not stop suddenly; follow-up exams will guide tapering. - Antibiotic drops may be continued after surgery per your surgeon’s instructions. - In severe cases, an injection of tPA into the anterior chamber can dissolve fibrin, but this is less commonly needed and has risks (temporary cloudiness, corneal swelling, IOP rise, bleeding, infection). - Surgery is rarely needed acutely unless the lens is dislocated or inflammation is uncontrolled. Warnings and side effects to watch for: - Signs suggesting possible infection or serious complication: severe eye pain, rapidly worsening vision, increasing redness, discharge, or new floaters — seek immediate care. - Steroid side effects: increased eye pressure (may cause glaucoma) and increased risk of infection; you will need IOP checks. - Report any new light sensitivity, worsening vision, or pain between visits. Follow-up and prevention: - Keep all scheduled follow-ups to monitor vision, inflammation, and eye pressure. - Use prescribed drops exactly as directed; do not skip doses. - Pre- and intraoperative anti-inflammatory measures can reduce risk in future procedures. If you have questions about your drops, symptoms, or follow-up schedule, contact your ophthalmology clinic.


CASE 17 — Subluxated Crystalline Lens
=====================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: variable decreased vision and bothersome glare in his left eye
DURATION: Variable (chronic/ongoing)
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "N/A"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 41-year-old man with known Marfan’s syndrome and a family history of the condition presents with variable decreased vision and bothersome glare in the left eye; no history of trauma. Left eye vision 20/70 with −5.00 D correction; right eye 20/20 with −0.75 D. Exam shows superior subluxation of the left crystalline lens with diaphanous zonules exposed inferiorly over at least six clock hours, no phacodonesis, no vitreous prolapse. Dilated right eye exam revealed a very subtle inferior zonular dialysis. Intraocular pressure and fundus exam normal.
History Narrative: A 41-year-old man with Marfan’s syndrome presents with variable decreased vision and bothersome glare in his left eye. He has a family history of Marfan’s syndrome and no history of trauma. Visual acuity is 20/70 with −5.00 D correction in the left eye and 20/20 with −0.75 D correction in the right eye. The left crystalline lens is subluxated superiorly with diaphanous zonules exposed across at least six clock hours inferiorly. There is no phacodonesis and no vitreous prolapse. Dilated examination of the asymptomatic right eye revealed a very subtle inferior zonular dialysis. Intraocular pressure and fundus examination are normal.
Symptom Onset: Variable (gradual) onset of decreased vision and glare; chronic/insidious.
Symptom Duration: Variable (no specific duration stated)
Progression: Variable decreased vision and bothersome glare in left eye; subluxation present but no worsening or acute complications described — so progression: Stable/gradual/variable. Provide one concise value.
Characteristics: Subluxated crystalline lens (ectopia lentis) associated with Marfan’s syndrome; left lens subluxated superiorly with at least six clock hours inferior zonular dialysis, diaphanous exposed zonules, no phacodonesis, no vitreous prolapse; right eye with subtle inferior zonular dialysis; symptomatic left eye with variable decreased vision and glare, myopic shift (−5.00 D left vs −0.75 D right), VA 20/70 OS, 20/20 OD.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: Variable decreased vision and bothersome glare
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes of trauma; bilateral subtle right-eye zonular dialysis noted but no prior surgical episodes.
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — variable decreased vision and bothersome glare in left eye causing difficulty with visual tasks (20/70), but right eye 20/20 preserves overall function.
Systemic Symptoms: tall stature, long thin extremities (arachnodactyly), joint laxity, pectus excavatum, kyphoscoliosis, decreased subcutaneous fat
Risk Factors: Marfan’s syndrome; hereditary zonulopathy; family history of Marfan’s; tall stature/arachnodactyly (systemic features increasing risk); no trauma

Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm

Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/70
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+ (left eye)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart (manifest refraction)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal

Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea with no abnormalities noted

Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Brisk
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions (no extraocular movement restriction)

Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: normal
