Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Lens subluxation (ectopia lentis) of the left eye secondary to Marfan’s syndrome with superiorly subluxated crystalline lens, extensive inferior zonular dialysis (~6 clock hours), variable decreased vision and glare in the left eye (VA 20/70 −5.00), subtle inferior zonular dialysis in the asymptomatic right eye (VA 20/20 −0.75), no phacodonesis, no vitreous prolapse, normal IOP and fundus.
Primary Diagnosis: Lens subluxation (ectopia lentis) associated with Marfan’s syndrome
Secondary Diagnoses: Marfan’s syndrome
Differential Diagnosis: Marfan syndrome–related ectopia lentis; Homocystinuria; Trauma-induced lens subluxation; Pseudoexfoliation syndrome
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Left


Plan
All Medications: Nd:YAG laser (neodymium: yttrium–aluminum–garnet)
All Procedures: Phacoemulsification with capsule retractors, capsular tension ring (CTR) strategies (including Cionni/Malyugin modified CTR with scleral fixation) and/or Ahmed capsular tension segment placement with possible sulcus three-piece foldable IOL implantation with optic-capture; pars plana lensectomy with vitrectomy (if posterior descent or significant vitreous prolapse); possible scleral- or iris-sutured posterior chamber IOL, intrascleral haptic fixation (“glued” IOL), or anterior chamber/iris-claw IOL as alternatives.
Follow-Up Instructions: Regular ophthalmologic follow-up with monitoring of visual acuity, IOP, and dilated fundus exam; periodic assessment for progression of zonular dialysis in both eyes and for retinal detachment; cardiac evaluation/echocardiography for Marfan’s-related aortic or valvular disease (if not already done); counsel regarding symptoms warranting prompt review (sudden vision loss, new floaters/photopsias, increased glare or diplopia); plan surgical intervention if visual symptoms/complications (progressive decentration, pupillary block, corneal compromise, or lens-induced glaucoma/amblyopia) develop.
Patient Education: Education about Marfan’s-associated lens subluxation, including that the displaced lens can cause blurred vision, glare, and changes in refractive error that may be managed initially with glasses or contact lenses; the importance of monitoring for complications (pupillary block/angle-closure, corneal endothelial damage, vitreous prolapse, retinal detachment); that bilateral subluxation often reflects a systemic disorder (Marfan’s) with serious cardiac risks so family screening and prompt echocardiography are recommended to evaluate for aortic or valvular disease; treatment options if vision is unacceptable—surgical choices include phacoemulsification with capsular support devices (capsule retractors, capsular tension rings/segments, sutured rings) or pars plana lensectomy with vitrectomy, and various IOL fixation methods (in-bag/sulcus with CTR, sutured or scleral-fixated posterior IOL, anterior chamber or iris-claw IOL); signs and symptoms that should prompt urgent care (sudden vision loss, severe pain, acute redness, sudden floaters or flashes); and the need for long-term ophthalmic follow-up and discussion of genetic counseling for family members.
Referrals: Echocardiography for evaluation of mitral/aortic valve abnormalities and ascending aorta dilation (cardiology)


CASE 18 — Congenital Cataract
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: White pupils (bilateral leukocoria) noted by pediatrician in a 10-day-old infant
LATERALITY: Bilateral
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 10-day-old infant noted by pediatrician to have bilateral white pupils; mother’s pregnancy uncomplicated, term birth, no maternal rash or febrile illness, negative maternal serologies; no family history of congenital/childhood cataracts; referred to ophthalmologist. On exam poor wince to light bilaterally, reactive pupils, normal EOMs, soft globes, clear normal corneas, central white lens opacities bilaterally with no view of posterior segment; B-scan showed no retinal detachment or mass.
History Narrative: A pediatrician noticed white pupils in a 10-day-old infant and made the diagnosis of bilateral cataracts. The mother had an uncomplicated pregnancy with no rash or febrile illness, the infant was born at term, and maternal serologies were all negative. There was no family history of congenital or childhood cataracts. The infant demonstrated a poor wince to light in both eyes, reactive pupils with no relative afferent pupillary defect, normal extraocular movements, soft globes to palpation, normal eyelids and conjunctiva, clear corneas of normal diameter, and lenses with central white opacities bilaterally. There was no view to the posterior segment in either eye. B-scan ultrasonography revealed no retinal detachment or mass. Laboratory testing and genetic consultation failed to reveal an underlying genetic syndrome.
Symptom Onset: 10 days of age
Symptom Duration: 10 days
Progression: present at birth
Characteristics: Bilateral central nuclear congenital cataracts causing leukocoria with poor wince to light, reactive pupils, clear normal-diameter corneas, no view of posterior segment, B-scan negative for RD or mass, globes soft, no systemic/genetic syndrome found (bilateral idiopathic)


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: leukocoria, poor wince to light (reduced visual responsiveness), no relative afferent pupillary defect, normal extraocular movements, soft globes to palpation
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant — severe visual impairment risk without timely surgery and intensive rehabilitation (risk of amblyopia affecting visual development and daily functioning)
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: None identified (idiopathic bilateral congenital cataract)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/75 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 140 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 37.0°C
Vitals – Respirations: 40 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: Poor wince to light
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: Poor fixation (approximately light perception)
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: Poor fixation (central, steady, unmaintained) equivalent to ~20/200 (count fingers range)
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: Central steady but uncooperative fixation (fix-and-follow)
Visual Acuity – Method: Preferential looking (Teller acuity cards)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Digital palpation


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normocephalic, orbits without proptosis or enophthalmos, no palpable masses.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: clear and normal in diameter
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and clear
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: central white opacities bilaterally (central nuclear congenital cataract)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Reactive pupils with no relative afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Reaction to Light: reactive
Pupils – Reaction to Near: reactive
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: reactive with no relative afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: normal
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Unable to perform slit-lamp exam on this infant; anterior segment examined with ophthalmoscopy/hand‑held slit lamp showing clear, normal‑diameter corneas and central bilateral white lens opacities (central nuclear congenital cataracts); no view to posterior segment.
Fundus Exam – Macula: No view to posterior segment in either eye
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No view to posterior segment in either eye.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Bilateral idiopathic congenital cataract — central nuclear opacities bilaterally causing poor visual response and obstructing view of the posterior segment; workup (labs, genetic consult) unrevealing and B‑scan showed no retinal detachment or mass.
Primary Diagnosis: Bilateral idiopathic congenital cataract
Differential Diagnosis: Retinoblastoma; Persistent hyperplastic primary vitreous (PHPV); Retinal detachment; Coats’ disease
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: posterior capsular opacification; secondary membrane formation/capsule opacification; glaucoma (including late-onset, higher if surgery <4 weeks); retinal detachment


Plan
All Medications: atropine
All Procedures: B-scan ultrasonography; ophthalmoscopy with dilated pupils; indirect ophthalmoscopy with 20-D lens; direct ophthalmoscopy; slit-lamp examination (hand‑held or standard); laboratory testing (blood and urine tests for TORCH titers, glucose, calcium, phosphorus, urine amino acids, galactose metabolism enzymes); genetic consultation; ophthalmic ultrasound examination; examination under anesthesia including intraocular pressure measurement; pharmacologic mydriasis (atropine) therapy; patching/occlusion therapy for amblyopia; cataract extraction (lensectomy) with posterior capsulotomy/capsulectomy; intraocular lens (IOL) implantation (with targeted refraction planning); postoperative pupil dilation/medical management; fitting with contact lenses or aphakic spectacles; long‑term glaucoma surveillance.
Follow-Up Instructions: Excellent refractive correction and prompt optical rehabilitation (contact lenses if unilateral; spectacles if bilateral) with power chosen to favor near (myopic) correction; aggressive occlusion/patching therapy for amblyopia as prescribed; regular long‑term follow‑up visits to monitor visual development, refractive changes, posterior capsular/membrane opacification, and intraocular pressure for glaucoma (may appear years later).
Patient Education: Congenital cataract is a lens opacity present at birth or within the first year that can cause vision loss if not treated early. Early detection and timely treatment are critical—dense bilateral cataracts are usually removed by about 10 weeks of age and unilateral cataracts by 4–6 weeks to reduce the risk of permanent amblyopia. Not all cataracts need immediate surgery; small, peripheral, or punctate opacities may be observed. Preoperative testing may include dilated eye exam, ultrasound if the retina cannot be seen, and blood/urine tests for infections or metabolic causes when cataracts are bilateral. If surgery is recommended, understand: - Procedure basics: removal of the cloudy lens; posterior capsule is typically opened to prevent re‑opacification. - IOL (artificial lens) considerations: usually avoided before age 1; if used, target hyperopia or emmetropia based on age and growth; IOLs carry higher inflammation and complication risk. - Risks: postoperative glaucoma (can occur years later), posterior capsule opacification, membrane formation, and rarely retinal detachment. Postoperative care and rehabilitation: - Immediate and consistent refractive correction is essential—aphakic infants need contact lenses or glasses; spectacles are commonly used for bilateral cases, contact lenses preferred for unilateral cases. - Amblyopia therapy (patching the better eye) and regular visual rehabilitation are crucial and require strict adherence. - Frequent follow‑up visits for refraction changes, intraocular pressure checks, and monitoring for late complications. When to seek urgent care: - Increased redness, swelling, discharge, marked pain (or excessive irritability in infants), sudden change in vision, or signs of infection. Support and resources: - Genetic counseling if dysmorphic features or family history. - Coordination with pediatrics for systemic workup if bilateral or syndromic features. - Ask your ophthalmologist about expected visual outcomes, lens options, patching schedule, and long‑term follow‑up plan.
Referrals: Genetic consultation and pediatrician for metabolic evaluation (laboratory testing of blood and urine)


CASE 19 — Ocular Hypertension
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Being told my eye pressures are high and I might have glaucoma
LATERALITY: OU
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 42-year-old Caucasian man referred for elevated eye pressures after optometrist noted high IOPs and possible glaucoma; asymptomatic, no visual complaints, unremarkable past medical and family history, not taking medications; exam: VA 20/20 OU, pupils and motility normal, clear anterior segment, IOPs 27 mm Hg OD and 28 mm Hg OS, gonioscopy open 360° with moderate TM pigmentation, normal optic nerves (CDR 0.2) and normal 24‑2 visual fields.
History Narrative: A 42-year-old Caucasian man was referred to the eye clinic after being told by an optometrist that his eye pressures were high and that he might have glaucoma. Past medical history and family history were unremarkable. He was not taking any medications. Ocular examination revealed visual acuity of 20/20 OU without correction. Pupils and motility were normal. Anterior segment biomicroscopic examination was unremarkable with a clear cornea and lens. Intraocular pressures (IOPs) were 27 mm Hg OD and 28 mm Hg OS. Gonioscopy revealed angles open to the ciliary body band 360 degrees with moderate pigmentation of the trabecular meshwork. Dilated funduscopic examination revealed symmetric optic nerves with normal cupping. The cup-to-disc ratio was 0.2, with an intact neuroretinal rim OU. The macula, vessels, periphery, and vitreous were normal in appearance. A 24–2 Humphrey automated perimetry was performed, which revealed no visual field defects in either eye.
Progression: No progression
Characteristics: Elevated intraocular pressure with normal-appearing optic nerves and visual fields; open angles with moderate trabecular meshwork pigmentation; 20/20 OU vision; IOPs 27 mm Hg OD and 28 mm Hg OS; cup-to-disc ratio 0.2 OU; intact neuroretinal rim; no visual field defects on 24-2 Humphrey.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: None reported
Impact on Daily Activities: None — no impact on daily activities (visual acuity 20/20 OU, normal fields, normal nerves).
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: thin central corneal thickness; race (particularly black), positive family history, younger age (longer lifetime risk)

Assessment Narrative: Ocular hypertension — 42-year-old Caucasian man with IOPs 27 mm Hg OD and 28 mm Hg OS, normal visual acuity 20/20 OU, open angles 360° with moderate TM pigmentation, symmetric optic nerves with C/D 0.2 and intact neuroretinal rims OU, normal 24-2 Humphrey visual fields; no evidence of glaucomatous optic neuropathy or secondary cause on slit-lamp or gonioscopy. Plan: observe with periodic IOP, optic nerve exams, annual VF/OCT; consider treatment based on risk factors and follow-up interval 6–12 months (or sooner if therapy initiated).
Primary Diagnosis: Ocular hypertension
Differential Diagnosis: Idiopathic ocular hypertension; Primary open-angle glaucoma (early POAG); Secondary glaucoma from pigment dispersion syndrome; Secondary glaucoma from pseudoexfoliation syndrome
Severity: Mild
Laterality: OU


Plan
All Procedures: Goldmann applanation tonometry Central corneal thickness measurement (pachymetry) Gonioscopy Slit-lamp examination (including red-free light) Dilated funduscopic examination (stereoscopic optic nerve assessment) Spectral domain optical coherence tomography (SD-OCT) 24–2 Humphrey automated perimetry Laser peripheral iridotomy (mentioned as possible treatment) Laser trabeculoplasty (mentioned) Glaucoma filtration surgery (mentioned)
Follow-Up Instructions: Initially be seen at least every 6–12 months. Yearly visual field testing and/or OCT and dilated optic nerve exam. If treated, return within 4 weeks after starting therapy; once IOP is stable after adjustments, exam every 4–6 months for at least 2 years. If stable for many years, exam every 6–12 months.
Patient Education: Educate patient that he has ocular hypertension — elevated eye pressure without optic nerve or visual field damage — and explain the risks: higher pressure can increase lifetime risk of developing glaucoma, especially with additional risk factors. Review the recommended monitoring plan: follow-up every 6–12 months with IOP checks, annual visual field testing and/or OCT and dilated optic nerve exams; if treatment is started, return in ~4 weeks to assess effectiveness and then every 4–6 months for at least 2 years until stable. Discuss central corneal thickness measurement importance in interpreting IOP, and that treatment is individualized (no single IOP “cutoff”); outline potential options if treatment becomes necessary (topical IOP-lowering medications first; laser or surgery generally avoided unless optic nerve damage or progression). Advise promptly reporting any vision changes, eye pain, or symptoms suggesting angle closure or secondary causes, and encourage adherence to follow-up and therapy if prescribed.


CASE 20 — Open-Angle Glaucoma
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Routine eye exam
LATERALITY: Bilateral
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 51-year-old African American man presented for a routine eye exam with no past ocular problems and no current visual complaints; history notable for mild emphysema treated with inhalers.
History Narrative: A 51-year-old African American man presented for a routine eye exam with no past ocular problems and no current visual complaints. His past medical history was notable for mild emphysema treated with inhalers. Visual acuities were 20/20 OU with refractions −3.00 +2.25 ×110 OD and −2.25 +2.00 ×86 OS. Pupils reacted normally without afferent pupillary defect. Slit-lamp exam was unremarkable. Applanation pressures were 26 mm Hg OD and 32 mm Hg OS. Gonioscopy showed grade IV open angles with a clear view of the ciliary body band 360° and 1+ trabecular meshwork pigment. Funduscopic exam demonstrated generalized cup enlargement with inferior rim thinning OU. Automated visual fields showed an early superior arcuate defect OD and a more advanced superior arcuate defect OS.
Symptom Onset: Routine eye exam (no acute symptoms) — at presentation (no specific onset noted)
Characteristics: Primary open-angle glaucoma with elevated IOPs, open angles (grade IV, ciliary body band visible 360°), optic nerve cupping with inferior rim thinning, arcuate visual field defects (superior arcuate defects OD early, OS more advanced), 1+ trabecular meshwork pigment, no corneal/retinal abnormalities, African American male, mild emphysema, best-corrected VA 20/20 OU, myopic refraction.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: No visual complaints
Impact on Daily Activities: No impact on daily activities; patient asymptomatic with 20/20 vision and no visual complaints.
Systemic Symptoms: mild emphysema (uses inhalers)
Risk Factors: - African American race - Age (51 years) - Myopia (moderate: -3.00 OD, -2.25 OS) - Elevated intraocular pressure (IOP) - Family history of glaucoma: N/A


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: Jäger 1+ (N5)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 26 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 32 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: No conjunctival or scleral abnormalities noted (normal)
External Exam – Lacrimal System: WNL
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Unremarkable
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: No transillumination defects; iris normal appearing, no rubeosis, atrophy, or pigment dusting.
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open, grade IV with clear view of ciliary body band 360° (Schaffer grade IV)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, and reactive to light (normal)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Reactive to light, no afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Normal
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: Reactive, no afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact in all directions of gaze.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Anterior slit-lamp examination was unremarkable
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Right: generalized enlargement of the cup with increased thinning of the inferior rim; Left: generalized cup enlargement with more marked thinning of the inferior neural rim
Fundus Exam – Macula: No macular abnormalities detected (macula normal)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: No abnormalities of the retina or vessels
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No peripheral retinal lesions; periphery within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Primary open-angle glaucoma in a 51-year-old African American man, characterized by elevated intraocular pressures (26 mm Hg OD, 32 mm Hg OS), open angles on gonioscopy (grade IV with clear ciliary body band 360°), optic nerve cupping with inferior rim thinning OU (more marked OS), and corresponding superior arcuate visual field defects (early OD, more advanced OS). No signs of secondary glaucoma (no Krukenberg spindle, iris transillumination defects, pseudoexfoliation, or inflammatory findings). Visual acuity 20/20 OU. Plan: initiate IOP-lowering therapy and close follow-up with target IOP at least 20% below baseline, baseline optic nerve imaging and pachymetry if not already obtained, and repeat visual fields and optic nerve assessment to monitor for progression; consider escalation to laser trabeculoplasty or surgical intervention if pressure or structural/functional progression occurs.
Primary Diagnosis: Primary open-angle glaucoma
Secondary Diagnoses: Mild emphysema
Differential Diagnosis: Primary open-angle glaucoma; Pigmentary glaucoma; Pseudoexfoliation glaucoma; Optic neuropathy from anterior ischemic optic neuropathy
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Bilateral


Plan
All Medications: β-blockers topical carbonic anhydrase inhibitors oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitors prostaglandin analogs adrenergic agonists miotics apraclonidine 5-fluorouracil mitomycin C
All Procedures: Applanation tonometry; Refraction; Pupillary reaction testing; Slit-lamp anterior segment examination; Gonioscopy; Dilated stereoscopic funduscopic optic nerve examination; Red-free nerve fiber layer assessment; Automated static threshold visual field testing; Ultrasound corneal pachymetry (recommended/mentioned); Retinal nerve fiber layer and optic nerve head imaging (OCT/confocal scanning laser ophthalmoscopy/scanning laser polarimetry)
Follow-Up Instructions: Frequency of follow‑up based on disease severity, achievement of target IOP, evidence of progression, and duration of control. At each visit assess medication side effects and compliance, visual acuity, IOP, and optic nerve. Repeat visual field testing and optic nerve evaluation (including baseline stereoscopic photos) as indicated. Adjust therapy for failure to reach target IOP, progressive optic nerve/visual field loss, or medication side effects/noncompliance.
Patient Education: You have primary open‑angle glaucoma, a chronic eye condition where elevated eye pressure damages the optic nerve and can cause progressive, irreversible vision loss if untreated. Key points: - Goal of treatment: lower your intraocular pressure (IOP) to a target at least ~20% below your pretreatment level (or lower if damage is advanced) to slow or prevent further nerve damage. - Importance of treatment adherence: take eye drops exactly as prescribed (dose, timing), and report side effects. Often start one eye first to assess response. - Monitoring: regular follow-up visits for visual acuity, IOP checks, optic nerve examination, and visual field testing are essential. Baseline optic nerve photos and periodic imaging (OCT/RNFL) help track progression. - Possible treatments: topical medications (beta‑blockers, prostaglandin analogs, carbonic anhydrase inhibitors, alpha agonists, miotics), laser trabeculoplasty (SLT) as an option if drops are insufficient or not tolerated, and surgical options (trabeculectomy, glaucoma drainage devices, or minimally invasive glaucoma surgeries) if medications/laser fail. - What symptoms to watch for: sudden vision loss, eye pain, redness, severe headache, nausea/vomiting — seek urgent care if these occur (could indicate acute problems or complications). - Lifestyle and risk factors: avoid smoking; use inhaled medications carefully and inform your ophthalmologist about all medical conditions and medicines (some systemic meds affect eye pressure). - Expected course: glaucoma is usually lifelong; with proper treatment and follow‑up you can often preserve useful vision. - Next steps for you: discuss medication choice and side effects, consider SLT if adherence is a concern, schedule regular follow‑ups and baseline imaging/visual fields as recommended.


CASE 21 — Primary Angle-Closure Glaucoma
========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Several hours of discomfort and blurred vision in the left eye
DURATION: several hours
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "I have had several hours of discomfort and my left eye is blurred."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 55-year-old woman with several hours of left eye discomfort and blurred vision; vision 20/20 OD and 20/60 OS; IOPs 14 mm Hg OD and 52 mm Hg OS; left pupil middilated and sluggish; mild conjunctival/episcleral hyperemia, mild corneal epithelial edema, shallow peripheral anterior chamber with midperipheral iris bowed anteriorly (iris bombé); no history of prior similar brief episodes.
History Narrative: A 55-year-old woman presented with several hours of discomfort and blurred vision in the left eye. She denied prior repeated brief episodes of similar symptoms. She was not using medications associated with angle closure and had no history or findings suggestive of lens-induced angle-closure glaucoma, trauma, uveitis, neovascular disease, nanophthalmos, posterior segment tumors, choroidal effusions, or prior surgery. Family history of angle-closure glaucoma was not reported.
Symptom Onset: several hours
Symptom Duration: several hours
Progression: Acute to resolved after treatment
Characteristics: Acute onset of eye pain, blurred vision, mid-dilated sluggish pupil, conjunctival/episcleral hyperemia, corneal epithelial edema, shallow peripheral anterior chamber, midperipheral iris bowed anteriorly (iris bombé) causing peripheral iris–cornea touch and closed anterior chamber angle; markedly elevated IOP (left 52 mm Hg), small optic cups, mild nuclear sclerosis of lens.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: close work, emotional state, dark/low ambient light
Alleviating Factors: Laser iridotomy (after medical therapy to lower IOP and clear the cornea), which opened the anterior chamber angle and reduced IOP to normal; medical therapy (osmotics, IV acetazolamide, topical agents including pilocarpine, beta‑blockers/alpha‑2 agonists, topical steroids) used to lower IOP and improve corneal clarity prior to definitive therapy
Associated Symptoms: discomfort; blurred vision; eye redness; pain; colored halos around lights; nausea and vomiting (mentioned as possible symptoms)
Previous Episodes: Denies prior similar episodes
Prior Treatments: medical therapy and laser iridotomy
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — acute left-eye visual impairment (20/60), pain and blurred vision for several hours causing difficulty with near tasks and driving until IOP reduced and iridotomy performed.
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Age 55–70 years; female sex; hyperopic (short eyes) tendency; shallow peripheral anterior chamber / narrow anterior chamber angle; family history of angle-closure glaucoma (increased risk)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/80 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 82 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 52 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Mild conjunctival and episcleral hyperemia
External Exam – Lacrimal System: No epiphora; lacrimal system appears normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normocephalic, no proptosis or enophthalmos, no eyelid or periorbital swelling or erythema.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Mild corneal epithelial edema
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Shallow, peripheral anterior chamber with absent peripheral anterior chamber in the left eye (iris bombé)
Anterior Segment – Iris: Markedly convex (iris bombé) with mid-dilated sluggish pupil; midperipheral iris bowed anteriorly and touching peripheral cornea.
Anterior Segment – Angle: Left eye: Closed (marked convexity/angle closure); Right eye: Narrow (open, but narrow)
Anterior Segment – Lens: mild nuclear sclerosis


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Middilated, sluggish
Pupils – Reaction to Light: sluggish
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Sluggish
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Pupils – Other: middilated
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Mild conjunctival and episcleral hyperemia, mild corneal epithelial edema, middilated sluggish pupil, markedly convex (bombé) iris with shallow/absent peripheral anterior chamber; mild nuclear sclerotic lens changes.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: optic nerve cups were small in both eyes
Fundus Exam – Vessels: small cups in both eyes, no mention of vascular abnormalities — vessels appear normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No peripheral pathology detected; peripheral retina flat and without lesions (within normal limits)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Right eye: Narrow anterior chamber angle, at risk for subsequent angle-closure glaucoma. Left eye: Acute primary angle‑closure glaucoma (pupillary block mechanism) with IOP 52 mm Hg, middilated sluggish pupil, corneal epithelial edema, iris bombé and closed angle on gonioscopy; responded to medical therapy and laser peripheral iridotomy with opening of the angle and normalization of IOP.
Primary Diagnosis: Acute primary angle-closure glaucoma (left eye)
Differential Diagnosis: Pupillary block primary angle-closure glaucoma; Plateau iris configuration; Phacomorphic (lens-induced) angle-closure glaucoma; Secondary angle-closure from uveitic or neovascular causes
Severity: Acute
Laterality: Left


Plan
All Medications: isosorbide glycerol mannitol acetazolamide pilocarpine (2% topical) topical beta-blockers alpha-2 agonists prostaglandin analogs topical corticosteroids antiemetics analgesics
All Procedures: laser iridotomy; medical therapy to lower IOP (osmotic agents such as isosorbide/glycerol or IV mannitol; IV or oral acetazolamide; topical beta-blockers; topical alpha-2 agonists; topical carbonic anhydrase inhibitors; topical pilocarpine); topical corticosteroids; therapeutic paracentesis (if needed); laser iridoplasty; surgical goniosynechialysis; cataract extraction (if lens-induced component)
Follow-Up Instructions: - Measure IOP within 30–120 minutes after laser surgery. - Examination within 1 week post‑laser. - Additional follow-up at 4–8 weeks postoperatively. - Taper topical corticosteroids during the postoperative period. - Consider pupillary dilation with postdilation IOP check and gonioscopy to confirm angle remains open; perform more frequent visits if response inadequate.
Patient Education: You have acute primary angle‑closure glaucoma in your left eye, which occurs when the drainage angle inside the eye becomes blocked, causing a rapid rise in eye pressure. This can cause blurred vision, eye pain, redness, halos around lights, nausea, and a mid‑dilated pupil. Immediate treatment is needed to lower the pressure and prevent vision loss. Initial treatment may include medicines to rapidly lower pressure (oral/IV osmotic agents like isosorbide or mannitol, carbonic anhydrase inhibitors such as acetazolamide, topical beta‑blockers or alpha‑agonists) and pilocarpine drops to constrict the pupil if the iris is responsive. Pain and nausea can be treated symptomatically. Once the cornea clears and pressure is controlled, a laser peripheral iridotomy (small hole in the iris) is usually performed to relieve the pupillary block and open the angle. The fellow (right) eye is often at risk and a prophylactic iridotomy is commonly recommended. After treatment you will need close follow‑up: IOP checks shortly after the procedure (within 30–120 minutes), again within a week, and at 4–8 weeks. Use prescribed topical steroid drops as directed and report increasing pain, redness, worsening vision, or severe nausea/vomiting immediately. Avoid pupil‑dilating drops until after the iridotomy and follow instructions about activity and medications. Family members may need screening because of increased risk.
Referrals: Ophthalmology (glaucoma specialist) for urgent evaluation and laser peripheral iridotomy; prophylactic iridotomy for the fellow eye.
Emergency Instructions: Seek immediate ophthalmic care (go to ER or eye clinic) if you develop severe eye pain, sudden vision loss or worsening blurred vision, colored halos, marked eye redness, nausea or vomiting. Do not dilate the pupil; avoid driving. If already diagnosed with angle closure, take prescribed IOP‑lowering medications (e.g., oral isosorbide/glycerol or acetazolamide, topical beta‑blocker/pilocarpine) only as instructed and get to urgent care for definitive treatment (laser iridotomy).


CASE 22 — Pigmentary Glaucoma
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Routine eye examination, no complaints
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "I currently have no complaints"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 32-year-old myopic man presenting for routine eye exam with no complaints and no significant past ocular or medical history; best-corrected VA 20/20 OU, no RAPD, IOPs 28 OD and 26 OS, slit-lamp with vertical pigment on corneal endothelium and numerous spoke-like iris transillumination defects OU, gonioscopy 360° open angles with dense trabecular pigmentation bilaterally; fundus: OD C/D ~0.7 with inferotemporal rim thinning, OS C/D 0.55; SD-OCT RNFL moderate inferotemporal thinning OD and mild thinning OS; automated perimetry moderate superior arcuate defect OD and early nasal step OS.
History Narrative: A 32-year-old myopic man presents for routine eye examination. He currently has no complaints and denies any significant past ocular or medical history. Examination revealed a best corrected visual acuity of 20/20 in each eye and no afferent pupillary defect in either eye. Intraocular pressures by applanation were 28 mm Hg OD and 26 mm Hg OS. Slit-lamp examination disclosed vertically oriented pigment deposition on each corneal endothelium and numerous spokelike iris transillumination defects in both eyes. Gonioscopy showed fully open angles (360 degrees) in each eye with dense pigmentation of the trabecular meshwork bilaterally. On funduscopic examination, the right optic nerve had a cup-to-disc ratio of approximately 0.7 with inferotemporal thinning at the neuroretinal rim; the left eye had a cup-to-disc ratio of 0.55. Retinal examination was normal. Spectral-domain OCT RNFL analysis showed moderate thinning of the inferotemporal RNFL in the right eye and mild thinning in the left eye. Automated visual field testing demonstrated a moderate superior arcuate defect in the right eye and an early nasal step in the left eye.
Symptom Onset: Routine (asymptomatic) visit; no specific symptom onset (patient currently has no complaints)
Progression: Moderate progressive disease (right > left)
Characteristics: Young myopic men; abnormal posterior iris bowing with iris–zonule chafing causing pigment dispersion (Krukenberg spindle, Sampaolesi’s line, dense trabecular pigmentation); exercise-induced pigment liberation and IOP spikes; secondary open-angle glaucoma with glaucomatous optic nerve changes and corresponding RNFL thinning and visual field defects.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Exercise
Associated Symptoms: Blurry vision with exercise; otherwise asymptomatic on routine exam.
Impact on Daily Activities: May experience intermittent blurred vision with exercise but otherwise normal daily activities given 20/20 acuity and no reported functional impairment.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: - Young age (disease of the young) - Male sex (affects men ≈2× women) - Myopia (myopic patients) - Iris–zonule mechanical interaction / posterior iris bowing (reverse pupillary block) - Exercise (can precipitate pigment liberation and IOP spikes)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 122/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+ (N5)
Visual Acuity – Method: Best corrected visual acuity (BCVA)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 28 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 26 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear, no injection or conjunctival abnormality noted.
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normocephalic, atraumatic; orbits without proptosis or tenderness.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Vertically oriented pigment deposition (Krukenberg spindle) on each corneal endothelium bilaterally
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep
Anterior Segment – Iris: Numerous spoke-like (radial) transillumination defects in both irides
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open, 360 degrees, densely pigmented trabecular meshwork bilaterally (Sampaolesi’s line present)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lens, no anterior segment lens abnormality noted


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, reactive to light (no afferent pupillary defect)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: No relative afferent pupillary defect (no RAPD)
Pupils – Reaction to Near: No relative afferent pupillary defect on either eye
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: no afferent pupillary defect in either eye
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all cardinal directions, no nystagmus or restriction
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Vertically oriented pigment deposition on each corneal endothelium and numerous spokelike iris transillumination defects in both eyes.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Right optic nerve cupping ~0.7 with inferotemporal rim thinning; left optic nerve cupping ~0.55.
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal caliber and course (no vascular abnormalities detected)
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Retinal examination was normal in both eyes.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 32-year-old myopic man with bilateral pigmentary glaucoma. Best-corrected VA 20/20 OU. IOPs elevated at 28 mm Hg OD and 26 mm Hg OS. Slit-lamp: Krukenberg spindles OU and extensive spoke-like iris transillumination defects bilaterally. Gonioscopy: open angles 360° OU with dense, even trabecular meshwork pigmentation (Sampaolesi’s line). Optic nerves: OD cup-to-disc ~0.7 with inferotemporal rim thinning; OS cup-to-disc ~0.55. SD-OCT RNFL: moderate inferotemporal thinning OD and mild thinning OS. Automated perimetry: moderate superior arcuate defect OD and early nasal step OS. Overall assessment: moderate-stage pigmentary glaucoma OD with superior arcuate defect; moderate-stage pigmentary glaucoma OS with early nasal step. Management considerations: initiate IOP-lowering therapy aiming for 20–30% reduction (medical therapy such as prostaglandin analogs, beta-blockers, CAIs or alpha agonists), consider miotic therapy if tolerated, and discuss laser options (initial monocular 180° ALT given heavy TM pigmentation; consider LPI to reduce iris–zonule chafe though evidence limited). If medical/laser therapy inadequate or progression continues, consider incisional surgery (trabeculectomy, Ex-Press, or drainage implant) or MIGS depending on severity and surgeon preference. Arrange baseline optic nerve photos, baseline and serial SD-OCT and automated perimetry, and close follow-up to monitor IOP, optic nerve, and fields.
Primary Diagnosis: Moderate-stage pigmentary glaucoma (OU)
Secondary Diagnoses: Myopia
Differential Diagnosis: Pigmentary glaucoma; Primary open-angle glaucoma; Pseudoexfoliative glaucoma; Intraocular/intraocular tumor–related pigment dispersion (e.g., melanoma)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Bilateral


Plan
All Medications: pilocarpine prostaglandin analogs beta-blockers carbonic anhydrase inhibitors alpha-adrenergic agonists
All Procedures: Laser peripheral iridotomy; Argon laser trabeculoplasty (ALT); Selective laser trabeculoplasty (SLT); Trabeculectomy; Ex‑Press glaucoma mini‑shunt implantation; Glaucoma drainage implant placement; Minimally invasive glaucoma surgeries (e.g., iStent trabecular microbypass, gonioscopy‑assisted transluminal trabeculotomy, endocyclophotocoagulation)
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up should be individualized based on treatment effectiveness, disease stability, severity, and patient reliability (schedule visits and testing accordingly).
Patient Education: Pigmentary glaucoma is caused by pigment released from the back of the iris blocking drainage and raising eye pressure. It most commonly affects young myopic men. Symptoms can include blurry vision, especially with exercise, but many patients are asymptomatic. Important points: - Regular monitoring is essential: you will need periodic IOP checks, optic nerve exams, visual field testing, and OCT imaging to track any damage. - Treatment aims to lower IOP to prevent progression and may include eye drops (prostaglandins, beta-blockers, carbonic anhydrase inhibitors, alpha agonists), laser procedures (laser trabeculoplasty; sometimes laser peripheral iridotomy), or surgery if needed. - Avoid activities that trigger pigment release and IOP spikes (intense exercise) until your doctor advises otherwise. - Report new symptoms such as vision changes, eye pain, or severe headache promptly. - Adhere to prescribed medications and appointments; bring a list of your eye drops to visits and alert any provider to eye medications before other treatments.


CASE 23 — Neovascular Glaucoma
==============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: "bad headache on the right side of my head"
DURATION: half a day
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "a bad headache on the right side of my head."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 72-year-old man with a history of ischemic central retinal vein occlusion in the right eye ~3 months ago who now presents with a half-day history of rapid onset severe right-sided headache, extreme right eye redness, photophobia, nausea and one episode of vomiting, and marked vision loss in the right eye (current acuity hand motions).
History Narrative: A 72-year-old man with a history of hypertension, diabetes, and arteriosclerosis presents to the emergency department with a half-day history of a severe right-sided headache, extreme redness and photophobia of the right eye, nausea and one episode of vomiting. He reports a prior abrupt, painless, severe decline in vision of the right eye approximately 3 months earlier, at which time he was diagnosed with an ischemic central retinal vein occlusion. No history of trauma.
Symptom Onset: half a day
Symptom Duration: half a day
Progression: Acute ocular hypertension secondary to neovascular glaucoma
Characteristics: neovascularization of anterior segment structures leading to secondary angle-closure glaucoma with fibrovascular membrane formation, iris neovascularization, hyphema, severe corneal edema, markedly elevated IOP, and pain/nausea/vomiting


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Nausea and vomiting, severe corneal edema, hyphema, prior ischemic central retinal vein occlusion (ocular ischemia/retinal ischemia), hypertension, diabetes, arteriosclerosis.
Associated Symptoms: headache, extreme eye redness, photophobia (light sensitivity), nausea, vomiting, decreased vision (abrupt severe decline in right eye)
Previous Episodes: Abrupt, painless severe decline in vision in the right eye ~3 months earlier (diagnosed ischemic CRVO)
Impact on Daily Activities: Severe — incapacitated for many activities (marked visual impairment, pain, photophobia, nausea/vomiting)
Systemic Symptoms: Nausea and one episode of vomiting
Recent Travel: No recent travel reported
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: Acute angle-closure glaucoma; Fuchs’ heterochromic iridocyclitis; acute iridocyclitis; angle recession glaucoma; traumatic hyphema with elevated IOP
Risk Factors: hypertension, diabetes, arteriosclerosis, ischemic central retinal vein occlusion (prior CRVO), severe hypertensive retinopathy


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 148/86 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 88 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 37.0°C
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: Hand motions
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: Hand motions
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 67 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 19 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: severe conjunctival injection
External Exam – Orbit: Normal appearing, no proptosis or enophthalmos noted
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Severe corneal edema with a “steamy” appearance (marked stromal edema)
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: small hyphema with cell and flare
Anterior Segment – Iris: Fine, irregular branching/new vessels on the anterior iris surface (iris neovascularization) with minimal pupillary reactivity and small hyphema.

Pupils – Reaction to Near: Minimally reactive
