Pupils – Other: minimally reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Severe corneal edema, conjunctival injection, small hyphema with cell and flare, iris minimally reactive with fine irregular branching neovascular vessels on its surface (no obvious iris bombe)
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Cup-to-disc ratio 0.3; optic disc appears normal (no glaucomatous cupping)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Severe hypertensive retinopathy


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Acute ocular hypertension secondary to neovascular glaucoma (right eye) in the setting of prior ischemic central retinal vein occlusion; right eye with severe corneal edema, iris neovascularization with small hyphema, markedly elevated IOP (67 mm Hg), limited posterior view with attached retina on B-scan. Fellow eye shows wide open angle and hypertensive retinopathy. Plan: urgent IOP lowering with oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitor and topical aqueous suppressants, consider hyperosmotic agents with caution, topical steroid and cycloplegic for inflammation/pain; obtain panretinal photocoagulation when view allows and perform intravitreal anti-VEGF injection once retinal detachment and intraocular tumor are excluded (consider anterior chamber paracentesis if IOP remains very high); if medical therapy fails, proceed to glaucoma drainage device (Ahmed or Baerveldt) after anti-VEGF to reduce bleeding risk; cyclodestructive procedures or enucleation reserved for blind, painful eyes.
Primary Diagnosis: Acute ocular hypertension secondary to neovascular glaucoma
Secondary Diagnoses: hypertension; diabetes; arteriosclerosis; ischemic central retinal vein occlusion (right eye)
Differential Diagnosis: Neovascular glaucoma; Acute angle-closure glaucoma (primary or secondary); Traumatic hyphema/angle-recession glaucoma; Acute iridocyclitis
Severity: Acute
Laterality: right


Plan
All Medications: oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitors topical beta-blockers topical alpha-2 agonists topical carbonic anhydrase inhibitors topical prednisolone cycloplegics intravitreal anti-VEGF injection hyperosmotic agents prostaglandin analogs antiemetics (non-oral/sublingual) aqueous shunting device (surgical implant)
All Procedures: Oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitor (systemic IOP lowering) Topical aqueous suppressants (beta‑blocker, alpha‑2 agonist, topical carbonic anhydrase inhibitor) Topical prednisolone Topical cycloplegic Antiemetics (sublingual or non‑oral route) Anterior chamber paracentesis Intravitreal anti‑VEGF injection Panretinal photocoagulation (PRP) when view permits Aqueous shunt implantation (e.g., Baerveldt or Ahmed) Trabeculectomy (rarely, after neovascularization controlled) Venting tube incisions / tube ligation management (for nonvalved implants) Cyclodestructive procedures (diode or Nd:YAG laser; cryotherapy) Retrobulbar alcohol injection Enucleation
Follow-Up Instructions: Urgent ophthalmology follow-up for IOP monitoring and further management; arrange prompt retina evaluation for panretinal photocoagulation and consideration of intravitreal anti‑VEGF once posterior pathology is assessed; if discharged, return sooner for any worsening pain, vision loss, or persistent nausea/vomiting.
Patient Education: Neovascular glaucoma is caused by abnormal blood vessels that block fluid drainage in the eye, causing a rapid and painful pressure rise that can damage vision. Because this often follows retinal ischemia (e.g., from a vein occlusion or diabetes), treating the underlying cause and promptly lowering eye pressure are essential. Emergency treatments may include oral and topical pressure-lowering medications, possible removal of fluid from the eye, and injections of anti-VEGF medicine to reduce abnormal vessel growth. Once stable, laser treatment of the retina (panretinal photocoagulation) or glaucoma surgery (implant/shunt) may be needed to control pressure long-term. Close follow-up is required to monitor pressure, inflammation, and vision; report worsening pain, vision loss, nausea/vomiting, or increasing redness immediately. If the eye is painful with no visual potential, procedures to reduce pain can be discussed.
Referrals: Ophthalmology (glaucoma specialist) urgently; retina specialist for panretinal photocoagulation and evaluation; emergency medicine for systemic management (if not already involved)
Emergency Instructions: Lower IOP quickly (oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitor ± topical aqueous suppressants such as beta‑blocker, alpha‑2 agonist, topical carbonic anhydrase inhibitor); consider hyperosmotic agents with caution; manage inflammation/pain with topical prednisolone and cycloplegic; control nausea/vomiting nonorally; if view permits, give panretinal photocoagulation; consider intravitreal anti‑VEGF once retinal detachment/tumor/traction ruled out (may perform anterior chamber paracentesis before/after IVI if IOP very high); prepare for glaucoma surgery (aqueous shunt) if medical therapy fails; in blind painful eyes consider cyclodestruction, retrobulbar alcohol, or enucleation.


CASE 24 — Inflammatory Glaucoma
===============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Blurry vision, photophobia, tearing, and pain of the left eye for 2 days
DURATION: 2 days
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "My left eye has been blurry, sensitive to light, tearing, and painful for two days."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 38-year-old Caucasian man with 2-day history of blurry vision, photophobia, tearing, and pain in the left eye; prior episode of left herpes simplex keratitis 12 months ago treated with trifluridine.
History Narrative: A 38-year-old Caucasian man presented with a 2-day history of blurry vision, photophobia, tearing, and pain of the left eye. He had a prior episode of herpes simplex keratitis in the left eye 12 months earlier that resolved after treatment with trifluridine eye drops.
Symptom Onset: 2 days
Symptom Duration: 2 days


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: blurry vision, photophobia, tearing, pain
Previous Episodes: One episode of herpes simplex keratitis involving the left eye 12 months ago.
Prior Treatments: trifluridine eye drops (treated herpes simplex keratitis 12 months prior)
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — vision in the left eye reduced to 20/60 (20/40 with pinhole) with pain, photophobia, tearing, and markedly elevated IOP (46 mm Hg) likely causing functional impairment but not complete disability.
Systemic Symptoms: No systemic symptoms reported
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: No known contacts with similar symptoms
Risk Factors: Recurrent herpes simplex keratitis (prior episode 12 months ago)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 124/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 46 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis or enophthalmos; orbit exam within normal limits
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Left cornea: fine, stellate keratic precipitates scattered in a diffuse pattern; no epithelial/stromal scarring noted; otherwise unremarkable. Right cornea: unremarkable.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep centrally and peripherally, with 2+ cells and aqueous flare
Anterior Segment – Iris: Patchy atrophy of the pupillary sphincter with transillumination defects; no iris nodules, no heterochromia, no posterior synechiae
Anterior Segment – Angle: open to the ciliary body with moderate trabecular meshwork pigmentation and prominent iris processes; no peripheral anterior synechiae
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: patchy atrophy of the pupillary sphincter (irregular/patchy pupil with sphincter atrophy)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive in both eyes
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Briskly reactive to near
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: Patchy atrophy of the pupillary sphincter; no posterior synechiae; reactive to light (no seclusio)
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion OU, no extraocular motility deficit
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Right eye unremarkable. Left cornea with fine, stellate keratic precipitates scattered diffusely. Anterior chamber deep centrally and peripherally with 2+ cells and aqueous flare. Patchy atrophy of the iris pupillary sphincter on transillumination. No iris nodules, heterochromia, or posterior synechiae. Lens clear.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: optic discs were symmetric with healthy rims
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal, macula appears unremarkable (no macular edema or lesions)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal appearing vessels
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No peripheral retinal lesions; periphery normal.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Herpes simplex anterior uveitis with secondary open-angle (inflammatory) glaucoma of the left eye. Acute presentation with decreased vision, corneal stellate keratic precipitates, patchy pupillary sphincter iris atrophy, 2+ anterior chamber cells and flare, open angles with trabecular meshwork pigmentation and no PAS, and markedly elevated IOP (46 mm Hg) consistent with inflammatory glaucoma likely due to herpetic etiology. Management directed at controlling inflammation (topical corticosteroids, cycloplegia, consider oral acyclovir) and acutely lowering IOP with aqueous suppressants; close follow-up with gonioscopy and optic nerve monitoring, and escalate to surgical intervention if pressure remains uncontrolled or glaucomatous damage develops.
Primary Diagnosis: Herpes simplex anterior uveitis
Secondary Diagnoses: Herpes simplex anterior uveitis; Secondary open angle glaucoma (inflammatory glaucoma)
Differential Diagnosis: Herpes simplex anterior uveitis; Fuchs’ heterochromic iridocyclitis; HLA-B27–associated anterior uveitis; Posner–Schlossman syndrome
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Left
Complications: glaucomatous optic neuropathy, peripheral anterior synechiae (PAS) formation, cataract, cystoid macular edema (CME), band keratopathy, steroid-induced IOP elevation


Plan
All Medications: trifluridine, prednisolone 1%, dexamethasone 0.1%, rimexolone, loteprednol, flurbiprofen, ketorolac, diclofenac, methotrexate, azathioprine, timolol, levobunolol, dorzolamide, brinzolamide, apraclonidine, dipivefrin, latanoprost, topical trifluridine (prophylaxis), oral acyclovir, atropine, cyclopentolate, 5-fluorouracil, mitomycin C
Follow-Up Instructions: Frequent follow-up with IOP checks, slit‑lamp exam and gonioscopy to monitor inflammation and angle/PAS formation; regular optic nerve evaluation and visual field testing to detect glaucomatous change; monitor for complications (CME, band keratopathy) and treat inflammation with steroids as needed despite IOP risk; adjust glaucoma therapy or consider surgery if IOP uncontrolled.
Patient Education: You have inflammation in your eye (herpes simplex anterior uveitis) that has caused a large rise in eye pressure (inflammatory glaucoma). Treatment has two goals: control the inflammation and lower the pressure to protect the optic nerve and your vision. You may need topical steroid eye drops (frequent at first, then tapered) to reduce inflammation and topical or oral glaucoma medicines (such as beta-blockers or carbonic anhydrase inhibitors) to lower the pressure. Cycloplegic drops (e.g., atropine) can relieve pain and help prevent synechiae. If infection is suspected or confirmed, antiviral therapy (topical trifluridine or oral acyclovir) is used. Some medicines (miotics, and sometimes prostaglandin drops) are avoided or used with caution in uveitis. If pressure remains high despite medication, laser or glaucoma surgery (drainage device or trabeculectomy) may be needed, but surgery is usually delayed until inflammation is controlled. You will need frequent follow-up exams with pressure checks, slit-lamp and gonioscopy to monitor inflammation, angle status, optic nerve appearance, and visual fields. Report promptly any increased pain, worsening vision, new floaters, or redness. Long-term monitoring is important because glaucoma can develop or progress after the inflammation appears to resolve.
Emergency Instructions: Seek immediate ophthalmic care if you experience sudden vision loss, severe eye pain, marked worsening of vision, new floaters or flashes, intense photophobia, or a rapid rise in eye pressure (symptoms such as severe headache with eye pain or nausea/vomiting). If instructed by your provider, stop any topical medications only as directed and present promptly to the emergency department or eye clinic for urgent assessment and treatment.


CASE 25 — Primary Congenital Glaucoma
=====================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: cloudy eyes and tearing for 3 to 4 weeks (photophobia)
DURATION: 3 to 4 weeks
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "cloudy eyes and tearing"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 3-month-old female infant referred for 3–4 weeks of bilateral “cloudy eyes and tearing,” with photophobia and preference for dim light; symptoms noted by mother, no birth trauma, full-term uncomplicated pregnancy and delivery, no family history of glaucoma; office exam showed diffuse corneal haze (R> L), corneal diameters ~12 mm OD and 11 mm OS, and IOP by iCare 45 mm Hg OD and 36 mm Hg OS while nursing.
History Narrative: A 3-month-old female infant was referred after her mother noted “cloudy eyes and tearing” for 3 to 4 weeks. The child was the product of a full-term uneventful pregnancy and delivery with no use of forceps. There was no known family history of glaucoma and she had a 3-year-old brother without any ocular problems. On examination in the office, the child was photophobic and was more comfortable in dim illumination. Both eyes had a diffuse corneal haze, the right more than the left. Estimated corneal diameters measured in the office were 12 mm in her right eye and 11 mm in her left eye. Intraocular pressure measurements were taken with iCare while the infant nursed on a bottle, and were 45 and 36 mm Hg for the right and left eyes, respectively.
Symptom Onset: 3 to 4 weeks before presentation
Symptom Duration: 3 to 4 weeks
Progression: Progressive
Characteristics: cloudy corneas, tearing (epiphora), photophobia, corneal enlargement/buphthalmos, diffuse corneal haze (right > left), elevated IOPs (45 mmHg OD, 36 mmHg OS), asymmetric bilateral involvement, infant female 3 months old, photophobic preferring dim illumination, Haab’s striae (Descemet’s breaks), increased corneal diameters (12 mm OD, 11 mm OS)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Photophobia, corneal haze/edema, elevated intraocular pressure (IOP) — increased IOP episodes (e.g., crying or facial compression during measurement)
Alleviating Factors: Nursing on a bottle (being pacified/comforted in dim illumination)
Associated Symptoms: cloudy eyes, tearing (epiphora), photophobia
Previous Episodes: No previous episodes reported
Prior Treatments: Topical or oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitor (medical therapy) initiated preoperatively; topical beta‑blocker (cautious/possible) was mentioned as an option — specifically, acetazolamide syrup 5–10 mg/kg every 6–8 hours could be given.
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant — infant photophobia, tearing, and corneal haze impair visual function and comfort, limiting normal visual engagement and feeding environment (requires dim lighting, frequent care)
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: 3-year-old brother without any ocular problems
Risk Factors: autosomal recessive inheritance, family history of glaucoma (10–40%), consanguinity, male sex (slightly increased risk)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 90/60 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 120 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 37.0 °C
Vitals – Respirations: 30 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/100
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: Fixes and follows parents at near (central, steady, maintained)
Visual Acuity – Method: Fix and follow (central, steady, maintained)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 45 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 36 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: iCare tonometer (while infant pacified on a bottle)


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal appearing lids and lashes
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: White sclera, no conjunctival injection
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Patent
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis; external examination notable for bilateral corneal enlargement (right > left) with diffuse corneal haze and photophobia.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Both corneas enlarged with diffuse corneal haze/edema (right > left); left with moderate central corneal haze, right with subtle epithelial edema; Haab's striae present (horizontal break in Descemet’s membrane, right eye).
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Shallow
Anterior Segment – Iris: stretched with thinning of the anterior stroma and a high flat insertion into the trabecular meshwork
Anterior Segment – Angle: high flat insertion with peripheral anterior stromal thinning
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear crystalline lens, no lenticular opacity observed


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Reactive to light
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Pupils – Other: photophobic
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact in both eyes
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Both corneas enlarged with diffuse corneal haze (right > left); right with subtle epithelial edema; left with moderate central corneal haze.
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Unable to visualize due to corneal haze; peripheral retina not assessable under initial office exam.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 3-month-old female with 3–4 weeks of bilateral corneal clouding, tearing, and photophobia; right eye > left with corneal diameters 12 mm OD and 11 mm OS, IOP by iCare 45 mm Hg OD and 36 mm Hg OS. Findings and history are most consistent with primary congenital glaucoma (bilateral, asymmetric). Plan: place on topical/oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitor pending clearance for general anesthesia and perform full examination under anesthesia with repeat IOP, corneal diameters, axial length, gonioscopy, and fundus evaluation; proceed with angle surgery (goniotomy or trabeculotomy, aiming for 360° treatment) at initial operative session if examination confirms diagnosis. Postop: topical cycloplegic and steroid 1–2 weeks; repeat EUA at 8–10 weeks and serial follow-up every 2–3 months until stable.
Primary Diagnosis: Primary congenital glaucoma
Differential Diagnosis: Primary congenital glaucoma; Peters anomaly/anterior segment dysgenesis; Congenital corneal endothelial dystrophy (e.g., CHED)/posterior polymorphous dystrophy; Nasolacrimal duct obstruction (if isolated epiphora)
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Bilateral


Plan
All Medications: iCare (for IOP measurement) Topical carbonic anhydrase inhibitors (dorzolamide) Topical beta‑blockers (selective; lower 0.25% dose in young children) Topical alpha‑2 agonists (not recommended/contraindicated in infants) Oral acetazolamide (5–10 mg/kg every 6–8 hours) Topical cycloplegics Topical corticosteroids (steroids) Mitomycin C (antimetabolite used adjunctively in trabeculectomy)
All Procedures: iCare IOP measurement; examination under anesthesia (including pressure measurement, corneal diameter measurement, ultrasonography/A-scan axial length, anterior segment exam, gonioscopy, dilated fundus exam and optic disc photos when not planning surgery); Tonopen/Perkins handheld applanation IOP measurement (in OR); topical medical therapy (carbonic anhydrase inhibitors, selective beta-blockers); oral acetazolamide; goniotomy; trabeculotomy (360-degree angle treatment); glaucoma drainage device insertion (Baerveldt shunt); trabeculectomy with mitomycin C (rare); cyclodestructive procedures; postoperative topical cycloplegics and steroids; disc photography; intraoperative ultrasound when view obscured.
Follow-Up Instructions: Place on topical cycloplegic and steroid for 1–2 weeks. Perform second examination under anesthesia at 8–10 weeks postoperatively with repeat measurements; if IOP acceptable, dilate for disc photos and fundus exam. Repeat EUA every 2–3 months until IOP stable or office exam possible; then exam in clinic every 3–4 months, reducing to every 6 months when stable. By age 3–4 years, transition to office exams.
Patient Education: Explain that this is most consistent with primary congenital glaucoma (PCG), a condition in infants caused by abnormal drainage of the eye leading to high pressure that can enlarge and cloud the cornea and damage the optic nerve. Emphasize that prompt surgery (angle surgery such as goniotomy or trabeculotomy) is the preferred, often curative treatment and is usually done at the time of the initial full exam under anesthesia. Explain preoperative medical therapy (topical or oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitors) is used temporarily to lower pressure and reduce corneal edema until surgery. Advise that general anesthesia and full exam (including IOP, corneal diameter, A-scan, gonioscopy, and possibly ultrasound) are required to confirm diagnosis and plan surgery. Discuss risks of untreated high IOP (vision loss, eye enlargement), typical postoperative care (short course of topical steroids and cycloplegics), and follow-up schedule (repeat exam/possible surgery at 8–10 weeks, then every 2–3 months until stable, later every 3–6 months). Warn about contraindicated medications in infants (alpha-2 agonists) and caution with beta-blockers; note need for pediatrician/anesthesiologist clearance. Encourage prompt treatment, provide reassurance about generally good success rates (80–90% with initial angle surgery), and recommend genetic/family counseling if concerned about inheritance.
Referrals: Pediatrician and anesthesiologist for clearance for general anesthesia; surgeon familiar with pediatric angle surgery (ophthalmic glaucoma surgeon)


CASE 26 — Ocular Hypotony
=========================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: worsening vision
DURATION: 1 year
LATERALITY: right
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 45-year-old woman with aphakic inflammatory glaucoma in the right eye (left eye light perception only from PHPV) presented preoperatively with vision 20/20 and IOP 40 mm Hg on maximum medical therapy; she underwent trabeculectomy with mitomycin C and subtotal vitrectomy. Postoperatively IOP was 9–12 mm Hg for the first year, then 1 year after surgery she developed worsening vision to 20/60 (improving to 20/20 with +1.50 D), with a highly elevated avascular cystic bleb, moderate chorioretinal folds at the posterior pole and macula, and an IOP of 3 mm Hg.
History Narrative: A 45-year-old woman was referred for surgical management of inflammatory glaucoma in her aphakic right eye. Her left eye had had only light perception since birth due to persistent hyperplastic primary vitreous. The preoperative visual acuity with aphakic correction was 20/20. The intraocular pressure (IOP) was 40 mm Hg on maximum medical therapy including oral agents. Central corneal thickness was 535 μm in each eye. There was 1+ cell and flare in the anterior chamber with scattered, fine, keratic precipitates. There was vitreous at the pupillary plane. The optic disc was markedly excavated with a cup-to-disc ratio of 0.9 and a notch inferiorly. The visual field had a superior altitudinal defect. A trabeculectomy with mitomycin C and subtotal vitrectomy was performed without incident. Postoperatively, the visual acuity remained 20/20. The IOP was well controlled in the range of 9 to 12 mm Hg for the first postoperative year. However, 1 year after surgery, the patient presented with a complaint of worsening vision. The visual acuity was 20/60, which improved to 20/20 with an increase in hyperopic correction of +1.50 diopters. The bleb was highly elevated, avascular, and cystic. The remainder of the examination was unchanged from baseline except for moderate chorioretinal folds involving the posterior pole and macula. The IOP was 3 mm Hg. She was not taking any topical or systemic medications that could suppress aqueous production. Gonioscopy excluded a cyclodialysis cleft. There was no retinal or choroidal detachment. The axial length was 0.75 mm shorter and refraction 1.5 diopters more hyperopic than baseline readings. The eye was quiet and noninflamed.
Symptom Onset: 1 year after surgery
Symptom Duration: 1 year
Progression: resolved
Characteristics: Seidel-negative, large, avascular, highly elevated cystic bleb with maculopathy (hypotonous)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Use of mitomycin C
Alleviating Factors: Discontinuation of aqueous suppressants; cycloplegia; topical corticosteroids (with caution); bandage contact lens; autologous blood patch; bleb compression sutures; resuturing scleral flap / surgical revision
Associated Symptoms: worsening vision; decreased visual acuity (20/60); hyperopic shift (+1.50 D); chorioretinal folds involving posterior pole and macula; maculopathy
Prior Treatments: Maximum medical therapy including oral agents; trabeculectomy with mitomycin C and subtotal vitrectomy; topical/systemic aqueous suppressants (prior to surgery)
Impact on Daily Activities: Mildly impaired (transient decrease in vision to 20/60 with reversible maculopathy; returned to normal after treatment)
Risk Factors: Filtration surgery (trabeculectomy) with mitomycin C, aphakia, prior inflammation (inflammatory glaucoma), young age (45), vitrectomy/subtotal vitrectomy, maximum preop aqueous suppression, thin/avascular bleb (overfiltration risk)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/76 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 76 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 3 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 3 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: highly elevated, avascular, cystic bleb
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea, central thickness 535 μm (each eye)
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Normal depth
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal appearing iris with no abnormality noted
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: Aphakic


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils equal, round, and reactive (no anisocoria)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Normal
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: The bleb was highly elevated, avascular, and cystic; the eye was quiet and noninflamed; anterior chamber depth was noted (AC may be shallow or normal) — in this case the AC was not shallow.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Markedly excavated with a cup-to-disc ratio of 0.9 and an inferior notch
Fundus Exam – Macula: Moderate chorioretinal folds involving the posterior pole and macula
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No retinal detachment; chorioretinal folds involving posterior pole and macula; no peripheral abnormalities reported


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Hypotony due to primary overfiltration after trabeculectomy with mitomycin C, manifested by a large, avascular, cystic, Seidel-negative bleb, IOP of 3 mm Hg, maculopathy with chorioretinal folds and 1.5 D hyperopic shift and 0.75 mm reduced axial length; no evidence of bleb leak, cyclodialysis cleft, retinal or choroidal detachment, inflammation, medication-induced hyposecretion, ocular ischemia, or globe perforation.
Primary Diagnosis: Hypotony due to primary overfiltration
Differential Diagnosis: Primary overfiltration; Bleb leak; Hyposecretion of aqueous (pharmacologic or inflammatory); Cyclodialysis cleft
Laterality: right
Complications: Ocular hypotony with maculopathy (moderate chorioretinal folds involving posterior pole and macula)


Plan
All Medications: mitomycin C oral aqueous suppressants topical aqueous suppressants cycloplegic agents topical corticosteroids
All Procedures: Trabeculectomy with mitomycin C; subtotal vitrectomy; Seidel test; gonioscopy; autologous blood patch; placement of two additional scleral flap sutures (scleral flap resuturing/bleb revision); suture lysis.
Follow-Up Instructions: Cycloplegic agents and conservative observation; if no improvement, perform autologous blood patch; if persistent hypotony, surgical revision with additional scleral flap sutures and postoperative suture lysis as needed; monitor IOP, axial length, refraction, and visual acuity with follow-up examinations.


CASE 27 — PosttrabeculectomyWound Leak
======================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Dramatic decrease in vision in the right eye
DURATION: 5 months
LATERALITY: Right
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 59-year-old woman with advanced primary open‑angle glaucoma who underwent a limbus‑based trabeculectomy with intraoperative mitomycin C in the right eye five months earlier, presenting with a dramatic decrease in vision in the right eye; on exam right eye best‑corrected visual acuity 20/100, very shallow anterior chamber, and intraocular pressure 2 mm Hg.
History Narrative: A 59-year-old woman with advanced primary open-angle glaucoma underwent a limbus-based trabeculectomy with intraoperative mitomycin C in the right eye. The scleral flap was closed with two 10–0 nylon sutures; Tenon’s capsule was closed with a running locking 8–0 Vicryl suture, and the conjunctiva was closed with a running nonlocking 8–0 dissolvable suture. Five months postoperatively she presented with a dramatic decrease in vision in the right eye. Best-corrected visual acuity was 20/100 OD and 20/30 OS. The right anterior chamber was very shallow and intraocular pressure was 2 mm Hg.
Symptom Onset: Five months postoperatively
Symptom Duration: 5 months
Characteristics: Late bleb leak: cystic, thin-walled, avascular bleb associated with hypotony and shallow anterior chamber.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: Dramatic decrease in vision in the right eye, very shallow anterior chamber, hypotony (IOP 2 mm Hg)
Prior Treatments: trabeculectomy with intraoperative mitomycin C
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant limitation — marked decrease in vision OD (20/100) with shallow anterior chamber and hypotony likely causing impaired vision and functional difficulty in daily activities.
Risk Factors: Use of antimetabolites (mitomycin C or 5‑fluorouracil), thin/walled cystic avascular bleb, prior trabeculectomy with antifibrotics, young male sex and high myopia (risk for hypotony maculopathy), overfiltration factors (large ostium, small flap, loose sutures), conjunctival buttonholes or trauma, thin/ischemic conjunctiva, prior wound dehiscence


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/100
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N8+
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen eye chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 2 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 20 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: thin-walled, avascular bleb (ischemic)
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Low filtering bleb with very shallow anterior chamber and hypotony; likely thin, ischemic conjunctival bleb overlying sclera (post-trabeculectomy bleb leak)
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Very shallow (flat)
Anterior Segment – Angle: Closed/flat (very shallow)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Phakic


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal and reactive, round pupils of normal size (approximately 3–4 mm)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive to light
Pupils – RAPD: PERRL, no RAPD
Pupils – Other: Pupils equal and reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion OU without restriction or diplopia
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Right eye: very shallow anterior chamber; low, very thin filtering bleb (low filter) with apparent leak; slit‑lamp photograph demonstrates a low filtering bleb. Left eye: unremarkable anterior segment.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Pale, no mention of cupping changes or hemorrhages (likely chronic glaucomatous optic neuropathy)
Fundus Exam – Macula: Hypotony maculopathy (macular folds)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Engorged and tortuous


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Late bleb leak with chronic hypotony of the right eye causing a very shallow anterior chamber and hypotony maculopathy risk; postoperative leak after mitomycin-C–augmented limbus-based trabeculectomy (IOP 2 mm Hg, VA 20/100 OD), requiring intervention to eliminate leak and reduce excessive filtration.
Primary Diagnosis: Late bleb leak
Differential Diagnosis: Late bleb leak; Chronic hypotony due to overfiltration; Choroidal effusion with hypotony
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Right
Complications: hypotony, shallow anterior chamber, hypotony maculopathy, choroidal effusion, cataract, corneal decompensation, synechiae, macular edema, blebitis, endophthalmitis


Plan
All Medications: mitomycin C 10–0 nylon 8–0 Vicryl topical corticosteroids topical aqueous suppressants systemic carbonic anhydrase inhibitors topical phenylephrine beta-blockers systemic corticosteroids
All Procedures: limbus-based trabeculectomy with intraoperative mitomycin C; scleral flap closure with two 10–0 nylon sutures; Tenon’s capsule closure with running locking 8–0 Vicryl suture; conjunctival closure with running nonlocking 8–0 dissolvable suture; Seidel test; pressure patch; use of Simmons shell or oversized bandage contact lens; aqueous suppression; autologous blood injection into bleb/subconjunctival tissue; argon laser treatment; continuous-wave Nd:YAG laser treatment; excision of bleb with conjunctival advancement; bleb revision with autologous conjunctival graft; patch grafts (clear cornea, amniotic membrane, or scleral patch graft); Tenon’s capsule pedicle plug; glue; rotational conjunctival–Tenon’s flap graft; free conjunctival patching over ischemic blebs
Follow-Up Instructions: Once the leak is closed, follow closely for recurrent leaks if an ischemic bleb remains; if definitive surgical intervention was successful and the bleb is no longer ischemic, the risk of recurrence will be low.


CASE 28 — Failing Filtering Bleb
================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Failing filtering bleb
DURATION: 3 months
LATERALITY: Right
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 53-year-old African American woman with advanced primary open-angle glaucoma who underwent a limbus-based trabeculectomy with intraoperative mitomycin C in the right eye; at 1 year postop IOP was at target with a moderately elevated diffuse ischemic bleb with microcysts, and three months later she presented with IOP of 28 mm Hg and an almost flat filtering bleb while the anterior chamber remained deep.
History Narrative: A 53-year-old African American woman with advanced primary open-angle glaucoma and a target intraocular pressure of 15 mm Hg underwent a limbus-based trabeculectomy with intraoperative mitomycin C in the right eye. At the 1-year postoperative visit the IOP was at target and a moderately elevated diffuse ischemic bleb was present with microcysts. Three months later she presented with an IOP of 28 mm Hg; the anterior chamber was deep but the filtering bleb was almost flat.
Symptom Onset: 3 months after the 1-year postoperative visit (i.e., ~15 months post-op)
Symptom Duration: 3 months
Progression: Late filtration failure
Characteristics: low to flat, heavily vascularized, diffuse ischemic with microcysts (prior), currently almost flat filtering bleb with deep anterior chamber


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: young age; African American race; excessive intraocular manipulation; prior conjunctival procedures; inflammatory conditions (e.g., uveitis); neovascular glaucoma; aphakia
Associated Symptoms: Elevated intraocular pressure (IOP) to 28 mm Hg with a nearly flat filtering bleb; otherwise no specific symptoms reported (deep anterior chamber, no pain or vision change documented)
Prior Treatments: limbus-based trabeculectomy with intraoperative mitomycin C
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: young age; African American race; inflammatory conditions (eg, uveitis); history of prior failed filtering procedure or other conjunctival procedure; neovascular glaucoma; aphakia; iatrogenic surgical factors (excessive cautery, failure to control hemorrhage, excessive intraocular manipulation)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Method: 20/40 (Snellen)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 28 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Flat, heavily vascularized filtering bleb with ischemic changes noted previously; overall conjunctiva shows a low/flat, scarred, fibrotic appearance over the bleb site.
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Slit‑lamp: filtering bleb almost flat, deep anterior chamber, moderately elevated diffuse ischemic bleb with microcysts (prior exam)
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open (deep anterior chamber noted)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Phakic


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils equal, round, and reactive to light (isocoric, normal size)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive to light
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Pupils – Other: equal and reactive
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions (intact)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Filtering bleb almost flat, deep anterior chamber, previously moderately elevated diffuse ischemic bleb with microcysts (now low/flat and heavily vascularized).
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: advanced cupping consistent with advanced primary open‑angle glaucoma


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Late filtration failure: elevated IOP with deep anterior chamber and an almost flat, heavily vascularized filtering bleb consistent with bleb fibrosis/episcleral fibrosis leading to filtration failure.
Primary Diagnosis: Late filtration failure
Differential Diagnosis: Encapsulated filtering bleb (Tenon’s cyst); Tight scleral flap sutures; Occlusion of the internal sclerostomy (tissue incarceration/membrane over ostium); Episcleral/bleb fibrosis (including “ring of steel”)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right


Plan
All Medications: mitomycin C, topical anti-inflammatory agents, IOP-lowering medication
All Procedures: limbus-based trabeculectomy with intraoperative mitomycin C; slit-lamp exam; gonioscopy; ultrasound biomicroscopy; anterior segment optical coherence tomography; digital ocular compression/massage; topical anti-inflammatory therapy; argon laser suture lysis; releasable suture release; needle revision of bleb; surgical bleb revision (more aggressive revision)
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow closely for any signs of recurrent failure; monitor IOP and bleb appearance; continue IOP-lowering medications as needed to maintain target pressure; schedule regular postoperative visits and intervene promptly if IOP rises.


CASE 29 — Flat Anterior Chamber
===============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Shallow anterior chamber in the right eye 1 day after trabeculectomy
DURATION: 1 day
LATERALITY: Right
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 53-year-old woman with a history of bilateral chronic angle-closure glaucoma who presented 1 day after a mitomycin trabeculectomy with a shallow anterior chamber in the right eye. She had right eye vision decline to 20/400, IOP 34 mm Hg in the right eye (12 mm Hg left), a moderately elevated filtration bleb without leak, central and peripheral anterior chamber shallowing with iridocorneal contact to within 1 mm of the pupillary margin, a posterior chamber IOL 0.5 mm posterior to the corneal endothelium, 3+ anterior chamber cells, a patent surgical iridectomy with visible ciliary processes, poor view but excellent red reflex on funduscopy, and B-scan showing flat retina without choroidal effusions.
History Narrative: 53-year-old woman with bilateral chronic angle-closure glaucoma who developed a shallow anterior chamber in the right eye 1 day after mitomycin trabeculectomy. She reported decreased vision in the right eye; exam showed VA 20/400 OD, 20/40 OS, IOP 34 mm Hg OD and 12 mm Hg OS. Right eye had a moderately elevated, Seidel-negative filtration bleb, central and peripheral anterior chamber shallowing with iridocorneal touch to within 1 mm of the pupil, a posterior chamber IOL situated 0.5 mm posterior to the corneal endothelium, and 3+ anterior chamber cells. A surgical peripheral iridectomy was present and patent with visible ciliary processes. Fundus view OD was poor but red reflex excellent; B-scan showed flat retina without choroidal effusions. Left eye had a filtration bleb, deep anterior chamber, patent iridectomy, and pseudophakia. Findings led to diagnosis of aqueous misdirection OD.
Symptom Onset: 1 day after trabeculectomy
Symptom Duration: 1 day
Progression: Stable
Characteristics: Central and peripheral shallowing of the anterior chamber with iridocorneal contact to within 1 mm of the pupil, flat (very shallow) central and peripheral anterior chamber, patent surgical iridectomy with visible anteriorly rotated ciliary processes, posterior chamber IOL 0.5 mm posterior to corneal endothelium, anterior chamber cells 3+, IOP elevated (34 mm Hg), no choroidal effusion on B-scan, bleb present and Seidel-negative


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: History of prior laser or incisional surgery, short axial length/predisposition to angle‑closure glaucoma, discontinuation of cycloplegics, use of miotics
Alleviating Factors: Cycloplegic–mydriatic therapy (e.g., atropine 1% ± phenylephrine 2.5%), topical aqueous suppressants and oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitors, oral/IV osmotic agents, intracameral viscoelastic injection, Nd:YAG laser disruption of anterior hyaloid/posterior capsule, argon laser ciliary process shrinkage, and pars plana vitrectomy with anterior hyaloid removal.
Associated Symptoms: Shallow anterior chamber, decreased vision (20/400 OD), eye pain not described (no acute severe pain), anterior chamber cells 3+, patent iridectomy, history of chronic angle-closure glaucoma, elevated IOP OD (34 mm Hg)
Prior Treatments: mitomycin trabeculectomy
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — significant visual impairment in the right eye (20/400) likely limits depth perception and tasks requiring fine vision, but left eye (20/40) preserves some function, so daily activities partially impacted.
Risk Factors: Short axial length/angle‑closure glaucoma (history of chronic bilateral angle‑closure), prior intraocular surgery (trabeculectomy/laser or incisional surgery), pseudophakia, use of antimetabolites (mitomycin), postoperative inflammation and cycloplegic discontinuation risk


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 132/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 34 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear, with a moderately elevated filtration bleb in the right eye; left eye with a filtration bleb.
External Exam – Orbit: Within normal limits
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Moderately deep central cornea with 0.5 mm clearance from PCIOL to endothelium; otherwise unremarkable corneal exam.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Flat (very shallow) centrally and peripherally
Anterior Segment – Iris: Moderately shallow with iridocorneal contact peripherally to within 1 mm of pupillary margin; surgical iridectomy patent; no iris bombe; pupil not dilated (small pupil not stated).
Anterior Segment – Angle: Closed (extensive peripheral anterior synechiae)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Posterior chamber intraocular lens, pseudophakic (right eye: PCIOL located 0.5 mm posterior to corneal endothelium; left eye: pseudophakia)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – RAPD: None
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions without restriction
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Right eye: moderately elevated filtration bleb negative for Seidel; anterior chamber shallow with iridocorneal contact from periphery to within 1 mm of pupillary margin and central shallowing with posterior chamber IOL located 0.5 mm posterior to corneal endothelium; anterior chamber cells 3+; surgical iridectomy patent with visible ciliary processes. Left eye: filtration bleb, deep anterior chamber, patent surgical iridectomy, pseudophakia.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Moderate glaucomatous cupping
Fundus Exam – Macula: Unable to visualize due to poor view; red reflex intact—macula not adequately assessed.
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Flat; poor view with excellent red reflex


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Aqueous misdirection (ciliary block/malignant glaucoma) of the right eye presenting with central and peripheral shallowing of the anterior chamber after mitomycin trabeculectomy. Right eye: VA 20/400, IOP 34 mm Hg, patent surgical peripheral iridectomy with visible anteriorly rotated ciliary processes, pseudophakic posterior chamber IOL positioned 0.5 mm posterior to corneal endothelium, anterior chamber cells 3+, no bleb or wound leak (Seidel negative), no choroidal effusion or hemorrhage on fundus exam and B-scan. Findings and absence of alternative causes (no hypotony to suggest overfiltration; patent iridectomy excludes pupillary block; ultrasound/fundus negative for choroidals or hemorrhage) support the diagnosis of aqueous misdirection in the right eye. Recommend initial medical management with cycloplegic/mydriatic agents (atropine 1% ± phenylephrine), topical aqueous suppressants and oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitor, consider osmotic agents and intracameral viscoelastic if lenticular–corneal touch with careful IOP monitoring; proceed to laser disruption of anterior hyaloid (Nd:YAG through pupil or iridectomy) or argon ciliary process ablation if medical therapy fails, and pars plana vitrectomy with anterior hyaloid removal and anterior chamber reformation if refractory. Maintain cycloplegia long-term as needed and counsel fellow eye on prophylactic iridotomy and avoidance of miotics.
Primary Diagnosis: Aqueous misdirection (malignant glaucoma)
Differential Diagnosis: Overfiltration with hypotony; Choroidal effusion; Pupillary block; Aqueous misdirection
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right


Plan
All Medications: Atropine 1% ophthalmic; Phenylephrine 2.5% ophthalmic; Topical aqueous suppressants (e.g., topical beta-blocker or alpha-agonist or topical carbonic anhydrase inhibitor); Oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitor (e.g., acetazolamide); Oral or intravenous osmotic agent (e.g., oral glycerin or IV mannitol); Intracameral viscoelastic agent (e.g., sodium hyaluronate)
All Procedures: mitomycin trabeculectomy; surgical iridectomy (patent)
Follow-Up Instructions: Continue cycloplegia (eg, atropine 1% daily) — may need indefinite use; avoid miotics; monitor for recurrence, especially after stopping cycloplegia; in fellow eye consider prophylactic laser iridotomy and avoid miotics; arrange regular IOP and anterior chamber depth checks and retinal/ultrasound evaluation as needed; if medical/laser therapy fails, plan for vitrectomy.
Patient Education: Aqueous misdirection (also called ciliary block or malignant glaucoma) is a rare cause of a very shallow front chamber of the eye that can occur after glaucoma surgery. It happens when fluid is diverted backward into the vitreous, pushing the lens and iris forward, which can raise eye pressure and reduce vision. Treatment starts with medicines to relax the eye and reduce fluid production (cycloplegics like atropine, topical aqueous suppressants, and oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitors), and may include osmotic agents or injection of viscoelastic to deepen the chamber. If medicines and laser treatments fail, a vitrectomy (removal of vitreous gel) to restore normal fluid flow may be needed. Avoid miotic drops (they can worsen the condition). If you have sudden vision change, pain, or significant shallowing of the eye after surgery, seek urgent ophthalmic care. Follow-up visits and possibly long-term use of cycloplegic drops may be required to prevent recurrence.


CASE 30 — Persistent Choroidal Detachment
=========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: shadow blocking her temporal vision
DURATION: 8 months
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "shadow"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: An 80-year-old woman with a 20-year history of glaucoma presented 8 months after combined cataract extraction, intraocular lens implant, and trabeculectomy with mitomycin C in the right eye complaining of a persistent “shadow” blocking her temporal vision since surgery; vision worsening was severe enough to nearly cause a motor vehicle accident.
History Narrative: An 80-year-old woman with a 20-year history of glaucoma presented for consultation 8 months following combined cataract extraction, intraocular lens implant, and trabeculectomy with mitomycin C in the right eye. She complained of a “shadow” since her surgery, which was blocking her temporal vision. This was so debilitating that she almost ran over a small boy with her car. The visual acuity was 20/30 in the affected eye and the intraocular pressure (IOP) was 7 mm Hg. A large, ischemic filtering bleb was present with a negative Seidel’s test. The cornea was clear, the anterior chamber deep and quiet, and the cup-to-disc ratio was 0.8. The peripheral fundus was not visible due to a small and fibrotic pupil. A B-scan ultrasound was performed, which showed 360-degree ciliochoroidal detachments with serous fluid inside the detachments. There was a large nasal choroidal detachment that measured 8 mm. There was no retinal detachment overlying the choroidal detachment.
Symptom Onset: 8 months after surgery
Symptom Duration: 8 months
Progression: Persistent
Characteristics: Serous, smooth, orange, bullous, 360-degree ciliochoroidal detachments with echographically clear suprachoroidal fluid; large nasal detachment measuring 8 mm; nonhemorrhagic (serous) and ischemic filtering bleb associated.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Overfiltration from trabeculectomy with mitomycin C leading to postoperative hypotony and a large ischemic bleb (low IOP), plus possibly topical/systemic aqueous suppressants and continued contralateral beta‑blocker use.
Alleviating Factors: Observation; elevation of IOP (e.g., reforming anterior chamber, stopping aqueous suppressants, discontinuing systemic CAIs and topical beta-blocker in fellow eye); topical steroids and cycloplegics; drainage surgery if vision blocked or persistent
Associated Symptoms: shadow blocking temporal vision; decreased vision (20/30); difficulty driving; hypotony-related symptoms (low IOP 7 mm Hg)
Prior Treatments: combined cataract extraction, intraocular lens implant, and trabeculectomy with mitomycin C (right eye)
Impact on Daily Activities: Severely impaired — shadow blocking temporal vision causing near miss of hitting a child while driving
Risk Factors: - Glaucoma filtration surgery (e.g., trabeculectomy with mitomycin C) - Postoperative hypotony / overfiltration - Large or ischemic filtering bleb - Late bleb leak - Use of topical/systemic aqueous suppressants (e.g., carbonic anhydrase inhibitors, topical beta‑blockers) - Recent cataract surgery (combined procedures) - Intraocular inflammation (iridocyclitis) - Cyclodialysis cleft - Advanced age


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/80 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart at 6 meters (best-corrected)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 7 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Tonopen applanation


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: large, ischemic filtering bleb
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: small and fibrotic pupil
Anterior Segment – Lens: Pseudophakic


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: small and fibrotic pupil
Pupils – RAPD: 0 (no RAPD)
Pupils – Other: small and fibrotic
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion (intact)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: A large, ischemic filtering bleb with a negative Seidel’s test; cornea clear; anterior chamber deep and quiet; small and fibrotic pupil.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Cup-to-disc ratio 0.8
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Not visible due to small and fibrotic pupil


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Right eye: Persistent serous choroidal detachment following combined cataract extraction, IOL implantation, and trabeculectomy with mitomycin C. Presentation 8 months postoperatively with a temporal visual “shadow,” visual acuity 20/30 and IOP 7 mm Hg. Exam notable for large ischemic filtering bleb (Seidel negative), deep quiet anterior chamber, small fibrotic pupil limiting peripheral fundus view. B-scan ultrasound: 360° ciliochoroidal detachments with serous fluid and a large nasal choroidal detachment measuring 8 mm; no overlying retinal detachment. Likely due to postoperative hypotony/overfiltration; differential includes bleb leak (Seidel negative), cyclodialysis cleft less likely without gonioscopic evidence, and suprachoroidal hemorrhage unlikely given serous appearance and low IOP without pain. Condition is persistent and symptomatic, causing visual axis obstruction and functional impairment. Surgical drainage indicated if nonresolving or vision remains affected; conservative measures (observation, stop aqueous suppressants, consider topical steroid and cycloplegic) may be attempted but intervention warranted given prolonged symptoms.
Primary Diagnosis: Right eye: Persistent serous choroidal detachment
Secondary Diagnoses: Glaucoma (20‑year history); Ischemic filtering bleb; Hypotony (IOP 7 mm Hg); Small fibrotic pupil; Pseudophakia (right eye after cataract extraction with IOL)
Differential Diagnosis: Overfiltration/functional hypotony; Filtering bleb leak; Serous choroidal effusion; Suprachoroidal hemorrhage
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Right
Complications: choroidal effusion; suprachoroidal hemorrhage; retinal detachment; bleb leak; cyclodialysis cleft; iridocyclitis; hypotony; corneal, retinal, or choroidal folds; lenticulocorneal touch; blocking of the visual axis; failing filtering bleb; overlying serous retinal detachment; persistent effusion; "kissing" choroidal detachments


Plan
All Medications: mitomycin C; systemic carbonic anhydrase inhibitors (discontinue recommended; e.g., oral acetazolamide mentioned); topical aqueous suppressants (discontinue recommended); topical beta‑blocker (discontinue in contralateral eye recommended); topical steroid; topical cycloplegic; topical antibiotic; balanced salt solution (BSS) and viscoelastic (used intraoperatively)
All Procedures: combined cataract extraction with intraocular lens implantation and trabeculectomy with mitomycin C; B-scan ultrasound; paracentesis with anterior chamber reformation (BSS or viscoelastic) and anterior chamber maintainer placement; radial conjunctival incision and inferior sclerostomy with suprachoroidal drainage and sclerostomy punching; conjunctival closure with absorbable suture; subconjunctival antibiotic and steroid injection
Follow-Up Instructions: Examine 1 day postoperatively; place on brief course of topical steroid, antibiotic, and cycloplegic (if phakic); monitor for persistent effusion/drainage and expect prompt resolution with dramatic visual recovery if visual axis was involved.


CASE 31 — Nonproliferative Diabetic Retinopathy
===============================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Blurred vision OU for 2 months
DURATION: 10-year history
LATERALITY: OU
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 53-year-old man with a 10-year history of type II diabetes mellitus presenting with a 2-month history of blurred vision OU; medical history notable for hypertension, peripheral neuropathy, and hepatitis C.
History Narrative: A 53-year-old man with a 10-year history of type II diabetes mellitus presented with a 2-month history of blurred vision OU. Medical history is positive for hypertension, peripheral neuropathy, and a history of hepatitis C. Best corrected visual acuity was 20/20 OU. Slit-lamp examination was unremarkable. The intraocular pressure was 11 mm Hg in the right eye and 12 mm Hg in the left eye. Funduscopic examination on the right showed a normal disc, no macular edema, macular lipid, or neovascularization. Intraretinal hemorrhages in two quadrants and a mild degree in the other two quadrants were present in both eyes. There was a cotton-wool spot superior to the right macula. Fluorescein angiography demonstrated microaneurysms with mild perifoveal capillary dropout, but no neovascularization.
Symptom Onset: 2 months
Symptom Duration: 2 months
Characteristics: Moderately severe nonproliferative diabetic retinopathy in both eyes characterized by microaneurysms and intraretinal hemorrhages in all four quadrants (moderate in two, mild in two), cotton-wool spot near the right macula, mild perifoveal capillary dropout on fluorescein angiography, no macular edema or neovascularization, BCVA 20/20 OU, IOP 11 mm Hg OD and 12 mm Hg OS.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Hypertension, hypercholesterolemia, poor glycemic control (duration of diabetes)
Alleviating Factors: Improved systemic glucose control and control of hypertension
Associated Symptoms: Blurred vision OU
Prior Treatments: None reported
Impact on Daily Activities: Minimal to no impact — patient has 20/20 OU vision and no macular edema, so daily activities unaffected.
Systemic Symptoms: hypertension, peripheral neuropathy, history of hepatitis C
Risk Factors: 10-year history of type II diabetes mellitus, hypertension, hypercholesterolemia (risk noted as possible), duration of diabetes, poor glycemic control (implied as important)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 140/85 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N8
Visual Acuity – Method: Best corrected visual acuity
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 11 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: WNL
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear and quiet conjunctiva and sclera (no injection)
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Unremarkable
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Unremarkable
Anterior Segment – Lens: Pseudophakia OU


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal and reactive, normal size.
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion OU
Slit Lamp Exam Description: unremarkable
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal disc
Fundus Exam – Macula: No macular edema, no macular lipid.
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Within normal limits
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal peripheral retina without neovascularization or peripheral lesions noted.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Moderately severe nonproliferative diabetic retinopathy, OU.
Primary Diagnosis: Moderately severe nonproliferative diabetic retinopathy, OU
Secondary Diagnoses: Hypertension; Peripheral neuropathy; Hepatitis C
Differential Diagnosis: Branch or central retinal vein occlusion; Radiation retinopathy; Perifoveal (idiopathic) retinal telangiectasia
Severity: Moderately severe nonproliferative diabetic retinopathy, OU
Laterality: OU
Complications: Hypertension; peripheral neuropathy; hepatitis C; intraretinal hemorrhages; cotton-wool spot; perifoveal capillary nonperfusion; risk of progression to proliferative diabetic retinopathy and macular edema


Plan
All Medications: Doxycycline
All Procedures: Fundus contact lens examination; fundus photography; fluorescein angiography; slit-lamp examination; intraocular pressure measurement; visual acuity testing; dilated funduscopic examination.
Follow-Up Instructions: - Annual exam for minimal or absent NPDR. - Every 6–12 months for mild to moderate NPDR without macular edema. - Every 4–6 months if nonclinically significant macular edema is present. - Every 1–2 months if clinically significant macular edema is present. - Every 2–4 months for severe or very severe NPDR.
Patient Education: Discussed importance of optimal systemic control (blood glucose and blood pressure) to reduce risk of retinopathy progression; explained nature and staging of NPDR, signs to report (worsening vision, new floaters, sudden vision loss), need for regular follow-up exams (frequency based on severity), and that treatment options (laser, intravitreal therapies) may be considered if progression or macular edema develops.


CASE 32 — Diabetic Macular Edema
================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: blurred vision of several weeks’ duration
DURATION: several weeks
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "I've had blurred vision for several weeks."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 58-year-old man with a 20-year history of type I diabetes, recent-onset hypertension, and chronic hypercholesterolemia who presents with several weeks of blurred vision.
History Narrative: This 58-year-old man with a 20-year history of type I diabetes, recent-onset hypertension, and chronic hypercholesterolemia sought consultation because of blurred vision of several weeks’ duration. He had best corrected visual acuity of 20/30 in each eye, no afferent pupillary defect, trace nuclear lens opacity on slit-lamp, and intraocular pressures of 20 mm Hg in each eye. Funduscopic exam showed moderate microaneurysms in all quadrants bilaterally; the right eye had clinically significant diabetic macular edema with a circinate lipid ring surrounding the macular center, and the left eye had similar findings plus early neovascularization at the disc. Fluorescein angiography demonstrated macular leakage in both eyes and NVD in the left eye; OCT confirmed macular edema OU.
Symptom Onset: several weeks
Symptom Duration: several weeks
Progression: Progression: clinically significant diabetic macular edema persists in OD after two anti-VEGF treatments; OS improved to normal macular contours after treatment with regression of early NVD.
Characteristics: Diabetic macular edema with circinate lipid ring surrounding center of macula; clinically significant, center-involving (ci‑CSME) OU; moderate microaneurysms OU; early neovascularization at disc (NVD) OS; trace nuclear lens opacity; no afferent pupillary defect; IOP 20 mmHg OU; 20/30 OD, 20/30 OS at presentation (later OS 20/20)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Systemic hypertension; chronic hypercholesterolemia; long-standing (20-year) type I diabetes
Associated Symptoms: Blurred vision of several weeks’ duration
Prior Treatments: Intravitreal anti-VEGF injections OU (two prior injections before third visit); focal laser OS not performed, focal laser planned/then performed OD at third visit.
Impact on Daily Activities: Mild — blurred vision for several weeks but best corrected visual acuity 20/30 OU with 20/20 in OS after treatment; able to perform most activities with minor visual limitation.
Systemic Symptoms: blurred vision for several weeks
Risk Factors: Type I diabetes x20 years; recent-onset hypertension; chronic hypercholesterolemia; moderate diabetic retinopathy (microaneurysms); early proliferative changes (NVD OS)

Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20

Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea with no corneal pathology noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Clear
Anterior Segment – Iris: normal

Pupils – Size/Shape: No afferent pupillary defect.

Pupils – Reaction to Near: Briskly reactive
