Pupils – Other: no afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact (no extraocular movement limitation)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: only trace nuclear lens opacity
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: nlr; early neovascularization of the disc OS
Fundus Exam – Macula: Right eye: clinically significant diabetic macular edema with circinate lipid ring surrounding center; macular thickening temporal to fovea approaching center. Left eye: similar with lipid and macular thickening temporal to macula; center-involving macular edema (ci-CSME) OU; OS OCT normal later after treatment.
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Moderate microaneurysms scattered in all quadrants of both eyes
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No peripheral retinal lesions; otherwise unremarkable peripheral fundus.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: This 58-year-old man with 20 years of type I diabetes presents with bilateral clinically significant, center-involving diabetic macular edema (ci-CSME) confirmed by clinical exam, fluorescein angiography, and OCT. Right eye (OD) shows circinate lipid ring and persistent center-involving edema with visual acuity 20/30; left eye (OS) has similar macular edema but early neovascularization at the disc (NVD) and OCT-confirmed macular edema with visual acuity 20/20. No afferent pupillary defect; trace nuclear sclerosis; IOP 20 OU. Diagnosis: ci-CSME OU with early proliferative diabetic retinopathy OS. Initial management with intravitreal anti-VEGF injections OU was undertaken; after two injections, OS demonstrated resolution of edema and normal OCT/macula and required no further treatment, while OD had unresolved edema prompting a third anti-VEGF injection and focal laser to the macula. Systemic factors (recent hypertension, chronic hypercholesterolemia) may worsen response; recommend optimization of systemic control and close follow-up in 4–6 weeks with consideration of additional anti-VEGF series, switching agents if poor response after three injections, adjunctive/focal laser for OD as performed, and deferral of PRP unless progression of proliferative disease OS. Consider intravitreal corticosteroid or surgical intervention only for refractory cases or tractional components on OCT.
Primary Diagnosis: Clinically significant diabetic macular edema, center-involving (ci‑CSME) OU
Secondary Diagnoses: Type I diabetes mellitus (20-year history); hypertension (recent-onset); chronic hypercholesterolemia; early proliferative diabetic retinopathy OS; trace nuclear sclerotic lens opacity (trace nuclear cataract)
Differential Diagnosis: Diabetic macular edema (DME); Hypertensive retinopathy with macular edema; Cystoid macular edema (postoperative or inflammatory); Radiation retinopathy
Severity: Clinically significant diabetic macular edema, center-involving (ci-CSME) OU
Laterality: Bilateral


Plan
All Medications: bevacizumab, ranibizumab, aflibercept, intravitreal corticosteroids, argon laser
All Procedures: intravitreal anti-VEGF injections OU; focal laser treatment OD; planned repeat anti-VEGF injections and follow-up examinations; (scheduled evaluation for possible PRP deferred)
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow up in 4–6 weeks for clinical and OCT re‑evaluation; retreat with anti‑VEGF if persistent center‑involving edema. Consider a series of three same‑agent injections at 4–6 week intervals before switching; if poor response after three, consider switching anti‑VEGF or intravitreal corticosteroid (if pseudophakic and no IOP history). Repeat focal/grid laser can be considered at 3–6 months. Monitor for progression of proliferative disease (treat PRP if indicated) and optimize systemic control.
Patient Education: Diabetic macular edema (DME) is swelling of the central retina from long‑standing diabetes that can reduce vision. Treatment options include intravitreal anti‑VEGF injections (first‑line for center‑involving DME), focal/grid laser (more for non–center‑involving DME or adjunctive use), and occasionally intravitreal steroids or surgery for select cases with traction. Good control of blood sugar, blood pressure, and cholesterol improves outcomes. OCT imaging is used to monitor response; follow‑up typically every 4–6 weeks after treatment. Expect a series of injections (often 3 initially) with retreatment as needed; switching drugs or adding laser/steroid may be considered for persistent edema. Prompt evaluation is important if vision worsens.
Referrals: Medical consultation for optimization of systemic hypertension and hypercholesterolemia.


CASE 33 — Proliferative Diabetic Retinopathy
============================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Second opinion regarding possible diabetic retinopathy (asymptomatic)
DURATION: 5 years
LATERALITY: Bilateral
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 50-year-old man with a 5-year history of type II diabetes mellitus, asymptomatic at presentation, referred for second opinion regarding possible diabetic retinopathy.
History Narrative: A 50-year-old man with a 5-year history of type II diabetes mellitus presented for a second opinion regarding possible diabetic retinopathy. He was asymptomatic on initial presentation. Visual acuity was 20/20 in each eye. Intraocular pressures were 12 mm Hg OD and 13 mm Hg OS. Slit-lamp exam and lenses were unremarkable and clear. Fundus exam OD showed mild hard exudates at the posterior pole without definite macular thickening, questionable neovascularization elsewhere at the distal portions of both temporal arcades, and definite neovascularization of the disc exceeding one disc area in extent. Fundus exam OS showed intraretinal hemorrhages with microaneurysms in all four quadrants with a moderate degree of hemorrhage limited to two quadrants, occasional hard exudates, and no intraretinal microvascular abnormalities or venous beading. No vitreous hemorrhage was present.
Symptom Onset: Asymptomatic
Symptom Duration: Asymptomatic
Progression: Progressive (right eye: progression to high‑risk PDR; left eye: moderately severe NPDR)
Characteristics: Proliferative diabetic retinopathy with high-risk characteristics


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: Asymptomatic
Prior Treatments: None documented
Impact on Daily Activities: Minimal to none
Systemic Symptoms: Asymptomatic
Risk Factors: 1. Any neovascularization. 2. Neovascularization at the disc (as compared to neovascularization elsewhere). 3. Severe neovascularization: a) Neovascularization within one disc diameter of the optic disc exceeding one-quarter to one-third disc area in size. b) Neovascularization elsewhere exceeding one-half disc area in extent. 4. Vitreous hemorrhage.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/80 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+ (Jaeger 1+)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart (measured with pinhole as needed)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 13 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear.
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Unremarkable
Anterior Segment – Angle: open angles
Anterior Segment – Lens: clear


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils are equal and reactive; normal size and shape
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk/reactive to light
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: Pupils equal and reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions, no restriction or pain
Slit Lamp Exam Description: unremarkable
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Neovascularization of the disc >1 disc area, elevated (neovascularization at the optic disc)
Fundus Exam – Macula: No definite macular thickening; lens clear; vision 20/20 in each eye (no macular edema clinically)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Right eye: Proliferative diabetic retinopathy with high‑risk characteristics — neovascularization of the disc exceeding one disc area, elevated neovascular fronds; no vitreous hemorrhage. Left eye: Moderately severe nonproliferative diabetic retinopathy — intraretinal hemorrhages and microaneurysms in multiple quadrants (moderate hemorrhage in two quadrants), occasional hard exudates, no intraretinal microvascular abnormalities or venous beading. Visual acuity 20/20 OU; IOPs 12 OD, 13 OS. No cataract or other anterior segment pathology. Recommend prompt panretinal photocoagulation for the right eye (consider staging and possible retrobulbar anesthesia), monitor left eye with routine diabetic retinopathy follow‑up and treat macular edema if present; consider adjunctive anti‑VEGF therapy if macular edema or if vitreous hemorrhage limits laser.
Primary Diagnosis: Proliferative diabetic retinopathy (right eye)
Secondary Diagnoses: Type II diabetes mellitus
Differential Diagnosis: Diabetic retinopathy (proliferative vs nonproliferative); Retinal neovascularization from vein occlusion (branch/central retinal vein occlusion with collaterals); Proliferative sickle cell retinopathy; Radiation retinopathy
Severity: Proliferative diabetic retinopathy with high-risk characteristics
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: - Pain in the immediate posttreatment period - Vitreous hemorrhage - Decreased night vision / diminished illumination perception - Peripheral visual field loss - Exacerbation of diabetic macular edema - Early persistent visual loss (≥2 lines)


Plan
Follow-Up Instructions: Observe within a few weeks after completion of PRP; if retinopathy progresses consider vitrectomy; if retinopathy regresses completely pursue conservative observational follow-up based on stability of retinopathy and visual acuity; consider follow-up PRP and/or anti-VEGF for recurrent vitreous hemorrhages or incomplete regression of neovascularization.
Patient Education: You have diabetic eye disease. The right eye has proliferative diabetic retinopathy (abnormal new blood vessels at the optic disc) which can cause bleeding and vision loss; the left eye has moderately severe nonproliferative diabetic retinopathy. Treatment options include panretinal photocoagulation (laser) to reduce risk of severe vision loss and intravitreal anti-VEGF injections especially if there is macular swelling or vitreous hemorrhage; vitrectomy is reserved for persistent vitreous hemorrhage or tractional retinal detachment. Good blood sugar, blood pressure, and cholesterol control reduce progression. You should have regular dilated eye exams, promptly report new floaters, sudden vision loss, or worsening vision, and follow recommended treatment and follow-up schedules to preserve vision.


CASE 34 — Retinal Arterial Occlusion
====================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: sudden visual loss in the right eye 4 weeks prior
DURATION: 4 weeks
LATERALITY: right
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 69-year-old woman who presented 4 weeks after sudden visual loss in the right eye; medical history notable for 6 years of systemic hypertension and 1 year of diabetes mellitus. Examination at presentation: visual acuity 20/30 OD and 20/25 OS, no afferent pupillary defect, IOP 16 mm Hg OU; slit-lamp notable only for early nuclear sclerosis OU. Fundus OD showed inferotemporal retinal artery attenuated with a glistening yellow cholesterol (Hollenhorst) plaque at its proximal part and an area of superficial retinal whitening in the posterior pole along the obstructed artery’s distribution; fundus OS normal. Fluorescein angiography demonstrated a corresponding filling defect in the inferior branch retinal artery distribution.
History Narrative: A 69-year-old woman with a 6-year history of systemic hypertension and a 1-year history of diabetes mellitus presented 4 weeks after sudden visual loss in the right eye. She reported acute onset vision loss in the right eye; there was no afferent pupillary defect. Visual acuity at examination was 20/30 OD and 20/25 OS. Intraocular pressures were 16 mm Hg OU. Slit-lamp exam showed early nuclear sclerosis bilaterally. Fundus exam of the right eye revealed an inferotemporal retinal artery that was sclerotic and attenuated with a glistening yellow cholesterol embolus (Hollenhorst plaque) at its proximal part and an area of superficial retinal whitening in the posterior pole along the distribution of the obstructed artery. The left fundus was normal. Fluorescein angiography demonstrated a corresponding filling defect in the inferior branch retinal artery distribution.
Symptom Onset: 4 weeks prior to presentation
Symptom Duration: 4 weeks
Progression: Improved (partial recovery)
Characteristics: Sudden, dense loss of vision corresponding to a zone of retinal whitening and arterial attenuation, often with a Hollenhorst cholesterol plaque and delayed arterial filling on fluorescein angiography; retinal whitening is transient and may show inner retinal atrophy on OCT later.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Systemic hypertension and diabetes mellitus
Associated Symptoms: Sudden visual loss in the right eye (acute, sectoral visual field defect corresponding to retinal whitening)
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — likely some difficulty with tasks requiring full visual field or fine detail (e.g., reading, driving) but overall visual acuity near-normal (20/30 OD, 20/25 OS) with expected partial recovery, so independent with some limitations.
Systemic Symptoms: hypertension and diabetes mellitus
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Systemic hypertension; diabetes mellitus; atherosclerosis (carotid plaque risk)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 140/88 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N5 (Right eye), N5 (Left eye)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: None notable (normal)
External Exam – Orbit: Unremarkable
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear, no corneal abnormality noted.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: within normal limits
Anterior Segment – Angle: open angles
Anterior Segment – Lens: early nuclear sclerosis


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: No afferent pupillary defect.
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk and symmetric
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Normal
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: no afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion OU (no restriction)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: remarkable only for early nuclear sclerosis of the lens in both eyes
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Normal appearing optic nerve (no pallor or swelling noted)
Fundus Exam – Macula: superficial retinal whitening in the posterior pole along the distribution of the obstructed (inferotemporal) artery; no macular cherry-red spot (macula involved by sectoral whitening)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: attenuated, sclerotic inferotemporal retinal artery with embolus (Hollenhorst plaque)
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits for peripheral retina (no peripheral hemorrhage, tears, or detachments noted)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Branch retinal artery occlusion (BRAO), right eye — inferotemporal branch with Hollenhorst plaque; associated inner retinal ischemic whitening in posterior pole consistent with embolic BRAO. Systemic vascular risk factors: hypertension and diabetes mellitus; recommend systemic vascular workup (carotid evaluation, ECG/echocardiography) and close follow-up for neovascularization. Visual acuity relatively preserved (20/30 OD, 20/25 OS); monitor for recanalization and consider IOP-lowering/embolus-displacing maneuvers only if within acute window.
Primary Diagnosis: Branch retinal artery occlusion, right eye
Secondary Diagnoses: Systemic hypertension; Diabetes mellitus
Differential Diagnosis: Branch retinal artery occlusion; Central retinal artery occlusion; Embolic retinal ischemia from carotid or cardiac sources
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right
Complications: Retinal neovascularization; iris neovascularization (rare after BRAO, up to 20% after CRAO); potential neovascular glaucoma; recurrent vascular events/stroke or myocardial infarction (systemic vascular morbidity)


Plan
All Medications: acetazolamide topical intraocular pressure–lowering drops calcium channel blockers
All Procedures: digital ocular massage (digital pressure on the globe), lowering intraocular pressure with intravenous acetazolamide, topical intraocular pressure–lowering drops, anterior chamber paracentesis, inhalation of carbogen (5% CO2/95% O2), retrobulbar or systemic calcium channel blockers, full-scatter panretinal photocoagulation, prism glasses (low vision aid)
Follow-Up Instructions: Monitor for subsequent neovascularization with scheduled follow-up exams; reinforce and coordinate systemic vascular risk management with the patient’s internist (carotid/cardiac evaluation as indicated).
Patient Education: Retinal arterial occlusion is a sudden blockage of an artery in the eye causing painless vision loss in part (branch) or all (central) of the visual field. It is often due to an embolus (cholesterol, platelet-fibrin, or calcific) or thrombosis and is commonly linked to vascular disease such as atherosclerosis, hypertension, diabetes, carotid artery plaque, or heart rhythm/valve problems. Immediate eye evaluation is important; however, vision often does not fully recover. Early treatments attempted acutely (within hours) may include lowering eye pressure (medications, paracentesis), ocular massage, and inhaled carbogen, but none are reliably proven to restore vision. The most important step is systemic evaluation by your primary care doctor or cardiologist (blood pressure and diabetes control, carotid imaging, ECG/echocardiogram, and vascular risk reduction) because this event increases risk of stroke and heart disease. Follow-up eye exams are needed to monitor for complications such as retinal or iris neovascularization; if these develop, laser treatment may be recommended. Low-vision aids and rehabilitation services can help adapt to persistent visual field loss. If you experience new sudden vision loss, seek urgent care.
Referrals: Internist for systemic vascular workup (including carotid evaluation and cardiac evaluation)


CASE 35 — Central Retinal Vein Occlusion
========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Decreased vision in the right eye for 3 weeks
DURATION: 3 weeks
LATERALITY: OD
PATIENT QUOTES: "N/A"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 45-year-old woman with 3-week history of decreased vision in the right eye; vision gradually diminished during first week after onset then stabilized. She is on oral contraceptives, otherwise no hypertension. Best corrected visual acuity 20/100 OD, 20/20 OS; right afferent pupillary defect. Pseudophakic OD with clear posterior capsule. Intraocular pressures 12 mm Hg OD, 14 mm Hg OS. Fundus OD: intraretinal hemorrhages in all quadrants, mild disc edema, no collaterals; macular edema ~1 disc area at fovea. OCT shows marked central macular edema. Treatment course: intravitreal triamcinolone with resolution and VA improvement to 20/30 at 3 months, recurrence at 6 months treated with repeat triamcinolone with similar response then recurrence at 3 months; maintained on intravitreal bevacizumab about every 3 months with stable OCT and VA 20/30.
History Narrative: A 45-year-old woman presented with a 3-week history of decreased vision in the right eye. Her only medications were oral contraceptives and she did not have hypertension. The vision had gradually diminished during the first week after onset and then stabilized.
Symptom Onset: 3 weeks prior to presentation
Symptom Duration: 3 weeks
Progression: Recurrent macular edema with periods of resolution on treatment; ultimately chronic, managed with maintenance intravitreal bevacizumab every ~3 months (stable at 20/30).
Characteristics: Intraretinal hemorrhage in all four quadrants, mild disc edema, tortuous vessels, macular edema (~1 disc area at fovea), no disc collaterals, pseudophakic, right afferent pupillary defect, IOP 12 mm Hg OD (12) and 14 mm Hg OS (14), cup-to-disc ratio 0.3, unilateral CRVO OD with secondary macular edema


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Oral contraceptives
Alleviating Factors: Cessation of oral contraceptives
Associated Symptoms: Decreased vision in the right eye for 3 weeks, gradual diminution then stabilization, right afferent pupillary defect.
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes reported
Prior Treatments: cessation of oral contraceptives; intravitreal triamcinolone (two injections); intravitreal bevacizumab q≈3 months; aspirin 80 mg daily recommended
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — decreased right eye vision (20/100 → 20/30 with treatment) causing visual impairment but not complete loss; able to function with adaptation.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: Oral contraceptive use; absence of hypertension (no HTN); younger age (45) as a risk context; possible hypercoagulable conditions (e.g., thrombophilia) — consider in younger patients.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/100
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/100
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: White, quiet conjunctiva and sclera
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis; external orbit exam within normal limits.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: No iris neovascularization.
Anterior Segment – Angle: open angles
Anterior Segment – Lens: Pseudophakic with a clear posterior capsule


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Right afferent pupillary defect (relative) — pupils reactive, normal size/shape.
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Right afferent pupillary defect (relative afferent pupillary defect)
Pupils – RAPD: Right afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Other: Right afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: no signs of iris neovascularization; pseudophakic with a clear posterior capsule
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: mild disc edema, cup-to-disc ratio 0.3
Fundus Exam – Macula: Macular edema involving about one disc area at the fovea
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Tortuous vessels
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Midperiphery well perfused without peripheral nonperfusion; intraretinal hemorrhages throughout all quadrants (no peripheral ischemia reported)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: CRVO OD with secondary macular edema: 45‑year‑old pseudophakic woman on oral contraceptives presenting with 3 weeks of decreased vision OD. BCVA 20/100 OD with a right RAPD, IOP 12 mm Hg, extensive intraretinal hemorrhages in all quadrants with mild disc edema, no iris neovascularization, macular edema ~1 disc area on OCT. FA showed good peripheral perfusion with focal foveal leakage (non‑ischemic/partially perfused CRVO). Initial treatment with intravitreal triamcinolone resolved edema and improved VA to 20/30, with recurrent edema requiring repeat steroid and subsequently maintenance intravitreal bevacizumab q~3 months with stable OCT and VA 20/30. Systemic risk factor: oral contraceptives (hypertension absent); cessation recommended. Follow‑up recommended monthly for 6 months to monitor for neovascularization.
Primary Diagnosis: Central retinal vein occlusion (CRVO) with secondary macular edema, right eye
Secondary Diagnoses: Macular edema; Pseudophakia OD; History of oral contraceptive use; Hypertension absent (noted)
Differential Diagnosis: Central retinal vein occlusion (CRVO); Branch or hemiretinal vein occlusion; Diffuse diabetic retinopathy/radiation retinopathy; Subhyaloid/subretinal hemorrhage
Severity: Nonstrict/Nonischemic (moderate CRVO)
Laterality: OD
Complications: - Macular edema (recurrent) - Neovascularization of the anterior segment / Neovascular glaucoma (risk) - Vitreous hemorrhage - Visual loss (reduced visual acuity)


Plan
All Medications: oral contraceptives; intravitreal triamcinolone; intravitreal bevacizumab; aspirin (80 mg daily)
All Procedures: Intravitreal triamcinolone injection (OD) Intravitreal triamcinolone injection (OD) — second injection Intravitreal bevacizumab injections (OD) approximately every 3 months
Follow-Up Instructions: Monthly follow-up examinations for 6 months after diagnosis (longer if receiving injections), with continued monitoring thereafter and vigilance for decreased visual acuity suggesting conversion to nonperfusion.
Patient Education: Cessation of oral contraceptives recommended due to thrombosis risk; importance of controlling systemic risk factors (blood pressure, diabetes, smoking) and follow-up with your primary doctor. CRVO can cause vision loss mainly from macular edema and, less commonly, neovascular glaucoma—report any sudden vision changes or eye pain. Macular edema often improves with intravitreal treatments (anti-VEGF or corticosteroid injections); expect an initial series of injections then maintenance treatments guided by OCT. Regular ophthalmic follow-up monthly for the first 6 months (more often if receiving injections) to monitor vision, OCT, and for early signs of neovascularization; prompt laser (PRP) or additional therapy may be needed if neovascularization develops. Discuss risks and benefits of treatments (injection-related infection, cataract progression, eye pressure rise) and notify the clinic for pain, redness, floaters, or sudden vision loss.
Referrals: Referral to primary care physician/internist for systemic evaluation and management (including cessation of oral contraceptives)
Work Restrictions: Avoid heavy visual tasks and driving until visual acuity/stability confirmed; limit activities with fall/eye-injury risk; follow monthly ophthalmology visits for 6 months.


CASE 36 — Branch Retinal Vein Occlusion
=======================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: 2-month history of decreased vision OS with a central scotoma centered superotemporally
DURATION: 2 months
LATERALITY: OS
PATIENT QUOTES: "I had a 2-month history of decreased vision OS characterized by a central scotoma that was centered superatemporally."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 66-year-old man with a 2-month history of decreased vision in the left eye characterized by a central scotoma centered superotemporally; history notable for a 20-year history of well-controlled hypertension. Visual acuity at presentation OS was 20/200; prior CRVO in the right eye treated with multiple anti-VEGF and intravitreal triamcinolone, with improvement OD from 20/400 to 20/80. Intravitreal bevacizumab initially given OS without improvement, then aflibercept every 6 weeks for ~1 year with stabilization of vision OS at 20/70. Intraocular pressures 15 mm Hg OU. Fundus exam OS showed anomalous arteriovenous crossing along the inferotemporal arcade with dot-and-blot hemorrhages extending from mid-inferotemporal arcade into the macula and moderate–marked macular edema.
History Narrative: A 66-year-old man with a 20-year history of well-controlled hypertension presented with a 2-month history of decreased vision in the left eye characterized by a central scotoma centered superatemporally. Visual acuity at first presentation was 20/200 OS. The right eye had a prior central retinal vein occlusion treated with multiple intravitreal anti-VEGF injections and a couple of intravitreal triamcinolone injections, improving VA OD from 20/400 to 20/80. Intraocular pressures were 15 mm Hg OU. Funduscopic exam OD showed a dry macula with collateral vessels at the optic nerve head. OS demonstrated an anomalous arteriovenous crossing along the inferotemporal arcade with dot-and-blot hemorrhages extending from the midportion of the inferotemporal arcade posteriorly into the macula and moderate to marked macular edema. A branch retinal vein occlusion (BRVO) with macular edema was diagnosed OS. Initial intravitreal bevacizumab produced no improvement; subsequent intravitreal aflibercept given about every 6 weeks for ~1 year produced better effect with stabilization of vision at 20/70 OS.
Symptom Onset: 2 months
Symptom Duration: 2 months
Progression: Progressive stabilization of macular edema with visual acuity improving from 20/200 to 20/70 after treatment; chronic but controlled on ongoing 6-weekly anti-VEGF injections
Characteristics: Segmental intraretinal hemorrhages and macular edema in the distribution of the inferotemporal branch retinal vein with an anomalous arteriovenous crossing, collateral vessels at the optic nerve head (OD), and propensity for retinal neovascularization in ischemic areas.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Systemic hypertension
Associated Symptoms: Decreased vision OS with a central scotoma (centered superotemporally)
Previous Episodes: Right eye: prior central retinal vein occlusion treated with multiple anti-VEGF and triamcinolone injections; Left eye: none prior (current BRVO)
Prior Treatments: - Intravitreal bevacizumab (one or more injections) - Intravitreal aflibercept every 6 weeks for about a year - Several intravitreal anti-VEGF agents (OD historically) - Intravitreal triamcinolone injections (OD historically)
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate—central scotoma and reduced vision (20/70–20/200) OS causing noticeable visual impairment affecting detailed tasks (reading, driving), but some functional vision preserved.
Systemic Symptoms: Hypertension (20-year history, well controlled); otherwise no systemic symptoms reported.
Risk Factors: Systemic hypertension; diabetes; other cardiovascular disease; glaucoma; increased body mass (at age 20); hyperopia; higher serum cholesterol; lower HDL cholesterol; certain coagulation disorders; alcohol consumption; age (older adult)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/80 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/70
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J9/36

Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: clear, without injection
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normocephalic, no periorbital swelling or ecchymosis; orbital exam unremarkable.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal and reactive to light, round, normal size (approximately 3–4 mm)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive in both eyes
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Briskly reactive to near
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Pupils – Other: Pupils equal and reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions (no EOM limitations)
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Nerve head with collateral vessels at the optic nerve head (OD); no specific optic nerve abnormality noted OS.
Fundus Exam – Macula: moderate to marked macular edema with dot-and-blot hemorrhages extending into the macula; macular edema involving the fovea (OCT: substantial edema spreading into center)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Anomalous arteriovenous crossing along the inferotemporal arcade with sectoral (inferotemporal) dot-and-blot hemorrhages and collateral vessels at the optic nerve head
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal peripheral retina without mention of peripheral lesions or retinal tears.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: BRVO of the left eye with macular edema causing decreased vision OS (central scotoma), vision OS 20/70–20/200 at presentation (initially 20/200, stabilized at 20/70) despite intravitreal bevacizumab; improved response to intravitreal aflibercept with injections every 6 weeks for ~1 year controlling edema. Right eye with prior CRVO treated with multiple anti-VEGF and intravitreal triamcinolone, now dry macula and vision OD 20/80. Intraocular pressures 15 mm Hg OU. Left eye fundus: inferotemporal BRVO with dot-and-blot hemorrhages extending into macula, anomalous AV crossing along inferotemporal arcade, moderate–marked macular edema on OCT. No retinal neovascularization or vitreous hemorrhage noted. Plan: continue intravitreal anti-VEGF therapy (aflibercept q6 weeks currently) with OCT monitoring; consider sectoral scatter laser only if neovascularization develops; inform primary care for systemic hypertension management and monitor for progression.
Primary Diagnosis: BRVO with macular edema, OS
Secondary Diagnoses: Hypertension (well-controlled)
Differential Diagnosis: Diabetic retinopathy; Central retinal vein occlusion (CRVO); Radiation retinopathy; Sickle cell retinopathy
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: OS
Complications: Macular edema; retinal neovascularization; vitreous hemorrhage; fibrovascular proliferation leading to tractional/rhegmatogenous retinal detachment; epiretinal membrane; decreased visual acuity resulting from these complications.


Plan
All Medications: bevacizumab; aflibercept; intravitreal triamcinolone; aspirin
All Procedures: intravitreal bevacizumab injection; intravitreal aflibercept injections every 6 weeks; intravitreal triamcinolone injections (prior, OD); focal/grid laser photocoagulation; sectoral/scatter laser photocoagulation to affected quadrant; vitrectomy with membrane peeling (for epiretinal membrane or nonclearing vitreous hemorrhage/tractional detachment); optic nerve sheath decompression/lysis of common adventitial sheath (experimental)
Follow-Up Instructions: Patients with vision better than 20/32: follow every 3–6 months initially. For macular edema treated with intravitreal therapy: initiate injections every 4–6 weeks, then extend interval based on clinical response; switch agents if inadequate response. Perform segmental scatter photocoagulation if retinal neovascularization is detected. Vitrectomy for nonclearing vitreous hemorrhage or vision‑threatening tractional retinal detachment.
Patient Education: Explain that a branch retinal vein occlusion (BRVO) is a blockage of a retinal vein causing bleeding and swelling in part of the retina, often leading to decreased central vision or a central scotoma. Reassure that BRVO is usually less severe than central retinal vein occlusion but can cause vision loss mainly from macular edema and, less commonly, from abnormal new vessels and vitreous hemorrhage. Emphasize controlling cardiovascular risk factors (especially blood pressure; notify/coordinate care with primary doctor) because hypertension and other vascular risks are associated. Describe the main treatments: intravitreal injections (anti-VEGF agents or steroids) are currently the primary therapy to reduce macular swelling and improve or stabilize vision; focal or sectoral laser may be used for persistent edema or to treat/prevent neovascularization; surgery (vitrectomy) is reserved for nonclearing vitreous hemorrhage or tractional retinal detachment. Explain typical course: many patients require repeated injections at intervals (often initial every 4–6 weeks then extended as response allows); monitoring with vision checks and OCT imaging is needed to guide treatment. Advise symptoms that warrant prompt return: sudden new floaters, dark curtain, marked further vision loss, or severe pain. Discuss prognosis: some spontaneous improvement can occur, but timely treatment improves chances of vision recovery or stabilization. Encourage adherence to follow-up and treatment, report medication side effects (eye pain, redness, worsening vision), and ask about systemic anticoagulant or bleeding risks before injections.


CASE 37 — Nonexudative Age-Related Macular Degeneration
=======================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Follow-up retina evaluation for nonexudative macular degeneration in both eyes
LATERALITY: OU
PATIENT QUOTES: "Vision was stable per patient"

Fundus autofluorescence (FAF) showed central hypo-autofluorescence in both eyes. Observational follow-up was recommended; the patient was instructed to use the Amsler grid daily, continue AREDS2 vitamin supplementation, and maintain a diet with sufficient green, leafy vegetables.
Progression: Stable
Characteristics: Nonexudative (atrophic) age-related macular degeneration with bilateral drusen, central RPE depigmentation and geographic/central macular atrophy; no subretinal or intraretinal fluid, hemorrhage, or exudate.

Emergency Instructions: Return for immediate reevaluation if any changes in visual symptoms, especially increased metamorphopsia.


CASE 38 — Exudative Age-Related Macular Degeneration
====================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Decreased vision in the right eye
DURATION: 4 weeks
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "N/A"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 87-year-old woman with prior nonexudative age-related macular degeneration in both eyes who presented complaining of decreased vision in her right eye. Vision was 20/40 OD and 20/50 OS. Examination showed pseudophakia OU, normal IOPs, no definite APD. Fundus OD had drusen with RPE changes in the macula with new subretinal fluid and pigment epithelial detachment (no hemorrhage); OS had drusen with RPE changes and no macular fluid. OCT OD confirmed PED with adjacent subretinal fluid; FA OD showed delayed stippled hyperfluorescence with late leakage consistent with an occult CNVM. She underwent intravitreal bevacizumab and was instructed to follow up in 4 weeks.
History Narrative: An 87-year-old woman with a prior history of nonexudative age-related macular degeneration in both eyes presented complaining of decreased vision in her right eye. She is pseudophakic OU. No history of ocular pain or trauma is mentioned. Intraocular pressures were within normal limits and there was no definite afferent pupillary defect.
Symptom Duration: 4 weeks
Progression: Conversion from nonexudative to exudative AMD (new onset exudative disease in the right eye)
Characteristics: Hemorrhage and exudation of fluid or exudate into the intraretinal, subretinal, and sub–retinal pigment epithelial spaces in the setting of atrophic RPE changes such as drusen, due to choroidal neovascularization (subretinal fluid, pigment epithelial detachment; may have hemorrhage)


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: Decreased central vision and metamorphopsia
Prior Treatments: Intravitreal injection of bevacizumab
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate difficulty with central vision tasks (reading, recognizing faces, detailed work); may require low-vision aids but can perform many activities with assistance.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: Age (elderly), history of nonexudative age-related macular degeneration (drusen, RPE changes), advanced age-related macular degeneration in fellow eye, pseudophakia (prior cataract surgery)


Objective Exam

Severity: Severe
Laterality: Right eye

Follow-Up Instructions: Follow up in 4 weeks for repeat examination and OCT; continue intravitreal anti-VEGF therapy as indicated (treat-and-extend or PRN per findings).
Patient Education: Counseled that decreased vision may reflect conversion from nonexudative to exudative AMD due to choroidal neovascularization causing subretinal fluid or hemorrhage; explained imaging (OCT and fluorescein angiography) findings and that OCT is primary for detecting macular fluid. Discussed treatment with intravitreal anti‑VEGF injections (bevacizumab, ranibizumab, aflibercept), need for repeated long‑term injections, and possible treatment strategies (monthly, as‑needed, treat‑and‑extend). Advised follow‑up in 4 weeks after initial bevacizumab injection and to return sooner for worsening vision or new metamorphopsia. Reviewed low‑vision rehabilitation options (magnifiers, focused lighting) and counseled about limitations and costs before purchase.


CASE 39 — Myopic Degeneration
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: 1-year history of visual loss in the right eye and 1-month history of distortion with a paracentral scotoma in the left eye
DURATION: 1 year
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "finding everything to be cloudy."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 38-year-old high myope (≈–10 D) urologist with 1-year history of visual loss in the right eye due to a diagnosed choroidal neovascular membrane; at presentation also 1 month of distortion with a paracentral scotoma in the left eye. Over ensuing 6 years left eye treated with laser and remained 20/20, then returned with a 1-month history of decreased central vision in the left eye described as everything appearing cloudy.
History Narrative: A 38-year-old urologist presented with a 1-year history of visual loss in the right eye; he had been told he had a choroidal neovascular membrane (CNVM) in the right eye that was not amenable to treatment. At presentation he had a 1-month history of distortion with a paracentral scotoma in his left eye. He was a high myope with a spherical equivalent of −10 D. He also had a history of glaucoma treated initially with Propine OU and later changed to a beta-blocker OU.
Symptom Onset: 1 year before presentation (right eye); 1 month before presentation (left eye)
Symptom Duration: 1 year
Progression: Progressive—development and enlargement of RPE atrophy with recurrent CNVM and visual decline over years
Characteristics: Pathologic myopia with features including tilted optic disc, posterior staphyloma, lacquer cracks, peripapillary retinal pigment epithelial atrophy, retinal lattice degeneration, and focal pigmented choroidal neovascular membranes often surrounded by RPE atrophy (Fuchs' spot).


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: High myopia (high axial myopia; spherical equivalent −10 D), lacquer cracks, posterior staphyloma, retinal pigment epithelium atrophy, prior CNVM/recurrence, retinal lattice degeneration
Associated Symptoms: distortion (metamorphopsia), paracentral scotoma, decreased central vision, cloudy vision
Previous Episodes: 1-year history of visual loss in the right eye (prior CNVM), and recurrent left-eye CNVM episodes with initial left-eye distortion 1 month before presentation; laser-treated left CNVM with recurrence 6 years later.
Prior Treatments: No treatment was recommended for the right eye; argon blue‑green laser (then later krypton laser) photocoagulation was performed on the left eye.
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — central vision loss and distortion (20/200 OD, reduced to 20/30 then worse in left eye episodes) causing difficulty with fine visual tasks and requiring monitoring/Amsler use and possible low-vision aids.
Systemic Symptoms: None
Recent Travel: Returned to Peru for further follow-up examination locally.
Risk Factors: High myopia (spherical equivalent −10 D / axial elongation), lacquer cracks/posterior staphyloma, older age relative risk not applicable here, bilateral involvement potential, retinal lattice degeneration, prior chorioretinal scars/atrophy, history of glaucoma (ocular pathology), increased axial length (>26.5 mm)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart (distance visual acuity)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 13 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: White and quiet conjunctiva with no scleral injection or abnormalities.
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea, slit‑lamp examination unremarkable (no corneal pathology)
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: quiet and deep
Anterior Segment – Iris: Unremarkable
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lenses OU


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk and equal OU
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: PERRL
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion OU, no deficits noted
Slit Lamp Exam Description: The slit-lamp examination was unremarkable.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: tilted (myopic) disc with some peripapillary atrophy
Fundus Exam – Macula: Right eye: central choroidal neovascularization with surrounding RPE atrophy; enlarged area of RPE atrophy ~3× original CNVM, chronic leakage. Left eye: lacquer crack across superior macula with hemorrhage nasal to fovea initially; later juxtafoveal recurrent CNVM inferior/temporal to previous laser scar with ~1 disc-area RPE atrophy adjacent; subretinal fluid initially near nasal fovea.
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal caliber and course (no explicit vascular occlusion or neovascularization of the retinal vessels)
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Retinal lattice degeneration in the periphery of both eyes, without atrophic holes


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Subretinal choroidal neovascularization and recurrence secondary to myopic degeneration, OU.
Primary Diagnosis: Subretinal choroidal neovascularization and recurrence secondary to myopic degeneration, OU
Secondary Diagnoses: - High myopia (spherical equivalent −10 D) - Glaucoma - Retinal lattice degeneration (OU) - Peripapillary retinal pigment epithelial atrophy
Differential Diagnosis: Pathologic myopia with choroidal neovascular membrane; Age-related macular degeneration with CNV; Presumed ocular histoplasmosis syndrome with CNV; Traumatic choroidal rupture with secondary CNV
Severity: Severe
Laterality: OU
Complications: - Choroidal neovascularization (CNVM) with recurrent/progressive subretinal hemorrhage and retinal pigment epithelial atrophy leading to central vision loss - Macular scarring (Fuchs' spot) - Progressive myopic degeneration features: lacquer cracks, posterior staphyloma, tilted optic disc, peripapillary atrophy - Retinal lattice degeneration increasing risk of retinal detachment - Glaucoma (preexisting) - Potential complications from treatments: laser-induced RPE atrophy/scar, risks of intravitreal injections (endophthalmitis), and surgical risks if performed (retinal detachment)


Plan
All Medications: Propine (dipivefrin) OU; beta‑blocker OU; intravitreal anti-VEGF agents (ranibizumab, bevacizumab); verteporfin (for PDT)
All Procedures: argon blue-green laser treatment; krypton laser treatment; intravitreal anti-VEGF injections; photodynamic therapy with verteporfin (PDT); laser photocoagulation; pars plana vitrectomy with subretinal CNVM excision; prophylactic laser or retinocryopexy (considered)
Follow-Up Instructions: - Follow-up 4 weeks after initial anti-VEGF treatment for myopic CNVM; interval may be extended based on response and stability. - Perform OCT at each visit to detect subclinical CNVM or macular schisis. - For nonexudative myopic degeneration, follow-up twice annually. - Patient to monitor central vision with an Amsler grid and seek prompt ophthalmologic evaluation for new distortion or central metamorphopsia.
Patient Education: - Monitor central vision daily with an Amsler grid and report any new distortion or central vision loss immediately. - Myopic degeneration can cause vision loss from atrophy or from choroidal neovascularization (CNV); CNV is treated with intravitreal anti-VEGF injections (e.g., ranibizumab/bevacizumab). - Regular follow-up is important: for CNV, return 4 weeks after initial anti-VEGF treatment, then extend intervals based on response; nonexudative disease typically seen every 6 months. - OCT and fluorescein angiography are used to monitor for recurrent or new CNV and macular changes. - There is no proven treatment for atrophic myopic degeneration; low-vision aids (magnifiers, high-plus lenses, telescopes, closed-circuit video) and rehabilitation can help maximize function. - Protect eyes from additional risk (avoid eye trauma); discuss retinal detachment symptoms and seek urgent care if flashes, many new floaters, or curtain-like vision loss occur.


CASE 40 — Idiopathic Central Serous Chorioretinopathy
=====================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Blurriness in the left eye for 3 weeks
DURATION: 3 weeks
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "I deny any past medical history, trauma, diabetes, hypertension, steroids, or previous surgeries."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 35-year-old engineer with 3 weeks of blurriness in the left eye, central metamorphopsia on Amsler grid, no pain; denies trauma, diabetes, hypertension, steroid use, past medical history, or prior surgeries.
History Narrative: A 35-year-old engineer presented with 3 weeks of blurriness in his left eye. He denied past medical history, trauma, diabetes, hypertension, steroid use, or prior surgeries. On exam visual acuity was 20/20 OD and 20/50 OS, improving to 20/30 OS with +2.00 lens. Anterior segments and intraocular pressures were unremarkable bilaterally. Dilated fundus exam showed a normal right eye and an elevated, circumscribed neurosensory macular detachment in the left eye without retinal tears, hemorrhages, or exudates. The patient reported central metamorphopsia on Amsler grid testing of the left eye. OCT demonstrated subretinal fluid under the fovea OS, and fluorescein angiography of the left eye revealed an expanding area of leakage in the macula. The patient was observed; one month later the subretinal fluid resolved and vision returned to 20/20.
Symptom Onset: 3 weeks
Symptom Duration: 3 weeks
Progression: Resolved (subretinal fluid resolved and vision returned to 20/20 one month after observation)
Characteristics: Elevated, circumscribed neurosensory detachment of the macula with subretinal fluid causing central metamorphopsia, mild hyperopic shift, and reduced visual acuity (typically painless, without intraocular inflammation).


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Stress, type A personality, corticosteroids (systemic or local), pregnancy, Cushing’s disease, psychotropic medications, hypertension, mineralocorticoid/aldosterone excess (e.g., hyperaldosteronism), H. pylori infection, systemic lupus erythematosus
Alleviating Factors: Observation/expectant management (spontaneous resolution within ~3 months)
Associated Symptoms: Blurriness in left eye for 3 weeks; central metamorphopsia on Amsler grid; hyperopic shift (+2.00) in left eye; decreased visual acuity (20/50 left)
Prior Treatments: Observation only (no prior medical or surgical treatments)
Impact on Daily Activities: Mild — temporary central blurriness and metamorphopsia in the left eye for 3 weeks causing reduced vision to 20/50 (improving with +2.00 to 20/30), resolved to 20/20 after 1 month, so transient minor impact on vision-related activities.
Systemic Symptoms: Denies systemic symptoms (no history of diabetes, hypertension, steroids; no systemic inflammation)—systemically asymptomatic
Risk Factors: Corticosteroids; Psychotropic medications; Hypertension; Systemic lupus erythematosus; Pregnancy; Cushing’s disease; Helicobacter pylori infection; Male gender; Type A personality; Excess stress; Aldosterone/aldosterone-related mineralocorticoid activity; Idiopathic


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/50
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N5 (OD), N8 (OS)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart (manifest refraction)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear and quiet
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Unremarkable
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear natural lens


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils equal, round, and reactive to light (no anisocoria)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk and equal in both eyes
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Normal
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: Pupils equal, round, and reactive to light (no afferent pupillary defect)
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion OU, no extraocular motility deficit
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Unremarkable in both eyes
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: cup-to-disc ratio 0.3, disc well-perfused, no edema, no pallor
Fundus Exam – Macula: Elevated, circumscribed neurosensory detachment of the macula (left eye)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Unremarkable


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 35-year-old man with 3-week history of blurring and central metamorphopsia in the left eye. Examination: VA 20/20 OD, 20/50 improving to 20/30 with +2.00 D OS; normal anterior segments and IOPs. Dilated fundus exam OS showed a circumscribed neurosensory macular detachment without hemorrhage, tears, or exudates. OCT OS confirmed subretinal fluid under the fovea. FA OS demonstrated an expanding focal macular leakage (“inkblot” pattern). No history of steroid use or systemic disease. Clinical picture consistent with acute central serous chorioretinopathy of the left eye. Plan: observation with close follow-up given spontaneous resolution is likely within 3 months; consider focal laser or PDT if persistent/chronic.
Primary Diagnosis: Central serous chorioretinopathy
Differential Diagnosis: Central serous chorioretinopathy (idiopathic); Choroidal neovascular membrane (exudative AMD or myopic CNV); Posterior uveitis/VKH or other inflammatory serous detachment; Choroidal tumor (melanoma/hemangioma)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Left
Complications: scotomas; subretinal neovascularization; expansion of laser burns


Plan
All Medications: +2.00 lens; oral rifampin; methotrexate; carbonic anhydrase inhibitors; metoprolol; propranolol; ketoconazole; mifepristone; eplerenone; spironolactone; finasteride; systemic corticosteroids (stopping advised); anti-vascular endothelial growth factor injections (eg, anti-VEGF)
All Procedures: Observation/close follow-up (no procedural intervention)
Follow-Up Instructions: Observe closely; return for follow-up exam with OCT and visual acuity monitoring within 4–12 weeks (or sooner if symptoms worsen). If subretinal fluid persists beyond ~3 months or vision/functionally impaired, consider treatment (PDT or focal laser) and referral for retinal specialist. Stop systemic corticosteroids if present. Return immediately for new pain, increasing vision loss, or metamorphopsia.
Patient Education: Central serous chorioretinopathy (CSCR) is a usually self‑limited condition where fluid collects under the central retina, causing blurry vision and distortion. Most cases improve on their own within 3 months; close observation with follow‑up visits and OCT imaging is standard. Avoid or stop systemic corticosteroids if possible and discuss any stress or medications that may contribute. If fluid persists or vision is significantly affected, treatments such as photodynamic therapy or focal laser may be recommended. Report any worsening vision, increased distortion, new flashes/floaters, or vision loss promptly. Follow‑up eye exams are needed to monitor for recurrence or contralateral involvement.
Emergency Instructions: Seek immediate ophthalmic care if you experience sudden worsening of vision, new severe eye pain, flashes of light, a sudden increase in floaters, or signs of infection (redness, discharge, fever).


CASE 41 — Epiretinal Membrane
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Progressively decreasing vision in the right eye for 18 months with metamorphopsia
DURATION: 18 months
LATERALITY: right
PATIENT QUOTES: "I've had progressively decreasing vision in my right eye for about 18 months."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 73-year-old woman with an 18-month history of progressively decreasing vision in the right eye, associated metamorphopsia; prior right retinal detachment repaired with pars plana vitrectomy, endolaser, and gas 2 years earlier; presenting best corrected visual acuity 20/200.
History Narrative: A 73-year-old woman presented with an 18-month history of progressively decreasing vision in the right eye, with metamorphopsia and best corrected Snellen visual acuity of 20/200. She had undergone a primary pars plana vitrectomy with endolaser and gas for a retinal detachment 2 years previously.
Symptom Onset: 18 months
Symptom Duration: 18 months
Progression: Progressively decreasing over 18 months
Characteristics: Prominent epiretinal membrane with vascular distortion, foveal contour distortion and increased foveal thickness (hyperreflective preretinal membrane on OCT)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Progressively decreasing vision over 18 months with metamorphopsia; prior retinal detachment and pars plana vitrectomy (previous retinal surgery) contributing as an aggravating factor
Associated Symptoms: Metamorphopsia
Previous Episodes: Retinal detachment repaired with primary pars plana vitrectomy, endolaser, and gas 2 years prior.
Prior Treatments: Pars plana vitrectomy, endolaser, and gas (for retinal detachment repair)
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant impairment — progressive decreased vision to 20/200 with metamorphopsia limiting visual function, improved to 20/60 after surgery.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Previous retinal detachment and prior retinal surgery (pars plana vitrectomy/endolaser)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Method: Best corrected Snellen
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: white and quiet
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea with no abnormalities noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Pseudophakic


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal and reactive, round pupils approximately 3–4 mm
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk and equal bilaterally
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions (intact)
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: cup-to-disc ratio 0.3, optic disc normal color and margins, no pallor
Fundus Exam – Macula: Prominent epiretinal membrane with foveal contour distortion and retinal thickening; vascular distortion temporal to the fovea.
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Moderate vascular distortion
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Lattice degeneration with a previous peripheral retinal break and signs of prior retinal detachment repair (sclerotomy/gas/endolaser changes) in the right peripheral retina


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: ERM secondary to prior peripheral retinal break and retinal detachment in the right eye, presenting with 18 months of progressive visual decline and metamorphopsia. Exam and OCT demonstrate a prominent epiretinal membrane with foveal contour distortion and increased foveal thickness causing best corrected visual acuity 20/200. Given symptomatic visual impairment and OCT findings, patient underwent pars plana vitrectomy with ERM peel (± ILM peel). At 3 months postoperatively visual acuity improved to 20/60 with marked reduction in metamorphopsia and no clinical or OCT evidence of recurrent ERM; foveal thickness decreased and contour improved.
Primary Diagnosis: ERM secondary to previous peripheral retinal break and retinal detachment, right eye
Secondary Diagnoses: Retinal detachment (repaired)
Differential Diagnosis: Cystoid macular edema; Vitreomacular traction; Hypotony/hypotony-induced choroidal folds (retinopathy)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right
Complications: Retinal detachment; progressive nuclear sclerosis/cataract formation


Plan
All Medications: Topical corticosteroid drops; topical antibiotic drops
All Procedures: Pars plana vitrectomy; Endolaser; Gas tamponade; Vitrectomy with epiretinal membrane peel (ERM peel); Internal limiting membrane peel (often performed with ERM removal)
Follow-Up Instructions: Topical corticosteroid and antibiotic eye drops postoperatively; routine follow-up to monitor visual acuity and OCT (visual improvement most rapid over first 6 weeks, maximal by 3–6 months); monitor for complications (retinal detachment, cataract) and consider cataract surgery if progressive nuclear sclerosis develops.
Patient Education: An epiretinal membrane (ERM) is a thin layer of tissue that forms on the surface of the retina and can cause blurred vision and visual distortion (metamorphopsia). It is commonly diagnosed by eye exam and optical coherence tomography (OCT). If vision and symptoms are mild, careful observation is appropriate because ERMs often stabilize. Surgery (vitrectomy with membrane peel, sometimes with internal limiting membrane peel) is recommended if vision worsens to about 20/40–20/60 or if distortion is severe; most patients experience visual improvement over 3–6 months after surgery. There is no effective medication to remove the membrane. After surgery you will use steroid and antibiotic eye drops, vision usually improves most rapidly in the first 6 weeks and continues up to 3–6 months, and cataract progression is common after vitrectomy. Risks of surgery are low but include retinal detachment; recurrence of ERM is uncommon. Follow-up exams and OCT are used to monitor status.


CASE 42 — Vitreomacular Traction
================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Progressively worsening blurred vision and metamorphopsia in the right eye for 3 months, interfering with driving and reading
DURATION: 3 months
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "I have had progressively worsening blurred vision and metamorphopsia in my right eye for 3 months; it's interfering with my driving and reading."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 78-year-old woman with a 3-month history of progressively worsening blurred vision and metamorphopsia in the right eye, interfering with driving and reading.
History Narrative: A 78-year-old woman presented with a 3-month history of progressively worsening blurred vision and metamorphopsia in the right eye interfering with driving and reading abilities. The vision was 20/60 OD and 20/40 OS. Slit-lamp examination demonstrated clear cornea, absence of cataract, and intraocular pressure of 13 mm Hg in both eyes. Fundoscopic examination showed focal areas of adherent posterior vitreous to the macula associated with raised retinal tissue, OD, and a nonspecific loss of foveal reflex, OS. Optical coherence tomography in OD demonstrated a hyperreflective layer correspondent to the posterior hyaloid partially attached to the macula, leading to elevation and distortion of the retina. The OCT OS also showed focal vitreomacular attachment with distortion of the inner foveal layers. An intravitreal ocriplasmin injection was performed OD without improvement in vision or OCT appearance after 1 month. A pars plana vitrectomy was performed OD with release of the vitreomacular traction and improvement of the foveal contour. The vision was 20/30 OD 1 month postoperatively. The OS has remained unchanged with vision of 20/40 without clinically important or progressive symptoms, so it has been observed.
Symptom Onset: 3 months
Symptom Duration: 3 months
Progression: Progressive
Characteristics: Vitreomacular traction with focal posterior hyaloid adherence causing elevation and distortion of the macula, associated metamorphopsia and decreased vision (progressive OD, nonprogressive OS)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Driving and reading (activities requiring central visual acuity), progressive symptoms over months, broad traction or intraretinal cystoid changes (if present), and tasks revealing metamorphopsia or decreased vision.
Alleviating Factors: Observation, pars plana vitrectomy (PPV), intravitreal ocriplasmin injection, or expansile intravitreal gas (pneumatic maculopexy)
Associated Symptoms: progressively worsening blurred vision and metamorphopsia interfering with driving and reading, decreased vision, metamorphopsia, photopsia, diplopia
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes
Prior Treatments: Intravitreal ocriplasmin injection OD; pars plana vitrectomy (PPV) OD
Impact on Daily Activities: Interfering with driving and reading abilities
Systemic Symptoms: None
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Age (older age), presence of epiretinal membrane (ERM), uveitis, retinal breaks, retinal vascular diseases (vein occlusions, diabetic retinopathy, macular telangiectasia), broad vitreomacular adhesion (>1,500 μm)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 76 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N8
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 13 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 13 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Lens: clear cornea, absence of cataract


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal and reactive
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk and equal bilaterally
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: brisk and equal OU
Extraocular Movements: Full extraocular motility without restriction
Slit Lamp Exam Description: clear cornea, absence of cataract, intraocular pressure 13 mm Hg OU
Fundus Exam – Macula: Focal areas of adherent posterior vitreous to the macula with raised retinal tissue OD and nonspecific loss of foveal reflex OS
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal appearing vessels
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits (no peripheral retinal breaks or lesions noted)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 78-year-old woman with symptomatic progressive vitreomacular traction (VMT) OD causing decreased vision and metamorphopsia; OCT confirmed focal posterior hyaloid attachment with retinal elevation/distortion. Prior intravitreal ocriplasmin OD failed; underwent pars plana vitrectomy with release of VMT and improved foveal contour and VA to 20/30 OD at 1 month. OS has focal, nonprogressive VMT with stable vision 20/40 and is being observed.
Primary Diagnosis: Vitreomacular traction OD
Differential Diagnosis: Vitreomacular traction (VMT); Full-thickness macular hole (FTMH); Epiretinal membrane (ERM); Myopic traction maculopathy
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: OD
Complications: dyschromatopsia; electroretinographic changes; ellipsoid layer disruption; cystoid macular edema; foveal neurosensory detachment; lamellar or full-thickness macular hole


Plan
All Medications: ocriplasmin
All Procedures: Intravitreal ocriplasmin injection OD; Pars plana vitrectomy (PPV) OD
Follow-Up Instructions: Educate patient to self-monitor with Amsler grid for worsening metamorphopsia, decreased vision, or new scotoma; schedule periodic evaluations (follow-up visits with OCT as indicated) for observation.
Patient Education: Educated to self-monitor vision with Amsler grid testing and to report worsening metamorphopsia, decreased vision, or new scotomas; advised that observation is acceptable for minimal symptoms, that spontaneous release can occur (especially with focal attachment), and that options if progressive include intravitreal ocriplasmin, expansile gas injection, or pars plana vitrectomy.


CASE 43 — Vitreous Hemorrhage
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Sudden decreased vision in the left eye for 3 months
DURATION: 3 months
LATERALITY: OS
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 90-year-old woman with a 3-month history of sudden decreased vision in the left eye.
History Narrative: This 90-year-old woman presented with a 3-month history of sudden decreased vision in the left eye. Past ocular history included vision of 20/50 OD and 20/200 OS three years earlier due to atrophic age-related macular degeneration OD and a previously treated subfoveal choroidal neovascular membrane OS. Medical history was negative for diabetes and hypertension.
Symptom Onset: 3 months
Symptom Duration: 3 months
Progression: Improved (visual acuity improved from 2/200 to 20/400 over follow-up; observed improvement over 2–3 months)
Characteristics: Dense vitreous hemorrhage in the left eye precluding posterior view; associated with posterior vitreous detachment and subfoveal/contiguous subretinal hemorrhage with breakthrough bleeding from choroidal neovascularization.


Subjective (cont.)
Alleviating Factors: Sleeping in a slightly inclined (head-elevated) position
Associated Symptoms: sudden decreased vision in the left eye
Prior Treatments: previously treated subfoveal choroidal neovascular membrane OS (laser)
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant impairment — left eye vision reduced to 2/200 (improving to 20/400), limiting binocular visual function but with good fellow-eye vision permitting some daily activities.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: Advanced age; prior treated subfoveal choroidal neovascularization (age-related macular degeneration); posterior vitreous detachment; history of subretinal hemorrhage/breakthrough bleeding; absence of diabetes or hypertension (i.e., non-diabetic etiology)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/80 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 2/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 2/200
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Within normal limits
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: clear, no injection
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: clear cornea
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal appearing iris with no abnormality noted
Anterior Segment – Lens: Posterior chamber intraocular lens OD; 2+ nuclear sclerosis OS


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: No relative afferent pupillary defect; pupils normal in size and shape
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: afferent pupillary defect absent
Extraocular Movements: Full and painless in both eyes
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Slit-lamp examination showed a well-positioned posterior chamber lens implant on the right; in the left eye, 2+ nuclear sclerosis; vitreous adherent to the wound superiorly.
Fundus Exam – Macula: subfoveal laser scar with contiguous subretinal hemorrhage extending inferiorly to the midperiphery
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Dense vitreous hemorrhage precluding posterior view; on follow-up, subretinal hemorrhage extending inferiorly to the midperiphery with contiguous subfoveal laser scar; no retinal tear or detachment on B-scan; peripheral retina intact to ora serrata on examination.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Vitreous hemorrhage OS most consistent with breakthrough bleeding from subfoveal choroidal neovascularization secondary to previously treated age-related macular degeneration. B-scan ultrasound demonstrated vitreous hemorrhage with posterior vitreous detachment OS without evidence of retinal tear or detachment. Visual acuity OS 2/200 (improving to 20/400 on follow-up) with limited visual potential (≈20/200) and good fellow-eye vision; no diabetes or hypertensive proliferative retinopathy suspected. Plan: observation with close follow-up given lack of tear/detachment and limited surgical benefit at this time; consider vitrectomy only if hemorrhage fails to clear by 3–6 months or if bilateral vision threatened.
Primary Diagnosis: Vitreous hemorrhage due to breakthrough bleeding associated with choroidal neovascularization, OS
Secondary Diagnoses: Posterior vitreous detachment; Atrophic age-related macular degeneration OD; Previously treated subfoveal choroidal neovascular membrane OS; Pseudophakia OD; Nuclear sclerosis OS
Differential Diagnosis: Breakthrough vitreous hemorrhage from choroidal neovascularization (AMD); Posterior vitreous detachment with retinal tear; Proliferative retinopathies (e.g., proliferative diabetic retinopathy, vein occlusion); Vitreous opacities from posterior uveitis/pars planitis
Severity: Very severe
Laterality: OS


Plan
All Procedures: B-scan/A-scan ultrasonography; Indirect ophthalmoscopy with scleral depression; Laser photocoagulation; Diagnostic vitrectomy; Pars plana vitrectomy (vitrectomy) including possible scleral buckling and other vitreoretinal surgical maneuvers; Positioning (sleeping in an inclined position)
Follow-Up Instructions: Observed with close follow-up; monitor for spontaneous clearing and visual improvement with repeat evaluation (including dilated exam and B‑scan as needed); if no clearing within 3–6 months consider vitrectomy (earlier—2–6 months—in proliferative disease); counsel re expectations and consider surgery sooner if bilateral loss or signs of retinal tear/detachment.
Patient Education: You have bleeding into the gel (vitreous) inside your eye, most likely from abnormal blood vessels under the retina related to your previously treated macular degeneration. This can cause sudden vision loss but sometimes clears on its own over weeks to months. Key points: - We’ll monitor you closely with exams and ultrasound to make sure there is no retinal tear or detachment that needs urgent treatment. - There is no medicine that reliably speeds clearing of vitreous blood; positioning (sleeping slightly upright) may let the blood settle so we can examine the eye better but won’t hasten recovery. - If the blood does not clear over 3–6 months or if a treatable cause (like proliferative disease or a retinal tear) is found, a vitrectomy (surgical removal of the blood) may be recommended; timing depends on visual potential and underlying disease. - If you notice worsening vision, new flashes, a shadow or curtain over your vision, increasing pain, or other sudden changes, contact us immediately. - Keep routine follow-up appointments and bring any changes in vision to care promptly; the goal is to preserve remaining vision and treat any treatable cause.


CASE 44 — Retinitis Pigmentosa
==============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Progressive difficulty seeing at night (nyctalopia) and decreased peripheral vision.
DURATION: 14 years
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "clumsy"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 24-year-old woman with progressive night vision difficulties since age 10, long-standing nyctalopia and clumsiness bumping into objects, stopped driving at night one year ago because unsafe; otherwise healthy, no family history of ocular problems, no consanguinity.
History Narrative: A 24-year-old woman with progressive visual difficulties at night was evaluated. Since the age of 10, she has had trouble performing outdoor activities after dusk. A year ago she stopped driving at night because she no longer felt safe. For many years she has been “clumsy” and often walked into surrounding objects she could not see well. She was otherwise healthy. Family history was negative for ocular problems, and there was no consanguinity.
Symptom Onset: 10 years
Symptom Duration: 14 years
Progression: Gradual progressive visual loss over years with moderately advanced disease progression
Characteristics: Progressive rod and cone photoreceptor dysfunction and death leading to nyctalopia, progressive peripheral visual field loss, midperipheral retinal atrophy with bone‑spicule pigment clumping and vascular attenuation; central vision relatively spared until late; often sporadic/genetically heterogeneous.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Reduced retinal exposure to bright light / sun exposure (phototoxic UV/visible light)
Alleviating Factors: Wearing sunglasses to reduce retinal exposure to UV light; low-vision aids; treatment of cystoid macular edema (e.g., oral acetazolamide); cataract extraction when cataract develops; retinal implant for very advanced disease; oral vitamin A/lutein/DHA supplements (controversial).
Associated Symptoms: Progressive night vision difficulties (nyctalopia), clumsiness with bumping into objects, progressive loss of peripheral vision (midperipheral ring scotomas)
Impact on Daily Activities: Stopped driving at night; clumsy and often walked into objects; difficulty with outdoor activities after dusk — significant limitation in night-time mobility and driving.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Genetic inheritance (autosomal recessive, autosomal dominant, X-linked) and positive family history; sporadic cases (~50%); consanguinity increases risk; certain gene mutations (e.g., ABCA4)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 122/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
