Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+
Visual Acuity – Method: Best-corrected visual acuity
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: No ptosis; lids and lashes normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normaleappeara—no conjunctival injection; sclera without injection or icterus
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea, no anterior segment abnormalities noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal appearing iris (no abnormalities noted)
Anterior Segment – Lens: No cataract; lens clear.


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, reactive to light (no relative afferent pupillary defect)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: No relative afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: No relative afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion bilaterally (intact)
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Within normal limits (no optic nerve pallor or swelling noted)
Fundus Exam – Macula: Macula relatively spared; no macular edema or atrophic macular lesion noted on exam
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Attenuated (vascular attenuation)
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Midperipheral and peripheral retinal atrophy with pigment clumping ("bone spicules") and retinal vascular attenuation; prominent choroidal vasculature visible in areas of atrophy.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 44-year-old woman with progressive nyctalopia and peripheral vision loss since childhood. Best-corrected visual acuity 20/25 OU, no RAPD. Goldmann fields show large midperipheral ring-shaped scotomas OU. Fundus: midperipheral and peripheral retinal atrophy with pigment clumping (“bone spicules”) and retinal vascular attenuation. Full-field ERG: nondetectable rod and cone responses. Family history negative. Diagnosis: retinitis pigmentosa, sporadic, moderately advanced. Recommend genetic testing to determine genotype and inheritance, consider OCT to evaluate for CME or macular atrophy, counseling regarding prognosis, sun protection, low vision rehabilitation as needed; discuss potential risks/benefits of vitamin A/DHA/lutein and consider referral for clinical trials or retinal implant evaluation if vision declines.
Primary Diagnosis: Retinitis pigmentosa, sporadic, moderately advanced
Differential Diagnosis: Retinitis pigmentosa; Choroideremia; Gyrate atrophy; Vitamin A deficiency
Severity: moderately advanced
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: cystoid macular edema; posterior subcapsular cataract; optic nerve atrophy; vitreous syneresis; mild vitritis; progressive visual field loss leading to severe vision loss (including bare light perception/no light perception)


Plan
All Medications: vitamin A palmitate 15,000 IU daily; vitamin E (oral); lutein (oral); docosahexaenoic acid (DHA, oral); acetazolamide (oral); dorzolamide (topical)
All Procedures: Goldmann visual field testing Funduscopic examination Full-field electroretinography (ERG) Optical coherence tomography (OCT) Automated 30-degree visual field testing Automated kinetic visual field testing Genetic testing Retinal implant surgery Oral vitamin A therapy (medication) Oral lutein and DHA supplementation (medication) Oral acetazolamide therapy (medication) Topical dorzolamide (medication) Cataract extraction (phacoemulsification) Laser posterior capsulotomy Low vision rehabilitation/low vision aids Periodic liver function testing
Follow-Up Instructions: If cataract or CME develops, consider treatment; monitor yearly and provide periodic follow-up for vision, OCT if decreased acuity to assess CME, and counseling/low-vision referral as needed.
Patient Education: - Retinitis pigmentosa (RP) is an inherited, progressive retinal degeneration causing night blindness and loss of peripheral vision; central vision is often preserved until late. - The disease is genetically heterogeneous; genetic testing can clarify inheritance, rule out syndromic forms, and enable trial eligibility. - Regular monitoring includes visual fields, OCT (to detect cystoid macular edema), and electroretinography as indicated. - Treatable complications: cystoid macular edema (may respond to oral acetazolamide), posterior subcapsular cataract (surgery may be needed), and low-vision rehabilitation. - Sunglasses to reduce retinal UV exposure are recommended. - Supplements (vitamin A palmitate, DHA, lutein) have uncertain benefit and potential genotype-specific harm (e.g., vitamin A may be harmful in ABCA4-related disease); discuss risks and benefits and consider genetic testing before starting supplements. - For very advanced disease (bare light perception or worse), retinal implants may offer rudimentary vision. - Counsel about variable prognosis, family counseling/genetic counseling, and available clinical trials.
Referrals: Genetic counseling/testing referral; low-vision rehabilitation services; retina specialist (for monitoring/treatment of CME, consideration of retinal implant in advanced disease)


CASE 45 — Choroiditis
=====================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: constant pain in the right eye for 3 weeks, blurred vision, redness, and tearing for 1 week
DURATION: 1 month
LATERALITY: Initially right eye, later became bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "I have constant pain in my right eye for 3 weeks, blurred vision, redness, and tearing for 1 week, and I've had a headache and stiff neck for a month."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 24-year-old Hispanic woman with 1-month history of headache and neck stiffness and 2-day prodromal flu‑like symptoms, who developed constant right eye pain for 3 weeks, and 1 week of blurred vision, redness, and tearing. No history of trauma. Lumbar puncture showed mild pleocytosis; initial visual acuity 20/20 OU but later right vision decreased to 3/200 with persistent pain and bilateral anterior chamber inflammation and right vitreous cells, optic disc edema, serous retinal detachments and focal RPE lesions.
History Narrative: A 24-year-old Hispanic woman complained of constant pain in the right eye for 3 weeks, blurred vision, redness, and tearing for 1 week, and a 1-month history of headache and neck stiffness. She had a 2-day history of flu-like symptoms prior to presentation and denied any history of trauma. CT of the brain was normal. Lumbar puncture showed a mild pleocytosis. Visual acuity was 20/20 OU. IOP was 18 mm Hg OD and 16 mm Hg OS. Slit-lamp exam disclosed moderate anterior chamber cell and flare OD with granulomatous keratic precipitates on the corneal endothelium; a mild anterior chamber inflammatory reaction was noted OS. Vitreous cells were present OD. Fundus exam OD showed optic disc edema with focal areas of serous retinal detachment and folding of the retina within the macular region; the left eye was unremarkable. B-scan ultrasonography OD showed diffuse choroidal thickening with low reflectivity and a shallow serous macular detachment. She was started on topical prednisolone acetate 1% and cycloplegics. One week later she returned with persistent pain and markedly decreased visual acuity OD to 3/200. The anterior chamber reaction had decreased slightly OD, while OS had increased cell and flare with a few keratic precipitates. Fundus exam showed increased optic disc edema, subretinal fluid and retinal folds, and focal yellow-white lesions at the level of the RPE in the macula OD. Vision was 20/25 OS with a new focal area of subretinal fluid in the nasal macula. Fluorescein angiography OD demonstrated multiple areas of pinpoint hyperfluorescence in the juxtapapillary region and macula that leaked in the later frames; a similar angiographic appearance was noted adjacent to the optic disc OS.
Symptom Onset: 3 weeks (constant right eye pain), with blurred vision/redness/tearing for 1 week, and headache/neck stiffness for 1 month
Symptom Duration: 3 weeks
Progression: Worsening — progressed from unilateral mild inflammation with serous detachments to bilateral involvement with increased optic disc edema and marked vision loss in right eye (20/20 → 3/200) over one week
Characteristics: Bilateral granulomatous panuveitis with diffuse choroidal thickening, optic disc edema, serous (exudative) retinal detachments, multiple focal yellow‑white RPE/choroidal lesions (pinpoint hyperfluorescence on FA), vitritis, CSF pleocytosis, and association with neurologic prodrome and pigmentary/cutaneous changes (VKH syndrome).


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: constant right eye pain, blurred vision, redness, tearing, headache, neck stiffness, flu-like symptoms, tinnitus (possible), photophobia
Previous Episodes: No prior ocular trauma or surgery.
Prior Treatments: topical prednisolone acetate 1% and cycloplegics
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant limitation — marked visual impairment in right eye (vision decreased to 3/200) with pain, photophobia, and bilateral inflammation likely causing difficulty with reading, driving, and routine visual tasks.
Systemic Symptoms: headache, neck stiffness, and flu-like symptoms (including fever prodrome)
Risk Factors: Hispanic ethnicity; female sex; age 20–40 years; HLA-DR4/DQ4 association; darker skin pigmentation (groups with greater skin pigmentation).


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/76 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 37.0°C
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 18 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: injection (redness) of the right conjunctiva; left conjunctiva mildly injected
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Granulomatous keratic precipitates on the corneal endothelium
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: moderate cell and flare in the right eye; mild cell and flare in the left eye
Anterior Segment – Iris: granulomatous keratic precipitates on the corneal endothelium with moderate anterior chamber cell and flare (iris: granulomatous iridocyclitis)
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: phakic


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal and reactive to light; normal size and round
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive bilaterally
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Moderate anterior chamber cell and flare in the right eye with granulomatous keratic precipitates on the corneal endothelium; mild anterior chamber inflammatory reaction in the left eye.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Optic disc edema
Fundus Exam – Macula: Serous retinal detachment with retinal folds and focal yellow-white RPE lesions in the macula; subretinal fluid and focal yellow-white RPE lesions superior and temporal to the macula.
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal appearing retinal vessels
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No peripheral retinal lesions; periphery within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 24-year-old Hispanic woman with bilateral granulomatous panuveitis, optic disc edema, multifocal serous retinal detachments, pinpoint RPE hyperfluorescence on FA, diffuse low-reflectivity choroidal thickening on B-scan, and CSF lymphocytic pleocytosis. No history of ocular trauma. Presentation, demographics, neurological signs, bilateral posterior segment involvement and imaging are most consistent with Vogt–Koyanagi–Harada (VKH) syndrome.
Primary Diagnosis: Vogt–Koyanagi–Harada syndrome
Differential Diagnosis: Vogt–Koyanagi–Harada (VKH) syndrome; sympathetic ophthalmia; sarcoidosis; infectious choroiditis (e.g., tuberculosis, syphilis)
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: posterior synechiae; angle-closure glaucoma; cataract formation; choroidal neovascularization


Plan
All Medications: prednisolone acetate 1% (topical), cycloplegics, oral prednisone (systemic corticosteroids 80–200 mg/day), ranitidine 150 mg twice daily, intravenous steroids (for severe cases), cyclosporine, cyclophosphamide, chlorambucil, azathioprine
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow frequently in the initial stages to adjust systemic corticosteroids for control of inflammation and prevention of sequelae; once inflammation and exudative detachment decrease, taper steroids gradually with less frequent exams; if steroids inadequate or intolerable, consider steroid-sparing immunosuppressants and monitor regularly for cataract, choroidal neovascularization, and angle‑closure glaucoma.
Patient Education: You have Vogt–Koyanagi–Harada (VKH) syndrome, an inflammatory condition that affects the eye and can cause vision loss if not treated promptly. Key points: - Treatment: High‑dose oral corticosteroids (often tapered slowly over 4–6 months) are required to control inflammation; sometimes intravenous steroids or other immunosuppressive medicines are used if needed. - Purpose: Early, aggressive treatment reduces swelling, resolves serous retinal detachments, and lowers the risk of permanent vision loss. - Eye care: Use prescribed topical steroid eye drops and cycloplegic drops as directed; attend frequent follow‑up visits initially so your doctor can adjust medications based on inflammation and vision. - Side‑effect prevention: Take stomach protection (e.g., ranitidine or other agent) while on high‑dose steroids and report any severe side effects (mood changes, high blood sugar, new infections, weight gain). - Symptoms to report urgently: sudden vision loss, increasing pain, redness, flashes/floaters, severe headache, hearing changes, or new skin/hair changes (vitiligo, hair loss). - Long‑term: Slow tapering of steroids over months reduces relapse risk; if steroids are insufficient or cause intolerable side effects, steroid‑sparing immunosuppressants may be recommended. - Monitoring: Regular eye exams to watch for complications (cataract, glaucoma, choroidal neovascularization) and systemic symptoms; consider baseline and periodic blood tests if on systemic immunosuppression.


CASE 46 — Retinitis
===================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: new onset of multiple black spots in the visual field of the right and left eyes for 2 weeks
DURATION: 2 weeks
LATERALITY: OU
PATIENT QUOTES: "new onset of multiple black spots in the visual field of the right and left eyes for 2 weeks"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 32-year-old woman with a 10-year history of AIDS and CD4 count 64 on HAART presenting with 2 weeks of new onset multiple black spots (floaters) in the visual fields of both eyes.
History Narrative: A 32-year-old woman with a 10-year history of acquired immune deficiency syndrome (AIDS) complained of new onset of multiple black spots in the visual field of the right and left eyes for 2 weeks. She had a CD4 count of 64 and was on highly active antiretroviral therapy (HAART). Examination revealed a best corrected visual acuity of 20/30 in the right eye and 20/40 in the left eye. The intraocular pressure was 10 mm Hg in each eye. The anterior segment was unremarkable with no anterior chamber cell or flare. Funduscopic examination of the right eye showed creamy yellowish-white retinitis with prominent, associated hemorrhage within the lesion. There was active vitritis and vascular sheathing superior to the disc extending along the superotemporal arcade. The left eye had a peripheral area of retinitis temporally.
Symptom Onset: 2 weeks
Symptom Duration: 2 weeks
Progression: Progressive
Characteristics: Infectious, typically progressive retinal inflammation due to opportunistic microbes (most commonly CMV) with creamy yellow-white necrotizing retinitis often with hemorrhage, possible vitritis and vascular sheathing, unilateral or bilateral, risk of retinal detachment.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Immunosuppression (low CD4 count/AIDS; systemic opportunistic infection)
Alleviating Factors: HAART (highly active antiretroviral therapy) and appropriate anti‑CMV therapy (e.g., ganciclovir, foscarnet, cidofovir, intravitreal antiviral or ganciclovir implant)
Associated Symptoms: floaters, photopsias, cloudy vision
Prior Treatments: HAART (highly active antiretroviral therapy)
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — floaters and reduced vision (20/30 OD, 20/40 OS) causing visual disturbance but not disabling; patient can likely perform many activities with some difficulty.
Systemic Symptoms: 10-year history of AIDS (HIV-positive), low CD4 count 64; on HAART; no fever or other systemic symptoms reported.
Risk Factors: HIV infection/AIDS with CD4 count 64; immunosuppression; prior CMV exposure (latent CMV); being on HAART (delayed CMV until later)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 118/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N5 (R) / N6 (L)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart (best corrected visual acuity)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 10 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 10 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: No conjunctival injection
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: No anterior chamber cell or flare
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: open
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils equal and reactive, normal size
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk and equal
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Brisk
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: Pupils equal and reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Unremarkable anterior segment with no anterior chamber cell or flare
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal appearing optic nerve with superior nerve head margin involved by retinitis (confluent wedge extending from superior nerve head)
Fundus Exam – Macula: Macula spared (no macular involvement)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: vascular sheathing superior to the disc extending along the superotemporal arcade
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Peripheral retinal detachment risk with areas of necrotic retinitis in midperiphery (temporal midperiphery OS; superior arcade extension into midperiphery OD)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: CMV retinitis, OU, in a patient with AIDS.
Primary Diagnosis: CMV retinitis, OU
Secondary Diagnoses: AIDS (HIV infection), cytomegalovirus (CMV) infection
Differential Diagnosis: Cytomegalovirus (CMV) retinitis; Acute retinal necrosis (herpetic retinitis); Ocular toxoplasmosis; Ocular syphilis
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: OU
Complications: Retinal detachment (40–50% within first year)


Plan
All Medications: highly active antiretroviral therapy (HAART); ganciclovir (IV 5 mg/kg induction, intravitreal 2000 μg, implant for sustained delivery); foscarnet (IV 60 mg/kg three times daily induction, intravitreal 1.2 mg); cidofovir (IV 5 mg/kg)
All Procedures: ophthalmoscopic screening every 3–6 months; indirect ophthalmoscopy; anterior chamber paracentesis for PCR; intravenous ganciclovir induction (5 mg/kg once daily); intravenous foscarnet induction (60 mg/kg three times daily); cidofovir IV induction (5 mg/kg weekly then q2 weeks maintenance); intravitreal ganciclovir injection (2000 µg); intravitreal foscarnet injection (1.2 mg); intravitreal ganciclovir implant insertion; pars plana vitrectomy with silicone oil tamponade; laser demarcation of small peripheral retinal detachments
Follow-Up Instructions: Close follow-up with photographic documentation.
Patient Education: Explain that retinitis in the setting of AIDS is most commonly caused by cytomegalovirus (CMV) and can progress to severe vision loss if untreated. Symptoms often start with floaters, blurred vision, or peripheral black spots; report any new visual changes immediately. Diagnosis is by eye exam and sometimes PCR of eye fluid. Treatment includes systemic antiviral therapy (ganciclovir, foscarnet, or cidofovir) and/or intravitreal antivirals or a ganciclovir implant; discuss side effects (bone marrow suppression with ganciclovir, renal toxicity with foscarnet). Continued HIV care and maintaining/optimizing HAART to raise CD4 counts is essential to prevent recurrence. Regular ophthalmic follow-up (every 3–6 months if CD4 <100, more often while active disease) and prompt evaluation for symptoms reduce risk of complications such as retinal detachment, which may require surgery. Emphasize adherence to medications and follow-up, and notify clinicians about fever, systemic infections, or toxicities from antivirals.


CASE 47 — The White Dot Syndromes
=================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: decreased vision and “blind spots” in both eyes
DURATION: 1 week
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "blind spots"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 26-year-old healthy Caucasian man with 1-week history of decreased vision and "blind spots" in both eyes, preceded 3 weeks earlier by fevers and headaches. Best-corrected VA 20/30 OU; no pain, no photophobia or floaters reported.
History Narrative: A 26-year-old healthy Caucasian man with a 1-week history of decreased vision and “blind spots” in both eyes, reporting recent fevers and headaches 3 weeks prior to his ocular symptoms.
Symptom Onset: 1 week
Symptom Duration: 1 week
Progression: Chronic with potential recurrences and risk of complications (can be self-limited in APMPPE but may progress to chronic/relentless placoid chorioretinitis or overlap with serpiginous choroiditis)
Characteristics: Multiple discrete deep, creamy‑yellow placoid lesions at the level of the outer retina/RPE, often bilateral, with minimal anterior or vitreous inflammation, early hypofluorescence and late staining on FA, hypofluorescent lesions on ICG, outer retinal and RPE disruption on OCT, typically following a viral prodrome and self‑limited with good visual prognosis.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: decreased vision, blind spots, recent fevers and headaches, photopsias
Prior Treatments: Oral corticosteroids (frequently used; recommended when lesions involve central macula or if cerebral vasculitis)
Impact on Daily Activities: Likely mild–moderate transient impairment (central vision blur/scotomas causing difficulty with reading, driving, and detailed tasks for weeks to months)
Systemic Symptoms: viral prodrome, headaches
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: Recent viral illness contacts (upper respiratory infection exposure)
Risk Factors: Young age (20–30 years), Caucasian race, recent viral prodrome (fever/headache/upper respiratory infection)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 37.0 °C
Vitals – Respirations: 16
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N5
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear.
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Normal
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: quiet (no anterior chamber cell)
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: clear


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Normal
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive bilaterally
Pupils – RAPD: No relative afferent pupillary defect (RAPD)
Pupils – Other: no afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: No anterior segment inflammation; anterior segment normal, no anterior chamber cell or flare.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Normal appearing optic nerve (no edema)
Fundus Exam – Macula: multiple, deep, creamy, placoid lesions in the posterior pole involving the macula
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Multiple small to medium deep creamy/placoid lesions in the posterior pole and midperiphery; otherwise peripheral fundus without mention of lattice, tears, or hemorrhage (no significant peripheral abnormalities)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 26-year-old healthy Caucasian man with 1-week history of bilateral decreased central vision and scotomas following a viral prodrome 3 weeks prior. Exam: BCVA 20/30 OU, normal IOPs, no APD, quiet anterior segment and vitreous. Fundus: multiple, flat, deep, creamy placoid lesions in the posterior pole OU. FA: multiple early hypofluorescent areas with late staining corresponding to lesions. ICG: decreased fluorescence of corresponding lesions. OCT: increased outer retinal reflectivity, disruption of outer segments and RPE abnormalities corresponding to clinical lesions. Findings most consistent with acute posterior multifocal placoid pigment epitheliopathy (APMPPE). Recommend observation given self-limited nature; consider oral corticosteroids if central macula involved or if neurologic signs suggesting CNS vasculitis. Evaluate for neurologic symptoms and consider neuroimaging if indicated. Follow with serial fundus photography, FA/ICG and OCT to document resolution and monitor for rare complications.
Primary Diagnosis: Acute posterior multifocal placoid pigment epitheliopathy
Differential Diagnosis: APMPPE; Serpiginous choroiditis; MEWDS; Birdshot chorioretinopathy
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: CNVM, CME, ERM, RPE atrophy/scarring, optic neuropathy, neovascular glaucoma


Plan
All Medications: oral corticosteroids periocular steroids immunosuppression (immunomodulatory therapy) cyclosporine mycophenolate azathioprine low-dose steroids antituberculosis therapy intravitreal steroids anti-vascular endothelial growth factor (anti-VEGF) laser photocoagulation
All Procedures: Fluorescein angiography (FA); Indocyanine green angiography (ICG); Optical coherence tomography (OCT); Dilated fundus examination (clinical fundus photography implied)
Patient Education: This condition involves inflammation of the outer retina and pigment layer (a “white dot” syndrome). It often follows a viral illness and typically improves on its own over weeks to months. Expect monitoring with eye imaging (fluorescein angiography, ICG, and OCT) to document lesions and healing. If lesions involve the central macula or if there are neurologic symptoms, oral corticosteroids or neurologic evaluation may be recommended. Report new symptoms promptly (worse vision, new central blind spot, severe headache, weakness, or confusion). Return for scheduled follow-up imaging and visual testing to watch for complications such as scarring or abnormal new blood vessels that may need treatment.
Referrals: neuroimaging with appropriate referral (neurology)


CASE 48 — Vascular Tumor
========================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Peripheral retinal lesion in the left eye found on routine ophthalmic examination
LATERALITY: Left
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 20-year-old man found to have a peripheral retinal lesion in the left eye on routine ophthalmic exam; asymptomatic with no ocular complaints, no past ocular history, no systemic medical problems. Family history notable for father and two siblings with von Hippel–Lindau disease. Visual acuity 20/20 OU; left fundus shows dilated tortuous arterioles and venules from the optic disc leading to reddish‑orange retinal nodules with small adjacent subretinal fluid and exudate; right eye normal.
History Narrative: A 20-year-old man presents for evaluation of a peripheral retinal lesion in the left eye found on routine ophthalmic examination. The patient is asymptomatic and has no other past ocular history. He has no known systemic medical problems. A family history screening discloses his father and two siblings carry a diagnosis of von Hippel–Lindau disease. Visual acuity is 20/20 bilaterally. External examination is unremarkable. There is no afferent pupillary defect. Visual field and motility examinations are unremarkable. Slit-lamp examination is normal. The left fundus contains several dilated, tortuous arterioles and venules emanating from the optic disc and traveling nasally. These vessels lead to a collection of reddish-pink nodules surrounding a large reddish-orange lesion emanating from the surface of the retina. There is a small associated area of subretinal fluid with adjacent exudate. Funduscopic examination of the right eye is normal.
Symptom Duration: Asymptomatic
Progression: Progressive enlargement with potential exudation leading to subretinal fluid and exudative retinal detachment
Characteristics: Multiple dilated, tortuous feeding arterioles and draining venules with reddish‑orange retinal nodules, associated subretinal fluid and exudate; endophytic inner retinal origin with potential for exudative or vitreoretinal (endophytic) growth patterns.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: Asymptomatic
Previous Episodes: No prior ocular history
Prior Treatments: Observation (no prior treatment)
Impact on Daily Activities: None — patient is asymptomatic with 20/20 vision bilaterally and unremarkable functional exams.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: Father and two siblings
Risk Factors: Family history of von Hippel–Lindau disease (autosomal dominant genetic predisposition)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen fraction
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Unremarkable
External Exam – Orbit: Unremarkable
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Normal
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lens; no lenticular abnormality noted


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Normal, equal, round, reactive (no afferent pupillary defect)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: No afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Briskly reactive to near
Pupils – RAPD: No afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Other: No afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full motility without restriction
Slit Lamp Exam Description: normal
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: unremarkable
Fundus Exam – Macula: Macula: Normal (20/20 vision, no macular exudation reported)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: dilated, tortuous arterioles and venules emanating from the optic disc and traveling nasally, including dilated feeding arteriole and draining venule
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Collection of reddish‑orange tumors in the nasal periphery with dilated feeding arterioles and draining venules, small adjacent subretinal fluid and exudate


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 1. Multiple retinal angiomas OS consistent with retinal angiomatosis (von Hippel lesions). 2. Associated von Hippel–Lindau disease given positive family history (father and two siblings) and characteristic funduscopic findings.
Primary Diagnosis: Multiple retinal angiomas
Secondary Diagnoses: von Hippel–Lindau disease
Differential Diagnosis: Cavernous hemangioma; Racemose hemangioma (arteriovenous malformation); Vasoproliferative tumor; Astrocytic hamartoma
Severity: Mild to moderate
Laterality: OS
Complications: - Subretinal fluid and adjacent exudation leading to exudative retinal detachment - Epiretinal membrane formation and vitreoretinal traction (possible tractional or combined tractional–rhegmatogenous retinal detachment) - Hemorrhage during surgical intervention - Recurrence of lesion after treatment - Visual loss from macular involvement or retinal detachment


Plan
All Medications: Vascular endothelial growth factor inhibitors (antiangiogenic medicines; systemic or intravitreal)
All Procedures: indirect ophthalmoscopy; slit‑lamp biomicroscopy; fluorescein angiography; fluorescein angioscopy; B‑scan ultrasonography; A‑scan ultrasonography; CT imaging; MRI with gadolinium; observation; laser photocoagulation; cryotherapy (double or triple freeze‑thaw); plaque radiotherapy; external beam radiotherapy; pars plana vitrectomy; intravitreal anti‑VEGF/anti‑PDGF therapy; systemic antiangiogenic therapy; genetic testing; 24‑hour urine collection for pheochromocytoma; CNS and thoracic/abdominal imaging; periodic ophthalmic examinations (annual to biannual)
Follow-Up Instructions: Annual to biannual ophthalmic examination with indirect ophthalmoscopy for affected individuals and first‑degree relatives; periodic systemic screening including CNS and thoracic/abdominal imaging and 24‑hour urine collection for pheochromocytoma.
Patient Education: Explain that the retinal lesions are retinal angiomas associated with von Hippel–Lindau (VHL) disease, an inherited condition that can affect the eye and other organs. Reassure that the eye lesions may be monitored if small and not threatening vision, but can cause leaking, retinal detachment, or traction and therefore require treatment when causing or threatening vision loss. Discuss treatment options briefly: observation, laser photocoagulation, cryotherapy, plaque or external beam radiotherapy, intravitreal anti‑VEGF in select cases, and possible vitreoretinal surgery for tractional complications, noting that treatment choice depends on lesion size and location. Emphasize need for regular ophthalmic follow-up (typically every 6–12 months or more often if active) with dilated indirect ophthalmoscopy and imaging (fundus photos, fluorescein angiography, and ultrasound as indicated). Advise genetic counseling and testing for VHL given family history, and explain systemic screening is essential (brain and spinal MRI, abdominal imaging for kidney and pancreatic lesions, and biochemical testing for pheochromocytoma such as annual plasma or 24‑hour urine catecholamines/metanephrines). Recommend that first‑degree relatives undergo screening. Warn about symptoms that require urgent attention: new vision loss, flashes, floaters, sudden pain, or systemic symptoms like headaches, hypertension, palpitations, or abdominal pain. Provide resources/referrals: genetic counselor, neuro‑ophthalmology/retina specialist, and multidisciplinary VHL clinic or center.
Referrals: Neuroimaging (CNS) and systemic screening with thoracic/abdominal imaging; referral to genetics for VHL testing and counseling; referral to oncology/urology or appropriate specialists for systemic evaluation (renal imaging, pheochromocytoma workup/endocrinology)


CASE 49 — Choroidal Melanoma
============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Photopsias and blurred central vision in the right eye for 3 days (with 2-week history of photopsias)
DURATION: 2 weeks
LATERALITY: right
PATIENT QUOTES: "N/A"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 55-year-old Caucasian man with 2 weeks of photopsias and 3 days of blurred central vision in the right eye.
History Narrative: A 55-year-old Caucasian man presented with a 2-week history of photopsias and blurred central vision in the right eye for the previous 3 days. Past medical history was notable for hypertension, hypercholesterolemia, and skin cancer. Past ocular history was remarkable for macular degeneration. Examination disclosed a visual acuity of 20/60 in the right eye and 20/20 in the left eye. Intraocular pressures were 15 mm Hg in both eyes. There was a 1+ right afferent pupillary defect. Slit-lamp examination was notable for mild nuclear sclerotic alterations in both eyes. Dilated funduscopic examination of the right eye revealed a dome-shaped moderately pigmented inferotemporal mass with an exudative retinal detachment located anterior to the mass. Examination of the left eye was unremarkable. The lesion had a thickness of 4.5 mm and base of 11.9 × 10.1 mm confirmed by echography. B-scan ultrasonography demonstrated a dome-shaped lesion with an exudative retinal detachment and choroidal excavation. There was no evidence of extrascleral extension. A-scan ultrasonography revealed low internal reflectivity and a regular internal structure. Fluorescein angiography of the right eye demonstrated a lesion with intrinsic vascularity and progressive late leakage. The patient underwent a medical workup to rule out the presence of metastasis, including liver function tests and a chest X-ray that were unremarkable. The patient was diagnosed with a medium-sized posterior uveal melanoma and underwent fine-needle aspiration biopsy for gene-expression profiling at the time of iodine-125 radioactive plaque placement. Gene profiling yielded a class 1A tumor. Seven months following radioactive plaque therapy, visual acuity in the right eye was 20/40 with progressive nuclear sclerosis of the lens. The mass had decreased in size posttreatment to a height of 2.3 mm with significant pigmentary alterations and resolution of the overlying neurosensory detachment; the remainder of the retina showed no radiation-related changes.
Symptom Onset: 2 weeks
Symptom Duration: 2 weeks
Progression: Decreased (tumor reduced from 4.5 mm to 2.3 mm at 7 months posttreatment)
Characteristics: Dome-shaped moderately pigmented inferotemporal choroidal mass with intrinsic vascularity, low–medium internal reflectivity on A-scan, dome configuration with choroidal excavation and exudative retinal detachment, regular internal structure, sound attenuation, decreased size after plaque therapy with pigmentary alterations.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: Photopsias; blurred central vision; visual acuity loss; persistent photopsias; visual field defects
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — decreased right-eye central vision (20/60 → 20/40 after treatment) with photopsias and some visual disturbance but retained functional vision in fellow eye.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: Age (55), Caucasian race, history of skin cancer, choroidal nevus/ocular history (macular degeneration not a risk), possible UV-related risk inferred from fair skin—primary risk factors present: middle age, Caucasian race, personal history of skin cancer.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/80 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Within normal limits
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Unremarkable
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis; extraocular movements full and painless.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea, no abnormalities noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Within normal limits
Anterior Segment – Lens: mild nuclear sclerotic cataracts in both eyes


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 1+ right afferent pupillary defect (pupils equal and reactive to light otherwise)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: 1+ right afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – RAPD: 1+
Pupils – Other: 1+ right afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: mild nuclear sclerotic alterations in both eyes
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Exudative retinal detachment located anterior to the mass involving macula (blurred central vision); posttreatment: resolution of overlying neurosensory detachment with pigmentary alterations and remainder of macula normal without radiation-related vasculopathy.
Fundus Exam – Vessels: normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Right eye: medium-sized choroidal melanoma. 55-year-old Caucasian man with 2-week history of photopsias and 3 days of central blurring OD. Exam: VA 20/60 OD, 1+ RAPD, mild nuclear sclerotic cataracts OU, dome-shaped moderately pigmented inferotemporal choroidal mass with overlying exudative retinal detachment. Ultrasound: tumor thickness 4.5 mm, basal diameter ~11.9 × 10.1 mm, dome-shaped with choroidal excavation, low internal reflectivity on A-scan, no extrascleral extension. FA: intrinsic tumor vascularity with late leakage. Systemic metastatic workup (LFTs, CXR) unremarkable. Diagnosis: medium posterior uveal (choroidal) melanoma. Management: iodine-125 plaque brachytherapy with concurrent fine-needle aspiration for gene-expression profiling. Tumor classified as Class 1A; surveillance with annual liver ultrasound. At 7 months post-therapy, tumor regressed to 2.3 mm height, resolution of subretinal fluid, pigmentary alterations of lesion, VA improved to 20/40 OD with progressive nuclear sclerosis; no radiation-related retinal vasculopathy.
Primary Diagnosis: Medium-sized choroidal melanoma (right eye)
Secondary Diagnoses: Hypertension; Hypercholesterolemia; Skin cancer; Macular degeneration; Nuclear sclerotic cataract (progressive)
Differential Diagnosis: Choroidal nevus; Metastatic carcinoma to the choroid; Choroidal hemangioma
Severity: Medium
Laterality: Right
Complications: radiation-related retinopathy, optic neuropathy, cataract, transient diplopia, radiation-vasculopathy, optic neuropathy (repeated), secondary glaucoma, vitreous hemorrhage


Plan
All Medications: hypertension medication (unspecified) hypercholesterolemia medication (unspecified) iodine-125 radioactive plaque (brachytherapy) perioperative topical antibiotics 7–0 Vicryl suture 5–0 nylon suture
All Procedures: fine-needle aspiration biopsy for gene-expression profiling; iodine-125 radioactive plaque placement (plaque brachytherapy); ultrasonography (A-scan and B-scan); fundus photography; fluorescein angiography; liver function tests; chest X-ray
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up every 3 months during the first year, then every 6 months for 2 years, then yearly thereafter; perform clinical exam, fundus photography, and ultrasonography at each visit (AF and OCT as indicated); for gene-expression class 1A: annual liver imaging (and clinical metastatic surveillance)
Patient Education: Choroidal melanoma is a tumor of the layer beneath the retina (the choroid). It can cause decreased vision, flashes, and a retinal detachment. Diagnosis is based on eye exam and tests such as ultrasound, OCT, fluorescein angiography, and sometimes biopsy for gene-expression profiling. Treatment options depend on tumor size and location and include plaque brachytherapy (radiation to save the eye), local resection, or enucleation (removal of the eye) for large tumors. Fine-needle biopsy can provide prognostic gene-expression results (class 1A, 1B, or 2) that guide metastatic surveillance. After plaque therapy the tumor usually shrinks; common side effects include cataract, radiation retinopathy, and optic neuropathy. Follow-up schedule: every 3 months in year 1, every 6 months for years 2–3, then yearly; exams include clinical exam, fundus photos, and ultrasonography, with OCT/autofluorescence as needed. Metastatic surveillance is based on risk class: class 1A—annual liver imaging; class 1B—liver imaging and liver enzymes annually, staggered by 6 months; class 2—liver imaging and liver enzymes every 6 months staggered by 3 months. Report new systemic symptoms (right upper quadrant pain, unintended weight loss, fatigue) promptly. If vision worsens, notice new flashes, increased floaters, pain, or redness, seek urgent ophthalmic care.


CASE 50 — Dislocated Posterior Chamber Intraocular Lens
=======================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Sudden decreased vision in the left eye
DURATION: 6 years
LATERALITY: OS
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 64-year-old woman with sudden decreased vision in the left eye 2 days after an extracapsular cataract extraction with PCIOL insertion; intraoperative central posterior capsular rupture occurred and the IOL was placed in the ciliary sulcus anterior to the remaining anterior capsule; postoperative exam showed 20/30 vision with aphakic correction, 2+ superior microbullous corneal edema, moderate anterior chamber cells, vitreous incarceration in the wound, clear pupillary space, no residual capsule noted, and a freely mobile PCIOL in the inferior vitreous cavity.
History Narrative: A 64-year-old woman presented with a sudden decreased vision in the left eye 2 days after an uncomplicated extracapsular cataract extraction surgery with posterior chamber intraocular lens (PCIOL) insertion. During the procedure, a central posterior capsular rupture was noted, but the IOL was placed anterior to the remaining anterior capsule into the ciliary sulcus. Her vision was 20/30 in the left eye with an aphakic correction. Slit-lamp examination showed a 2 + microbullous corneal edema superiorly and moderate cells in the anterior chamber. The pupil was round, but there was vitreous incarceration in the wound. The pupillary space was clear. Residual capsule was not noted. Funduscopic examination disclosed a freely mobile PCIOL within the vitreous cavity inferiorly. The retina was attached.
Symptom Onset: 2 days after surgery
Symptom Duration: 2 days
Progression: Resolved


Subjective (cont.)
Alleviating Factors: Observation only, topical miotics, topical anti-inflammatory or ocular hypotensive agents, aphakic contact lens correction
Associated Symptoms: sudden decreased vision, glare, monocular diplopia, pain
Prior Treatments: Extracapsular cataract extraction with posterior chamber IOL placement into the ciliary sulcus (after posterior capsular rupture)
Impact on Daily Activities: Improved to normal vision (20/20) — no lasting impairment; minimal long-term impact on daily activities.
Systemic Symptoms: None
Risk Factors: - Compromise of zonular integrity (from surgical trauma or nonsurgical trauma) - Pseudoexfoliation syndrome - Posterior capsular rupture / inadequate posterior capsular support - Asymmetric haptic placement - Vitreous incarceration in wound


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/80 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 20 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: 2+ microbullous corneal edema superiorly
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: 2+ microbullous corneal edema superiorly and moderate cells in the anterior chamber
Anterior Segment – Iris: Round pupil
Anterior Segment – Lens: Posterior chamber intraocular lens (PCIOL) — dislocated, later repositioned and scleral-fixated


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Round
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Briskly reactive (round pupil)
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Pupils – Other: Round
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: 2+ microbullous corneal edema superiorly and moderate cells in the anterior chamber; pupil round with vitreous incarceration in the wound; pupillary space clear; residual capsule not noted
Fundus Exam – Macula: macula intact
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal caliber and configuration
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Retina attached, no retinal tears or detachment on peripheral exam


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Dislocated posterior chamber intraocular lens, left eye (PCIOL dislocation into vitreous cavity) following posterior capsular rupture during extracapsular cataract extraction; associated early postoperative corneal edema, anterior chamber inflammation with vitreous incarceration in the wound; retina attached. Surgical plan: pars plana vitrectomy with retrieval and scleral fixation of IOL haptics under partial-thickness scleral flaps; postoperative course: IOL well positioned, vision 20/60 at 1 month then 20/20 by 6 months and stable at 6 years.
Primary Diagnosis: Dislocated posterior chamber IOL, OS
Differential Diagnosis: Dislocated posterior chamber IOL due to posterior capsular rupture/insufficient capsular support; Zonular dehiscence (including pseudoexfoliation-related zonulopathy); IOL haptic malposition/rotation leading to sulcus instability; Traumatic IOL dislocation
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: OS
Complications: Cystoid macular edema; elevated intraocular pressure; retinal detachment (low incidence); suture knot erosion; endophthalmitis; hemorrhage; IOL torsion or malposition; recurrent dislocation due to suture breakage; corneal endothelial trauma; postsurgical astigmatism; microbullous corneal edema; anterior chamber inflammation (cells); vitreous incarceration


Plan
All Medications: Topical miotics; topical anti-inflammatory agents; ocular hypotensive agents; aphakic correction (contact lens)
All Procedures: Extracapsular cataract extraction with posterior chamber IOL insertion; pars plana vitrectomy; retrieval and scleral fixation (transscleral suturing) of dislocated posterior chamber IOL into the ciliary sulcus with placement of two partial‑thickness scleral flaps to cover suture knots
Follow-Up Instructions: Routine postoperative ophthalmic follow-up with serial exams to monitor visual acuity, intraocular pressure, anterior chamber inflammation, and retinal status; careful postoperative peripheral retinal examination for tears/detachment; timely visits within 1–2 weeks post-op (and sooner if symptoms), then periodic follow-up until stable (eg, 1 month and as clinically indicated).
Patient Education: Educate the patient about the diagnosis of a dislocated posterior‑chamber intraocular lens (PCIOL), causes (capsular rupture, zonular weakness, trauma, pseudoexfoliation), symptoms to report (new decreased vision, glare, monocular diplopia, pain, flashes/floaters), and the treatment options: observation with medical management (topical anti‑inflammatories, aqueous suppressants, topical miotics for symptomatic decentration), or surgical options (pars plana vitrectomy with IOL repositioning and transscleral suture fixation, IOL exchange/removal, or limbal approaches when appropriate). Explain benefits and risks of surgery (improved vision in most cases; risks include cystoid macular edema, elevated IOP, retinal tears/detachment, suture erosion, infection, recurrent dislocation), typical timing (usually within 2 weeks for acute symptomatic dislocation), and expected outcomes (most achieve 20/40 or better; long‑term good positioning possible). Advise perioperative care: need for postoperative visits, ocular inflammation control, activity restrictions, and to seek urgent care for worsening vision, increasing pain, new floaters or flashes.


CASE 51 — Cystoid Macular Edema
===============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Decreased visual acuity in the right eye
DURATION: 8 weeks
LATERALITY: OD
PATIENT QUOTES: "the visual acuity was excellent postoperatively but declined substantially after the first 4 weeks."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 66‑year‑old woman presented 8 weeks after phacoemulsification complaining of decreased visual acuity in the right eye. She had an acrylic IOL placed in the ciliary sulcus due to a central posterior capsular rupture. Vision was excellent postoperatively but declined substantially after the first 4 weeks. She denied other medical or ophthalmic history including diabetes or hypertension.
History Narrative: A 66-year-old woman presented 8 weeks after phacoemulsification complaining of decreased visual acuity in the right eye. An acrylic intraocular lens was inserted into the ciliary sulcus at the time of surgery due to a central posterior capsular rupture. The patient reported that the visual acuity was excellent postoperatively but declined substantially after the first 4 weeks. The patient reported no other contributory medical or ophthalmic history including diabetes mellitus or hypertension.
Symptom Onset: 8 weeks after surgery
Symptom Duration: 8 weeks
Progression: Worsening after initial 4 weeks
Characteristics: Posterior vitreous detachment; macular thickening with cystic intraretinal spaces (inner nuclear layer) showing petaloid perifoveal leakage on fluorescein angiography; aphakia/pseudophakia with sulcus-placed IOL after posterior capsular rupture; decreased BCVA to 20/80 OD 8 weeks post-op; no anterior chamber inflammation (deep and quiet)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Posterior capsular rupture with vitreous prolapse/retained lens material and complicated cataract surgery (sulcus IOL placement)
Alleviating Factors: Spontaneous improvement over 3–4 months; topical NSAIDs and corticosteroids (combined therapy more effective); periocular or intravitreal corticosteroid injections; pars plana vitrectomy if traction/retained lens material addressed.
Associated Symptoms: Decreased visual acuity in the right eye (declining after 4 weeks)
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes of cystoid macular edema reported
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — decreased vision to 20/80 in the right eye causing noticeable difficulty with tasks requiring fine vision (reading, detailed work), but peripheral vision intact and fellow eye presumably compensating.
Systemic Symptoms: None
Risk Factors: Posterior capsular rupture with vitreous loss/retained lens material; intraocular surgery (phacoemulsification); intracapsular/sulcus IOL placement; iris trauma; vitreous prolapse/incarceration; prior intraocular inflammation; absence of diabetes/hypertension (not risk factors here)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.4°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: White and quiet
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: clear and of normal thickness
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: The iris appeared normal without peaking
Anterior Segment – Angle: wide open
Anterior Segment – Lens: Acrylic intraocular lens placed in the ciliary sulcus (pseudophakic), well centered and stable.


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: normal
Pupils – Reaction to Light: normal
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Normal
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Extraocular Movements: Full without restriction
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Cornea clear and of normal thickness with a well‑healed temporal corneal incision; anterior chamber deep and quiet; pupil normal without peaking; iris normal; intraocular lens well centered and stable.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal with a cup-to-disc ratio of 0.2
Fundus Exam – Macula: Thickened macula with cystic spaces in the fovea (cystoid macular edema)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: The peripheral retina was normal without holes, breaks, or tears.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Postoperative (pseudophakic) cystoid macular edema of the right eye (Irvine–Gass syndrome) 8 weeks after phacoemulsification with posterior capsular rupture and sulcus IOL placement. Patient reports initial excellent vision with decline beginning at 4 weeks; BCVA OD 20/80. Exam: quiet anterior segment, well-centered sulcus IOL, posterior vitreous detachment, macular thickening with cystic spaces on fundus exam and OCT (cysts in inner nuclear layer), fluorescein angiography with late petaloid perifoveal leakage. No history of diabetes or hypertension. Peripheral retina without breaks. Plan: initiate topical NSAID (e.g., ketorolac) and topical corticosteroid therapy with follow-up in 4–6 weeks; consider periocular/intravitreal steroid or surgical intervention (vitrectomy) if medical therapy fails or if vitreous traction/retained lens material is identified.
Primary Diagnosis: Postoperative (pseudophakic) cystoid macular edema, OD
Secondary Diagnoses: Posterior vitreous detachment; Posterior capsular rupture (with vitreous loss); Pseudophakia (sulcus IOL)
Differential Diagnosis: Pseudophakic (postoperative) cystoid macular edema (Irvine–Gass syndrome); Diabetic macular edema; Uveitic (inflammatory) cystoid macular edema
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: OD
Complications: Posterior vitreous detachment; cystoid macular edema (pseudophakic CME)


Plan
All Medications: 0.5% ketorolac ophthalmic drops (topical) four times daily for 4–6 weeks; 1% prednisolone ophthalmic drops four to eight times daily; topical flurbiprofen or suprofen (preoperative NSAIDs) as prophylaxis; periocular or intravitreal corticosteroid injections (e.g., triamcinolone) as second-line; intravitreal anti-VEGF agents (ranibizumab, bevacizumab, aflibercept)
All Procedures: phacoemulsification with acrylic intraocular lens implantation into the ciliary sulcus
Follow-Up Instructions: Observe within 4–6 weeks after initiating medical treatment; if no improvement, escalate therapy; continue careful follow-up even after resolution due to risk of recurrence.
Patient Education: Cystoid macular edema (CME) is swelling in the central retina that can cause decreased vision after eye surgery. It often improves on its own within 3–4 months, but treatment can speed recovery and prevent permanent damage. Typical first-line treatment is topical anti-inflammatory eye drops (a steroid such as prednisolone and/or an NSAID such as ketorolac) used frequently for several weeks; if there is no improvement in 4–6 weeks, injections (periocular or intravitreal steroids) or referral for surgical options (e.g., vitrectomy) may be recommended in select cases. Follow-up is important because CME can recur and chronic CME can cause permanent vision loss; report any worsening vision promptly. If vitreous prolapse, retained lens fragments, or pupil/iris abnormalities are present, surgical correction may be needed. Also tell your surgeon about a history of diabetes, use of prostaglandin drops, or other inflammatory eye disease, as these affect management.


CASE 52 — Endophthalmitis
=========================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: 10/10 eye pain and decreased vision in her left eye upon awakening.
DURATION: 5 days
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "I woke up with 10/10 eye pain and decreased vision in my left eye."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 59-year-old woman awoke 5 days after an uneventful combined cataract and LASIK procedure with severe (10/10) left eye pain and decreased vision. Postoperative course had been otherwise unremarkable until this abrupt onset. On presentation her left eye vision was hand motion with pronounced conjunctival hyperemia, corneal edema, anterior chamber inflammation with hypopyon, and fibrin on the intraocular lens; B‑scan showed moderately dense membranous vitreous opacities.
History Narrative: This 59-year-old woman awoke with 10/10 eye pain and decreased vision in her left eye. An uneventful combined cataract and LASIK procedure had been performed 5 days previously with a hitherto standard postoperative course. Her vision was hand motion, and there was pronounced conjunctival hyperemia, corneal edema, and anterior chamber reaction with hypopyon, as well as fibrin present on the intraocular lens (IOL). There was no view to the posterior pole and B-scan ultrasonography demonstrated moderately dense membranous vitreous opacities.
Symptom Onset: Awoke with symptoms 5 days after surgery
Symptom Duration: 5 days
Progression: Improved — vision progressed from hand motion at presentation to 20/40 at 1 week and 20/20 several months later after laser capsulotomy.
Characteristics: Vision-threatening inflammatory reaction of the intraocular fluids or tissues, presenting with pain, decreased vision, intraocular inflammation (hypopyon, conjunctival hyperemia, corneal edema, anterior chamber fibrin), often with vitreous opacities and reduced view of the posterior pole, occurring postoperatively, posttrauma, bleb-associated, post–intravitreal injection, or endogenous hematogenous spread.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Diabetes, posterior capsular rupture, wound leaks, bleb leakage, use of antimetabolites, inferior surgical location, intraocular foreign body, lens rupture, dirty wound, delayed primary repair, immunodeficiency, intravenous drug use, indwelling catheters, malignancy
Alleviating Factors: Prompt treatment with intravitreal antibiotics (vancomycin and ceftazidime), vitreous tap ± vitrectomy when indicated, topical fortified antibiotics and corticosteroids, povidone-iodine prophylaxis, and systemic antibiotics/antifungals when endogenous infection suspected
Associated Symptoms: Eye pain; decreased vision; conjunctival hyperemia; corneal edema; anterior chamber inflammation with hypopyon; fibrin on the intraocular lens; no view of posterior pole (vitreous opacities)
Prior Treatments: uneventful combined cataract and LASIK procedure 5 days prior
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant temporary impairment: initial hand-motion vision and pain limited daily activities for at least one week, with progressive recovery to normal (20/20) over months after treatment.
Risk Factors: diabetes; posterior capsular rupture; wound leaks; bleb leakage; use of antimetabolites; inferior surgical location; intraocular foreign body; lens rupture; dirty wound; delayed primary repair; immunodeficiency; intravenous drug use; indwelling catheters; malignancy


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 88 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: Hand motion
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: Hand motion
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 18 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 28 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Pronounced conjunctival hyperemia
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: corneal edema
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Marked anterior chamber reaction with hypopyon and fibrin on the intraocular lens
Anterior Segment – Lens: Posterior chamber intraocular lens (PCIOL) with fibrin on the IOL; capsular bag present (pseudophakic)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Briskly reactive bilaterally
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Conjunctival hyperemia, corneal edema, anterior chamber reaction with hypopyon, and fibrin on the intraocular lens.
Fundus Exam – Periphery: View limited — no view to posterior pole; B-scan shows moderately dense membranous vitreous opacities without retinal detachment.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Acute infectious postoperative endophthalmitis, left eye.
Primary Diagnosis: Acute postoperative endophthalmitis, left eye
Differential Diagnosis: Acute postoperative endophthalmitis; Toxic anterior segment syndrome; Retained lens material with inflammation; Exacerbation of preexisting uveitis
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Left


Plan
All Medications: vancomycin; ceftazidime; dexamethasone; topical fortified vancomycin; topical fortified ceftazidime; prednisolone; cyclopentolate; amikacin; fourth-generation fluoroquinolones; intracameral cefuroxime; povidone-iodine; topical antibiotics; subconjunctival antibiotics; preservative-containing triamcinolone; aflibercept; systemic antibiotics; systemic antifungals
All Procedures: combined cataract and LASIK procedure; vitreous tap and intravitreal injection of vancomycin, ceftazidime, and dexamethasone; topical fortified vancomycin and ceftazidime application; laser capsulotomy
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow clinically with gradual tapering of topical fortified antibiotics and prednisolone; monitor visual acuity and inflammation; perform retinal evaluation (ultrasound if view limited); arrange laser capsulotomy later if indicated.
Patient Education: You have an infection inside the eye called endophthalmitis, most likely related to your recent cataract/LASIK procedure. This is a serious condition that can cause rapid vision loss but can often be treated successfully if addressed promptly. What caused it - Bacteria entering the eye after surgery (most commonly skin bacteria such as Staphylococcus epidermidis). Symptoms to expect - Severe eye pain, redness, decreased vision, light sensitivity, possible pus or whitish material in the eye. What we did and why - We took a sample of the vitreous (vitreous tap) to identify the organism. - We injected antibiotics directly into the eye (vancomycin and ceftazidime) to rapidly treat the infection. - We may add an anti-inflammatory injection (dexamethasone) to reduce damaging inflammation. - Topical fortified antibiotics and steroid drops were started to control surface and intraocular inflammation. - Ultrasound was used to assess the back of the eye since the view was blocked. What this means for your vision - Many patients improve substantially with prompt treatment; in similar cases vision often recovers to near normal over weeks to months, but severe infections can cause permanent loss. Close follow-up is essential. Home care and follow-up - Use all eye drops exactly as prescribed (frequency and schedule). - Keep follow-up appointments; you will need close monitoring (often daily at first). - Avoid touching or rubbing the eye; use a protective shield if advised. - Do not miss any urgent calls or visits — worsening pain, new floaters, sudden vision loss, or increasing redness require immediate evaluation. When additional surgery may be needed - If vision is only light perception or infection is severe, a pars plana vitrectomy (surgical removal of infected vitreous) may be recommended. - Chronic or indolent infections (e.g., Propionibacterium acnes) may require more extensive surgery including capsulectomy or IOL removal. Prevention for future procedures - Preoperative povidone-iodine antisepsis and sterile technique reduce risk. - The role of prophylactic topical antibiotics is unclear; intracameral antibiotics are used in some settings. If you have fever, chills, or other systemic symptoms or concerns, inform your primary doctor or seek emergency care. If you notice any worsening eye pain or vision changes, contact your ophthalmologist immediately.
Referrals: Infectious disease or medical specialist (for endogenous endophthalmitis)


CASE 53 — Suprachoroidal Hemorrhage
===================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: acute-onset severe pain and loss of vision in the left eye
DURATION: 8 days
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "N/A"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 78-year-old woman presented 4 days after a complicated left cataract surgery with acute-onset severe left eye pain and vision loss; history notable for cardiac bypass surgery 2 years ago, systemic hypertension, high myopia (−9.00 D), primary open-angle glaucoma; on timolol, dorzolamide bilaterally and prednisolone acetate 1% in the left eye; exam at presentation: left vision hand motion, IOP 39 mm Hg, shallow anterior chamber, aphakia with temporal clear cornea incision closed with nylon sutures, and apparent bullous appositional retinal/choroidal detachment; B-scan confirmed appositional (“kissing”) suprachoroidal hemorrhages.
History Narrative: A 78-year-old woman presented 4 days after a complicated cataract surgery in her left eye, complaining of acute-onset severe pain and loss of vision in the left eye. Past medical history was notable for cardiac bypass surgery 2 years previously and systemic hypertension. Past ocular history was significant for myopia (–9.00 sphere) and primary open-angle glaucoma. Ocular medications included timolol 0.5% in both eyes twice daily, dorzolamide 2% in both eyes three times per day, and prednisolone acetate 1% in the left eye four times per day.
Symptom Onset: 4 days after a complicated cataract surgery
Symptom Duration: 4 days
Progression: Resolved after surgical drainage with improvement to 20/400 at 3 months
Characteristics: Appositional (“kissing”) suprachoroidal hemorrhage, dark‑brown appositional choroidal detachments posterior to the iris plane, clotted then liquefying suprachoroidal blood on echography, associated shallow anterior chamber, aphakia, elevated IOP (39 mm Hg), severe acute ocular pain and profound vision loss (hand motion)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: advanced age, atherosclerotic cardiovascular disease, systemic anticoagulation, hypertension, myopia, glaucoma, aphakia, recent intraocular surgery
Alleviating Factors: Observation including serial echography and medical management of elevated intraocular pressure (IOP) with ocular antihypertensive medications; pain control and topical/systemic steroids to stabilize vessel permeability (though unproven); delayed surgical drainage after liquefaction (waiting 7–14 days) with pars plana vitrectomy and suprachoroidal drainage when indicated.
Associated Symptoms: Severe ocular pain and acute loss of vision (hand motion in left eye)
Prior Treatments: timolol 0.5% OU twice daily; dorzolamide 2% OU three times daily; prednisolone acetate 1% OS four times daily; cataract surgery (complicated) with temporal clear corneal incision closed with interrupted nylon sutures; medical management of elevated IOP in left eye; pars plana vitrectomy with drainage of suprachoroidal hemorrhage and fluid–gas exchange (performed 8 days after presentation)
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant impairment — unable to perform usual visual-dependent daily activities in the affected eye (marked reduction to hand motion progressing to 20/400), likely requiring assistance for tasks requiring vision.
Systemic Symptoms: acute-onset severe pain (ocular) and history of cardiac bypass surgery 2 years prior and systemic hypertension
Risk Factors: advanced age; atherosclerotic cardiovascular disease; systemic anticoagulation; hypertension; myopia; glaucoma; aphakia; recent intraocular surgery


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 150/90 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: Hand motion
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: Hand motion
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen visual acuity
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 39 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal appearing lids and lashes
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: 2+ conjunctival injection
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal (no external orbital abnormality noted)
Anterior Segment – Cornea: temporal clear cornea incision reapproximated with interrupted nylon sutures
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: shallow
Anterior Segment – Iris: shallow anterior chamber with iris displaced anteriorly (iris–lens diaphragm bowed forward)
Anterior Segment – Angle: Shallow/likely narrow (anterior chamber shallow)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Aphakia


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in both eyes (no extraocular movement restriction)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: 2 + conjunctival injection, temporal clear cornea incision reapproximated with interrupted nylon sutures, shallow anterior chamber, aphakia, and the appearance of a bullous appositional retinal detachment posterior to the iris plane
Fundus Exam – Macula: Appositional retinal detachment involving the macula (macular involvement with anterior retinal displacement)
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Bullous appositional (kissing) choroidal detachments/hemorrhages posterior to the iris plane with retinal apposition (suprachoroidal hemorrhage)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Appositional (“kissing”) suprachoroidal hemorrhage of the left eye following complicated cataract surgery, with acute-onset severe pain, hand-motion vision, shallow anterior chamber, aphakia, and markedly elevated IOP (39 mm Hg). B-scan ultrasound confirmed appositional suprachoroidal hemorrhages with clotted blood initially and liquefaction by day 8. Elevated IOP was medically managed initially; after liquefaction, pars plana vitrectomy with drainage of the suprachoroidal hemorrhage and fluid–gas exchange was performed without a draining retinotomy because no retinal breaks were identified. Risk factors present include advanced age, hypertension, myopia, glaucoma, aphakia, recent intraocular surgery, and prior cardiac disease. Prognosis guarded; at 3 months postoperatively left-eye visual acuity was 20/400.
Primary Diagnosis: Appositional (“kissing”) suprachoroidal hemorrhage
Secondary Diagnoses: Myopia (high myopia), Primary open-angle glaucoma, Systemic hypertension, History of cardiac bypass (coronary artery bypass graft)
Differential Diagnosis: Rhegmatogenous retinal detachment; Exudative (serous) retinal detachment; Serous choroidal effusion; Suprachoroidal hemorrhage
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Left
Complications: Appositional (“kissing”) suprachoroidal hemorrhage; elevated intraocular pressure (left eye); shallow anterior chamber; aphakia; acute severe pain; loss of vision (hand motion → 20/400 at 3 months)


Plan
All Medications: timolol 0.5% OU BID; dorzolamide 2% OU TID; prednisolone acetate 1% OS QID; balanced salt solution (used intraoperatively)
All Procedures: complicated cataract surgery (left eye); temporal clear cornea incision reapproximated with interrupted nylon sutures; anterior chamber paracentesis (considered/preoperative normalization mentioned); medical management of elevated intraocular pressure (topical/systemic IOP-lowering agents); pars plana vitrectomy with drainage of suprachoroidal hemorrhage and fluid–gas exchange; serial echography (B-scan ultrasonography); sclerotomy/draining sclerotomy (described as surgical option); 360-degree conjunctival limbal peritomy with isolation of rectus muscles (described as part of drainage procedure); placement of pars plana infusion cannula or limbal infusion (described); limited anterior vitrectomy (described as possible); perfluorocarbon liquid injection (described as option); scleral buckle and/or intraocular gas tamponade for coexisting retinal detachment (described)
Patient Education: You have a suprachoroidal hemorrhage, which is bleeding between the choroid and the sclera that can cause sudden severe eye pain and vision loss. It most often occurs around eye surgery or trauma and is more likely with high blood pressure, blood-thinning medicines, glaucoma, high myopia, or prior heart disease. Immediate goals: - Control eye pain and high eye pressure with prescribed drops and systemic medicines. - Protect the eye: keep head elevated, avoid heavy lifting/straining, and use any patching or shield recommended. - Attend all follow-up visits and scheduled ultrasounds (B-scan) to monitor whether the blood is liquefying. Treatment overview: - If the clot is still firm, we usually observe for about 7–14 days to allow liquefaction before surgical drainage. - If the pressure remains dangerously high, pain is severe, or the retina is apposed (“kissing” choroidals), urgent surgical drainage (often with vitrectomy) may be recommended. - Surgery aims to drain liquefied blood, relieve pressure, and repair any associated retinal problems; outcomes vary and vision may not fully recover. What to watch for (call immediately): - Worsening pain, sudden worsening of vision, increasing redness, discharge, or fever. - New light flashes, more floaters, or a curtain/veil over vision. Prognosis and expectations: - Recovery is variable; some vision may return but permanent vision loss is possible, especially with extensive hemorrhage or delayed drainage. - Rehabilitation may include additional surgeries, low-vision aids, and close glaucoma and retinal follow-up. Medications/precautions to discuss with your doctor: - Review blood thinners or anticoagulants before any intervention. - Continue or adjust glaucoma and pain medications as advised. - Use prescribed steroid or antibiotic eye drops if given. Bring a list of all medications, any bleeding disorders, recent surgeries, and your primary care/cardiology contact to appointments.


CASE 54 — Ruptured Globe
========================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Poor vision in the left eye after a fall (sudden vision loss)
DURATION: 3 months
LATERALITY: OS
PATIENT QUOTES: "I realized poor vision and sought consultation."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 80-year-old woman who fell at her nursing home the morning prior, with periocular ecchymoses suggesting direct blunt trauma to the eye from furniture; initially no pain or visual loss but woke the next morning with poor vision. Past ocular history: extracapsular cataract surgery with nuclear expression 3 years earlier and pre-injury vision 20/30 limited by atrophic macular degeneration.
