On presentation vision was hand motions, IOP 10 mm Hg; cornea relatively clear, formed anterior chamber, temporal and superior conjunctival chemosis, extensive subconjunctival hemorrhage, and scleral dehiscence at prior cataract wound with the posterior chamber IOL displaced into the subconjunctival space; dense vitreous hemorrhage limited posterior view. No peaked pupil, no vitreous anterior to the iris, and no afferent pupillary defect.
History Narrative: An 80-year-old woman presented the morning after falling at her nursing home. Exact history was unclear, but periocular ecchymoses suggested direct ocular trauma against a piece of furniture. Initially she had not noticed pain or visual loss, but upon waking the next morning she realized poor vision and sought consultation. She had undergone cataract surgery with a nuclear expression extracapsular technique 3 years previously; before the injury her vision had been 20/30 limited by atrophic macular degeneration. On presentation vision was hand motions and intraocular pressure 10 mm Hg. The cornea appeared relatively clear with a formed anterior chamber; there was conjunctival chemosis temporally and superiorly and extensive subconjunctival hemorrhage. No conjunctival laceration was seen, but a scleral dehiscence was evident at the previous cataract wound with the posterior chamber intraocular lens in the subconjunctival space in the superotemporal quadrant. The posterior view was limited by a dense vitreous hemorrhage. The pupil was not peaked and there was no vitreous anterior to the iris. No afferent pupillary defect was present.
Symptom Onset: The morning after falling at her nursing home
Symptom Duration: 1 day
Progression: Improved
Characteristics: Ruptured globe involving dehiscence of previous cataract wound with subconjunctival IOL, conjunctival chemosis, dense vitreous hemorrhage, no APD, formed anterior chamber, aphakia, focal subretinal hemorrhage (superotemporal midperiphery), no retinal detachment.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Fall (blunt ocular trauma) — impact on prior cataract incision leading to wound dehiscence
Associated Symptoms: Periocular ecchymoses, pain (initially not noticed), poor vision/vision loss (hand motions), subconjunctival hemorrhage, chemosis, dense vitreous hemorrhage, epiretinal membrane (postop minimal), subretinal hemorrhage
Prior Treatments: Cataract surgery (nuclear expression extracapsular technique) 3 years prior.
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant — monocular visual impairment to 20/100 (aphakic) leading to notable functional limitations and likely need for rehabilitation/vocational assistance.
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Advanced age, prior cataract surgery (previous corneal/scleral incision), blunt ocular trauma (falls), thin sclera near rectus muscle insertions, and prior ocular surgery leading to wound dehiscence.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/80 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 82 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: Hand motions
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/100
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart (standard)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 10 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 10 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Periocular ecchymoses (bruising) with temporal and superior conjunctival chemosis.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Temporal and superior conjunctival chemosis with subconjunctival hemorrhage; scleral dehiscence at previous cataract wound with exposed/subconjunctival intraocular lens in superotemporal quadrant
External Exam – Orbit: Periocular ecchymoses
Anterior Segment – Cornea: relatively clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: formed
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open/normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Aphakia


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: The pupil was not peaked
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk and symmetric (no afferent pupillary defect)
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Pupils – Other: no afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full but limited by pain/trauma (effectively intact)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Cornea relatively clear; formed deep anterior chamber; conjunctival chemosis temporally and superiorly with dense subconjunctival hemorrhage; no conjunctival laceration visible; scleral dehiscence at prior cataract wound with subconjunctival intraocular lens haptic visible; pupil not peaked; no vitreous in the anterior chamber; dense vitreous hemorrhage limiting posterior view.
Fundus Exam – Macula: Unable to visualize posterior pole due to dense vitreous hemorrhage initially; three months post-op macula attached with minimal epiretinal membrane and mild residual intraretinal hemorrhage — overall macula attached.
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Atherosclerotic-appearing (mild to moderate arteriolar narrowing)
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Temporal and superior subretinal hemorrhage/organized subretinal hemorrhage at superotemporal midperiphery with no retinal detachment; minimal epiretinal membrane and attached posterior retina.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 80-year-old woman with left eye ruptured globe from blunt trauma (fall) with prior extracapsular cataract incision dehiscence and subconjunctival dislocation of the posterior chamber IOL. Presentation: hand motions vision, IOP 10 mm Hg, formed anterior chamber, clear cornea, temporal and superior conjunctival chemosis and extensive subconjunctival hemorrhage; no conjunctival laceration but scleral dehiscence at prior cataract wound with IOL in subconjunctival space; dense vitreous hemorrhage limiting posterior view; pupil not peaked; no vitreous anterior to iris; no afferent pupillary defect. Underwent same-day pars plana vitrectomy, removal of intraocular lens, and placement of encircling scleral buckle. Intraoperatively there was vitreous incarceration/streaming to the wound and a focal subretinal hemorrhage in the superotemporal midperiphery; no retinal detachment. Postoperative course: at 3 months retina attached posteriorly with minimal epiretinal membrane; organized subretinal hemorrhage at buckle base causing localized traction but nonprogressive; final visual acuity 20/100 with aphakic correction. Impression: Ruptured globe OS due to dehiscence of previous cataract wound with subconjunctival IOL and vitreous hemorrhage, successfully managed with primary globe repair and early vitrectomy with scleral buckle and IOL removal, resulting in attached retina and moderate visual recovery to 20/100 (aphakic).
Primary Diagnosis: Ruptured globe, OS
Differential Diagnosis: Occult globe rupture at previous cataract incision; Open globe rupture from limbal/scleral laceration; Traumatic lens/IOL dehiscence with vitreous prolapse; Traumatic vitreous hemorrhage without full-thickness rupture
Severity: Severe
Laterality: OS
Complications: subconjunctival hemorrhage; vitreous hemorrhage; scleral dehiscence with extrusion of intraocular lens; aphakia; subretinal hemorrhage; epiretinal membrane; traction at buckle/base of sclera; potential risk of endophthalmitis; reduced visual acuity (20/100)


Plan
All Medications: broad-spectrum intravenous antibiotics; tetanus immunization (if not given within 5 years); viscoelastic substance (intraoperative)
All Procedures: vitrectomy; placement of encircling scleral band (scleral buckle); removal of intraocular lens (IOL explantation); primary closure of corneoscleral laceration (wound repair)
Follow-Up Instructions: As long as anatomic and visual stabilization is observed, lengthen follow-up intervals; monitor for cicatricial response for ~6 weeks post-op; consider additional surgery (epiretinal membrane removal or secondary IOL) at ~3 months if indicated; recommend shatter‑proof safety glasses and vocational rehabilitation as needed.
Patient Education: - Place a protective eye shield over the injured eye to avoid further trauma or pressure. - Receive broad-spectrum intravenous antibiotics to reduce risk of endophthalmitis. - Ensure tetanus vaccination if not given within the last 5 years. - Avoid manipulating the eye; keep head elevated and activity limited until seen by ophthalmology. - Expect prompt surgical repair to close the wound; possible later (1–2 weeks) secondary vitrectomy, scleral buckle, or lens removal depending on injury. - Understand that vision may be reduced (this case resulted in 20/100 with aphakic correction) and further rehabilitative options (IOL implantation, contact lenses, epiretinal membrane surgery) may be considered after recovery (~3 months). - Wear shatter-proof safety glasses for the other eye to prevent future injury. - Follow-up closely in the first week and for several months; seek urgent care for increasing pain, redness, discharge, or sudden vision loss.
Work Restrictions: Avoid heavy lifting, straining, and activities with risk of ocular trauma; wear protective/shatter-proof eyewear; limit work requiring fine visual acuity or binocular depth perception until cleared by ophthalmology.
Emergency Instructions: Place a protective shield over the eye to protect it from further trauma and pressure; give parenteral broad‑spectrum antibiotics; administer tetanus booster if not given within 5 years; arrange urgent ophthalmic surgical evaluation for primary globe repair.


CASE 55 — Intraocular Foreign Body
==================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Decreased vision in left eye after metal-on-metal impact (suspected intraocular foreign body)
DURATION: 25 years
LATERALITY: OS
PATIENT QUOTES: "I felt a minor pain in my left eye and later noticed my vision getting a bit worse, like something small had gone into my eye."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 32-year-old man working aboard a docked marine biology research ship repairing a metal banister struck the metal rail with a hammer and felt minor left eye pain. Over the next 2 hours he noted a subtle but definite decrease in vision. On presentation his left eye vision was 20/30. He reported the incident of metal-to-metal impact without protective eyewear; exam showed a temporal peripheral corneal slitlike entry about 1 mm from the limbus, a defect in the temporal iris, a sectoral white opacity in the peripheral lens, and an intraocular foreign body embedded in the temporal midperipheral retina with a collar of retinal edema.
History Narrative: A 32-year-old man was working aboard a docked marine biology research ship repairing a metal banister. While striking the metal rail with a metal hammer, he experienced a minor pain in his left eye. Over the ensuing 2 hours, he noticed a subtle but definite decrease in vision. On presentation, his visual acuity was 20/30. Slit-lamp examination showed a deep anterior chamber with minimal cell and flare. There was a defect in the temporal iris approximately 1mm from the limbus. Corresponding to this site was a slitlike corneal defect that was not leaking aqueous fluid by Seidel testing. Posterior to the iris, there was a sectoral, white opacity in the peripheral lens. Examination of the posterior pole disclosed a small intraocular foreign body embedded in the retinal midperiphery with a collar of retinal edema surrounding it but no hemorrhage.
Symptom Onset: 2 hours
Symptom Duration: 2 hours
Progression: Resolved
Characteristics: Retained magnetic metallic intraocular foreign body (metallic, likely magnetic/siderotic)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Working without appropriate protective eyewear
Associated Symptoms: Minor pain in the left eye; subtle decrease in vision (visual acuity 20/30)
Prior Treatments: vitrectomy with lensectomy and intraocular lens implantation, magnetic extraction of IOFB, low encircling scleral buckle, laser photocoagulation; postoperative IV antibiotics and topical corticosteroids/antibiotics/cycloplegic agents
Impact on Daily Activities: No significant impact; patient regained and maintained 20/20 vision for 25 years
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Recent Travel: None
Risk Factors: metal-to-metal impact during industrial/workplace activities without protective eyewear; occupational/manual labor (e.g., hammering metal)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J10/15
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry, 14 mmHg


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal appearing conjunctiva and sclera without injection or laceration
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normocephalic, atraumatic; left periorbital soft tissues unremarkable.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: slitlike temporal corneal defect (≈1 mm), self-sealing, Seidel negative
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep anterior chamber with minimal cell and flare
Anterior Segment – Iris: sectoral iris defect temporally approximately 1 mm from the limbus
Anterior Segment – Angle: normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Sectoral peripheral white opacity (traumatic lens opacity consistent with localized cortical/ anterior subcapsular cataract)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk and symmetric to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Normal
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Extraocular Movements: Full range of extraocular movements intact in both eyes
Slit Lamp Exam Description: deep anterior chamber with minimal cell and flare; defect in the temporal iris ~1 mm from the limbus; corresponding slitlike temporal corneal defect not leaking on Seidel; sectoral white opacity in peripheral lens posterior to the iris
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Within normal limits
Fundus Exam – Macula: Macula: intact, no hemorrhage; retina attached with IOFB in midperiphery (macula uninvolved)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal appearing vessels
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Small intraocular foreign body embedded in the temporal midperiphery with a collar of retinal edema surrounding it; no hemorrhage; retina otherwise attached.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Retained magnetic intraocular foreign body OS with entry through temporal peripheral cornea, iris, and lens causing localized peripheral lens opacity and a small midperipheral retinal impact with surrounding edema. Globe integrity maintained with self-sealing corneal wound. No initial vitreous hemorrhage or endophthalmitis. Management: prompt pars plana vitrectomy with lensectomy, posterior chamber IOL implantation using fellow-eye biometry, intraoperative induction of PVD, magnetic extraction of metallic IOFB, laser photocoagulation around retinal impact, and placement of a low encircling scleral buckle. Postoperative course notable for attached retina and recovery to 20/20 vision, maintained over 25 years.
Primary Diagnosis: Retained magnetic intraocular foreign body, OS
Differential Diagnosis: Retained intraocular foreign body (metallic/siderotic IOFB); Traumatic cataract with lens penetration; Penetrating globe injury with vitreous hemorrhage/retinal impact; Endophthalmitis from contaminated ocular penetration
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: OS
Complications: endophthalmitis; retinal detachment; siderosis (from metallic IOFB); hemorrhage; persistent decreased vision/epiretinal membrane; cataract/lens opacity; vitreous hemorrhage; retinal breaks; intraoperative expulsive hemorrhage (if wound leaking)


Plan
All Medications: intravenous antibiotics; topical corticosteroids; topical antibiotics; topical cycloplegic agents; systemic antibiotics; intravitreal antibiotics
All Procedures: vitrectomy; lensectomy; intraocular lens implantation; magnetic extraction of intraocular foreign body; low encircling scleral buckle placement; laser photocoagulation surrounding retinal defect; induction of posterior vitreous detachment with vitrectomy instrument; scleral wound closure (as needed)
Follow-Up Instructions: Observed at approximately 1–2 week intervals in the first month, then less frequently thereafter; monitor for recurrent retinal detachment and visually significant epiretinal membrane; if no endophthalmitis within 24–48 hours and good progress, discontinue systemic antibiotics.
Patient Education: Seek immediate care for eye injuries, especially after metal-on-metal work—do not delay if you notice pain, decreased vision, or a feeling of something in the eye. Always wear proper protective eyewear when working with metal or power tools. Do not undergo MRI until a metallic foreign body is ruled out. If an intraocular foreign body is suspected, prompt evaluation and likely imaging (X-ray, CT, or ultrasound) are needed, and early surgical removal and prophylactic antibiotics may be required to prevent infection and vision loss. Postoperatively, follow prescribed topical/systemic antibiotics and antiinflammatories, attend frequent follow-up visits to monitor for retinal detachment or infection, and discuss timing of intraocular lens placement if aphakic.
Work Restrictions: Avoidance of heavy lifting, straining, and contact work; no return to hazardous/industrial tasks without protective eyewear until cleared by ophthalmology.
Emergency Instructions: Urgent evaluation and prompt surgical removal of suspected intraocular foreign body (ideally within 6–12 hours); avoid MRI until nonmagnetic status confirmed; obtain plain films/CT and/or B-scan as indicated; start broad‑spectrum systemic (and consider intravitreal) antibiotics for endophthalmitis prophylaxis; protect globe and arrange immediate ophthalmic surgical consultation for vitrectomy/lensectomy/IOFB removal.


CASE 56 — Optic Neuritis
========================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Blurred vision in the right eye with retro-orbital pain made worse by eye movement, onset 12 days ago
DURATION: 12 days
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "I noticed sudden blurred vision in my right eye about 12 days ago. I have moderately severe retro-orbital pain that gets worse with eye movements. My vision worsened over the first 3–4 days and then stabilized. When I do aerobic exercise my vision becomes worse."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 35‑year‑old woman with acute onset 12 days ago of blurred vision in the right eye associated with moderately severe right retro‑orbital pain worsened by eye movement. Vision worsened over first 3–4 days then stabilized; exercise (aerobic activity) worsened vision (Uhthoff’s phenomenon). No prior neurologic symptoms except rare diffuse headaches; otherwise healthy with no family history of visual impairment.
History Narrative: A 35-year-old woman noted the acute onset of blurred vision in her right eye 12 days ago. She complained of moderately severe retro-orbital pain on the right that was made worse by eye movements. The vision deteriorated over the first 3–4 days then stabilized. She reported worsening of vision with aerobic exercise (Uhthoff’s symptom). She denied precipitating factors, prior neurologic symptoms except rare diffuse headaches, other systemic illness, and family history of visual impairment. She was otherwise healthy.
Symptom Onset: 12 days ago
Symptom Duration: 12 days
Progression: acute
Characteristics: Acute, usually unilateral loss of visual acuity and/or visual field; relative afferent pupillary defect; periocular pain (especially with eye movement); normal (65%) or mildly swollen (35%) optic nerve head; young adult (<40 years); eventual visual improvement.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Aerobic exercise and heat exposure (e.g., hot shower, sauna)
Associated Symptoms: Retro-orbital pain worse with eye movement; vision worsens with exercise/heat (Uhthoff’s phenomenon); decreased color vision (dyschromatopsia); relative afferent pupillary defect (RAPD); superior arcuate visual field defect.
Previous Episodes: None
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — difficulty with aerobic exercise (vision worsens with exertion) and reduced vision in right eye (20/80) affecting activities requiring binocular vision and color discrimination.
Systemic Symptoms: Denies systemic symptoms (otherwise healthy; no neurologic symptoms except rare headaches)
Recent Travel: No recent travel reported
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: None known
Risk Factors: Young adult age (<40 years), female sex, history of demyelinating disease/multiple sclerosis (or MRI white-matter lesions), prior optic neuritis, positive CSF IgG/oligoclonal bands, Uhthoff’s symptom (heat/exercise-induced worsening)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 122/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.4 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N18/36
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen acuity with correction (manifest refraction)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear.
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: clear and deep
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open and quiet
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lens.


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 5mm bilaterally
Pupils – Reaction to Light: reacted well to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: reacted well to near
Pupils – RAPD: Right significant relative afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Other: significant relative afferent pupillary defect (RAPD) on the right
Extraocular Movements: Full and painless in all directions of gaze
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Normal
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Right optic disc moderately swollen and hyperemic without hemorrhages or exudates
Fundus Exam – Macula: No macular edema; macula normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: A 35-year-old woman with acute unilateral (right) visual loss over 12 days, periocular pain worsened with eye movement, decreased color vision (3/10 plates), a right RAPD, superior arcuate visual field defect, VA 20/80 OD and 20/20 OS, and a mildly hyperemic/swollen right optic disc without hemorrhages or significant vitreous inflammation. Clinical picture is most consistent with typical demyelinating optic neuritis (papillitis/anterior ON). MRI of the brain/orbits to evaluate for demyelinating lesions and to assess risk for multiple sclerosis is indicated; consider testing for NMO (AQP4-IgG) and MOG antibodies if MRI is atypical or if clinical course is atypical. Recommend treatment per ONTT with IV methylprednisolone followed by oral prednisone for more rapid recovery and consideration of MS risk reduction if MRI shows demyelinating lesions. Follow visual function closely for improvement over weeks to months and pursue further infectious or inflammatory workup only if atypical features or lack of expected recovery.
Primary Diagnosis: Typical optic neuritis (papillitis)
Differential Diagnosis: Demyelinating optic neuritis (idiopathic/MS-related); Neuromyelitis optica spectrum disorder (AQP4‑IgG associated); MOG-antibody–associated optic neuritis; Infectious/inflammatory optic neuropathy (e.g., syphilis, Lyme, sarcoidosis)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right
Complications: Risk of developing multiple sclerosis; potential for residual visual loss (persistent decreased acuity, dyschromatopsia, visual field defects); possible progression to neuromyelitis optica with worse visual prognosis.


Plan
All Medications: methylprednisolone sodium succinate (IV), oral prednisone
All Procedures: Magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) of the brain and orbits; Intravenous methylprednisolone therapy (high-dose IV steroids) followed by oral prednisone taper
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up for visual acuity and visual fields in 2–4 weeks; neuro-ophthalmology/neurology referral and brain and orbital MRI with contrast (if not already done) to assess for demyelinating lesions; consider AQP4 (NMO) and MOG antibody testing if atypical features or poor recovery; consider IV methylprednisolone treatment if rapid recovery desired or MRI abnormal (discuss risks/benefits); monitor for improvement over months and reassess if no improvement by 3–4 weeks.
Patient Education: You have optic neuritis — inflammation of the optic nerve that commonly causes sudden, usually one-sided vision loss, eye pain with movement, and color vision changes. Most cases in young adults improve over weeks to months; with typical optic neuritis many people begin to see better within 2–4 weeks and continue to improve for up to a year. Your doctor ordered an MRI to look for signs of demyelinating disease (which increases the risk of multiple sclerosis) and to rule out other causes. Treatment options include high‑dose IV corticosteroids to speed recovery (often followed by an oral taper); standard‑dose oral prednisone alone is not recommended. If tests suggest neuromyelitis optica (NMO) or MOG-related disease, additional immunologic testing and different long‑term treatments may be needed. Rest, avoid overheating or strenuous exercise if it worsens your vision (Uhthoff’s phenomenon), and report any new neurologic symptoms or worsening vision promptly. Follow-up visits are important to monitor recovery and discuss longer‑term neurologic evaluation and treatment options if MRI or blood tests are abnormal.

Patient Education: You have neuroretinitis (optic disc swelling with a macular star), most likely from cat‑scratch disease caused by Bartonella. This condition often improves over weeks to months, but vision can take time to recover and some permanent changes can occur. Recommended points: - Avoid close contact with cats’ saliva and scratches; wash any cat scratches thoroughly and seek care for infected wounds. - Take prescribed antibiotics as directed (treatment is recommended when a specific infectious cause like Bartonella is identified). - Report new or worsening vision loss, increasing eye pain, new headaches, fever, or signs of systemic infection right away. - Expect follow‑up eye exams (including vision, pupils, and retinal exam) over weeks to months to monitor resolution and watch for a macular star that can appear later. - If steroid therapy is discussed, understand benefits are uncertain and it would be used only in specific situations under specialist guidance. - Contact information for urgent eye care and schedule regular ophthalmology follow‑up as instructed.
Work Restrictions: Avoid heavy visual tasks, driving, and operating machinery until visual acuity and fields have stabilized; limit activities requiring good depth perception or binocular vision; follow-up in 2 weeks or sooner for reassessment.


CASE 58 — Anterior Ischemic Optic Neuropathy—Nonarteritic
=========================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Severe visual loss in the left eye upon awakening 3 days ago
DURATION: 3 days
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "on awakening from sleep 3 days ago she noted severe visual loss in the left eye."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 65-year-old woman with sudden, painless severe vision loss in the left eye noticed on awakening 3 days ago; denies headache, eye pain, jaw claudication, transient visual loss, or other neurologic/ophthalmologic symptoms; history of hypertension, diabetes mellitus, and hyperlipidemia.
History Narrative: A 65-year-old woman reports sudden severe painless visual loss in the left eye noted upon awakening 3 days ago. She denies headache, eye pain, jaw claudication, transient visual loss, or other neurologic/ophthalmologic symptoms. Past medical history is significant for hypertension, diabetes mellitus, and hyperlipidemia.
Symptom Onset: 3 days ago upon awakening
Symptom Duration: 3 days
Progression: Progression beyond 2 to 4 weeks
Characteristics: Sudden painless unilateral vision loss upon awakening with sectoral/ diffuse optic disc edema, peripapillary hemorrhages, relative afferent pupillary defect, color dyschromatopsia, and altitudinal visual field defect in an older patient with vascular risk factors and a small fellow-eye cup-to-disc ratio.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Sleeping/being supine/nocturnal hypotension (awakening from sleep)
Associated Symptoms: None (denies headache, eye pain, jaw claudication, transient visual loss, or other neurologic/ophthalmologic complaints)
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes of visual loss (no recurrent episodes reported)
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant impairment — left-eye visual acuity reduced to 20/200 with dense inferior altitudinal field defect and color loss, causing notable difficulty with depth perception, reading, and tasks requiring peripheral/binocular vision (likely interferes with driving and other daily activities).
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: hypertension, diabetes mellitus, hyperlipidemia, small fellow-eye cup-to-disc ratio (disc at risk), age >50 (elderly), nocturnal hypotension (possible)


Objective Exam

Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea with no abnormalities noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 3 mm bilaterally
Pupils – Reaction to Light: right: reacted well to light; left: reacted poorly to light

Extraocular Movements: Full motility without restriction

Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Peripheral retina: within normal limits (no peripheral lesions detected)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: A 65-year-old woman with sudden, painless left monocular vision loss on awakening 3 days prior. Exam: OD 20/20, OS 20/200; color vision 10/10 OD, 2/10 OS; dense inferior altitudinal visual field defect OS; 3 mm pupils bilaterally with a left relative afferent pupillary defect; OD cup-to-disc ratio 0.1 (small “disc at risk”); OS diffuse optic disc swelling with peripapillary hemorrhages. Vascular risk factors: hypertension, diabetes mellitus, hyperlipidemia. Clinical presentation and findings are most consistent with nonarteritic anterior ischemic optic neuropathy (NAION), OS. Giant cell arteritis is less likely given absence of systemic symptoms, but ESR and CRP should be obtained to exclude arteritic AION in the workup. Control of vascular risk factors and consideration of aspirin discussed; no proven effective therapy for NAION.
Primary Diagnosis: Nonarteritic anterior ischemic optic neuropathy (NAION), left eye
Secondary Diagnoses: Hypertension; Diabetes mellitus; Hyperlipidemia
Differential Diagnosis: Arteritic (giant cell) anterior ischemic optic neuropathy; optic neuritis; compressive optic neuropathy; infiltrative/inflammatory optic neuropathy
Severity: Severe
Laterality: OS
Complications: Risk of involvement of the fellow eye (≈12% lifetime risk); permanent visual loss in the affected eye (optic atrophy); decreased color vision; visual field defects (e.g., altitudinal defect); potential cardiovascular events (stroke, myocardial infarction) related to shared vascular risk factors


Plan
All Medications: Aspirin, corticosteroids (considered), phenytoin, erythropoietin, intravitreal anti-VEGF agents, topical brimonidine, levodopa
Follow-Up Instructions: Control vascular risk factors (hypertension, diabetes, hyperlipidemia), consider aspirin therapy, monitor vision and the fellow eye for AION, obtain ESR and CRP to evaluate for giant cell arteritis (and consider temporal artery biopsy if elevated or clinically suspicious), avoid iatrogenic hypotension, and arrange ophthalmology follow-up for visual and fundus reassessment.
Patient Education: You have nonarteritic anterior ischemic optic neuropathy (NAION), which causes sudden, usually painless vision loss in one eye due to decreased blood flow to the optic nerve. Common contributing factors include high blood pressure, diabetes, high cholesterol, and a small optic cup in the other eye. Important points: - There is no proven effective treatment to reverse NAION, though some patients have partial spontaneous improvement over months. - Control vascular risk factors: tightly manage blood pressure, blood sugar, and cholesterol; stop smoking; avoid episodes of low blood pressure (especially at night) and significant blood loss. - Your risk of NAION in the other eye is increased (~10–15% lifetime); aspirin may be discussed to possibly reduce risk of further vascular events. - Giant cell arteritis (a treatable cause of arteritic AION) must be excluded if you develop new symptoms such as headache, scalp tenderness, jaw claudication, fever, or unexplained muscle aches; blood tests (ESR, CRP) or urgent evaluation may be needed. - Regular follow-up with your ophthalmologist and primary care physician or cardiologist is recommended to monitor vision and optimize systemic risk factor control. Consider low-vision rehabilitation if visual function affects daily activities.


CASE 59 — Anterior Ischemic Optic Neuropathy—Arteritic
======================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: severe visual loss in the left eye
DURATION: Hours
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "Over a period of hours, the vision became markedly blurry." "I had two or three episodes of painless, transient visual loss lasting minutes in the left eye." "I denied any periocular or orbital pain." "Over the last several months I had diffuse, dull headaches with occasional superimposed 'ice pick–like' pains affecting my left or right temples." "I have jaw claudication and polymyalgia rheumatica (PMR)-like symptoms."

Over several months she had diffuse dull headaches with intermittent sharp temple pains, jaw claudication, and polymyalgia rheumatica–like symptoms. Denies periocular/orbital pain. Past history notable for hypertension.
History Narrative: A 68-year-old woman with a history of hypertension and polymyalgia rheumatica–like symptoms presented with sudden severe visual loss in the left eye that progressed over hours to marked blurring. Over the prior 2 weeks she experienced two to three episodes of painless, transient visual loss lasting minutes in the left eye. Over several months she had diffuse dull headaches with occasional sharp “ice pick–like” pains in the temples and reported jaw claudication. She denied periocular or orbital pain.
Symptom Onset: Over a period of hours (acute); had two or three transient episodes over the last 2 weeks and headaches for several months.
Symptom Duration: Several months (headaches), 2 weeks (transient visual loss), hours (acute severe visual loss)
Progression: Sudden vision loss over a period of hours with preceding transient visual loss episodes over 2 weeks and progressive symptoms over several months (headaches, PMR-like symptoms) leading to acute severe left eye vision loss.
Characteristics: Elderly woman with sudden, severe unilateral visual loss over hours; preceding transient monocular visual obscurations; systemic symptoms (headache, jaw claudication, polymyalgia rheumatica); pale (chalky) swollen optic disc with peripapillary hemorrhages; relative afferent pupillary defect; fellow eye cup-to-disc ratio 0.5 (>0.2); elevated ESR and CRP; temporal artery biopsy positive for giant cell arteritis — consistent with arteritic anterior ischemic optic neuropathy.


Subjective (cont.)

Previous Episodes: Two or three episodes of painless, transient visual loss lasting minutes in the left eye over the last 2 weeks
Prior Treatments: High‑dose corticosteroids (oral prednisone 1.0–1.5 mg/kg/day; intravenous methylprednisolone 1,000 mg/day considered for severe vision loss)
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant impairment — severe unilateral vision loss (left 20/400 with near–complete color vision loss and visual field defect) causing functional visual disability and high risk of further vision loss affecting daily activities.
Systemic Symptoms: Headache, jaw claudication, polymyalgia rheumatica–like symptoms, weight loss, anorexia, fever, malaise
Risk Factors: Age >50; female sex; history of polymyalgia rheumatica; hypertension; systemic symptoms (headache, jaw claudication); elevated ESR/CRP (systemic inflammation)

External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal

Pupils – Other: Left relative afferent pupillary defect
