Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 68-year-old woman with acute, severe, left visual loss over hours with preceding transient monocular visual obscurations, headaches, jaw claudication, and polymyalgia rheumatica–like symptoms. Exam: VA 20/20 OD, 20/400 OS, marked color loss OS, left RAPD, left optic disc swollen, pale with peripapillary hemorrhages; fellow eye cup-to-disc 0.5 (no “disc at risk”). ESR 70 mm/h and CRP 20 mg/dL. Temporal artery biopsy with transmural lymphocytic infiltrate, multinucleated giant cells, and internal elastic lamina disruption. Impression: Arteritic anterior ischemic optic neuropathy due to giant cell arteritis. Immediate high‑dose corticosteroid therapy indicated to prevent further vision loss; temporal artery biopsy confirms diagnosis and systemic rheumatologic management and steroid‑sparing considerations recommended.
Primary Diagnosis: Arteritic anterior ischemic optic neuropathy (arteritic AION)
Secondary Diagnoses: Hypertension; Polymyalgia rheumatica; Giant cell arteritis
Differential Diagnosis: Arteritic anterior ischemic optic neuropathy (giant cell arteritis); Nonarteritic anterior ischemic optic neuropathy; Central retinal artery occlusion; Posterior ischemic optic neuropathy
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Left
Complications: Further visual loss in the involved or fellow eye; permanent severe vision loss (including bilateral vision loss); ocular ischemic events (CRAO, PION, choroidal ischemia); systemic complications of high‑dose corticosteroid therapy (e.g., steroid side effects)


Plan
All Medications: prednisone (oral, initial dose 1.0–1.5 mg/kg/day); methylprednisolone (IV, 1000 mg/day); methotrexate (as a steroid-sparing option)
All Procedures: temporal artery biopsy
Follow-Up Instructions: Careful monitoring for visual changes and constitutional symptoms; serial ESR/CRP testing; regular ophthalmology follow-up to monitor involved and fellow eye; monitor for steroid adverse effects with internist/rheumatologist collaboration; consider temporal artery biopsy confirmation and evaluate need for steroid-sparing therapy (e.g., methotrexate) if steroid side effects occur.
Patient Education: Giant cell arteritis (GCA) is an inflammation of medium–large blood vessels that can cause sudden, severe vision loss. Your symptoms (sudden visual loss in one eye, prior brief episodes of vision loss, headaches, jaw pain, and pale swollen optic disc) and blood tests (high ESR and CRP) indicate arteritic anterior ischemic optic neuropathy from GCA. Immediate treatment with high‑dose corticosteroids is essential to reduce the risk of further or bilateral vision loss; do not delay treatment for biopsy. A temporal artery biopsy is recommended to confirm the diagnosis. You will need close follow‑up with ophthalmology and a physician (rheumatologist or internist) to monitor vision, inflammatory markers (ESR/CRP), and steroid side effects. Most patients taper steroids over many months, but some require longer therapy or steroid‑sparing medications if side effects occur. Seek urgent care for any new visual symptoms, worsening vision, new severe headache, scalp tenderness, jaw claudication, fever, or signs of steroid complications (e.g., high blood sugar, severe weakness).
Referrals: Internist and/or rheumatologist
Emergency Instructions: Start high‑dose corticosteroids immediately to prevent further visual loss; do not delay treatment for temporal artery biopsy. Seek urgent ophthalmology/rheumatology and consider hospitalization for IV methylprednisolone if severe, bilateral, monocular status, or vision loss <48 hours. Monitor vision, systemic symptoms, and ESR/CRP; arrange temporal artery biopsy and coordination with internist for steroid side‑effect management.


CASE 60 — Progressive Optic Neuropathy—Tumor
============================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: painless progressive visual loss in her right eye
DURATION: 3 years
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "vision was no better after the surgery"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 64-year-old woman with a several-year history of painless, progressive visual loss in the right eye beginning ~3 years ago with initial mild blurring and acuity 20/40 OD, progressive decline to 20/60 then to 20/100 despite right cataract surgery one year after onset; associated decreased color vision OD, diffuse visual field depression OD, a right relative afferent pupillary defect, and progressive optic nerve pallor, with no headache or eye pain.
History Narrative: A 64-year-old woman with a multi-year history of painless progressive visual loss in the right eye. Three years earlier she noticed mild blurring and was found to have right VA 20/40 and left 20/20, attributed to nuclear sclerotic cataract and observed. Vision worsened over the next year to 20/60 in the right eye, and she underwent right cataract surgery, with no improvement. She continued to worsen without headache or eye pain. At presentation her vision was 20/100 OD and 20/20 OS; color vision 2/10 OD and 9/10 OS. Visual field testing showed diffuse depression OD and normal OS. Pupils 5 mm bilaterally, poorly reactive on the right and brisk on the left with a right relative afferent pupillary defect. Ocular motility was normal. Hertel exophthalmometry (base 95) measured 22 mm OD and 19 mm OS. Slit-lamp exam showed a posterior chamber IOL OD and mild nuclear sclerotic cataract OS. Fundus exam demonstrated a pale, atrophic right optic nerve and a normal left optic disc.
Symptom Onset: 3 years ago
Symptom Duration: 3 years
Progression: Progressive over years (gradual worsening over ~3 years)
Characteristics: Painless progressive unilateral visual loss with relative afferent pupillary defect, decreased color vision, diffuse visual field depression, optic nerve pallor/atrophy, mild proptosis, optic nerve sheath enhancement on MRI consistent with optic nerve sheath meningioma


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: painless progressive visual loss, decreased color vision, mild proptosis, no headache or eye pain
Previous Episodes: Recurrent progressive visual blurring in right eye over 3 years with gradual worsening despite cataract surgery (symptoms present for ~3 years)
Prior Treatments: Right cataract surgery (right posterior chamber intraocular lens)
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — significant impairment in right-eye–dependent tasks (reduced acuity, color vision, and visual field) but overall bilateral vision preserved (left eye 20/20), allowing most activities with adaptations.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/100
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/100
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: 16 mmHg – Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Mild right proptosis (Hertel 22mm OD, 19mm OS at 95mm base)
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Posterior chamber intraocular lens on the right; mild nuclear sclerotic cataract on the left


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 5mm bilaterally poorly reactive to light on the right and briskly reactive on the left
Pupils – Reaction to Light: right: poorly reactive; left: briskly reactive
Pupils – RAPD: Right relative afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Other: 5mm bilaterally, poorly reactive on the right and briskly reactive on the left
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions (motility normal)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: a posterior chamber intraocular lens on the right and a mild nuclear sclerotic cataract on the left
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Pale, atrophic nerve on the right
Fundus Exam – Macula: Macula flat and unremarkable bilaterally


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Compressive optic neuropathy of the right eye secondary to an optic nerve sheath meningioma. Painless progressive visual loss over years with decreased visual acuity (20/100 OD), decreased color vision (2/10 Ishihara OD), diffuse visual field depression OD, right relative afferent pupillary defect, mild right proptosis, and optic disc pallor consistent with chronic optic nerve compression. MRI brain and orbits with gadolinium demonstrated optic nerve sheath enhancement on the right consistent with an optic nerve sheath meningioma. Plan: close observation with serial clinical exams, visual fields and repeat MRI (e.g., every 6 months x2 years then yearly if stable); consider stereotactic conformal radiation therapy if progressive visual deterioration.
Primary Diagnosis: Compressive optic neuropathy (right) due to optic nerve sheath meningioma
Differential Diagnosis: Compressive optic neuropathy from optic nerve sheath meningioma; optic nerve glioma; orbital/intracanalicular metastasis (or lymphoma); thyroid-related compressive optic neuropathy
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Right
Complications: Optic atrophy with permanent visual loss (progressive vision loss), relative afferent pupillary defect, decreased color vision, visual field loss, possible proptosis, potential need for radiation therapy or vision loss from tumor progression.


Plan
All Procedures: observation; cataract surgery (right posterior chamber intraocular lens implantation); magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) of brain and orbit with gadolinium; stereotactic/conformal radiation therapy (if progressive); surgical debulking or gross total/subtotal resection (for intracranial meningioma with optic canal extension)
Follow-Up Instructions: Serial clinical reevaluation (e.g., every 6 months) including visual fields and repeat MRI of the brain and orbit (e.g., every 6 months for 2 years, then yearly if no growth or clinical progression).
Patient Education: Compressive optic neuropathy from an optic nerve sheath meningioma causes gradual, painless vision loss and may produce an afferent pupillary defect and optic nerve pallor. MRI of the brain and orbits with gadolinium is the key test to confirm the diagnosis. Management options include careful observation with regular visual acuity, color vision, and visual field testing and periodic MRIs (about every 6 months for 2 years, then yearly if stable), or radiotherapy if vision worsens. Surgery is rarely recommended for primary optic nerve sheath meningioma except for select cases with exophytic growth or intracranial extension. Report any new or worsening vision changes, pain, headaches, or double vision promptly. Discuss low-vision aids and rehabilitation if vision loss is significant.


CASE 61 — Papilledema–Pseudotumor Cerebri
=========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Headache (8 months), with pulsatile tinnitus and transient visual obscurations
DURATION: 8 months
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "pulsating sound"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 22-year-old obese woman with an 8-month history of almost daily diffuse headaches (rarely with nausea), worsening over last 2 months with new pulsatile, posture‑synchronous tinnitus (most noticeable when rising from lying to standing), and 2 weeks of transient visual obscurations lasting seconds in either eye; denies diplopia or other neurologic symptoms; not pregnant and not taking medications known to raise intracranial pressure.
History Narrative: A 22-year-old obese woman with an 8-month history of almost daily diffuse headaches, rare nausea, 2 months of pulse-synchronous tinnitus worse with postural change, and 2 weeks of transient visual obscurations lasting seconds in either eye; denied diplopia or other neurologic symptoms, not pregnant, not taking medications known to increase intracranial pressure. Weight ~250 lb; blood pressure 135/85 mmHg. Visual acuity 20/20 OU, normal color vision, visual fields normal except bilateral enlarged blind spots, pupils 5 mm reactive OU with no RAPD, normal motility. Funduscopic exam showed marked bilateral optic disc swelling with exudates, hemorrhages, and dilated vessels. Neuroimaging (MRI) showed enlarged optic nerve sheaths and empty sella; MRV normal. Lumbar puncture opening pressure 350 mm H2O with normal CSF contents. Diagnosis: papilledema due to idiopathic intracranial hypertension (pseudotumor cerebri).
Symptom Onset: 8 months ago
Symptom Duration: 8 months
Progression: Progressive over 8 months with worsening over last 2 months (new tinnitus) and last 2 weeks (transient visual obscurations)
Characteristics: Obese young woman with headaches, pulsatile tinnitus, transient visual obscurations, bilateral severe optic disc swelling (papilledema) with normal visual acuity and color vision, enlarged blind spots, MRI showing optic nerve sheath CSF and empty sella, normal MRV, elevated opening pressure (350 mm H2O) with normal CSF contents consistent with idiopathic intracranial hypertension.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Changing posture (especially going from lying to standing)
Associated Symptoms: Headache, pulsatile tinnitus, transient visual obscurations
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — headaches and pulsatile tinnitus frequent; transient visual obscurations occur but vision largely preserved (20/20), may interfere intermittently with activities; obesity may limit activity.
Systemic Symptoms: Headaches, pulsatile tinnitus, transient visual obscurations, obesity (weight gain)
Risk Factors: Obesity (young obese woman), female sex (childbearing age), recent weight gain (implied), possible use of causative medications excluded (none), reproductive age (pregnancy risk noted)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 135/85 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal (no proptosis or external orbital abnormalities)
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear, normal appearing corneas bilaterally
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Phakic, clear lens


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 5 mm bilaterally
Pupils – Reaction to Light: reacted well to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Briskly reactive to accommodation (reacted well to near)
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: 5 mm bilaterally
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion OU (motility normal)
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Marked bilateral optic disc swelling with diffuse exudates, hemorrhages, and dilated vessels
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal (no macular abnormality)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: dilated
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No peripheral abnormalities detected (peripheral retina within normal limits)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Papilledema due to idiopathic intracranial hypertension (pseudotumor cerebri): 22‑year‑old obese woman with an 8‑month history of daily diffuse headaches, 2 months of pulsatile (pulse‑synchronous) tinnitus, and 2 weeks of transient visual obscurations. Examination: obese (250 lb), BP 135/85, VA 20/20 OU, normal color vision, enlarged blind spots OU, no RAPD, normal motility, bilateral severe optic disc swelling with exudates and hemorrhages. MRI showed signs of increased intracranial pressure (bilateral optic nerve sheath CSF signal, empty sella); MRV normal. Lumbar puncture: opening pressure 350 mm H2O with normal CSF contents. No secondary cause identified. Recommend weight reduction and medical therapy (acetazolamide) with close serial monitoring of visual function; consider surgical options (CSF shunting or optic nerve sheath fenestration) if visual function worsens or medical therapy fails.
Primary Diagnosis: Pseudotumor cerebri (idiopathic intracranial hypertension)
Secondary Diagnoses: Obesity (morbid obesity)
Differential Diagnosis: Papilledema from idiopathic intracranial hypertension (pseudotumor cerebri); Cerebral venous sinus thrombosis; Intracranial mass lesion/hydrocephalus causing raised intracranial pressure; Malignant hypertension (hypertensive retinopathy)
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: Vision loss


Plan
All Medications: acetazolamide; methazolamide; furosemide; corticosteroids (systemic); ventricular/lumboperitoneal shunt (surgical, not a medication)
All Procedures: MRI head with and without contrast; MR venography (MRV); Lumbar puncture with opening pressure measurement and CSF analysis; Acetazolamide therapy (medical management); Serial formal visual fields, stereo-optic disc photography, OCT of the optic nerves, visual acuity and color vision testing (ongoing monitoring); CSF shunting (ventriculoperitoneal or lumboperitoneal) (surgical option); Optic nerve sheath fenestration (surgical option)
Follow-Up Instructions: Return monthly for 6–12 months with careful evaluation of formal visual fields, stereo optic-disc photos, OCT of the optic nerves, visual acuity, color vision, and RAPD testing; spacing of visits may be lengthened once findings are stable, but continue long-term follow-up for possible recurrence or shunt failure.
Patient Education: Weight loss is important and can improve intracranial pressure; avoid medications known to raise intracranial pressure when possible. IIH requires regular monitoring of vision (visual fields, acuity, color, OCT) and prompt reporting of any worsening vision, new visual obscurations, persistent headache changes, or new double vision. Medical treatment often includes acetazolamide (1–2 g/day) and sometimes furosemide; discuss pregnancy risks with your obstetrician before using carbonic anhydrase inhibitors. Repeated lumbar punctures are not a routine primary therapy. If vision worsens despite medical therapy, surgical options (optic nerve sheath fenestration or CSF shunting) may be considered. Maintain follow-up monthly for 6–12 months, then less often if stable. Seek urgent care for sudden vision loss.


CASE 62 — Visual Field Defect—Junctional Scotoma
================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Progressive painless visual impairment in the right eye for 1½ years
DURATION: 1.5 years
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "I have progressive painless visual impairment in my right eye over the past 1 and 1/2 years."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: Progressive painless visual impairment in the right eye for 1½ years; denied visual difficulty in the left eye or other neurologic/ophthalmologic symptoms.
History Narrative: A 55-year-old man complained of progressive painless visual impairment in his right eye over a period of 1½ years. He denied any visual difficulty in his left eye and denied any other history of neurologic or ophthalmologic impairment. Examination revealed visual acuity of 20/80 on the right and 20/20 on the left. He could identify 4 of 10 pseudoisochromatic plates on the right and 10 of 10 on the left. Pupils were 4 mm bilaterally and both reacted well to light and near, but there was a right relative afferent pupillary defect. Visual fields revealed generalized diffuse depression on the right and a superotemporal field defect respecting the vertical meridian on the left. Motility exam was normal. Slit‑lamp examination was normal. The right optic disc was diffusely pale. The left optic disc, vessels, and macula were normal.
Symptom Onset: 1.5 years
Symptom Duration: 1.5 years
Progression: Progressive over 1.5 years
Characteristics: Progressive painless right optic neuropathy with right central scotoma, right optic atrophy, right RAPD, generalized diffuse depression on right visual field and contralateral superior temporal (junctional) visual field defect due to compressive lesion (pituitary adenoma)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Progressive visual loss was painless and worsened over 1.5 years; compressive lesion (pituitary adenoma) causing optic nerve/chiasm compression—so tumor growth/compression is the aggravating factor.
Associated Symptoms: progressive painless visual impairment in the right eye; denied other neurologic or ophthalmologic symptoms
Previous Episodes: None
Prior Treatments: Surgical decompression (decompressive surgery)
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — impaired right-eye vision with central scotoma and contralateral superior-temporal field loss causing reduced visual function (reading, depth perception, driving) but retained useful vision in left eye.
Systemic Symptoms: Denies any other neurologic or ophthalmologic impairment
Recent Travel: No recent travel reported
Risk Factors: Age 55; pituitary adenoma (suprasellar compressive mass)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen acuity with correction (OD 20/80, OS 20/20)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Normal
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Nuclear sclerotic changes consistent with age-related cataract (mild)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 4 mm bilaterally
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Both reacted well to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Both reacted well to near
Pupils – RAPD: Right relative afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Other: both reacted well to light and near
Extraocular Movements: Normal
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Normal
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Diffuse pale optic disc on the right; left optic disc normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Right optic neuropathy with junctional scotoma caused by compressive lesion at the right optic nerve–chiasm junction (pituitary adenoma) leading to progressive painless right visual loss, right RAPD, right optic atrophy with central scotoma and contralateral superotemporal field defect.
Primary Diagnosis: Right optic neuropathy with junctional scotoma secondary to compressive lesion at the junction of the right optic nerve and chiasm (pituitary adenoma)
Differential Diagnosis: Pituitary adenoma; suprasellar meningioma; craniopharyngioma; supraclinoid aneurysm
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right
Complications: Optic atrophy with persistent visual field loss (junctional scotoma) and potential endocrine dysfunction from pituitary adenoma (requiring hormone replacement); risk of residual or recurrent tumor postoperatively requiring further surgery, radiation, or chemotherapy.


Plan
All Medications: dopamine agonists; hormone replacement
All Procedures: Visual field testing (perimetry) Pupillary examination (including RAPD testing) Color vision testing (pseudoisochromatic plates) Visual acuity testing Slit-lamp examination Dilated fundus/optic disc examination Magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) of the brain/sellar region Surgical decompression (transsphenoidal pituitary tumor resection) Histopathologic examination of resected tissue Postoperative serial visual field testing and ophthalmic follow-up
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up ophthalmologic exam with visual field testing every 4 months for 1 year, then every 6 months thereafter, with periodic MRIs to monitor for tumor growth.
Patient Education: You have a compressive lesion (pituitary tumor) pressing where the right optic nerve meets the optic chiasm, causing progressive, painless vision loss in your right eye and a small visual field defect in the left eye. Recommended steps: - Undergo MRI to confirm and monitor the tumor. - Treatment usually involves surgical decompression to relieve pressure on the optic nerves; additional therapy (radiation or medication) depends on tumor type. - If the tumor affects hormone glands, hormone replacement or medical therapy (e.g., dopamine agonists for prolactinomas) may be needed. - Visual function (acuity, color vision, visual fields) and MRI should be followed regularly after treatment (every ~4 months for the first year, then about every 6 months). - Report any new symptoms immediately (worsening vision, double vision, headaches, changes in pituitary/hormonal symptoms). - Ask your team about visual rehabilitation options and referral to low-vision services if needed.
Referrals: Neurosurgery and neuro-ophthalmology (neurosurgical decompression with neuro-ophthalmic follow-up)
Emergency Instructions: Seek urgent neuroimaging (MRI brain with pituitary protocol) and urgent neurosurgical/endocrine consultation for evaluation and decompression; if acute severe vision loss or progressive deficit, arrange immediate transfer to tertiary center for urgent surgical decompression.


CASE 63 — Visual Field Defect—Pituitary Lesion
==============================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Blurred vision bilaterally and poor driving performance with multiple accidents
DURATION: 1 year
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "poor vision"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 45-year-old woman with a 1-year history of slowly progressive bilateral blurred vision and deteriorating driving performance with multiple accidents; occasional frontal headaches for 6 months; no prior neurologic or ophthalmologic disease; takes only acetaminophen. Visual acuity 20/25 OD and 20/20 OS; color vision normal; pupils equal and reactive without RAPD; motility, slit-lamp, and fundus exams normal. Visual field testing demonstrated bitemporal hemianopic defects.
History Narrative: A 45-year-old woman complained of blurred vision bilaterally and a poor driving performance with multiple accidents due to “poor vision.” The visual difficulty had been present for the last year and was slowly deteriorating. She also complained of occasional frontal headaches over the last 6 months. She denied any past history of neurologic or ophthalmologic illnesses and took no medicines except acetaminophen for her headaches. Examination revealed visual acuity to be 20/25 on the right and 20/20 on the left. She identified 10 of 10 pseudoisochromatic color plates bilaterally. The pupils were 4mm bilaterally and were equally reactive to light and near, and there was no relative afferent pupillary defect. Motility examination was normal. Slit-lamp examination and fundus exam were normal. Visual field testing showed bitemporal hemianopic defects.
Symptom Onset: 1 year
Symptom Duration: 1 year
Progression: Slowly deteriorating over the last year
Characteristics: Bitemporal hemianopic defects consistent with chiasmal compression (peripheral bitemporal visual field loss respecting the vertical meridian)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Driving (poor driving performance leading to multiple accidents)
Associated Symptoms: blurred vision bilaterally, poor driving performance with multiple accidents, occasional frontal headaches
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes reported
Prior Treatments: None (no prior treatments)
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant — impaired driving causing multiple car accidents and difficulty with vision for one year
Systemic Symptoms: Occasional frontal headaches for 6 months; no other systemic symptoms (denies past neurologic or ophthalmologic illnesses; no medications except acetaminophen).
Risk Factors: Age (45), female sex, presence of pituitary adenoma (parasellar mass)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 132/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 97.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+ (Jaeger 1+)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lenses


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 4 mm bilaterally, equal, reactive to light and near
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Equally reactive to light and near
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Yes
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: 4 mm bilaterally, equally reactive to light and near, no RAPD
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions; motility examination normal.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Normal.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 63-year-old woman with progressive painless bilateral visual blurring and headaches for ~1 year, visual acuity 20/25 OD and 20/20 OS, normal pupils and fundi, visual fields demonstrating bitemporal hemianopia consistent with chiasmal compression. MRI of the sellar region demonstrates a pituitary macroadenoma causing optic chiasm compression. Plan: neuroimaging-confirmed pituitary adenoma; refer for endocrinologic evaluation and neurosurgical consultation for likely surgical decompression (medical therapy if prolactinoma), with postoperative visual field and hormonal follow-up.
Primary Diagnosis: Bitemporal visual field defect secondary to pituitary macroadenoma
Secondary Diagnoses: Pituitary macroadenoma
Differential Diagnosis: Pituitary adenoma; Craniopharyngioma; Meningioma; Chiasmal glioma
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Bilateral


Plan
All Medications: acetaminophen
All Procedures: Magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) of the sella with and without gadolinium; Computed tomography (CT) scan (if MRI contraindicated or emergent); Endocrinologic evaluation; Surgical resection of pituitary adenoma (transsphenoidal surgery); Postoperative hormonal replacement as needed; Postoperative radiation therapy (if required); Serial visual field testing and ophthalmologic examinations (postop at ~3 months, then 6-month intervals for 2 years, yearly for 5 years, then every 2 years); Serial MRI surveillance (e.g., 6 months, 1 year, then yearly for several years).
Follow-Up Instructions: Postoperative: visual field and ophthalmologic exam as soon as tolerated. Then visual fields and serial ophthalmologic exams at ~3 months, then every 6 months for 2 years, then yearly for 5 years, then every 2 years thereafter. MRI of the sella at ~6 months, 1 year, then yearly for several years. Endocrinologic follow-up and hormonal replacement as indicated.
Patient Education: You have a lesion near the optic chiasm (most likely a pituitary tumor) causing the loss of peripheral (side) vision in both eyes. This explains your blurred vision and driving problems. Recommended next steps: - Obtain MRI of the brain with special attention to the sella (with and without gadolinium) to define the mass. - Have blood tests for pituitary hormone levels (prolactin, ACTH/cortisol, TSH, free T4, LH/FSH, IGF-1) to check for a functioning tumor. - Consultation with a neurosurgeon and an endocrinologist to discuss treatment options (medical therapy for prolactinomas, or transsphenoidal surgery for most compressive adenomas; radiation may be needed in some cases). - If vision is severely affected or worsening, surgery is often urgent to relieve pressure on the optic chiasm. - After treatment: expect visual field testing and ophthalmology follow-up soon post‑op and periodically thereafter; endocrine follow-up for hormone replacement or monitoring. Safety and lifestyle: - Avoid driving until your visual fields are reassessed and considered safe. - Report new or worsening headaches, vision loss, double vision, nausea, or unexplained fatigue immediately. If you have questions about MRI safety, medications, or the surgical process, ask your treating team; they can provide details tailored to your situation.
Referrals: Neurosurgery (for pituitary mass evaluation and management); Endocrinology (preoperative hormonal evaluation and postoperative follow-up)
Work Restrictions: Avoid driving; restriction from operating vehicles until vision and visual fields improve and are cleared by physician. Return-to-work limited from duties requiring safe driving, heavy machinery, or tasks needing full peripheral temporal visual fields; follow-up visual field testing before resuming such duties.


CASE 64 — Visual Field Defect—Homonymous Hemianopia
===================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: unable to read well because he “lose his place” or “miss lines” and has bumped his left front fender when parking
DURATION: 6 weeks
LATERALITY: right
PATIENT QUOTES: "lose his place" or "miss lines"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: Six weeks after uncomplicated coronary bypass surgery a 57‑year‑old man noted he “loses his place” and “misses lines” when reading and has bumped his left front fender several times when parking; symptoms began within weeks after surgery and he had no other neurologic symptoms.
History Narrative: A 57-year-old man with a past history of hypertension, diabetes mellitus, and hyperlipidemia underwent coronary bypass surgery 6 weeks prior. His recovery was apparently uneventful, but within several weeks after surgery he began to “lose his place” or “miss lines” when reading and reported bumping his left front fender several times while parking. He had no other neurologic symptoms.
Symptom Onset: Within several weeks after coronary bypass surgery (about 6 weeks prior to evaluation).
Symptom Duration: 6 weeks
Characteristics: Dense, congruous left homonymous hemianopia indicating a retrochiasmal (right occipital lobe) lesion


Subjective (cont.)
Alleviating Factors: Use of a ruler to guide reading; training large saccades/search strategies; monocular prism glasses; vision restoration therapy (VRT)
Associated Symptoms: losing his place/missing lines when reading; bumping the left front fender when parking
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes reported
Prior Treatments: aspirin and control of stroke risk factors
Impact on Daily Activities: Difficulty reading (loses place/misses lines) and impaired driving/parking (bumps left front fender), causing functional vision impairment in daily tasks.
Systemic Symptoms: hypertension, diabetes mellitus, hyperlipidemia, recent coronary bypass surgery
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Hypertension; diabetes mellitus; hyperlipidemia; recent coronary artery bypass surgery (cardiac surgery)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 140/85 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N15/15
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart (distance)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: white without injection
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear corneas bilaterally
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open, deep
Anterior Segment – Lens: mild bilateral nuclear sclerotic cataracts


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 4 mm bilaterally, reactive to light and near
Pupils – Reaction to Light: reacted well to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: reacted well to near
Pupils – RAPD: No relative afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Other: 4mm bilaterally reacted well to light and near, no RAPD
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion OU, motility exam normal
Slit Lamp Exam Description: mild bilateral nuclear sclerotic cataracts
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: mild hypertensive changes (arteriovenous nicking, arteriolar sclerosis)
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No peripheral retinal lesions; periphery within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Right occipital lobe infarction causing a dense, congruous left homonymous hemianopia in a patient with vascular risk factors (hypertension, diabetes, hyperlipidemia) following recent coronary bypass surgery; retrochiasmal visual pathway lesion confirmed on visual field testing, no RAPD, visual acuity 20/20, recommend neuroimaging (MRI preferred), stroke risk factor control and antiplatelet therapy, and ophthalmic follow-up with repeat visual fields in ~6 months for recovery/rehabilitation evaluation (consider visual scanning training and prism/low-vision aids).
Primary Diagnosis: Right occipital lobe infarction causing a dense, congruous left homonymous hemianopia
Secondary Diagnoses: Hypertension; Diabetes mellitus; Hyperlipidemia
Differential Diagnosis: Right occipital lobe infarction (PCA stroke); optic radiation lesion (temporal/parietal lobe infarct or tumor); lateral geniculate body or optic tract lesion (infarct, tumor, demyelination)
Severity: Dense
Laterality: Right


Plan
All Medications: aspirin (for stroke prophylaxis)
Follow-Up Instructions: Repeat visual fields and ophthalmologic examination approximately 6 months after onset to assess for improvement.
Patient Education: You have a left homonymous hemianopia — a loss of the left half of your visual field in both eyes caused by a stroke in the right occipital (vision) part of your brain. Your central vision and acuity are preserved, but you may “lose your place” when reading and miss objects on your left (e.g., bumping the left side of your car). Key points and practical steps: - Cause and prognosis: This is due to damage in the brain (not the eyes). Some recovery can occur over months, but many patients have persistent field loss. Control of stroke risk factors (blood pressure, diabetes, cholesterol, quitting smoking) and taking recommended stroke-prevention medications (e.g., aspirin as advised) are essential to reduce further strokes. - Safety and daily strategies: Use a ruler or line guide when reading, turn your head and make deliberate large eye movements (scan) toward the left to bring items into your seeing field, and be cautious when driving — discuss driving restrictions with your physician and local licensing authority (most patients with this defect are not safe to drive). - Rehabilitation options: Visual scanning training and occupational therapy can improve scanning behaviors and function. Prism glasses can shift some images into your seeing field and may help in certain situations. Vision restoration therapies exist but have limited objective evidence. - Follow-up: Repeat visual field testing and ophthalmology/neurology follow-up at about 6 months to assess for improvement. Promptly report any new weakness, numbness, speech changes, sudden vision loss, or other neurologic symptoms. If you have questions about specific aids, driving, or rehabilitation referrals, ask your ophthalmologist or neurologist for individualized recommendations and referral to vision rehabilitation services.


CASE 65 — Transient Monocular Visual Loss
=========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Transient monocular visual loss in the right eye (3 episodes of graying out, 5–6 minutes each)
DURATION: 5–6 minutes
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "gray-out"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 65-year-old woman with three episodes over 3 weeks of transient monocular visual loss in the right eye characterized by a “gray-out” lasting 5–6 minutes each, with no precipitating factors, no headaches, jaw claudication, persistent visual loss, or other transient neurologic symptoms; history of hypertension and hyperlipidemia controlled on medications.
History Narrative: A 65-year-old woman with a history of hypertension and hyperlipidemia, both controlled on medications, reported three episodes over 3 weeks of transient monocular visual loss in the right eye. Each episode lasted about 5–6 minutes, described as a “gray-out,” with no precipitating factors, no headaches, jaw claudication, persistent visual loss, or transient neurologic symptoms.
Symptom Onset: 3 weeks
Symptom Duration: 3 weeks
Progression: stable
Characteristics: Monocular, transient visual loss in right eye lasting ~5–6 minutes with gray-out episodes; associated with a visible intravascular cholesterol embolus (Hollenhorst plaque); episodic (three episodes over 3 weeks); no associated headache, jaw claudication, persistent visual loss, or focal neurologic symptoms.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Bright light exposure
Associated Symptoms: None (denies headaches, jaw claudication, persistent visual loss, or transient neurologic dysfunction)
Previous Episodes: Three episodes over the last 3 weeks
Prior Treatments: antihypertensive medication and lipid‑lowering therapy (for hypertension and hyperlipidemia); no prior ocular treatments reported
Impact on Daily Activities: Mild; episodes were brief (5–6 minutes), monocular, resolved spontaneously and visual acuity largely preserved (20/25 OD, 20/20 OS), so limited interference with daily activities.
Systemic Symptoms: No systemic symptoms reported.
Risk Factors: Hypertension; hyperlipidemia; age 65 years


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear with mild bilateral nuclear sclerotic cataracts (no corneal pathology)
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open angles
Anterior Segment – Lens: Mild bilateral nuclear sclerotic cataracts


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 4mm bilaterally
Pupils – Reaction to Light: both reacted well to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Yes—both reacted well to light and near.
Pupils – RAPD: No relative afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Other: 4mm bilaterally
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion (normal)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: mild bilateral nuclear sclerotic cataracts
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: No optic nerve pallor; optic discs appear normal (no edema)
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Hollenhorst plaque in right retinal arterioles; otherwise vessels without occlusive changes or hemorrhage
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal periphery


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 65-year-old woman with hypertension and hyperlipidemia presenting with three episodes of transient monocular visual loss of the right eye over 3 weeks, each lasting ~5–6 minutes, with funduscopic evidence of a cholesterol embolus (Hollenhorst plaque) in the right retinal arterioles. Examination notable for VA 20/25 OD, 20/20 OS, normal pupils, no RAPD, normal visual fields, mild bilateral nuclear sclerotic cataracts. Clinical impression: transient monocular visual loss due to retinal emboli most consistent with thromboembolic disease from ipsilateral carotid/aortic atherosclerotic source (retinal embolus secondary to right carotid stenosis favored). Recommend urgent vascular and cardiac evaluation (carotid duplex/MR angiography, consider conventional angiography as indicated; transthoracic/transesophageal echocardiography), stroke risk factor optimization, and initiation of antiplatelet therapy unless a cardiac/aortic source or anticoagulation indication identified. If high-grade ipsilateral carotid stenosis (70–99%) and patient is a suitable surgical/stenting candidate, consider carotid endarterectomy or stenting. Consider ESR/CRP to exclude giant cell arteritis if clinical suspicion. Follow with periodic carotid surveillance.
Primary Diagnosis: Retinal emboli secondary to right carotid stenosis causing transient monocular visual loss
Secondary Diagnoses: Hypertension; Hyperlipidemia
Differential Diagnosis: Thromboembolic retinal ischemia from carotid/aortic atherosclerotic plaque (Hollenhorst plaque); Cardioembolic retinal embolism (valvular disease, atrial fibrillation); Giant cell arteritis; Ocular hypoperfusion (ocular ischemic syndrome/venous stasis retinopathy)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right
Complications: Stroke


Plan
All Medications: antihypertensive medication (unspecified), lipid-lowering medication (unspecified), aspirin (antiplatelet therapy suggested)
All Procedures: Carotid Doppler/ultrasound; MR angiography of carotid vessels; Conventional (catheter) angiography; Transthoracic echocardiography; Transesophageal echocardiography; Cardiac MR imaging; Erythrocyte sedimentation rate (ESR) and C‑reactive protein (CRP) testing; Ophthalmodynamometry; MR brain with MR angiography; Carotid endarterectomy; Carotid stenting; Periodic noninvasive carotid surveillance (carotid Doppler or MR angiography)
Follow-Up Instructions: Medical supervision of stroke risk factors with periodic reevaluation of the carotid artery for restenosis using noninvasive studies (e.g., carotid Doppler or MR angiography).
Patient Education: You had brief episodes of vision “graying out” in your right eye caused by tiny cholesterol plaque (Hollenhorst plaque) seen in a retinal artery. This most commonly comes from atherosclerotic disease in the carotid arteries or heart and can increase your risk of stroke. Recommended steps: - Urgent vascular and cardiac evaluation (carotid imaging such as carotid Doppler/CTA/MRA; echocardiogram) to find the source of emboli. - Control vascular risk factors: strict blood pressure, cholesterol, smoking cessation, and diabetes management if present. - Antiplatelet therapy (usually aspirin) unless another indication requires anticoagulation — your doctor will decide. - If significant ipsilateral carotid stenosis is found (typically 70–99%), carotid endarterectomy or stenting may be recommended after specialist review. - If age >55 and symptoms or labs suggest giant cell arteritis (new headaches, jaw claudication, high ESR/CRP), urgent evaluation and treatment are needed. - Seek immediate care for any new or prolonged vision loss, weakness, speech changes, or other stroke-like symptoms. Follow-up with neurology, vascular surgery, and cardiology as arranged; regular monitoring of carotid status with noninvasive imaging.
Referrals: Carotid vascular evaluation (vascular surgery or stroke/neurology); cardiology (echocardiography and cardiac source evaluation); rheumatology or internal medicine for giant cell arteritis workup if indicated (ESR/CRP); hematology/coagulation if hypercoagulable workup indicated.

====================================

Findings are consistent with an isolated, pupil-sparing, complete right third nerve palsy most consistent with ischemic etiology in the setting of diabetes and hypertension. Plan: control vasculopathic risk factors, observe with follow-up at 1–2 months for improvement (expect recovery over 3–6 months); obtain neuroimaging only if no improvement by 3 months or if pupils become involved or other neurologic signs develop. Consider Tensilon testing if fluctuating weakness suggestive of myasthenia gravis and consider strabismus or lid surgery for persistent deficits.

Severity: Complete
Laterality: Right


Plan

Patient Education: This is most likely an ischemic (diabetic/vascular) third nerve palsy causing your drooping eyelid and double vision. Control vascular risk factors—tightly manage blood sugar, blood pressure, and cholesterol and stop smoking. Expect gradual improvement over 3–6 months; follow-up visits every 1–2 months to monitor recovery. If symptoms worsen (new pupil dilation, increasing pain, new neurological signs) or do not begin to improve by ~12 weeks, further testing (neuroimaging) is recommended. If diplopia or ptosis remain after recovery, surgical options for the eyelid or strabismus can be considered. If you develop symptoms suggestive of giant cell arteritis (new persistent headache, jaw or tongue claudication, scalp tenderness, sudden vision loss), seek immediate evaluation.
Referrals: Neuroimaging if no improvement by 3 months (or earlier if atypical features) — consider MRI/MRA or CT/CTA; ESR and CRP (and temporal artery biopsy if elevated/suspicious for GCA); Tensilon test for suspected myasthenia gravis.


CASE 67 — Third Nerve Palsy—Aneurysm
====================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Acute onset binocular diplopia, left-sided frontal-periorbital pain, and left lid droop
DURATION: 8 days
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "I noted the acute onset of binocular diplopia 8 days ago. I had sudden left-sided frontal-periorbital pain, then noticed diplopia and left-sided lid droop."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 45-year-old man with acute onset 8 days earlier of binocular diplopia preceded by left-sided frontal–periorbital pain, who developed left-sided lid droop and progressive eye misalignment; exam showed left ptosis, anisocoria with a larger left pupil and minimal left pupil reaction, partial paresis of elevation, adduction, and depression of the left eye, and otherwise normal vision and neurologic exam.
History Narrative: A 45-year-old man noted the acute onset of binocular diplopia 8 days prior to evaluation. He experienced acute left-sided frontal–periorbital pain followed by left-sided lid droop and diplopia. He was previously well with no history of significant illnesses. Examination showed visual acuity 20/20 OU, pupils 3 mm OD and 5 mm OS in light (4 mm OD and 5 mm OS in dark) with the left pupil reacting minimally to light and near, no RAPD, and full confrontation fields. Ocular motility revealed mild left hypotropia and moderate exotropia with normal ductions OD and partial paresis of elevation, adduction, and depression OS. There was 3 mm left ptosis with mildly impaired levator function. Facial sensation and strength were normal with intact corneal reflexes bilaterally. Slit-lamp and fundus exams were normal, and general neurologic exam was otherwise normal. CT/CTA, MRI/MRA, and cerebral angiography demonstrated an ipsilateral posterior communicating artery aneurysm.
Symptom Onset: 8 days prior to evaluation
Symptom Duration: 8 days
Progression: Acute (onset 8 days prior)
Characteristics: Acute onset binocular diplopia, left frontal-periorbital pain, left ptosis, anisocoria with left pupil larger and minimally reactive, partial left CN III palsy (impaired elevation, adduction, depression), pupil-involving third nerve palsy due to ipsilateral posterior communicating artery aneurysm


Subjective (cont.)
Alleviating Factors: Patching the eye
Associated Symptoms: Binocular diplopia; left-sided frontal-periorbital pain; left-sided lid droop (ptosis); anisocoria (left pupil larger); mild left hypotropia; moderate exotropia; partial paresis of elevation, adduction, and depression in the left eye; impaired levator function.
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes reported
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — diplopia requiring eye patching and ptosis causing functional lid droop affecting daily activities (e.g., reading/driving)
Systemic Symptoms: No systemic symptoms reported


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 76 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Left ptosis 3 mm; mildly impaired levator function; right lids/lashes normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear.
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea with no abnormalities noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal iris, left pupil dilated and minimally reactive (anisocoria)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lenses bilaterally


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Right 3 mm, Left 5 mm (light); Right 4 mm, Left 5 mm (dark)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Left pupil reacted minimally to light; right pupil reacted normally.
Pupils – Reaction to Near: minimal
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: Left pupil 5 mm in light, minimally reactive; right pupil 3 mm in light.
Extraocular Movements: Left: partial paresis of elevation, adduction, and depression; Right: normal.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Normal
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Normal optic nerve
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Partial left third nerve palsy with pupillary involvement secondary to a left posterior communicating artery aneurysm confirmed on CT/CTA, MRI/MRA and cerebral angiography; presenting with acute binocular diplopia, left ptosis, anisocoria (left pupil larger and minimally reactive), and partial paresis of elevation, adduction, and depression of the left eye.
Primary Diagnosis: Partial third nerve palsy due to posterior communicating artery aneurysm
Secondary Diagnoses: Posterior communicating artery aneurysm
Differential Diagnosis: Posterior communicating artery aneurysm compressive third nerve palsy; Diabetic ischemic (microvascular) third nerve palsy; Intracranial mass lesion (tumor) affecting the oculomotor nerve; Meningeal infiltration (e.g., carcinomatosis, meningitis)
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Left
Complications: Aneurysmal rupture with subarachnoid hemorrhage leading to high morbidity and mortality


Plan
All Procedures: CT/CTA, MRI, MRA, cerebral digital subtraction angiography (DSA), surgical or endovascular repair of posterior communicating artery aneurysm (recommended)
Follow-Up Instructions: Patching the eye to relieve diplopia; surgical or endovascular repair of the posterior communicating artery aneurysm with neurosurgical/neuroradiologic follow-up and postprocedural ophthalmic monitoring.
Patient Education: You have a partial third nerve palsy caused by an aneurysm of the posterior communicating artery. This aneurysm can press on the nerve and affect eyelid position, eye movements, and the pupil, and it can rupture with serious, potentially life‑threatening consequences. Treatment (surgical clipping or endovascular repair) is recommended to prevent rupture. Imaging tests (CT/CTA, MRI/MRA, and often cerebral angiography) are used to confirm and fully evaluate the aneurysm. In the short term, patching one eye can relieve your double vision. Report any sudden worsening of headache, sudden severe pain around the eye, sudden worsening of vision, new weakness, numbness, altered consciousness, or vomiting immediately to emergency services. Follow closely with neurosurgery/neuroradiology and neuro‑ophthalmology for definitive treatment planning and postoperative visual follow‑up.
Referrals: Neurosurgery / Endovascular neurosurgery for aneurysm repair and cerebral angiography consideration
Emergency Instructions: Surgery or endovascular therapy to repair the aneurysm is necessary to prevent aneurysmal rupture, which carries a high morbidity and mortality.


CASE 68 — Fourth Nerve Palsy—Congenital
=======================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Vertical binocular diplopia for 2 weeks
DURATION: 2 weeks
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "below and to the side of the other"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 10-year-old boy with acute onset vertical binocular diplopia for 2 weeks; describes one image “below and to the side of the other,” denies subjective torsion, no precipitant, no headaches, facial numbness, ptosis, or other complaints; tends to tilt his head to the left.
History Narrative: A 10‑year‑old boy with no significant past medical history experienced acute onset vertical binocular diplopia for 2 weeks. He described one image as “below and to the side of the other” and denied subjective torsion, headaches, facial numbness, ptosis, or other complaints. Visual acuity was 20/20 OU with normal color vision. Pupils 5 mm equal and reactive, no RAPD. He habitually tilted his head to the left. Ocular exam showed a right hypertropia (4 PD at distance; 8 PD on left gaze, 1 PD on right gaze; 2 PD on up gaze; 7 PD on down gaze), head tilt right 8 PD and left 2 PD, 4° right excyclodeviation on double Maddox rod, and vertical fusional amplitudes of 10 PD. Ductions, versions, saccades, pursuits, slit‑lamp and fundus exams were otherwise normal, though indirect ophthalmoscopy revealed right excyclotropia. Findings were consistent with a congenital right fourth nerve palsy with recent decompensation.
Symptom Onset: 2 weeks
Symptom Duration: 2 weeks
Characteristics: Congenital fourth nerve palsy with decompensation: right hypertropia worse on left gaze and right head tilt, head tilt to the left, 4° right excyclotropia, large vertical fusional amplitudes (10 PD), acute-onset diplopia from longstanding phoria.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Looking to the left gaze and right head tilt
Associated Symptoms: vertical binocular diplopia; one image "below and to the side of the other"; denied subjective torsion, headaches, facial numbness, ptosis, or other complaints
Previous Episodes: Head tilt present for years (old decompensated congenital fourth nerve palsy)
Prior Treatments: None
Systemic Symptoms: Denies headaches, facial numbness, ptosis, or any other systemic complaints.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/72 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+ (20/20)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: clear and normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: normal
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Normal

Pupils – Reaction to Light: equally reactive to light and near

Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: 5mm bilaterally and equally reactive to light and near

Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal


Assessment

Primary Diagnosis: Decompensation of old right fourth cranial nerve palsy
Secondary Diagnoses: Congenital right fourth nerve palsy (decompensated)
Differential Diagnosis: Right superior oblique (fourth nerve) palsy; decompensated congenital fourth nerve palsy; myasthenia gravis; thyroid eye disease
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right


Plan
All Procedures: Observation; prism correction; strabismus surgery
Follow-Up Instructions: No rehabilitation and follow-up are required if the problem is resolved.
Patient Education: Observation and reassurance that this is a longstanding (congenital) fourth nerve palsy that recently decompensated. Explain why the head tilt developed and how large vertical fusional amplitudes allowed compensation for years. Discuss treatment options: observation if symptoms mild, prism glasses to relieve diplopia, or strabismus surgery if prisms ineffective or misalignment cosmetically or functionally significant. Advise to return sooner if sudden worsening, new double vision, pain, numbness, drooping, or other neurologic symptoms occur. Offer referral to orthoptics for prism fitting and to strabismus surgeon for surgical discussion as needed.


CASE 69 — Sixth Nerve Palsy
===========================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Binocular horizontal diplopia for 3 months, primarily at distance and worse on left gaze.
DURATION: 3 months
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "I have had binocular double vision for 3 months, mostly when looking at things far away and worse when I look to the left. It goes away when I look at things up close."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 56-year-old man with a 3-month history of binocular horizontal diplopia, primarily at distance and absent at near, worse on left gaze; associated mild left frontotemporal headaches and numbness/tingling of the left forehead. Symptoms worse on left gaze; no visual loss, trauma, or prior neurologic/ophthalmologic symptoms. Has type II diabetes controlled with oral agents.
History Narrative: A 56-year-old man with type II diabetes controlled on oral agents presented with a 3-month history of binocular horizontal diplopia, primarily at distance and absent at near, worse on left gaze. He also reported numbness and tingling in the left forehead and mild left frontotemporal headaches. He denied recent head trauma, visual loss, or prior neurologic/ophthalmologic symptoms. Examination showed 20/20 OU visual acuity, full color plates, pupils 4 mm equal and reactive, no RAPD, full confrontational fields, a 4 PD esotropia at distance and no esotropia at near (nil on right gaze, 8 PD on left gaze, 4 PD on up and down gaze), underaction of left eye abduction, no ptosis, decreased soft-touch sensation over the left forehead with slightly depressed left corneal reflex, normal slit-lamp and fundus exam without papilledema, and otherwise normal neurologic exam. MRI with contrast revealed a cavernous sinus meningioma causing a left sixth nerve palsy with involvement of the left ophthalmic (V1) branch of the trigeminal nerve.
Symptom Onset: 3 months ago
Symptom Duration: 3 months
Progression: Progressive over 3 months (worsening on left gaze)
Characteristics: Horizontal diplopia primarily at distance, worse on left gaze, 4 PD esotropia at distance (no ET at near), left abduction limitation consistent with left sixth nerve palsy, associated left V1 sensory loss and mild left frontotemporal headache.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: left gaze
Alleviating Factors: absent at near
Associated Symptoms: Binocular horizontal diplopia (worse on left gaze), numbness and tingling in the left forehead, mild left frontotemporal headaches, decreased sensation to soft touch over the left forehead, slightly depressed left corneal reflex.
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes reported
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — intermittent distance diplopia worse on left gaze causing functional difficulty with activities requiring distance vision (e.g., driving, stairs), but near tasks unaffected and vision 20/20, allowing most daily activities with adaptations (patching/prisms).
Systemic Symptoms: numbness and tingling in the left forehead; mild left frontotemporal headaches; diabetes mellitus type II
Risk Factors: Type II diabetes mellitus; middle-aged (56 years)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 76 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: No ptosis
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: White, quiet conjunctivae; normal sclera (no injection or chemosis)
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis; normal external adnexa and globe position.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Normal exam; left corneal reflex slightly depressed (no corneal pathology)
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear natural lens bilaterally


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 4 mm bilaterally, equal, reactive to light and near
Pupils – Reaction to Light: equally reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Equal
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: 4 mm bilaterally and equally reactive to light and near
Extraocular Movements: Left lateral rectus underaction; limitation of abduction OS; ductions: underaction of abduction in the left eye, full ductions OD.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: slit-lamp exam was normal
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: No optic disc edema; optic nerves normal (no papilledema)
Fundus Exam – Macula: normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Left sixth nerve palsy with underaction of left lateral rectus causing 4 PD esotropia at distance (worse on left gaze to 8 PD; none at near), accompanied by decreased sensation in the left forehead and mildly depressed left corneal reflex, consistent with involvement of the left ophthalmic (V1) branch of the trigeminal nerve. MRI with contrast demonstrated a cavernous sinus meningioma causing compressive left sixth nerve palsy and trigeminal neuropathy. Visual acuity 20/20 OU, pupils equal and reactive, no RAPD, full confrontation fields, no papilledema. Plan: treat underlying lesion (neurosurgical/radiation referral), symptomatic diplopia management (patching, prisms, consider early ipsilateral medial rectus botulinum injection), and close neurologic/ophthalmic follow-up; strabismus surgery if residual stable diplopia persists.
Primary Diagnosis: Left sixth nerve palsy
Secondary Diagnoses: Type II diabetes mellitus; trigeminal (V1) neuropathy (left forehead sensory loss)
Differential Diagnosis: Sixth nerve palsy due to cavernous sinus lesion (meningioma/other compressive tumor); Ischemic (vasculopathic) sixth nerve palsy; Cavernous sinus processes (thrombosis, fistula, aneurysm, inflammatory/infectious); Myasthenia gravis
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Left
Complications: diplopia (persistent binocular horizontal diplopia), limitation of abduction (left lateral rectus paresis), decreased corneal reflex (risk of corneal exposure/keratopathy), residual ophthalmoplegia requiring strabismus surgery, potential recurrence or progression of neurologic deficits from cavernous sinus meningioma


Plan
All Medications: oral agents for diabetes
All Procedures: MRI with contrast; lumbar puncture (in some cases); spinal tap; patching; prisms; botulinum injection into ipsilateral medial rectus; strabismus surgery; surgery or radiation therapy for underlying lesion
Follow-Up Instructions: Observe for recurrence of further neurologic and ophthalmologic deficits depending on the etiology; provide symptomatic diplopia treatment (patching, prisms, or botulinum and consider strabismus surgery if chronic/stable); monitor for improvement and pursue neuroimaging or further evaluation if progressive or unresolved.
Patient Education: You have a left sixth (abducens) nerve palsy causing double vision, most likely due to a meningioma in the cavernous sinus pressing on nearby nerves. This explains your horizontal double vision (worse when looking left and at distance) and decreased sensation on the left forehead. Further evaluation with MRI with contrast is important to confirm the diagnosis and to plan treatment. Treatment targets the underlying lesion and may include observation, surgery, or radiation; diplopia can be managed temporarily with an eye patch, prism glasses, or botulinum injection into the opposing muscle, and strabismus surgery can be considered if weakness persists. Report any new symptoms (worsening vision, increased double vision, new numbness, weakness, severe headache, or vomiting) promptly. Follow-up visits are needed to monitor neurologic signs and eye alignment.
Referrals: Neuroimaging (MRI with contrast) and neurosurgery/neurology consultation (for cavernous sinus meningioma evaluation and management)


CASE 70 — Internuclear Ophthalmoplegia
======================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: difficulty focusing
DURATION: several months
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "difficulty focusing"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 27-year-old woman with known multiple sclerosis and prior right optic neuritis not on medications who for several months has had “difficulty focusing” her eyes. She reports no current medications; associated history includes mild gait instability, bladder disturbance, and leg numbness. On exam vision 20/20 OU with reduced color vision and a small paracentral scotoma on the right, a mild right afferent pupillary defect and mild temporal pallor of the right optic nerve; ocular signs began several months ago and consist of mild left adduction deficit with slow adducting saccades, right abducting monocular nystagmus, intact convergence, and a 2 PD comitant left hypertropia (skew deviation). No ptosis; slit-lamp otherwise normal.
History Narrative: A 27-year-old woman with a history of multiple sclerosis (MS) complained of “difficulty focusing” her eyes. Her previous MS course included a history of optic neuritis in the right eye. She did have mild gait instability, bladder disturbance, and leg numbness. Her eye difficulty started several months prior to ophthalmologic evaluation. She was taking no medications.
Symptom Onset: several months prior to ophthalmologic evaluation
Symptom Duration: several months prior to ophthalmologic evaluation
Progression: Chronic (started several months prior; stable to chronic course)
Characteristics: Left internuclear ophthalmoplegia: mild left adduction deficit with slow adducting saccades, right-beating abducting nystagmus, intact convergence, comitant 2 prism diopter left hypertropia (skew), full ductions otherwise, no ptosis.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Horizontal gaze (rightward) — attempted rightward saccades exacerbate adduction weakness and elicit abducting nystagmus (i.e., horizontal gaze/attempted rightward saccades)
Alleviating Factors: Convergence (able to overcome the adduction deficit)
Associated Symptoms: difficulty focusing, mild gait instability, bladder disturbance, leg numbness, diplopia (intermittent/possible), oscillopsia
Previous Episodes: Optic neuritis in the right eye (prior episode of optic neuritis).
Prior Treatments: None
Impact on Daily Activities: Mild — patient reports “difficulty focusing” but has 20/20 acuity, no major functional impairment; possible intermittent diplopia/oscillopsia that may mildly affect reading or tasks requiring precise binocular vision.
Systemic Symptoms: mild gait instability, bladder disturbance, leg numbness
Risk Factors: Multiple sclerosis (history of MS), prior optic neuritis, age 27, neurological symptoms (gait instability, bladder disturbance), prior demyelinating disease — autoimmune demyelinating disease (MS) as primary risk factor.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20

External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Normal
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: normal

Pupils – Reaction to Light: equal and reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: reactive
Pupils – RAPD: Mild right afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Other: mild right afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Mild adduction paresis of the left eye; otherwise ductions full; slow adducting saccades in left eye with right abducting (dissociated) nystagmus; saccades to left and vertical normal; convergence intact.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Normal
