Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Peripheral retina flat to ora, no hemorrhages or lesions detected


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 27-year-old woman with known multiple sclerosis presenting with several months of “difficulty focusing.” Examination: VA 20/20 OU, color vision 7/10 OD and 10/10 OS, small right paracentral scotoma, pupils 5 mm equal with mild right afferent pupillary defect, mild temporal pallor OD. Ocular motility: mild left adduction paresis with slowed adducting saccades, right abducting monocular nystagmus on dextroversion, intact convergence, and a comitant 2 PD left hypertropia consistent with a skew deviation. Findings consistent with a left internuclear ophthalmoplegia due to a lesion of the left medial longitudinal fasciculus in the setting of MS; right optic neuropathy is likely prior optic neuritis. Management directed at underlying MS; symptomatic diplopia from skew may be treated with prisms, with strabismus surgery considered only for persistent residual diplopia. Follow-up to monitor resolution recommended.
Primary Diagnosis: Optic neuropathy (right) and left internuclear ophthalmoplegia due to multiple sclerosis
Secondary Diagnoses: Multiple sclerosis; Right optic neuritis; Mild gait instability; Bladder disturbance; Leg numbness; Right optic neuropathy (residual optic neuritis)
Differential Diagnosis: Left internuclear ophthalmoplegia due to multiple sclerosis; brainstem (pontine/midbrain) infarction; myasthenia gravis (pseudo-INO)
Severity: Mild
Laterality: Left
Complications: diplopia (due to skew deviation)


Plan
All Medications: None
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up for resolution of the INO is important.
Patient Education: Internuclear ophthalmoplegia (INO) is caused by a small lesion in the brainstem (medial longitudinal fasciculus) often from multiple sclerosis; it produces difficulty moving one eye inward with involuntary jerking of the other eye on outward gaze and may cause double vision. Your vision and color testing indicate prior right optic neuritis related to MS. Treatment targets the underlying cause (MS); many INOs improve over time. Measures to help now: use a prism in glasses for symptomatic vertical or horizontal diplopia, patch one eye or use an eye shield for bothersome double vision, and avoid driving if diplopia or imbalance impair safety. If your symptoms change, worsen, or you develop new weakness, numbness, severe headache, fever, or vision loss, seek prompt medical evaluation. Follow up with your neurologist and neuro‑ophthalmologist for MRI as indicated, monitoring, and management of MS (disease‑modifying therapy discussion) and consider vision rehabilitation if diplopia persists.


CASE 71 — Diplopia—Ocular Myasthenia Gravis
===========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Intermittent diplopia for 6 months, worse as the day goes on, with intermittent eyelid droop
DURATION: 6 months
LATERALITY: bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "two lines instead of one"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 57‑year‑old woman with a 6‑month history of intermittent diplopia that is minimal on waking but worsens as the day progresses, often causing double vision while driving (“two lines instead of one”); intermittent left or right eyelid droop; no headache, facial numbness/pain, dysphagia, dysarthria, breathing difficulty, or generalized weakness; history of pernicious anemia on monthly B12.
History Narrative: A 57-year-old woman presented with a 6-month history of intermittent diplopia that was often absent on awakening but worsened as the day progressed. She reported seeing “two lines instead of one” while driving and had intermittent unilateral (left or right) eyelid droop. She denied headache, facial numbness or pain, dysphagia, dysarthria, breathing difficulty, generalized muscle weakness, or sensory deficits. Past medical history was notable only for pernicious anemia treated with monthly B12 injections. On examination pupils were 5 mm and reactive, visual afferent testing was normal, and there was an esotropia of 10 PD in primary position increasing with lateral gaze. Sustained lateral gaze worsened her diplopia and esotropia; duction testing suggested bilateral lateral rectus paresis. She had bilateral, mildly weak levator function with ptosis that worsened when the opposite lid was elevated (enhanced ptosis), and on prolonged upgaze developed a left hypertropia. Mild orbicularis/eye-closure weakness was present. Slit-lamp, fundus exam, thyroid testing, and chest CT were unremarkable. A Tensilon (edrophonium) test produced improvement of esotropia and ptosis; serum acetylcholine receptor antibodies were negative. Findings were consistent with ocular myasthenia gravis.
Symptom Onset: 6 months ago
Symptom Duration: 6 months
Progression: Ocular myasthenia gravis with symptoms progressive over 6 months, fluctuating/worsening throughout each day and with sustained gaze; potential to generalize (risk ~50% to 80% over time) but currently ocular only.
Characteristics: Intermittent, fatigable diplopia and bilateral ptosis that worsen as the day progresses; ocular-only weakness of extraocular muscles (bilateral lateral rectus paresis) with orbicularis oculi weakness, enhanced ptosis, pupil-sparing, Tensilon-positive, AChR-antibody–negative, chest CT negative for thymoma


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Worsening as the day goes on (fatigue with sustained use); prolonged upward gaze; sustained lateral gaze; elevation of body temperature
Alleviating Factors: Symptoms improved by rest and by edrophonium (Tensilon) test; often better on awakening and improved by cold (ice-pack test)
Associated Symptoms: Intermittent diplopia worsening with fatigue over the day, intermittent unilateral eyelid droop (ptosis), increased ptosis with elevation of opposite eyelid (enhanced ptosis), difficulty driving (seeing two lines), worsened diplopia with sustained lateral gaze, mild orbicularis oculi/eye closure weakness, no headache, facial numbness or pain, dysphagia, dysarthria, breathing difficulty, or generalized muscle weakness.
Previous Episodes: Intermittent diplopia for 6 months, with intermittent eyelid droop during that period
Prior Treatments: Vitamin B12 injections monthly for pernicious anemia.
Impact on Daily Activities: Difficulty driving due to worsening diplopia (seeing “two lines instead of one” in the middle of the road); intermittent functional impairment with activities that require sustained gaze as symptoms worsen through the day.
Systemic Symptoms: No systemic symptoms reported (denies headache, facial numbness or pain, dysphagia, dysarthria, breathing difficulty, generalized muscle weakness, or extremity paresis or sensory symptoms)
Risk Factors: Age (57 years), female sex, pernicious anemia (autoimmune disease)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Bilateral ptosis, mildly weak levator function, ptosis worsens with opposite lid elevation (enhanced ptosis)
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear.
External Exam – Lacrimal System: No epiphora; tear film and puncta not mentioned — likely normal.
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Normal
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lenses bilaterally


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 5 mm and equally reactive to light and near
Pupils – Reaction to Light: equally reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Equally reactive to near
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: 5 mm and equally reactive to light and near
Extraocular Movements: Limited — paresis of both lateral rectus muscles; worsens with sustained gaze (fatigable)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Slit-lamp and fundus exams were normal.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: disc sharp with distinct margins, pink, normal cup-to-disc ratio
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No peripheral retinal lesions; peripheral fundus within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Ocular myasthenia gravis presenting with fluctuating, fatigable binocular diplopia and bilateral ptosis. Examination notable for esotropia increasing on lateral gaze (10 PD primary, up to 18 PD), paresis of both lateral rectus muscles, bilateral mild levator weakness with enhanced ptosis, and mild orbicularis oculi/eyelid closure weakness. Pupils normal, no afferent defect, slit-lamp and fundus exams normal, and general neurologic exam otherwise unremarkable. Tensilon test produced improvement of esotropia and ptosis. Acetylcholine receptor antibodies negative, TSH normal, and chest CT without thymoma. Findings consistent with ocular myasthenia gravis.
Primary Diagnosis: Ocular myasthenia gravis
Secondary Diagnoses: Pernicious anemia
Differential Diagnosis: Myasthenia gravis (ocular); Bilateral sixth nerve palsies; Ocular myopathy; Lambert-Eaton myasthenic syndrome
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Bilateral


Plan
All Medications: B12 (monthly injections), edrophonium (Tensilon), neostigmine (Prostigmin), pyridostigmine (Mestinon), prednisone, azathioprine, cyclophosphamide, cyclosporine, intravenous immunoglobulin, plasmapheresis.
All Procedures: Tensilon (edrophonium) test Computed tomography (CT) scan of the chest
Follow-Up Instructions: Close supervision of medications; inform physician immediately if dysphagia, respiratory difficulty, or limb weakness develop; periodic reassessment for generalization of myasthenia and medication adjustment.
Patient Education: Explain ocular myasthenia gravis (MG) as an autoimmune condition causing weakness and fatigue of the eyelid and eye‑movement muscles that typically worsens through the day and improves with rest. Review the specific findings in this case (intermittent double vision, fluctuating drooping eyelids, improvement with edrophonium/Tensilon, negative AChR antibodies, normal chest CT). Teach warning signs that require immediate medical attention: new or worsening difficulty breathing, swallowing, speaking, or limb weakness (possible generalization). Discuss home measures: use rest breaks, avoid overheating, consider ice‑pack test for ptosis, and use patching or prism/eye patching while driving to manage diplopia temporarily. Explain treatment options and rationale: symptomatic anticholinesterase (pyridostigmine) for eyelid/eye muscle weakness, corticosteroids or other immunosuppression if symptoms are significant or refractory, and that thymoma was not seen on her chest CT so thymectomy is not indicated now. Discuss possible course: some patients remain ocular, others may develop generalized MG within 2–3 years, and follow‑up with neurology/oculoplastics is important. Arrange follow‑up: prompt neurologic/neuromuscular clinic referral if not already, periodic rechecks of ocular findings and strength, and repeat testing (single‑fiber EMG or antibody panels) if diagnosis remains uncertain. Advise medication precautions and interactions; carry a medical alert card noting MG and current medications, and inform all treating clinicians and dentists. Provide resources for patient education and support (MG patient organizations) and encourage questions.
Emergency Instructions: Inform physician immediately if you develop dysphagia, respiratory difficulty, or limb weakness.


CASE 72 — Thyroid Ophthalmopathy
================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: 2-month history of mild to moderate eye irritation (foreign body sensation), diplopia, and eye bulging
DURATION: 2 months
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "eye irritation" "foreign body–type" "diplopia" "bulging"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 35-year-old woman with 2 months of constant, fluctuating mild–moderate foreign body–type eye irritation, binocular vertical diplopia (worse on up and down gaze) causing her to patch one eye for activities, and progressive proptosis noted by her husband; history of hyperthyroidism treated with radioactive iodine 1 year ago and current thyroid supplements.
History Narrative: A 35-year-old woman with a history of hyperthyroidism treated with radioactive iodine one year ago, currently taking thyroid supplements, presents with a 2-month history of constant but fluctuating mild–moderate eye irritation described as a foreign body sensation, binocular constant vertical diplopia for which she often patches one eye to read or drive, and progressive apparent bulging of the eyes noted by her husband. She denies other medical problems.
Symptom Onset: 2 months
Symptom Duration: 2 months
Progression: Progressive over 1–3 years with an acute active inflammatory phase lasting 6–18 months followed by a chronic fibrotic phase
Characteristics: Thyroid ophthalmopathy with eyelid retraction, lid lag, bilateral proptosis (R 22mm, L 24mm), restrictive extraocular myopathy causing diplopia (limitation of elevation, depression, and abduction), mild punctate keratopathy, binocular constant vertical diplopia, prior hyperthyroidism treated with radioactive iodine.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Recumbency (lying flat)/morning periorbital edema; smoking (worsens ophthalmopathy); radioactive iodine treatment (preceding trigger)
Alleviating Factors: Patching one eye to read or drive; elevating head of bed and sleeping partially supine; methylcellulose artificial tears and ointment at night; taping eyelids shut at night or use of goggles to humidify; wearing dark glasses with side protection; stopping smoking (advised).
Associated Symptoms: eye irritation; foreign body sensation; diplopia; eyes bulging; lid retraction; lid lag; proptosis; mild punctate keratopathy; photophobia (mentioned as common) — but in this case specifically: eye discomfort, diplopia, bulging eyes, lid retraction, lid lag, proptosis, mild punctate keratopathy
Prior Treatments: Radioactive iodine treatment 1 year ago; thyroid supplements.
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — intermittent impairment (uses eye patching to read/drive, symptomatic diplopia and irritation but visual acuity largely preserved)
Systemic Symptoms: Hyperthyroidism treated with radioactive iodine 1 year ago (on thyroid supplements)
Risk Factors: History of hyperthyroidism treated with radioactive iodine; current thyroid dysfunction (on thyroid supplements); smoking (advised to stop — inferred risk factor if present)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: lid retraction and lid lag bilaterally
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: mild punctate keratopathy bilaterally
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Proptosis with Hertel measurements (base 95 mm) 22 mm OD, 24 mm OS; lid retraction and lid lag bilaterally; scleral show; mild periorbital edema; extraocular motility restriction: limitation of elevation and depression OU and limitation of abduction OU (worse OS).
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Mild punctate keratopathy bilaterally
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: clear lenses


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 4mm bilaterally and equally reactive to light and near
Pupils – Reaction to Light: equally reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: reactive
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: 4 mm bilaterally
Extraocular Movements: Limitation of elevation and depression in both eyes; limitation of abduction in both eyes (worse on the left); adduction relatively normal.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: mild punctate keratopathy bilaterally
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 35-year-old woman with history of hyperthyroidism treated with radioactive iodine 1 year ago presenting with 2 months of bilateral eye irritation, constant binocular vertical diplopia, and progressive proptosis. Exam: VA 20/25 OU, full color vision, pupils equal and reactive, no RAPD, full static fields. Motility: left hypertropia 4 PD primary, increases on up and down gaze, esotropia 5 PD comitant; ductions show limitation of elevation and depression OU and limitation of abduction OU (worse OS), consistent with restrictive extraocular myopathy. Lid retraction and lid lag bilaterally. Proptosis with Hertel 22 mm OD and 24 mm OS (base 95 mm). Mild punctate keratopathy OU; fundi normal. Clinical picture most consistent with thyroid orbitopathy (Graves’ ophthalmopathy) with restrictive EOM involvement and mild exposure keratopathy. Recommend thyroid function assessment, imaging of orbits (CT/MRI) to document EOM enlargement, smoking cessation, lubrication for exposure, consider prisms/patching for diplopia, and close follow-up for progression; systemic corticosteroids or orbital radiation reserved if active inflammation worsens or optic neuropathy/severe exposure develops. Further interventions (orbital decompression, strabismus and lid surgery) to be considered in chronic stable phase.
Primary Diagnosis: Thyroid orbitopathy
Secondary Diagnoses: Hyperthyroidism (history of), treated with radioactive iodine; Dry eye (exposure keratopathy / punctate keratopathy)
Differential Diagnosis: Thyroid orbitopathy (Graves’ ophthalmopathy); orbital pseudotumor (idiopathic orbital inflammation); myasthenia gravis
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: Exposure keratopathy; diplopia (restrictive extraocular myopathy); optic neuropathy from compressive apical crowding; cosmetic disfigurement (proptosis, lid retraction); vision loss from corneal breakdown or optic nerve compression


Plan
All Medications: thyroid supplements; methylcellulose artificial tears; ophthalmic ointment; systemic corticosteroids (as possible treatment); orbital radiation (not a medication)
Follow-Up Instructions: Close ophthalmologic supervision with regular monitoring of visual acuity, color vision, visual fields, and fundus for signs of optic neuropathy; monitor corneal status for epithelial breakdown and treat promptly (e.g., tarsorrhaphy if severe); coordinate care with endocrinology and other specialists; observe throughout active inflammatory period with follow-up visits until stabilization and during chronic phase for potential surgical planning.
Patient Education: Stop smoking; maintain thyroid treatment and follow endocrinology. Use lubricating artificial tears during the day and ointment at night; tape lids or use humidified goggles at night if exposure; elevate head of bed and sleep partially upright. Wear dark glasses for photophobia. For diplopia, patch one eye or use prisms as needed. Seek urgent care for severe exposure keratopathy, worsening vision, or signs of optic neuropathy. Follow regularly with ophthalmology for monitoring during active disease.
Referrals: Endocrinology (thyroid management) and ophthalmology (orbital/strabismus specialist); multidisciplinary team including oculoplastics and possible neuro-ophthalmology as needed.
Emergency Instructions: Seek immediate ophthalmology care for severe exposure keratopathy, severe proptosis or globe luxation, sudden vision decrease, signs of optic neuropathy (worsening visual acuity, color vision loss, new visual field defects), or severe orbital pain—these require prompt aggressive therapy (e.g., high‑dose steroids, orbital decompression, or radiation).


CASE 73 — Anisocoria—Tonic Pupil
================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Anisocoria
DURATION: 2 weeks
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "it could be due to a brain tumor."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 45‑year‑old woman noted to have one pupil larger than the other by a friend at a cocktail party 2 weeks earlier; she denied eye pain, visual symptoms, eye drop use, head or eye trauma, and other illnesses, had occasional headaches for several years, and sought evaluation including a normal brain MRI by a neurosurgeon.
History Narrative: A 45-year-old woman was evaluated for anisocoria after a friend noted one pupil larger than the other at a cocktail party 2 weeks earlier. She complained of occasional headaches over several years but denied other illnesses, significant head or eye trauma, use of any eye drops, or any visual symptoms. A neurosurgeon obtained a normal brain MRI and suggested she might need a cerebral angiogram. On exam visual acuity was 20/20 OU, color vision and visual fields were normal. In darkness the right pupil measured 6 mm and the left 3 mm. The right pupil reacted poorly and segmentally to light and showed vermiform movements of one iris sector on slit‑lamp exam; it slowly constricted to near and slowly redilated on distance fixation. The left pupil reacted briskly and symmetrically to light and constricted briskly to near with quick redilation at distance. Motility was normal and there was no ptosis. Fundus exam was normal.
Symptom Onset: 2 weeks ago
Symptom Duration: 2 weeks
Characteristics: Poor pupillary light reaction; vermiform (segmental) iris movements to light; segmental sphincter palsy; tonic near response with light–near dissociation; cholinergic supersensitivity to dilute pilocarpine; accommodative paresis (with possible induced near astigmatism and ciliary cramp).


Subjective (cont.)
Alleviating Factors: Reassurance
Associated Symptoms: occasional headaches, no visual symptoms, no ptosis, no motility deficits
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes reported
Prior Treatments: Magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) of the brain (normal)
Impact on Daily Activities: None/No impact noted
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: Idiopathic (Adie’s syndrome); local ocular/orbital lesions (trauma affecting the ciliary ganglion or nerve); diffuse neuropathic processes; prior ocular exposure to parasympathomimetic agents (rare)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 76 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: No ptosis.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear, no injection
External Exam – Orbit: Normal.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Within normal limits
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: vermiform movements of one sector of the right iris; segmental sphincter palsy; no iris injury noted
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: Phakic, clear lens


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Right pupil 6 mm (poor/segmental reaction), left pupil 3 mm (brisk reaction)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Right pupil reacted poorly and segmentally to light; left pupil reacted briskly and symmetrically to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: The right pupil slowly constricted to near and then slowly redilated on looking in the distance; the left pupil constricted briskly to near and quickly redilated at distance.
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: Right pupil 6 mm in dark, left pupil 3 mm in dark; right pupil poorly and segmentally reactive to light with vermiform movements and light‑near dissociation; tonic near response of right pupil; left pupil briskly reactive.
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion OU, normal motility
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Vermiform movements of one sector of the right iris to light; no iris injury noted.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Normal optic nerves bilaterally
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits (no peripheral retinal pathology)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Isolated, idiopathic tonic (Adie’s) pupil of the right eye causing anisocoria with light-near dissociation and segmental vermiform iris movements; no treatment required other than reassurance and follow-up.
Primary Diagnosis: Isolated, idiopathic tonic pupil
Secondary Diagnoses: Anisocoria; Tonic pupil (Adie’s pupil)
Differential Diagnosis: Third cranial nerve palsy; Pharmacologic mydriasis; Tonic (Adie’s) pupil; Iris structural damage
Severity: Mild
Laterality: Right


Plan
All Medications: Pilocarpine (0.125%), Pilocarpine 1%
Follow-Up Instructions: No treatment other than reassurance; consider unequal bifocal or unilateral frosted bifocal for near tasks if accommodative paresis persists. Reexamine periodically — note that pupil may shrink over time and fellow eye may become involved (~4%/yr). If bilateral pupils develop, obtain serologic testing for syphilis.
Patient Education: This is an isolated (idiopathic) tonic (Adie’s) pupil: a benign cause of one dilated pupil due to loss of parasympathetic input to the iris. It commonly causes a poor light response, slow near response, and sometimes mild difficulty with focusing or glare. No treatment is usually required—reassurance is appropriate. Reading aids (unequal bifocals or a frosted segment) can be used if near vision is bothersome. The affected pupil often improves or becomes smaller over time; rarely the other eye can become involved. If symptoms change (pain, double vision, drooping eyelid, sudden vision loss) or new systemic symptoms occur (fainting, numbness), seek prompt medical evaluation.


CASE 74 — Anisocoria—Horner’s Syndrome
======================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Left frontotemporal and periorbital headaches and drooping of the left upper eyelid for 3 weeks
DURATION: 3 weeks
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "some drooping of his left upper eyelid"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 50-year-old man with a history of hypertension presented with 3 weeks of left frontotemporal and periorbital headaches and noted drooping of his left upper eyelid; he denied visual complaints, diplopia, head or neck trauma, facial numbness or weakness, or other neurologic symptoms.
History Narrative: A 50-year-old man with a history of hypertension presented with a 3-week history of left frontotemporal and periorbital headaches and reported drooping of his left upper eyelid. He denied visual complaints, diplopia, head or neck trauma, facial numbness or weakness, or other neurologic symptoms.
Symptom Onset: 3 weeks
Symptom Duration: 3 weeks
Progression: Progressive over 3 weeks (gradual onset of headaches and worsening left ptosis over 3 weeks)
Characteristics: Anisocoria greater in the dark, left miosis with 2 mm left ptosis, poor dilation of left pupil with cocaine and hydroxyamphetamine (postganglionic Horner’s), left frontotemporal/periorbital headache, normal vision and motility.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: Left frontotemporal and periorbital headache, left upper‑eyelid drooping (ptosis)
Previous Episodes: No prior similar episodes reported
Prior Treatments: Coumadin for 3 months, then maintained on aspirin
Impact on Daily Activities: Minimal to none
Systemic Symptoms: Hypertension
Risk Factors: Hypertension


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 140/86 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: 15 mmHg — Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: 2 mm left upper eyelid ptosis, mild upside-down ptosis on left; right lids/lashes normal.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear, no injection
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Mild left periorbital fullness with 2 mm left upper eyelid ptosis; globe position normal, no proptosis or enophthalmos.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Unremarkable
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: miosis (left) with poor dilation on pharmacologic testing; otherwise iris unremarkable
Anterior Segment – Lens: unremarkable


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Right 4 mm (bright), 5 mm (dark); Left 3 mm (bright and dark) — pupils equal shape, round.
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Both pupils reacted well to light and near
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Both pupils reacted well to near.
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: Both pupils react well to light and near; no RAPD; anisocoria greater in the dark (right 5 mm, left 3 mm); pupils 4 mm right / 3 mm left in bright light; left pupil poorly dilates with cocaine and hydroxyamphetamine.
Extraocular Movements: Full motility in all directions; extraocular movements intact.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: unremarkable
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Unremarkable
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 50-year-old man with 3 weeks of left frontotemporal/periorbital headache and 2 mm left ptosis. Visual acuity 20/20 OU, color vision normal, pupils 4 mm OD and 3 mm OS in light, 5 mm OD and 3 mm OS in dark; pupils react to light and near, no RAPD. Cocaine 10%: marked dilation OD, poor dilation OS, confirming Horner syndrome. Hydroxyamphetamine 1%: full dilation OD, poor dilation OS, localizing to a postganglionic (third-order) Horner syndrome. MR angiography showed a dissecting aneurysm of the high cervical internal carotid artery. Diagnosis: postganglionic Horner syndrome due to spontaneous internal carotid artery dissection. Managed with anticoagulation (3 months of warfarin) then aspirin; no surgical intervention indicated.
Primary Diagnosis: Postganglionic Horner’s syndrome due to spontaneous dissection of the internal carotid artery
Secondary Diagnoses: Hypertension
Differential Diagnosis: Horner’s syndrome (postganglionic due to internal carotid artery dissection); Physiologic anisocoria (pseudo‑Horner’s from levator dehiscence); Cavernous sinus process (thrombosis, tumor, or infection)
Severity: Mild
Laterality: Left


Plan
All Medications: hydroxyamphetamine 1% (Paredrine); topical cocaine 10%; Coumadin (warfarin) for 3 months; aspirin
All Procedures: Topical cocaine 10% pupillary testing; Hydroxyamphetamine 1% (Paredrine) pupillary testing; MR angiography of the carotid artery; Anticoagulation with warfarin (Coumadin) for 3 months; Aspirin maintenance thereafter
Follow-Up Instructions: Close observation with serial evaluation to monitor for development of other neurologic or medical signs or symptoms; pursue appropriate imaging and specialist follow‑up as indicated by clinical course (e.g., repeat neuroimaging/vascular follow‑up for carotid pathology)
Patient Education: You have Horner’s syndrome from a tear (dissection) in the internal carotid artery on the left side. This causes a small pupil and mild drooping of the upper eyelid on that side and can be associated with head/neck pain. The eye problem itself usually does not require treatment, but the underlying carotid dissection is potentially serious because it can reduce blood flow to the brain or cause stroke. Typical care includes blood-thinning treatment (anticoagulation initially, often followed by antiplatelet therapy) and close follow-up with your vascular/neurology team and repeat imaging to make sure the artery heals. Watch for new symptoms — sudden weakness, numbness, trouble speaking, sudden vision loss, severe worsening headache, fainting, or new double vision — and seek emergency care if any occur. Keep scheduled follow-ups and imaging appointments, report new or worsening eye or neurologic symptoms promptly, and discuss activity restrictions and medication side effects (bleeding risk) with your treating physicians.


CASE 75 — Anisocoria—Eye Drops
==============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: headaches and anisocoria
DURATION: several years (worse over the last few months)
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "blurry"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 24-year-old student nurse with several years of daily, diffuse headaches that worsened over the past few months; associated longstanding blurred vision (worse with headaches); noted anisocoria by neurologist leading to ophthalmology referral; denies diplopia, nausea, vomiting; on medications only for headaches.
History Narrative: A 24-year-old student nurse was referred for long-standing headaches, blurred vision, and anisocoria. The headaches had been present for several years but were worse over the last few months; they occurred daily, were diffuse, and not associated with nausea or vomiting. Because of the headaches she saw a neurologist who noted anisocoria and sent her for ophthalmologic examination. She reported that her vision had always been “blurry,” especially with her headaches. She denied diplopia or other eye problems. She was taking medications only for her headaches. Exam revealed visual acuity 20/20 bilaterally, color vision 10/10 bilaterally, full visual fields, pupils 8 mm right and 4 mm left with the right pupil nonreactive to light or near and the left pupil reacting briskly, no relative afferent pupillary defect, normal motility, no ptosis, normal facial sensation and strength, slit-lamp showing smoothly round pupils without segmental contraction or iris irregularity, and a normal fundus without disc swelling.
Symptom Onset: Several years (worse over the last few months)
Symptom Duration: Several years, worse over the last few months
Progression: Stable/gradual chronic with recent worsening (headaches worse over last few months)
Characteristics: Unilateral large, smoothly dilated right pupil (8 mm) that is nonreactive to light or near, without ptosis or motility deficits — consistent with pharmacologic mydriasis.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Headaches (worse over the last few months)
Associated Symptoms: Headaches
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes reported
Prior Treatments: Medications only for her headaches
Impact on Daily Activities: Mild — headaches and intermittent blurry vision but able to perform daily activities (no diplopia, normal acuity 20/20, full fields)
Systemic Symptoms: Headache (daily, diffuse, worse over months), blurred vision; denies nausea, vomiting, diplopia, other systemic symptoms.
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: Health care workers (nurses, physicians) exposed to topical mydriatics or anticholinergic/adrenergic agents
Risk Factors: Health-care worker (student nurse) with potential inadvertent exposure to topical/mydriatic agents (e.g., anticholinergics, adrenergic agents)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/80 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: white and quiet
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis or enophthalmos; orbit exam within normal limits.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea, no abnormalities noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Pupils smoothly round without irregularities; right pupil dilated to 8 mm, left 4 mm; no segmental contraction of right pupil; no visible structural iris abnormality.
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear crystalline lens bilaterally


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Right 8 mm (nonreactive), Left 4 mm (briskly reactive)

Pupils – Reaction to Near: Right pupil did not react to near; left pupil reacted briskly to near
Pupils – RAPD: No relative afferent pupillary defect documented
Pupils – Other: Right 8 mm nonreactive; Left 4 mm briskly reactive

Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Pharmacologic mydriasis of the right eye causing unilateral large, nonreactive pupil. Findings (8 mm right pupil, 4 mm left; right pupil nonreactive to light and near; normal motility, no ptosis, smooth round iris without segmental contraction; full visual acuity and fields; normal fundus) argue against third nerve palsy, tonic pupil, or iris damage. Pilocarpine testing showed no constriction, consistent with pharmacologic blockade. Recommend reassurance, counseling regarding possible exposures, and follow-up to document resolution.
Primary Diagnosis: Pharmacologic mydriasis
Differential Diagnosis: Right third nerve palsy; Tonic (Adie) pupil; Iris structural damage; Pharmacologic mydriasis
Severity: Mild
Laterality: Right
Complications: None


Plan
All Medications: medications only for her headaches
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up to ensure resolution of the symptoms.
Patient Education: Reassure that the enlarged, nonreactive pupil is due to medication exposure (pharmacologic mydriasis), not nerve damage; avoid touching eyes after handling eye-care products or medications that can dilate the pupil (eg, anticholinergics, decongestant sprays, scopolamine); expect gradual return to normal pupil size without treatment over days to weeks; seek follow-up if symptoms worsen, vision changes persist, new eyelid droop or double vision develop, or the pupil does not improve.
Work Restrictions: None (no restrictions)
Emergency Instructions: Return to clinic or emergency department if new neurologic symptoms develop (worsening headache, double vision, ptosis, weakness, numbness, or sudden vision loss). Otherwise no urgent treatment; follow-up to ensure pupil returns to normal.


CASE 76 — Leukocoria
====================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Leukocoria
DURATION: 3 months
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "funny"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: The family noted a white “funny” pupil in the left eye for 3 months in a previously healthy 9‑month‑old girl; born 2 weeks premature, birth weight 6 lb 8 oz. The pediatrician detected leukocoria and referred urgently. The child fixes and follows with the right eye but not the left and strongly objects to occlusion of the right eye. No other systemic symptoms, no systemic disease, no animal exposures, and no family history of childhood ocular disease.
History Narrative: The family of a 9-month-old girl noted that her left eye has looked “funny” for the last 3 months. The pediatrician detected leukocoria and immediately referred her to an ophthalmologist. The child is otherwise healthy with negative review of systems except for the white pupil. She was born 2 weeks prematurely with a birth weight of 6 lb., 8 oz. There is no history of systemic disease or animal exposure, and no family history of childhood ocular disease. On examination the right eye appeared normal though only the posterior pole could be seen on retinal exam. The left eye had an obvious white, vascular lesion with a large surrounding serous retinal detachment. The child fixes and follows well with her right eye but does not fix or follow with her left eye; strong objection to occlusion of the right eye was noted.
Symptom Onset: 3 months prior to presentation
Symptom Duration: 3 months
Progression: Progression: Bilateral retinoblastoma with left eye advanced (massive tumor with large serous retinal detachment and vitreous seeding) and smaller tumor in the right eye; left eye recommended for enucleation; right eye managed with medical/local therapy and close surveillance.
Characteristics: Bilateral retinoblastoma: left eye large vascular intraocular tumor with massive exudative/serous retinal detachment and vitreous seeding; right eye small inferior retinal tumor; leukocoria; patient 9‑month‑old, no family history; optic nerve tumor extension risk.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: White pupil (leukocoria), failure to fix or follow with left eye, strabismus (implied by objection to occlusion of right eye)
Previous Episodes: None
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant — monocular vision with loss of the left eye; impacts depth perception and may limit activities requiring binocular vision; requires protective measures and long‑term follow‑up.
Systemic Symptoms: Negative review of systems except for the white pupil (no systemic symptoms)
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Hereditary RB1 gene mutation (possible given bilateral disease), prematurity (born 2 weeks early), family history negative (no family history) — main risk: germline RB1 mutation (hereditary retinoblastoma)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Pulse: 120 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 24 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: Fixes and follows with right eye
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: NLP (no light perception)
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: No fixation with left eye (no measurable visual acuity)
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: Fixes and follows with right eye; does not fix or follow with left eye.
Visual Acuity – Method: Fix and follow (behavioral testing)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 28 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Tonopen (applanation tonometry)


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Clear and unremarkable
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal appearing iris (no abnormality noted)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear anterior lens (no cataract)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Left: white/leukocoric pupil (abnormal, nonreactive); Right: normal size and round
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Right eye: fixes and follows well — likely briskly reactive; Left eye: does not fix or follow and has leukocoria — likely nonreactive to light.
Pupils – RAPD: Left afferent pupillary defect (RAPD)
Pupils – Other: Left eye: leukocoria (white pupillary reflex); right eye: normal pupillary appearance
Extraocular Movements: Full extraocular movements intact bilaterally
Fundus Exam – Macula: Not visualized due to large left retinal mass and serous retinal detachment
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal vessels in both eyes
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Nasal and inferior peripheral retina of the left eye obscured by large vascular tumor with extensive serous (exudative) retinal detachment; small tumor in the inferior peripheral retina of the right eye.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Bilateral retinoblastoma: large, vascular left intraocular mass with surrounding massive serous retinal detachment and vitreous seeding, and a smaller tumor in the inferior retina of the right eye. Left eye has no visual fixation and poor prognosis—recommend enucleation with long optic nerve segment to assess for optic nerve invasion. Right eye has smaller tumor amenable to focal and/or chemotherapy (systemic and/or intra-arterial/periocular/intravitreal as indicated) with close monitoring. MRI performed to evaluate for extraocular or CNS extension; genetic testing for RB1 germline mutation and counseling recommended. Close follow-up with examinations under anesthesia every 3–4 months until age 5 (more frequently if germline mutation), long-term surveillance for secondary malignancies, and ocular prosthesis/monocular protection and rehabilitation planning.
Primary Diagnosis: Bilateral retinoblastoma
Differential Diagnosis: Retinoblastoma; Coats’ disease; Persistent fetal vasculature (PFV); Toxocariasis
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: Secondary malignancies (soft‑tissue and bony sarcomas), extraocular/orbital recurrence including optic nerve invasion and metastasis, vision loss/monocular vision, socket complications post‑enucleation, recurrence in contralateral eye, treatment‑related complications from chemotherapy/radiation (local tissue damage, vitreoretinal toxicity)


Plan
All Procedures: examination under anesthesia; magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) of the head and orbits; ultrasound (B‑scan and A‑scan); genetic testing/counseling for RB1; enucleation (left eye) with long optic nerve segment; chemotherapy (intravenous, intra‑arterial, periocular, intravitreal as indicated); focal therapies: laser therapy, cryotherapy, plaque (radiation) therapy; intravitreal chemotherapy; pathologic examination of enucleated eye
Follow-Up Instructions: - Examine enucleated socket at each visit when examining the contralateral eye. - If RB1 germline mutation present: EUA every 3–4 months until age 5, then continued frequent exams for several years and at least annual eye exams thereafter. - Monitor long-term for secondary malignancies (soft-tissue and bony sarcomas); provide long-term follow-up. - Protect remaining eye (polycarbonate safety glasses) and provide education on eye safety.
Patient Education: Urgent evaluation and treatment are needed for leukocoria because it can signal retinoblastoma, a potentially life‑threatening eye tumor. Recommended steps: exam under anesthesia and MRI of the brain and orbits to assess both eyes and look for extraocular or CNS disease; genetic testing for RB1 to guide prognosis, family screening, and surveillance. Treatment options include chemotherapy (intravenous, intra‑arterial, periocular, or intravitreal as indicated), focal therapies (laser, cryotherapy, plaque radiotherapy), or enucleation for an eye with extensive tumor and no visual potential. Follow‑up: frequent EUA and exams (every 3–4 months until age 5 if germline RB1 mutation, then continued periodic surveillance and annual exams thereafter) to monitor for recurrence and contralateral disease. Long‑term: patients with hereditary retinoblastoma have increased risk of secondary malignancies (especially sarcomas) and need lifelong surveillance; protect the seeing eye (polycarbonate safety glasses) and provide education on eye safety. Family members should be examined and offered genetic counseling/testing.
Referrals: Genetic counseling and testing; pediatric oncology for chemotherapy planning; oculoplastic/orbital surgeon for enucleation; pediatric radiology for MRI; ophthalmology for examination under anesthesia and long-term follow-up
Emergency Instructions: Urgent ophthalmologic evaluation (exam under anesthesia) and MRI of the head and orbits to assess for intraocular tumor extent and extraocular spread; prompt genetic testing and counseling; arrange oncology/retinoblastoma treatment (chemotherapy ± focal therapy or enucleation) and close follow-up with frequent EUA every 3–4 months if germline RB1 mutation.


CASE 77 — The Child Who Sees Poorly Out of One Eye
==================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: The Child Who Sees Poorly Out of One Eye
DURATION: Several weeks to months
LATERALITY: right
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 4-year-old boy who failed a preschool vision screening confirmed by his pediatrician and referred for ophthalmologic evaluation. He is otherwise healthy with no prior medical or eye problems, no history of eye injury, strabismus, spectacle wear, squinting, or family history of amblyopia or childhood eye disease. Technician testing showed 20/40 OU by Allen figures; HOTV testing by ophthalmologist showed 20/50 OD and 20/30 OS. Stereoacuity 60" arc (Titmus). Motility: orthotropia at distance, 4Δ exophoria at near. Cycloplegic refraction after 1% cyclopentolate: +3.00 OD, +1.00 OS.
History Narrative: A 4-year-old boy failed his preschool vision screening, and the failure was confirmed by his pediatrician; he was referred for ophthalmologic evaluation. He is a robust 4-year-old with no prior medical or ophthalmologic problems. Review of systems is negative — specifically no history of eye injury, strabismus, spectacle wear, squinting, or other ophthalmologic problems. He has not previously had an ophthalmologic examination and there is no family history of amblyopia or childhood eye disease.
Progression: Improved with spectacle correction; may require patching or penalization if vision remains reduced.
Characteristics: Anisometropic amblyopia in the right eye due to uncorrected hyperopia; right eye cycloplegic refraction +3.00, left eye +1.00; visual acuity OD 20/50, OS 20/30; orthotropic at distance with 4Δ exophoria at near; stereoacuity 60″ on Titmus; age 4 years.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: Negative (no history of eye injury, strabismus, spectacle wear, squinting, or other ophthalmologic problems; review of systems negative)
Previous Episodes: None
Prior Treatments: None
Impact on Daily Activities: Mild — some difficulty with near/fine visual tasks but able to function with corrective treatment; may affect preschool activities and learning until treated.
Systemic Symptoms: Negative
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Anisometropia (unequal hyperopic refractive error), uncorrected high hyperopia in the right eye, age (young child during critical visual development), lack of prior ophthalmic evaluation, failure of vision screening.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Pulse: 92 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 20 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/50
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/50
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Method: HOTV acuity test
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Not measured (tonometry not performed)


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal.
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal examination of the orbits (no abnormalities noted)
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear, normal appearing cornea
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear (no lens opacity)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils equal, round, reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk and equal
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Pupils – Other: Pupils equal and reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full ductions and versions; orthotropia at distance, 4Δ exophoria at near
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Normal anterior segment; no media opacities observed (clear corneas, quiet conjunctiva, deep/quiet anterior chambers, normal-appearing lenses)
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Anisometropic amblyopia of the right eye due to uncorrected hyperopia (cycloplegic refraction +3.00 OD, +1.00 OS) with reduced best-recognized acuity OD (HOTV 20/50 OD vs 20/30 OS); orthotropia at distance, 4Δ exophoria at near; plan to initiate full (or symmetrically reduced) hyperopic spectacle correction, monitor for improvement, and institute amblyopia therapy (patching or atropine penalization) if acuity does not improve with glasses alone.
Primary Diagnosis: Anisometropic amblyopia in the right eye due to uncorrected hyperopia
Secondary Diagnoses: Anisometropia (hyperopic anisometropia: OD +3.00, OS +1.00)
Differential Diagnosis: Anisometropic (refractive) amblyopia; Strabismic amblyopia; Deprivational amblyopia (media opacity/ptosis); Poor effort/functional visual loss
Severity: Mild
Laterality: Right eye


Plan
All Medications: 1% cyclopentolate; atropine (topical)
All Procedures: Cycloplegic refraction with 1% cyclopentolate Visual acuity testing (Allen figures, HOTV acuity, Titmus stereo) Ocular motility evaluation (ductions/versions, phoria assessment) Spectacle prescription/optical correction of hyperopia Atropine cycloplegia/optical penalization (medication instillation) Occlusion therapy (patching of sound eye) Follow-up ophthalmologic examinations
Follow-Up Instructions: Ophthalmologic follow-up examinations as needed to monitor vision and refractive error, with repeat visits several weeks to months after starting spectacles to assess for improvement; continue monitoring for recurrence (may require maintenance patching until age 7–8 years) and update prescriptions as the child’s refractive error changes.
Patient Education: Explain that the child’s poorer vision in the right eye is due to amblyopia from unequal farsightedness (anisometropic hyperopia). Treatment goals are to improve clarity in the affected eye and encourage use of that eye to restore vision during the critical period of visual development. Key points: - Full or slightly reduced glasses prescription will be provided to correct the refractive error; glasses may sometimes be started after a brief course of atropine to aid adaptation. - If vision does not improve adequately with glasses alone, patching the good eye for prescribed hours per day or pharmacologic penalization (atropine drops in the good eye) will be recommended to force use of the amblyopic eye. - Regular follow-up visits are essential to monitor vision, adjust glasses, and assess response to treatment; treatment often takes weeks to months. - Consistent adherence to glasses and patching/atropine is critical—partial or inconsistent use reduces the chance of improvement. - Vision improvements can be maintained but can relapse as the child grows; maintenance therapy and periodic refractions are needed until about age 7–8 years (or as advised). - Warn about possible patching side effects (skin irritation, initial resistance) and atropine effects (light sensitivity, temporary near blur) and provide strategies to improve cooperation (short, scheduled patching times, positive reinforcement, stickers, supervised activities). - Encourage eye-healthy behaviors: wearing prescribed glasses at all times, attending all follow-up appointments, and prompt reporting of any redness, pain, or vision change.


CASE 78 — Childhood Torticollis
===============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Anomalous left head tilt since infancy.
DURATION: Since infancy (since he first gained head control during the first year of life)
LATERALITY: Right
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 5-year-old boy with a lifelong, relatively constant left head tilt noted since infancy (since he gained head control in the first year of life), resistant to attempts to straighten it; over the past 2 months parents report intermittent episodes where his eyes do not move together; associated frequent eye rubbing and blinking; no neck muscle abnormalities on orthopedic evaluation; family history unremarkable.
History Narrative: A 5-year-old boy is brought to his ophthalmologist’s office at the request of his pediatrician for evaluation of an anomalous head tilt. The child has undergone orthopaedic evaluation and neck muscle abnormalities are absent. The child has had a relatively constant left head tilt since he first gained head control during the first year of life. His parents note that he strongly resists any attempts to straighten his head. During the last 2 months, they have noticed that his eyes sometimes do not appear to move together. Family history is unremarkable and the review of systems is notable only for frequent eye rubbing and blinking behavior.
Symptom Onset: Since first gained head control during the first year of life (infancy)
Symptom Duration: Since infancy (approximately since first year of life)
Progression: Chronic
Characteristics: Right superior oblique palsy with chronic left head tilt and mild facial asymmetry.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Attempts to straighten his head
Associated Symptoms: frequent eye rubbing and blinking behavior
Prior Treatments: None reported
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — avoids straightening head, occasional diplopia/eyes not moving together, frequent eye rubbing/blinking but fixes and follows well; may have mild functional limitations and cosmetic facial asymmetry.
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Infantile onset (congenital) superior oblique palsy leading to chronic head tilt; history of childhood torticollis; possible facial asymmetry from long-standing head tilt.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Pulse: 96
Vitals – Temperature: 37.0 °C
Vitals – Respirations: 20 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Method: Fix and follow
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal external examination except for mild facial asymmetry (left side smaller) and a 10–15° constant left head tilt.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Normal
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open.
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear natural lenses (no cataract)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Normal
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk and symmetric
Pupils – RAPD: No RAPD detected
Pupils – Other: Normal
Extraocular Movements: Full in primary; limited elevation of the right eye in adduction with moderate overelevation (inferior oblique overaction) on dextroversion.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Anterior segment and pupillary examination are normal.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Mild excyclotorsion of the left fundus; peripheral retina otherwise normal (no lesions)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Right superior oblique palsy with chronic left head tilt and mild facial asymmetry.
Primary Diagnosis: Right superior oblique palsy with chronic left head tilt and mild facial asymmetry
Differential Diagnosis: Superior oblique palsy; Brown’s syndrome; Dissociated vertical deviation; Sternocleidomastoid (nonocular) torticollis
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right
Complications: - Over- or under-correction after surgery with possible need for additional surgery - Development of postoperative Brown’s syndrome (limitation of elevation in adduction) - Amblyopia (preexisting or developing if misalignment persists) - Diplopia or persistent anomalous head posture if surgery unsuccessful


Plan
All Procedures: Inferior oblique myectomy or recession; superior oblique tuck; possible combination surgery on superior and inferior oblique and rectus muscles; vertical prism therapy (non-surgical)
Follow-Up Instructions: Periodic ophthalmologic evaluations with attention to fusion, stereopsis, and visual acuity to detect, prevent, and treat amblyopia; monitor for over- or under-correction after surgery and for postoperative Brown’s syndrome, with additional surgeries as needed.
Patient Education: The child’s head tilt is caused by a weakness of the right superior oblique eye muscle (a type of ocular torticollis). Because this likely developed early in life, it has caused the child to adopt a persistent left head tilt and mild facial asymmetry. Many children with mild superior oblique palsy do well without surgery because they can fuse and maintain single vision; if symptoms are minimal, no treatment may be needed. Small vertical prisms can help with minor vertical misalignment but are usually limited to about 4–5 prism diopters. Surgery is offered to improve head posture, fusion, or double vision and typically involves weakening the opposite (inferior oblique) muscle, tightening the superior oblique, or a combination; surgery may require future adjustments and can sometimes cause temporary limitation of upgaze in adduction (a Brown’s syndrome) that often improves over time. If onset is longstanding and congenital-appearing, neuroimaging is not usually required; if onset is unclear or suspected to be acquired, brain and orbital imaging should be obtained. After treatment, regular follow-up is important to monitor vision, binocular fusion, and to detect or treat amblyopia early.
Referrals: Neuroimaging of brain and orbit if acquired palsy suspected or timing unclear; pediatric orthopedics previously evaluated (no neck abnormality)


CASE 79 — Aniridia
==================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: "jiggling eyes" and large pupils noticed during the first month of life
DURATION: 9 months
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: “jiggling eyes”
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 9-month-old boy referred for “jiggling eyes” and large pupils noted during the first month of life. Parents report photophobia (closes eyes in bright sunlight) and apparently reduced vision compared with siblings. Born at term with routine prenatal care and no perinatal complications. Medical history notable for hypospadias repair 2 months earlier and developmental delay. No family history of ophthalmologic or medical problems; parents and two older siblings have normal eye exams. On exam he fixates and follows but with abnormal fixation and a low-amplitude/high-frequency horizontal pendular nystagmus; peripheral corneal opacification with vascularization, near-total iris hypoplasia with only small peripheral remnant, and bilateral anterior pyramidal cataracts; fundus shows absence of an obvious foveal umbo/foveal pigmentation (foveal hypoplasia). Genetic testing shows 11p13 deletion.
History Narrative: A 9-month-old boy referred for “jiggling eyes” and large pupils noted during the first month of life. He closes his eyes in bright sunlight and appears to have poorer vision than his two older siblings. Born at term with prenatal care and no perinatal complications. Medical history notable for hypospadias repair 2 months earlier and developmental delay. No family history of ophthalmologic or medical problems; two healthy parents and two healthy siblings. On exam he fix-and-follows with abnormal fixation, low-amplitude/high-frequency horizontal pendular nystagmus, peripheral corneal opacification and vascularization, severe iris hypoplasia with only a small peripheral remnant, bilateral anterior pyramidal cataracts, and absent foveal umbo/foveal pigmentation on retinal exam. Parents and siblings have normal ophthalmologic exams. An 11p13 deletion is present.
Symptom Onset: First month of life
Symptom Duration: Since first month of life (present at 9 months)
Progression: Progressive corneal opacification, vascularization, cataract formation, and possible development of glaucoma leading to future vision loss
Characteristics: Panocular congenital malformation with near-complete iris hypoplasia/aniridia, foveal hypoplasia causing reduced visual acuity and infantile nystagmus, associated anterior segment abnormalities (corneal opacification/vascularization, anterior pyramidal cataracts), risk of glaucoma and ectopia lentis, photosensitivity (photophobia); sporadic form here with 11p13 deletion associated with developmental delay and genitourinary anomaly (WAGR syndrome) and high risk of Wilms’ tumor.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Bright sunlight (photophobia)
Alleviating Factors: Wearing sunglasses or shaded contact lenses
Associated Symptoms: Photophobia, nystagmus (horizontal pendular), reduced visual behavior/fixation (poor vision), developmental delay, hypospadias (genitourinary abnormality)
Previous Episodes: None known
Prior Treatments: Hypospadias repair 2 months earlier
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant impairment — photophobia, nystagmus, foveal hypoplasia and cataracts cause reduced visual acuity and abnormal fixation, limiting visual function and requiring protective measures (sunglasses/filters), low-vision support, and early educational intervention.
Systemic Symptoms: Developmental delay; history of hypospadias (genitourinary abnormality); increased risk for Wilms' tumor (renal mass potential)
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: No known contacts with similar symptoms.
Risk Factors: Sporadic 11p13 deletion (WAGR syndrome) with associated genitourinary abnormality (hypospadias), developmental delay — high risk for Wilms’ tumor; family history negative (sporadic)

Fundus Exam – Periphery: No peripheral abnormalities noted

Assessment Narrative: Sporadic aniridia in an infant with associated developmental delay and genitourinary abnormality (hypospadias), consistent with WAGR syndrome; 11p13 deletion present. High risk for Wilms tumor; panocular involvement with bilateral severe iris hypoplasia/aniridia, corneal opacification/vascularization, anterior pyramidal cataracts, foveal hypoplasia, and infantile horizontal pendular nystagmus. Recommend genetic and pediatric oncology consultation, regular abdominal ultrasounds through age ~3 years, periodic ophthalmic surveillance (including glaucoma monitoring), low-vision evaluation, and consideration of nonsurgical treatments for photophobia (sunglasses/shaded contact lenses).

Secondary Diagnoses: Hypospadias (repaired); developmental delay; foveal hypoplasia; corneal opacification/vascularization; anterior pyramidal cataracts; nystagmus; 11p13 deletion (WAGR association; high risk for Wilms tumor)
Differential Diagnosis: Aniridia (with foveal hypoplasia and congenital nystagmus); Albinism (oculocutaneous or ocular) causing foveal hypoplasia and nystagmus; Congenital cataract with sensory nystagmus; Anterior segment dysgenesis (Axenfeld-Rieger spectrum)
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: progressive corneal opacification and vascularization; progressive cataracts; glaucoma; foveal hypoplasia leading to reduced visual acuity and nystagmus; risk of Wilms’ tumor (WAGR syndrome)


Plan
All Procedures: hypospadias repair
Follow-Up Instructions: Periodic ophthalmologic screening examinations to monitor for progressive corneal opacification, cataract, and glaucoma; regular abdominal ultrasounds until ~3 years of age (with pediatrician coordination) and abdominal physical exams to screen for Wilms’ tumor, with MRI if ultrasound abnormality is seen and oncology referral/biopsy if suspicious; early childhood educational intervention and low-vision evaluation.
Patient Education: Recommend sunglasses or shaded contact lenses for photophobia; educate parents about risk of progressive corneal opacification, cataract, and glaucoma and need for regular ophthalmologic screening (including glaucoma checks); arrange periodic abdominal ultrasounds until ~3 years of age with prompt MRI/oncology referral if renal abnormalities suggest Wilms tumor; refer for genetic counseling; initiate early childhood educational intervention and low-vision evaluation.
Referrals: Geneticist; Pediatrician; Pediatric oncologist (if Wilms’ tumor confirmed)


CASE 80 — Retinopathy of Prematurity
====================================
Subjective

DURATION: 11 weeks
LATERALITY: Both eyes
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 11-week-old former 24-week estimated gestational age premature female, birth weight 650 g, presented for routine ROP screening. Also has anemia of prematurity and had necrotizing enterocolitis 4 weeks ago. Blinks briskly to light; fundus exam now shows dilation and tortuosity of posterior pole vessels consistent with plus disease in zone I.
History Narrative: An 11-week-old, former 24-week estimated gestational age premature girl presented for consultation. She is due for another retinopathy of prematurity (ROP) screening examination. Since this last examination, she has developed sepsis and respiratory compromise, requiring intravenous antibiotics and re-intubation/ventilatory support. Her birth weight was 650 g. She has anemia of prematurity and had an episode of necrotizing enterocolitis 4 weeks ago. Examination reveals a frail-appearing infant girl who is intubated. She is receiving 50% oxygen. She blinks briskly to the light of the indirect ophthalmoscope with either eye. On fundus examination, she is found to have dilation and tortuosity of the posterior pole retinal vessels.
Symptom Onset: 1 week ago
Symptom Duration: 1 week
Progression: Progression from immature retina in zone I to development of plus disease (type I ROP) within 1 week
Characteristics: Type I retinopathy of prematurity in both eyes: zone I location with plus disease (posterior pole vessel dilation and tortuosity), stage 3 features (severe ROP/ROP with neovascularization) in an 11‑week‑old former 24‑week GA female infant, birth weight 650 g, intubated, on 50% oxygen, with sepsis, anemia of prematurity, and prior necrotizing enterocolitis.

Associated Symptoms: sepsis, respiratory compromise (requiring re‑intubation/ventilatory support), anemia of prematurity, necrotizing enterocolitis, frailty, poor pupillary dilation (possible), brisk blink to light
Previous Episodes: 1 (last exam 1 week ago showed immature retina in zone 1)
Systemic Symptoms: sepsis, respiratory compromise requiring re-intubation/ventilatory support, anemia of prematurity, necrotizing enterocolitis, frail appearance, receiving 50% supplemental oxygen
Risk Factors: extreme prematurity (low gestational age), low birth weight (650 g), maternal bleeding, prolonged intravenous nutrition, hypocarbia, prolonged ventilation/respiratory compromise and supplemental oxygen, multiple birth status, intraventricular hemorrhage, hypotension, anemia of prematurity, sepsis, necrotizing enterocolitis, unstable clinical course

Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: Fix and follow right
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: Fix and follow
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: Fix and follow

Visual Acuity – Method: Fix and follow (behavioral assessment)

Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear, normal appearing cornea without abnormality noted

Pupils – Size/Shape: Briskly reactive to light; pupillary size and shape within normal limits for age
Pupils – Reaction to Light: blinks briskly to light with either eye

Pupils – RAPD: briskly to light in either eye (no RAPD)
Pupils – Other: briskly reactive to light

Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 11-week-old former 24-week EGA premature female with birth weight 650 g, currently intubated and on 50% oxygen with recent sepsis and NEC history, presenting with zone I retinopathy demonstrating posterior pole venous dilation and arteriolar tortuosity (plus disease) consistent with Type I ROP in both eyes. High risk for progression given extreme prematurity, low birth weight, and unstable clinical course; urgent treatment recommended (e.g., intravitreal anti-VEGF or laser photocoagulation) with close frequent follow-up for regression and possible reactivation.
Primary Diagnosis: Type I retinopathy of prematurity, bilateral
Secondary Diagnoses: Sepsis; Respiratory compromise requiring re-intubation/ventilatory support; Anemia of prematurity; Necrotizing enterocolitis (history)
Differential Diagnosis: Familial exudative vitreoretinopathy; Eales’ disease; Norrie disease
Severity: Type I ROP
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: Retinal detachment; posterior retinal fold; cataract; subretinal hemorrhage; intraretinal hemorrhage; vitreous hemorrhage; severe myopia; amblyopia; strabismus; significant refractive errors (myopia, astigmatism, anisometropia)


Plan
All Medications: bevacizumab, intravenous antibiotics
All Procedures: Retinopathy of prematurity screening examinations (indirect ophthalmoscopic retinal examinations with scleral depression as needed); retinal photographic screening (telemedicine); intravitreal injection of bevacizumab (anti–VEGF therapy); peripheral retinal photocoagulation (laser treatment); cryotherapy
Follow-Up Instructions: Examine frequently until disease regression; for bevacizumab-treated infants, continue screening up to 60–70 weeks postmenstrual age to monitor for late reactivation.
Patient Education: The infant has retinopathy of prematurity (Type I ROP) due to extreme prematurity and low birth weight. This can progress rapidly and may threaten vision; prompt treatment is recommended (laser photocoagulation or intravitreal anti‑VEGF such as bevacizumab). Treatment often causes regression but may have risks (laser: cataract, hemorrhage, higher myopia; anti‑VEGF: unknown long‑term ocular/systemic effects and potential late reactivation). After treatment, frequent eye exams are required until full retinal vascularization (often up to 60–70 weeks postmenstrual age with anti‑VEGF). Premature infants remain at higher risk for amblyopia, strabismus, and significant refractive errors and will need long‑term ophthalmic follow‑up.
Referrals: Pediatric ophthalmology for urgent treatment (laser photocoagulation or intravitreal anti‑VEGF) and ongoing ROP screening/follow‑up; neonatology for stabilization/oxygen management; pediatric infectious disease for sepsis management.


CASE 81 — Childhood Esotropia
=============================

DURATION: 4 years and 10 months
LATERALITY: bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "N/A"

She has never had occlusion therapy; current glasses are at least 1 year old. Parents note a sister with esotropia. Current spectacles: +2.00 OD, +2.50 OS. Visual acuity 20/30 OD, 20/50 OS.

Symptom Duration: Since 2 months of age (patient is 5 years old)

Previous Episodes: Yes — history of crossed eyes since 2 months of age with strabismus surgery at 8 months; alignment recurred recently with intermittent left eye drift.
Prior Treatments: Strabismus surgery at 8 months of age
Impact on Daily Activities: Likely mild–moderate impact (intermittent inward drift, reduced stereoacuity and amblyopia may affect depth perception, reading/near tasks, and activities requiring fine binocular vision)
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: Family history of esotropia; early-onset strabismus (onset at 2 months); history of prior strabismus surgery; uncorrected/undercorrected hyperopia; amblyopia.

Follow-Up Instructions: Follow up in a few months after prescribing new glasses with full cycloplegic refraction to recheck ocular alignment; monitor vision for amblyopia and adjust treatment as needed; perform regular follow-ups throughout childhood (intervals based on angle stability, visual acuity, and age) and consider surgery if residual deviation >10–12 PD at distance despite full correction.
