CASE 82 — Childhood Exotropia
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Intermittent drifting out of the right eye for 2 years, worse at distance and when tired (now outward ~60% of the day)
DURATION: 2 years
LATERALITY: Right eye
PATIENT QUOTES: "drifting out of the right eye" "worse when ... tired or when he is looking at distant objects" "initially noted this deviation approximately two to three times a day" "now deviated outward approximately 60% of the day" "right eye closure in sunlight"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 4-year-old boy with a 2-year history of intermittent outward drifting of the right eye, initially occurring 2–3 times daily but recently worsening to about 60% of the day; worse when tired and when looking at distance, with right eye closure in sunlight; no trauma, fever, medical problems, or family history of strabismus/amblyopia.
History Narrative: A 4-year-old boy with a 2-year history of intermittent drifting out of the right eye. Mother reports the deviation is worse when the child is tired or looking at distant objects. Initially noticed two to three times a day, recently worsening to being outward approximately 60% of the day. No history of head trauma, febrile episode, or other medical problems. No family history of strabismus or amblyopia. Review of systems notable for right eye closure in sunlight.
Symptom Onset: 2 years prior (around age 2)
Symptom Duration: 2 years
Progression: Worsening — increased from occurring 2–3×/day to ~60% of the day (progressive increase in frequency/poorening control)
Characteristics: Intermittent exotropia worse at distance than near (pseudodivergence excess), comitant, intermittent (present ~60% of day), recovery on refixation/blinking, recurs with occlusion, full ductions/versions, normal stereopsis (40″), no amblyopia, cycloplegic refraction low hyperopia (+0.50 OD, +0.75 OS).


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: tiredness and looking at distant objects
Alleviating Factors: Recovery of orthotropia on refixation or blinking
Associated Symptoms: Right eye closure in sunlight
Previous Episodes: two to three times a day initially
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — exotropia present ~60% of day, worse when tired/distance; causes right eye closure in sunlight and intermittent control affecting activities like outdoor play and distance viewing.
Systemic Symptoms: No systemic symptoms reported
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: No family history of strabismus or amblyopia; no antecedent trauma, febrile episode, or medical problems; risk factors: intermittent exotropia worse with fatigue and distance fixation, onset in early childhood (age 2), right-eye light sensitivity (squinting), slight hyperopia (+0.50 OD, +0.75 OS), poor control (present ~60% of day) — overall pediatric intermittent exotropia with early onset and decreased control.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 110/70 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 110 bpm
Vitals – Respirations: 20 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Tono-Pen applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Normal
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: 3 mm, round, briskly reactive
Anterior Segment – Angle: open and flat
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lens, no cataract


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 3mm in diameter, round
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk response to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Briskly reactive to near
Pupils – RAPD: No afferent pupillary defect detected
Pupils – Other: 3mm in diameter, round, with brisk response to light
Extraocular Movements: Ductions and versions are full.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Unremarkable; anterior segment normal on slit‑lamp examination
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Normal appearing optic nerves bilaterally
Fundus Exam – Macula: normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal.
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Pseudodivergence excess type intermittent exotropia in a 4‑year‑old boy with a 2‑year history of intermittent right exotropia worse at distance, intermittently present ~60% of the day, 50Δ at distance and 25–45Δ at near (poorly controlled), full ductions/versions, visual acuity 20/30 OU, normal stereopsis (40″), no amblyopia, cycloplegic refraction +0.50 OD, +0.75 OS.
Primary Diagnosis: Pseudodivergence excess type intermittent exotropia
Differential Diagnosis: Intermittent exotropia (pseudodivergence-excess type); Basic exotropia; Sensory exotropia; Third nerve palsy
Severity: Moderate (intermittent exotropia ~50 PD distance, present ~60% of day, poor control)
Laterality: Right eye
Complications: Recurrence of exotropia (20–40%); amblyopia; decreased stereopsis; cosmetic/psychosocial concerns; need for repeat surgery.


Plan
All Procedures: Titmus stereo fly test Alternate cover testing Patch test (20–45 minutes occlusion) 30-minute patch test Cycloplegic refraction Vision (visual acuity) testing Ductions and versions (ocular motility) examination Simultaneous prism and cover testing Overminus spectacle trial Alternate or dominant eye part-time occlusion (patching) Pencil pushup convergence exercises Computer orthoptic exercises Progressively increasing base-out prism therapy Bilateral lateral rectus recessions Lateral rectus recession with ipsilateral medial rectus resection Bilateral medial rectus resections
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up every 2–6 months while on medical management; if surgery performed, allow ~6 weeks for healing and continue long‑term follow‑up for recurrence and monitoring of stereopsis/amblyopia.
Patient Education: Intermittent exotropia is a common, lifelong condition where one eye sometimes drifts outward, often when tired or looking at distance. In your child’s case it’s worse at distance and classified as pseudodivergence excess intermittent exotropia. Key points: - Many children retain good vision and stereopsis; amblyopia is uncommon but will be monitored. - Non‑surgical options aim to improve control (reduce time eye is outward) and include observation, part‑time occlusion (patching), over‑minus glasses temporarily, and, when age/ability allow, convergence (orthoptic) exercises (e.g., pencil pushups). - Surgery is considered if control worsens (deviation present >40–50% of the day or poor control) and is typically delayed until around age 4 if control is adequate; procedures depend on the type of exotropia. - Regular follow‑up every 2–6 months while managed medically, and long‑term follow‑up after surgery, is important because recurrence can occur and vision/stereopsis can change. - Watch for signs of decreased vision, reduced depth perception, or increased frequency of the eye drifting; seek prompt re‑evaluation if these occur.


CASE 83 — Childhood Ptosis
==========================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: droopy right upper lid
DURATION: Since birth
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "this right upper lid has been 'lazy' since birth" "he holds his chin up when he is walking or watching TV" "occasionally the child lets the lid block his pupil"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: Right upper eyelid droop present since birth. Parents report he holds his chin up (about 15°) when walking or watching TV and occasionally lets the lid block his pupil. No noted ocular motility disturbance. No family history of ptosis, strabismus, or hereditary muscular disorders. Otherwise healthy and developing normally; review of systems negative.
History Narrative: A 17-month-old boy with a right upper lid that has been “lazy” since birth. Parents note a persistent chin-up posture when walking or watching TV (approximately 15°) and occasional obstruction of the pupil by the lid. No history of trauma, no family history of ptosis, strabismus, or hereditary muscular disorders. Otherwise healthy with normal development and a negative review of systems. Visual behavior shows good fixation and following in both eyes without a fixation preference. External exam notable for moderate to severe right upper lid ptosis present since birth. Interpalpebral fissure: right 6.5 mm, left 10 mm. Margin-reflex distance (MRD1) in primary position: right 0.5 mm, left 3.5 mm; with chin-up posture MRD1: right 2 mm, left 3 mm. Levator function: right 6 mm, left 12 mm. No appreciable lid crease on the right. Pupils equal (3 mm), round, briskly reactive; no afferent pupillary defect. Eyes orthotropic with full versions. Cycloplegic refraction: right −0.25 +1.50 ×90°, left +0.50 D. Rest of comprehensive exam unremarkable.
Symptom Onset: Since birth
Symptom Duration: Since birth
Progression: Stable since birth
Characteristics: Congenital right upper lid ptosis since birth; moderate–severe severity; absent/right rudimentary lid crease; levator function reduced (6 mm right vs 12 mm left); chin‑up head posture (~15°); intermittent visual axis occlusion; no anisocoria, normal pupils and motility; minimal anisometropia; no amblyopia.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Looking down (improved in downgaze) — chin‑up posture worsens ptosis impact; activities where lid occludes pupil (e.g., watching TV, walking)
Alleviating Factors: Chin-up head posture (15°) which increases eyelid margin-to-reflex distance from 0.5mm to 2mm on the right, partially exposing the visual axis
Associated Symptoms: chin-up head posture; occasional eyelid blocking pupil; no motility disturbance; no diplopia; no systemic symptoms
Previous Episodes: None
Prior Treatments: None
Impact on Daily Activities: The child holds a 15° chin-up posture and occasionally lets the droopy lid block his pupil, but has good fixation/following and no amblyopia—impact is mild: adaptive head posture needed with occasional visual obstruction but no major functional impairment.
Systemic Symptoms: Negative
Risk Factors: Congenital levator muscle maldevelopment (levator hypoplasia with fibrous/fatty infiltration); family history negative (no hereditary muscular disorders); neonatal factors not specified — thus primary risk: developmental abnormality of levator muscle (congenital).


Objective Exam
Vitals – Pulse: 110 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.4°F
Vitals – Respirations: 20 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: central, steady, maintained (fix and follow)
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: central, steady, maintained (fix and follow)
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: Central, steady, maintained (CSM) in both eyes
Visual Acuity – Method: Fix and follow
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Tonopen applanation


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Moderate to severe right upper lid ptosis; interpalpebral fissure R 6.5 mm, L 10 mm; MRD1 R 0.5 mm, L 3.5 mm (improves to R 2 mm, L 3 mm with chin-up); levator function R 6 mm, L 12 mm; absent/rudimentary right upper lid crease; preferred 15° chin-up head posture.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Within normal limits.
Anterior Segment – Iris: 3 mm, round, briskly reactive (no heterochromia)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Within normal limits.


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 3 mm, round, briskly reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Briskly reactive to light (preserved near reaction)
Pupils – RAPD: None (no RAPD)
Pupils – Other: round and briskly reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact in all directions (versions full)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Unremarkable anterior segment exam; no heterochromia or other slit‑lamp abnormalities noted.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Within normal limits (optic nerves normal)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Congenital ptosis of the right upper lid with anomalous chin-up head posture and absence of amblyopia.
Primary Diagnosis: Congenital ptosis of the right upper lid with anomalous chin-up head posture and absence of amblyopia.
Differential Diagnosis: Congenital ptosis; Horner’s syndrome; Third nerve palsy; Marcus Gunn jaw‑winking ptosis
Severity: Moderate to severe
Laterality: Right
Complications: Early and late infection; suture granuloma; exposure keratopathy; retrobulbar hemorrhage; overcorrections


Plan
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow the child throughout childhood with vigilant surveillance for amblyopia and anisometropia until at least age 7–10 years; after any ptosis surgery, follow lid height and healing for ~1 month (monitor for lagophthalmos and provide nighttime lubrication as needed); if Bell’s phenomenon is absent, plan for ongoing lubrication and close monitoring for exposure keratopathy; regular refraction checks and amblyopia management as indicated.
Patient Education: Congenital ptosis is a droopy upper eyelid present since birth due to underdevelopment of the lid muscle. Important points: - Risk: It can block the visual axis and cause amblyopia (lazy eye) or induce astigmatism from eyelid pressure, so regular vision checks are essential. - Head posture: Children often tilt the chin up to see better; this is common and may improve after surgery. - When to seek care: Prompt evaluation and regular follow-up are needed if the lid covers the pupil, vision changes, or a fixation preference develops. - Treatment: Non-surgical care addresses amblyopia and refractive errors (glasses, patching/penalization). Significant ptosis is treated surgically to raise the lid and restore the lid crease; choice of procedure depends on severity and levator function. - Postoperative expectations: Healing takes ~1 month; temporary incomplete eyelid closure (lagophthalmos) and need for nighttime lubrication are common; long-term monitoring for recurrence and amblyopia is required until around age 7–10. - Warning signs: new eyelid droop, asymmetric pupils, eye misalignment, decreased vision, or systemic symptoms (weakness, ptosis variability) warrant urgent re-evaluation.


CASE 84 — The Apparently Blind Infant
=====================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Parents concerned their 3-month-old may be blind
DURATION: 3 months
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "I do not fixate on or follow any target"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 3-month-old girl brought by parents for concerns she may be blind; parents report poor eye contact, no response to parental facial expressions, and lack of response to bright lights. Prenatal and birth histories unremarkable. Past ophthalmologic and medical histories unremarkable. Two siblings developmentally and visually normal and had more visual responsiveness at 3 months. Child appears developmentally normal but has not seen pediatrician in several weeks. On exam: does not fixate or follow targets, does not respond to facial gestures, blinks to bright light; OKN equivocal with occasional nystagmus beats; eyes orthotropic with full oculocephalic versions, no spontaneous nystagmus. Pupils 5 mm, moderately reactive, no afferent pupillary defect or paradoxical reaction. Anterior segment and fundus normal. Parents requested VEP testing.
History Narrative: A 3-month-old girl is brought to her ophthalmologist by her parents because of concerns that she may be blind. They report that she has poor eye contact, that she does not respond to parental facial expressions, and that she does not appear to respond to bright lights. The child’s prenatal and birth histories are unremarkable. Her past ophthalmologic and medical histories are unremarkable. The child has two siblings, aged 2 years and 5 years, who visually responded much more at 3 months of age than the patient does and who are developmentally and visually normal at this time. The review of systems is otherwise negative. The child appears to be normal from a developmental standpoint, though the child has not been seen by her pediatrician in several weeks. On examination, a healthy-appearing 3-month-old girl is sitting on her mother’s lap. The ocular adnexal and other facial features appear normal. The child does not fixate on or follow any target and does not respond to facial gestures. She does blink when a bright light is directed into her eyes. Optokinetic nystagmus (OKN) testing demonstrates an occasional beat of nystagmus, but attention is poor and the test results are equivocal. The eyes are orthotropic with full versions to oculocephalic testing. Spontaneous nystagmus is not seen. The pupillary examination reveals pupils that are 5mm in size and moderately reactive to light with no afferent pupillary defect, and no paradoxical pupillary reaction is present. Anterior segment and fundus examinations are normal.
Symptom Onset: 3 months of age
Symptom Duration: 3 months
Progression: Improving
Characteristics: Isolated delayed visual maturation in a developmentally and neurologically normal 3‑month‑old girl with poor fixation and following, occasional OKN beats, normal anterior segment and fundus, moderately reactive 5 mm pupils without APD, normal neurologic exam, and expected spontaneous visual improvement by 6–8 months.


Subjective (cont.)
Alleviating Factors: Explaining carefully to the parents what delayed visual maturation is and its generally favorable prognosis, thereby alleviating parental and referring physician fear
Associated Symptoms: poor eye contact; does not respond to parental facial expressions; does not appear to respond to bright lights; blinks to bright light; occasional beat of OKN; poor attention; no fixation or following; no developmental or neurologic abnormalities on exam
Previous Episodes: None
Prior Treatments: None
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — temporary significant impact on visual-related interactions (poor eye contact and response) but expected to resolve by 6–8 months with normal development.
Systemic Symptoms: Negative.
Recent Travel: No recent travel.
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: No known contacts with similar symptoms


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 90/60 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 120 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 24 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: Fixation absent, no central steady maintained (no measurable fixation)
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: Fixation only (no clear fixation to light)
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: Central, unsteady fixation (no consistent fix-and-follow)
Visual Acuity – Method: Fixation and following (qualitative assessment)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Tonopen applanation


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Normal
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lenses bilaterally


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 5 mm, moderately reactive (no afferent pupillary defect)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Moderately reactive to light
Pupils – RAPD: No relative afferent pupillary defect (absent)
Pupils – Other: Moderately reactive to light, 5 mm, no afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full versions to oculocephalic testing
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Anterior segment and corneas normal on slit‑lamp exam; anterior segment examination normal.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal.
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Isolated delayed visual maturation (DVM): 3-month-old developmentally and neurologically normal infant with poor fixation and lack of visual attention but normal anterior segment and fundus exams, moderately reactive 5 mm pupils without RAPD, equivocal OKN, no spontaneous nystagmus, and normal screening neurological examination. VEP requested; expect spontaneous improvement of visual behavior by 6–8 months if isolated DVM; further electrophysiologic testing and neuroimaging indicated only if vision fails to improve or if neurologic signs develop.
Primary Diagnosis: Isolated delayed visual maturation
Secondary Diagnoses: Delayed visual maturation (isolated)
Differential Diagnosis: Delayed visual maturation; Cortical visual impairment; Bilateral structural ocular abnormalities (e.g., congenital cataract/optic nerve hypoplasia); Idiopathic infantile nystagmus
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Bilateral


Plan
All Procedures: exam under anesthesia; funduscopy; refraction; corneal diameter measurement; measurement of intraocular pressure; optokinetic nystagmus testing; oculocephalic testing; pupillary examination; electrophysiologic testing (VEP, ERG, EEG); neuroimaging
Follow-Up Instructions: Periodic developmental and neurologic assessments by the pediatrician; monitor vision—expect improvement by 6–8 months; if vision fails to improve by 6–8 months, obtain electrophysiologic testing and neuroimaging; if associated neurologic/developmental abnormalities, continue prolonged follow-up (may take up to 1–2 years).
Patient Education: Delayed visual maturation (DVM) — many infants who appear visually unresponsive will improve as their visual system matures. In isolated DVM (normal development and neurologic exam) most infants show normal vision by 6–8 months of age. Regular pediatric follow-up and developmental monitoring are recommended. Immediate extensive testing is not usually required unless there are neurologic abnormalities or vision fails to improve by 6–8 months, in which case electrophysiologic testing (VEP/ERG) and neuroimaging may be indicated. Reassurance, close observation, and clear communication with the pediatrician and neurologist are important.
Referrals: Pediatrician and neurologist for developmental and neurological assessment; ophthalmology follow-up; neuroimaging and electrophysiologic testing if vision fails to improve (as secondary referrals if indicated).


CASE 85 — Optic Nerve Hypoplasia
================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Poor vision in the right eye and esotropia (referred for second opinion and treatment)
DURATION: Approximately 1 year
LATERALITY: Bilateral
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 3-year-old boy referred after diagnosis of right esotropia and amblyopia at age 2; prescribed full-time patching of the left eye done sporadically for ~6 months with no improvement. Parents note decreased vision in right eye. Medical history notable for weight and height in 15th percentile; otherwise unremarkable. Review of systems negative except ophthalmologic complaints; no family history of strabismus or amblyopia.
History Narrative: A 3-year-old boy referred by another ophthalmologist after a diagnosis of right esotropia and amblyopia at age 2. The referring doctor prescribed full-time patching of the left eye, performed sporadically for ~6 months with no visual improvement. Medical history notable only for weight and height in the 15th percentile; review of systems otherwise negative; no family history of strabismus or amblyopia. Examination showed best-corrected visual acuity 20/200 OD and 20/30 OS, esotropia 25 PD at distance and near with full ductions and versions, a 1+ relative afferent pupillary defect in the right eye, cycloplegic refraction +1.25 D OU, and initial posterior pole exam suggesting normal disc, macula, and vessels until closer inspection revealed a slightly pale, small right disc with crowded vessels and a double-ring sign; the left disc slightly smaller than normal.
Symptom Onset: 2 years of age
Symptom Duration: Approximately 1 year
Progression: Static/N/A
Characteristics: Bilateral, asymmetric optic nerve hypoplasia with small optic discs (double‑ring sign), crowded nerve vessels, right optic disc pallor, 1+ RAPD OD, associated septo‑optic dysplasia with absence of the septum pellucidum and growth hormone deficiency (short stature, low weight), esotropia OD (25 PD), potential for variable vision (OD 20/200, OS 20/30).


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: esotropia, decreased vision (right eye 20/200, left 20/30), amblyopia, relative afferent pupillary defect (right), small optic discs/double-ring sign, short stature/low weight (growth hormone deficiency), possible nystagmus (noted as associated finding)
Prior Treatments: Full-time patching of the left eye (sporadically for ~6 months)
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — limited by poor vision in the right eye (20/200) but able to function with better-seeing left eye (20/30); requires protection of good eye and may need accommodations (amblyopia therapy, endocrine monitoring).
Systemic Symptoms: weight and height in the 15th percentile; otherwise unremarkable; review of systems completely negative except ophthalmologic complaints


Objective Exam
Vitals – Pulse: 100 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 20 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+/- (approximately 20/200 equivalent)
Visual Acuity – Method: Best-corrected visual acuity (BCVA)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Tonopen (applanation)


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal appearing lids and lashes
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Normal
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: clear, no cataract


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Normal-sized, round pupils bilaterally
Pupils – Reaction to Light: 1+ RAPD in the right eye
Pupils – RAPD: 1+ RAPD in the right eye
Pupils – Other: 1+ right relative afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full ductions and versions.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Bilateral optic nerve hypoplasia with a small, pale right optic disc (double‑ring sign) and a slightly small left optic disc
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Crowded on the optic nerve


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Septo‑optic dysplasia with bilateral, asymmetric optic nerve hypoplasia causing decreased vision in the right eye (20/200) and mild reduction in the left eye (20/30). Right optic nerve hypoplasia evidenced by small disc with double‑ring sign, crowded vessels, pallor, and a 1+ RAPD. Associated growth hormone deficiency with short stature; absence of the septum pellucidum on MRI consistent with midline CNS malformation. Recommend urgent endocrine evaluation and pituitary hormone testing/replacement as indicated, neuroimaging correlation already consistent with septo‑optic dysplasia, trial of amblyopia therapy for potential functional component, and delayed strabismus surgery until amblyopia therapy optimized. Provide protective eyewear for the better eye and ongoing growth/endocrine surveillance.
Primary Diagnosis: Septo-optic dysplasia with bilateral, asymmetric optic nerve hypoplasia
Secondary Diagnoses: Optic nerve hypoplasia; Septo‑optic dysplasia; Growth hormone deficiency; Short stature; Esotropia; Amblyopia; Relative afferent pupillary defect (right eye)
Differential Diagnosis: Optic nerve hypoplasia (septo‑optic dysplasia); Amblyopia (functional) secondary to strabismus; Optic atrophy/optic neuritis.
Severity: Septo-optic dysplasia with bilateral, asymmetric optic nerve hypoplasia — moderate to severe visual impairment (right eye 20/200, left eye 20/30)
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: - Visual impairment ranging from mild reduced acuity to severe vision loss or no light perception (amblyopia, permanent decreased vision) - Strabismus (esotropia) with risk of recurrence after surgery - Nystagmus (when severe/bilateral ONH) - Endocrine dysfunctions (growth hormone deficiency, corticotrophin/ACTH deficiency leading to adrenal insufficiency) - Midline CNS abnormalities (septo‑optic dysplasia: absence of septum pellucidum, corpus callosum hypoplasia, posterior pituitary ectopia) - Increased risk of sudden death during stress/illness if corticotrophin deficiency untreated - Need for repeated surgeries and long‑term rehabilitation and endocrine follow‑up


Plan
All Medications: steroids (stress-dose corticosteroids as indicated), growth hormone replacement, botulinum toxin (as an option for strabismus), amblyopia patching (occlusion therapy)
All Procedures: full-time patching (occlusion therapy) cycloplegic refraction direct ophthalmoscopy / modified monocular indirect ophthalmoscopy magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) of the brain endocrine testing / pituitary hormone evaluation pituitary hormone replacement therapy amblyopia therapy strabismus surgery or botulinum toxin injection eye examination under anesthesia (when needed) prescription of polycarbonate protective spectacles
Follow-Up Instructions: Ongoing evaluation of growth and endocrine parameters throughout early childhood with endocrinology follow-up; continue amblyopia therapy and reassess before strabismus surgery; prescribe polycarbonate protective spectacles for the better-seeing eye; administer stress‑dose corticosteroids when indicated for patients with corticotrophin abnormalities.
Patient Education: Explain optic nerve hypoplasia (small optic nerves) and that it can cause a wide range of vision loss; the right eye has reduced vision and may have both anatomic underdevelopment and amblyopia, so a trial of patching/amblyopia therapy may help. Because optic nerve hypoplasia can be linked with midline brain and pituitary problems (septo‑optic dysplasia), neuroimaging (MRI) and endocrine evaluation are important—growth hormone and adrenal (corticotrophin) deficiencies can occur and need prompt treatment. Continue regular growth and endocrine follow‑up; give stress‑dose steroids if corticotrophin deficiency is present during illness, surgery, or injury. Discuss timing of strabismus surgery (usually after amblyopia therapy and often delayed until school age) and higher chance of recurrence. Recommend protective polycarbonate glasses for the better eye. Encourage regular ophthalmology and endocrinology/pediatric follow‑up and promptly report any new symptoms (poor growth, fatigue, hypoglycemia, severe illness).
Referrals: Endocrinology (endocrinologist) and pediatrician for pituitary/hormone evaluation and ongoing growth/endocrine follow-up; ophthalmology referral for second opinion/amblyopia and strabismus management; neuroimaging (MRI) referral.
Emergency Instructions: Stress doses of corticosteroids should be administered in patients with corticotrophin abnormalities in situations such as acute illness, surgery, or serious injury.


CASE 86 — Ptosis
================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Drooping of her upper eyelids (bilateral eyelid droop/ptosis)
DURATION: 3 to 4 years
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "the drooping had slowly progressed over the last 3 to 4 years." "the drooping was now severe enough to interfere with her vision, and she often found herself manually lifting her lids to improve her visual field." "She did not complain of diplopia or significant fluctuation of the lid position during the day."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 72-year-old woman referred for slowly progressive bilateral upper eyelid drooping over 3–4 years, now severe enough to interfere with vision; often manually lifts lids to improve visual field. No periocular trauma or surgery prior; had bilateral cataract extractions with IOLs 4 years ago. No muscle weakness, fatigue, neurologic disease, diplopia, or significant diurnal fluctuation. Old photos showed no prior ptosis.
History Narrative: A 72-year-old woman was referred for drooping of her upper eyelids. The patient stated that the drooping had slowly progressed over the last 3 to 4 years. The drooping was now severe enough to interfere with her vision, and she often found herself manually lifting her lids to improve her visual field. There was no previous history of periocular trauma or surgery. She had undergone bilateral cataract extraction with placement of intraocular lenses 4 years previously. There was no history of muscle weakness or fatigue, and she had no known neurologic disease. She did not complain of diplopia or significant fluctuation of the lid position during the day. Inspection of old photographs did not show the presence of ptosis.
Symptom Onset: 3 to 4 years ago
Symptom Duration: 3 to 4 years
Progression: Slowly progressive over the last 3–4 years
Characteristics: Acquired, bilateral upper eyelid ptosis (aponeurotic) with absent lid creases, deep superior sulci, good levator function (14 mm), worse on right, progressive over 3–4 years interfering with vision.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Sustained downgaze and aging-related levator aponeurosis dehiscence (progressive with time)
Alleviating Factors: Manually lifting her lids (manually elevating eyelids)
Associated Symptoms: Interference with vision; manual lifting of lids to improve visual field; no diplopia; no muscle weakness or fatigue; no significant fluctuation of lid position.
Previous Episodes: No previous episodes reported
Prior Treatments: bilateral cataract extraction with intraocular lens placement 4 years prior
Impact on Daily Activities: Interfered with vision; frequently had to manually lift lids to improve visual field (symptomatic impairment)
Systemic Symptoms: No systemic symptoms reported
Recent Travel: No recent travel reported
Risk Factors: Age-related levator aponeurosis dehiscence (advanced age)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: JND 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Bilateral upper eyelid ptosis worse on the right; absent upper eyelid creases and deepened superior sulci OU; palpebral fissures 5 mm OD, 6 mm OS; MRD1 0 mm OD, 1 mm OS; levator function 14 mm OU.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: No significant abnormalities on tarsal conjunctival inspection; conjunctiva/sclera normal
External Exam – Orbit: Orbit: No proptosis or enophthalmos; no palpable orbital masses; full extraocular motility; orthophoria.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: No corneal abnormalities noted; cornea clear OU
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal.
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open angles OU
Anterior Segment – Lens: Posterior chamber intraocular lens (pseudophakia) OU


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, reactive to light (no anisocoria)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Normal
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: Pupils equal and reactive to light (no anisocoria)
Extraocular Movements: Full ductions and versions on motility examination.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Unable to visualize optic nerves due to focus on eyelids; no fundus abnormality reported
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Ptosis of both upper eyelids due to levator aponeurosis disinsertion (aponeurotic ptosis), worse on the right, with preserved levator function and deep superior sulci consistent with age-related aponeurotic dehiscence.
Primary Diagnosis: Ptosis of both upper eyelids due to levator aponeurosis disinsertion
Differential Diagnosis: Aponeurotic (age-related levator aponeurosis dehiscence); Neurogenic ptosis (e.g., Horner’s syndrome or CN III palsy, myasthenia gravis); Myogenic ptosis (congenital levator deficiency or systemic myopathy); Mechanical ptosis (eyelid mass or severe blepharochalasis)
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: undercorrection; overcorrection; asymmetric or unsatisfactory lid contour; lagophthalmos with exposure symptoms


Plan
All Procedures: bilateral cataract extraction with intraocular lens placement; external levator advancement on both upper eyelids
Patient Education: Aponeurotic (age‑related) ptosis occurs when the levator aponeurosis loosens from the eyelid, causing drooping. Because your levator muscle strength is good, surgery to reattach/advance the levator is the usual long‑term treatment when vision is affected. Non‑surgical temporizing options include taping the lid or eyelid crutches on glasses, but these are often temporary. The common surgical approach is an external levator advancement (through a small skin crease incision) to restore the eyelid crease and height; an alternative is a posterior approach (Müller’s muscle resection) in selected cases. Surgery is often done with local anesthesia so eyelid position can be adjusted during the procedure. Expected benefits: improved superior visual field and eyelid symmetry. Possible complications: undercorrection or overcorrection, asymmetry or contour irregularity, and incomplete eyelid closure (lagophthalmos) with exposure symptoms. If there is any sudden change, fluctuating weakness, double vision, or pupil changes, notify the clinic promptly for further neurologic workup (e.g., testing for myasthenia gravis or Horner’s syndrome).


CASE 87 — Thyroid Eye Disease
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Pain around both eyes and increasing prominence of both eyes (bilateral eye pain and proptosis)
DURATION: 18 months
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "gritty." "Both eyes felt 'gritty.'" "She had noted increasing prominence of both eyes over the past 18 months" "both eyes felt “gritty.”" "no complaints of diplopia or blurred vision" "felt 'gritty.'" "She reported no recent change in weight, body hair growth or loss, or change in the quality of her voice."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 44‑year‑old woman with an 18‑month history of progressively prominent, gritty‑feeling eyes and bilateral dull periocular pain worsened by eye movement; she denied diplopia or blurred vision. She noted heat and cold intolerance but no other systemic thyroid symptoms. No prior ocular surgery or trauma. Symptoms included bilateral eyelid retraction, increased tearing/exposure with 2 mm lagophthalmos, and corneal discomfort from exposure.
History Narrative: A 44-year-old woman was referred for evaluation of pain around both eyes. The pain was described as a dull ache and worsened with eye movement. She had noted increasing prominence of both eyes over the past 18 months and complained that both eyes felt “gritty.” She had no complaints of diplopia or blurred vision, and there was no previous ocular history of surgery or trauma. Her past medical history was unremarkable, and there was no history of thyroid dysfunction. Her review of systems was significant for heat and cold intolerance, but she reported no recent change in weight, body hair growth or loss, or change in the quality of her voice. There was no known cardiovascular or neurologic disease.
Symptom Onset: 18 months
Symptom Duration: 18 months
Progression: Progressive over 18 months
Characteristics: Thyroid eye disease with bilateral proptosis, eyelid retraction, mild restrictive upgaze, lagophthalmos with corneal exposure signs, increased IOP (21/20 mm Hg), and enlargement of inferior rectus (bilateral) and left medial rectus (tendon-sparing) on CT.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Eye movement (worsening pain with eye movement)
Alleviating Factors: Wearing a moisture shield at bedtime; frequent artificial tears and lubricating ointment; punctal occlusion; patching one eye for diplopia (if present)
Associated Symptoms: dull ache around both eyes worsened with eye movement; gritty sensation in both eyes; heat and cold intolerance; no diplopia or blurred vision; nocturnal dryness/lagophthalmos (2 mm) with exposure symptoms; intermittent ocular pain.
Previous Episodes: None
Prior Treatments: Frequent artificial tears and punctal occlusion (prior to eyelid surgery)
Impact on Daily Activities: Mild impact — gritty sensation, exposure symptoms managed with drops/occlusion; no diplopia or vision loss, able to perform daily activities with some discomfort.
Systemic Symptoms: Heat and cold intolerance
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: No known contacts with similar symptoms
Risk Factors: Smoking; thyroid dysfunction (Graves’ disease/hypothyroidism); middle‑aged adult (age around 44); female sex; autoimmune thyroid disease.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 21 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 20 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry

External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Upper and lower eyelid retraction with temporal flaring of the upper lids; palpebral fissures 16 mm OD, 15 mm OS; 2 mm bilateral lagophthalmos; scleral show and mild bulbar conjunctival injection.

Anterior Segment – Cornea: Scattered punctate epithelial erosions inferiorly in both corneas
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal

Fundus Exam – Periphery: No chorioretinal lesions; periphery flat and unremarkable.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 44-year-old woman with bilateral thyroid eye disease (Gravesʼ orbitopathy) manifesting as progressive bilateral proptosis (exophthalmometry 22 mm OD, 21 mm OS), upper and lower eyelid retraction with widened palpebral fissures (16 mm OD, 15 mm OS) and 2 mm lagophthalmos, mild (-1) upgaze restriction OU, increased retropulsion and mild corneal exposure with punctate inferior epithelial erosions. IOPs 21 mm Hg OD and 20 mm Hg OS. Color vision, pupils, visual acuity (20/20 OU) and automated visual fields normal; CT orbit shows tendon-sparing enlargement of inferior rectus muscles bilaterally and left medial rectus without optic nerve compression. Diagnosis: thyroid eye disease with eyelid retraction and bilateral proptosis; endocrine evaluation confirmed hypothyroidism on replacement. Initial management with lubricants and punctal occlusion; after stable symptoms for 4 months underwent successful bilateral eyelid recession with symptomatic and cosmetic improvement.
Primary Diagnosis: Thyroid eye disease (Graves’ orbitopathy)
Secondary Diagnoses: Hypothyroidism; Corneal exposure keratopathy; Lagophthalmos; Elevated intraocular pressure (ocular hypertension)
Differential Diagnosis: Thyroid eye disease (Graves’ orbitopathy); Idiopathic orbital inflammation (orbital myositis); Bilateral orbital metastases; Diffuse infiltrative processes (amyloidosis or sarcoidosis)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Bilateral


Plan
All Medications: prednisone; artificial tears; lubricating ointment; punctal occlusion (procedure); moisture shield (device); topical intraocular pressure–lowering drops; orbital radiation therapy (treatment); thyroid hormone replacement therapy
All Procedures: artificial tears instillation; punctal occlusion; bilateral eyelid recession (recession of all four eyelids)
Follow-Up Instructions: Maintain thyroid hormone replacement and follow with endocrinology; continue frequent artificial tears and lubricating ointment with punctal occlusion as needed; use moisture shield at bedtime for nocturnal drying; follow ophthalmology for postoperative care after eyelid surgery and routine monitoring of vision, IOP, corneal surface, and motility.
Patient Education: Educated that thyroid eye disease is the most common cause of her proptosis, that the eye disease and thyroid dysfunction can run independent courses, and that evaluation by an endocrinologist is necessary; advised on corneal exposure measures (frequent artificial tears, lubricating ointment at bedtime, moisture shield at night, and punctal occlusion as an option); counseled that active inflammation or optic neuropathy may require steroids, orbital radiation, or decompression; warned that decompression can cause or worsen diplopia and that strabismus surgery is often needed afterward; explained that eyelid surgery is performed only after orbital and muscle disease are stable and that motility should be stable for 3–4 months before strabismus surgery.
Referrals: Endocrinology (internist/endocrinologist referral for thyroid evaluation)

===================

Characteristics: Involutional (age-related) lower eyelid ectropion with horizontal laxity, medial punctal eversion, tarsal conjunctival injection, and mild inferior SPK.

CASE 89 — Entropion
===================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Severe right eye pain progressively worsening for 2 years; can be relieved by manually retracting the right lower eyelid.
DURATION: 2 years
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "I can alleviate my pain if I manually retract my right lower eyelid."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 75-year-old woman with a 2-year history of progressive severe right eye pain due to right lower eyelid “in-turning” causing lashes to rub the eye; pain is partially relieved by manually retracting the right lower eyelid. On exam the right lower lid shows entropion with moderate horizontal laxity; lid can be restored with gentle downward traction but in-turning recurs with blinking.
History Narrative: A 75-year-old woman presented with complaints of severe right eye pain that has been progressively worsening for 2 years. The patient has discovered that she can alleviate her pain if she manually retracts her right lower eyelid. Examination shows “in-turning” of the right lower eyelid causing the lashes to rub against the epibulbar surface. Moderate horizontal laxity of the lower lid is present. With gentle downward traction on the right lower eyelid, the lid margin can be restored to its normal position; however, the in-turning recurs when the patient blinks.
Symptom Onset: 2 years
Symptom Duration: 2 years
Progression: Progressively worsening for 2 years
Characteristics: In-turning of the right lower eyelid with lashes rubbing on the globe; moderate horizontal lower lid laxity; eyelid can be manually everted (digital eversion positive); involutional (age-related) etiology.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Blinking; eyelash rubbing against the globe (lash–epibulbar surface contact)
Alleviating Factors: manually retracts her right lower eyelid
Associated Symptoms: Severe right eye pain relieved by manually retracting the right lower eyelid; ocular irritation from lashes rubbing on epibulbar surface (foreign-body sensation)
Previous Episodes: The patient reported that her severe right eye pain had been progressively worsening for 2 years; no prior episodes are mentioned—so infer: none/first episode (2-year progressive onset).
Prior Treatments: Taping the lid; lubricating drops and ointment; temporizing everting sutures (Quickert‑Rathbun sutures)
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant impairment — severe right eye pain for 2 years causing manual eyelid retraction to relieve discomfort, ocular irritation from lash rubbing, and need for surgical correction (Wies procedure).
Risk Factors: Advanced age, horizontal eyelid laxity, disinsertion/dehiscence of lower eyelid retractors, overriding of preseptal orbicularis, relative enophthalmos (orbital soft‑tissue atrophy)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.4°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J+1
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen — 20/60 (uncorrected); 20/40 (pinhole)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Right lower eyelid entropion with lashes rubbing on the epibulbar surface; moderate horizontal lower lid laxity; overriding of the preseptal orbicularis.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Mild conjunctival injection with lash-induced epithelial irritation on the exposed inferior bulbar conjunctiva; otherwise sclera without scleral icterus or notable scleral abnormalities
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Lashes rubbing on epibulbar surface with corneal irritation from trichiasis; cornea with epithelial irritation/abrasion likely (exposure to lash trauma)
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: Nuclear sclerosis, age-related changes consistent with no recent cataract surgery (phakic)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, reactive to light (normal)
Pupils – RAPD: Unable to assess
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions; no extraocular motility deficits noted
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: disc normal appearing/no optic nerve abnormality noted
Fundus Exam – Macula: Unable to visualize/Not performed


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 89-year-old woman with involutional entropion of the right lower eyelid presenting with 2 years of progressive right eye pain relieved by manual eversion. Examination: inward rotation of right lower lid lashes abrading the epibulbar surface, moderate horizontal lid laxity, lid margin can be manually everted but re-inverts with blinking; lid not restricted, consistent with involutional (age-related) entropion due to horizontal laxity, retractor disinsertion and orbicularis override. Plan: temporizing measures (lubrication, taping, possible Quickert-Rathbun sutures) with definitive surgical correction via lower eyelid tightening/repair (Wies procedure performed with satisfactory correction and no postoperative complications).
Primary Diagnosis: Involutional entropion of the right lower eyelid
Differential Diagnosis: Involutional entropion; Spastic entropion (due to ocular irritation/lid edema); Cicatricial entropion (eg, Stevens–Johnson syndrome, mucous membrane pemphigoid, posttraumatic scarring)
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Right
Complications: None


Plan
All Medications: lubricating drops and ointment
All Procedures: Wies procedure of the right lower eyelid
Patient Education: - Entropion is when the lower eyelid turns inward so the lashes rub the eye, causing pain and irritation. - Cause: in older adults it’s usually involutional — eyelid laxity, weakened lower lid retractors, and muscle overriding. - Short-term measures: lubricating drops/ointment and taping or temporary everting (Quickert-Rathbun) sutures can relieve symptoms. - Definitive treatment: surgical options include Wies procedure (performed for you with good result), lateral tarsal strip (horizontal tightening), or reattachment of the lower lid retractors; choice depends on the underlying problem. - After surgery: expect improved comfort and lid position; follow wound-care instructions, use prescribed drops/ointments, avoid rubbing the eye, and attend scheduled postoperative visits. - Seek care promptly for increasing pain, sudden vision changes, heavy discharge, wound opening, or signs of infection (redness, swelling, fever).


CASE 90 — Trichiasis
====================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Chronic irritation of the left eye for 2 years
DURATION: 2 years
LATERALITY: Left
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 64-year-old woman with 2-year history of chronic left eye irritation, gradually worsening, with no prior ocular history. Exam showed left lower lid entropion easily repositioned, row of fine nonpigmented posterior lashes contacting the globe, mild inferior punctate corneal changes, inferior bulbar rose bengal staining, diffuse lid margin thickening with telangiectasia, and scattered lash debris/collarettes on all lids.
History Narrative: A 64-year-old woman presented with complaints of chronic irritation of the left eye that had been worsening over the last 2 years. There was no previous ocular history. Ophthalmic examination was remarkable for an entropion of the left lower eyelid. The lid was easily returned to its normal position using gentle manual downward pressure. The tarsal conjunctiva showed mild erythema without evidence of cicatricial changes, and the inferior fornix was deep without symblepharon. When the lid was everted, a row of fine nonpigmented lashes was noted posterior to the gray line. These lashes were in contact with the epibulbar surface. Mild punctate corneal changes were present inferiorly. The inferior bulbar conjunctiva showed rose bengal staining in the areas associated with the abnormal lashes. Diffuse thickening of the lid margin was noted as well as the presence of fine telangiectatic vessels. Scattered debris, including collarettes, were present on the lashes of all four eyelids.
Symptom Onset: 2 years
Symptom Duration: 2 years
Progression: Chronic, progressively worsening over 2 years
Characteristics: Chronic irritation of left eye from lower lid entropion with a row of fine nonpigmented distichiasis lashes posterior to the gray line contacting the globe; mild inferior punctate corneal staining, inferior bulbar rose bengal staining, diffuse lid-margin thickening with telangiectasia and scattered lash debris (blepharitis/MGD).


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Chronic prolonged inflammation, eyelid malposition (entropion), blepharitis/meibomian gland dysfunction
Alleviating Factors: Manual downward pressure returning the lid to normal position; lubricating drops and ointment; lid hygiene and treatment of blepharitis (artificial tears)
Associated Symptoms: chronic irritation of the left eye, ocular surface irritation with foreign body sensation, punctate corneal staining, inferior bulbar conjunctival staining (rose bengal), lid margin thickening with telangiectasia, scattered lash debris/collarettes
Previous Episodes: No previous ocular history
Prior Treatments: Lubricating drops and ointment; epilation with forceps; electrolysis; cryotherapy (including postoperative and repeat cryotherapy); argon laser ablation; Wies procedure (surgical entropion repair)
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — intermittent ocular irritation impairing comfort and activities (worsened over 2 years) but manageable with treatments and surgery.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: Chronic posterior lamellar scarring and chronic inflammation (blepharitis/meibomian gland dysfunction); eyelid malposition (entropion); prior ocular surface disease (e.g., cicatricial disease)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N8
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen history and refraction
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Left lower eyelid entropion with trichiasis/distichiasis; diffuse lid margin thickening, telangiectatic vessels, scattered collarettes on lashes of all four lids.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Mild inferior bulbar conjunctival erythema with rose bengal staining inferiorly; no conjunctival cicatrization or symblepharon.
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Mild inferior punctate epithelial erosions with inferior corneal staining on fluorescein and focal rose bengal staining corresponding to areas of lash contact
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep, quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal irridial anatomy; no iris abnormalities noted
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open (normal)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lens (no cataract)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive to light
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all gazes, no restrictions or diplopia reported
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Entropion of the left lower eyelid with easily eversible lid, tarsal conjunctival mild erythema without cicatricial change, deep inferior fornix without symblepharon, row of fine nonpigmented lashes posterior to the gray line contacting the globe, mild inferior punctate corneal epithelial staining, inferior bulbar conjunctival rose bengal staining, diffuse lid margin thickening with fine telangiectasia and scattered collarettes on all lashes.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Disc exam normal for age, no optic nerve pallor or cupping noted
Fundus Exam – Macula: Within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Trichiasis of the left lower eyelid due to aberrant lashes (distichiasis) in the setting of left lower lid entropion. Associated blepharitis and meibomian gland dysfunction of all four eyelids with mild inferior punctate corneal epithelial change and focal inferior bulbar conjunctival staining from lash contact. Lid margin thickening with telangiectasia and scattered collarettes. Overall symptoms attributable to eyelid malposition plus distichiasis causing mechanical irritation; tear-film insufficiency/ocular surface disease likely contributory.
Primary Diagnosis: Trichiasis of the lower eyelid due to aberrant lashes (distichiasis) and lower lid entropion
Secondary Diagnoses: Blepharitis; Meibomian gland dysfunction; Distichiasis; Lower eyelid entropion
Differential Diagnosis: Entropion with trichiasis; Distichiasis (metaplastic meibomian gland lashes); Blepharitis/meibomian gland dysfunction
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Left
Complications: Loss of skin pigmentation and eyelid notching


Plan
All Medications: lubricating drops and ointment
All Procedures: Wies procedure (lower eyelid entropion repair); cryotherapy to aberrant lashes (initial and subsequent retreatment)
Follow-Up Instructions: Postoperative follow-up with repeat cryotherapy for any focal recurrent distichiasis; maintain lid hygiene program for blepharitis/meibomian gland dysfunction; use lubricating drops/ointment as needed.
Patient Education: - Explanation: Your lower eyelid is turning inward (entropion) and there are extra, fine lashes growing from oil gland openings (distichiasis). These lashes rub the eye and cause irritation and corneal damage (trichiasis). - Immediate measures: Keep eyelids clean with daily lid hygiene (warm compresses and gentle lid scrubs) and use preservative-free artificial tears frequently; apply ointment at night for added protection. - Temporary relief: Individual offending lashes can be plucked (epilation) but often recur within months. - Definitive treatments: Options include cryotherapy or laser/electrolysis to destroy aberrant follicles, or surgery (wedge excision, lash follicle excision, lid-splitting, and repair of the entropion). Your surgeon recommended entropion repair (Wies procedure) plus cryotherapy in this case. - What to expect after treatment: Improvement of rubbing and irritation, but lashes can recur and may need repeat treatment. Possible side effects include temporary swelling, pigment changes, or small notches in the eyelid. - When to seek care: Return promptly for increased pain, redness, worsening vision, pus, or if symptoms recur. Routine follow-up will monitor for recurrence and manage blepharitis/meibomian dysfunction.


CASE 91 — Dacryocystitis
========================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Tearing and pain of the right lower eyelid medially
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "constant epiphora of the right eye since the time of her surgeries" "intermittent redness of the right lower eyelid, associated with tenderness and mucous discharge from the eye"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 44-year-old woman with a long history of constant right-sided epiphora since multiple prior midface surgeries for lethal midline granuloma (including nasal septum removal and frontoethmoidectomies), who more recently developed intermittent redness, tenderness, mucous discharge from the right lower eyelid medially, and pain; previously treated with an unknown topical antibiotic; on exam erythema and tenderness over the right medial lower lid with mucoid discharge expressed from the punctum; nasolacrimal irrigation showed distal nasolacrimal duct obstruction with patent proximal system.
History Narrative: A 44-year-old woman was referred for evaluation of tearing and pain of the right lower eyelid medially. Her past medical history was significant for lethal midline granuloma (midfacial necrotizing lesion) for which she had undergone multiple procedures, including removal of the nasal septum and frontoethmoidectomies. She reported constant epiphora of the right eye since the time of her surgeries and had more recently noted intermittent redness of the right lower eyelid, associated with tenderness and mucous discharge from the eye. She had been previously treated with an unknown ophthalmic antibiotic drop. Examination showed erythema of the right lower lid medially with associated tenderness to palpation. No definite masses were palpated in the lacrimal sac region. There was a whitish mucoid discharge expressed from the punctum upon palpation of the lacrimal sac, and the conjunctiva was mildly injected. The puncta of the right upper and lower eyelid were present and appeared patent. There was a well-healed surgical incision over the nose with residual distortion of the nasal architecture. The remainder of the ophthalmic examination was completely normal. Nasolacrimal irrigation was performed and demonstrated complete obstruction of the distal nasal lacrimal system. The upper system was patent as demonstrated by the reflux of irrigating solution through the upper and lower puncta. Inspection of the inner nose showed a large nasal defect with an absent septum.
Symptom Onset: Since the time of her surgeries
Symptom Duration: Constant epiphora of the right eye since the time of her surgeries (chronic, long-standing)
Progression: Resolved after treatment with oral antibiotics followed by dacryocystorhinostomy with silicone tubing; epiphora improved and inflammation resolved.
Characteristics: Infection of lacrimal sac due to nasolacrimal duct obstruction causing tearing, pain, redness, swelling in medial canthal area, mucous discharge, tenderness, and possible abscess formation.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Nasal surgery with removal of nasal septum and frontoethmoidectomies (leading to disrupted nasolacrimal duct)
Alleviating Factors: Warm compresses and oral antibiotics
Associated Symptoms: tearing, pain, redness, swelling in medial canthal area, tenderness, mucous discharge, epiphora, conjunctival injection
Previous Episodes: Constant epiphora of the right eye since her prior nasal surgeries, with more recent intermittent redness, tenderness, and mucous discharge (recurrent episodes of dacryocystitis)
Prior Treatments: multiple procedures including removal of the nasal septum and frontoethmoidectomies; previous treatment with an unknown ophthalmic antibiotic drop; treated with oral antibiotics for acute infection; subsequent dacryocystorhinostomy (DCR) with placement of silicone tubing
Impact on Daily Activities: Intermittent tearing, pain, redness, and mucous discharge causing discomfort and requiring medical attention and surgery, but after antibiotics and dacryocystorhinostomy with silicone tubing, good resolution of epiphora and return to normal daily activities.
Risk Factors: Chronic nasolacrimal duct obstruction from prior nasal/facial surgery (removal of nasal septum, frontoethmoidectomies), prior facial/nasal trauma/surgery, chronic inflammation from sinus disease, autoimmune disorders, viral infections, lacrimal stones, medications (5‑fluorouracil, echothiophate/Phospholine Iodide), congenital NLD obstruction, prior infections leading to stasis


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 76 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 99.1 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+ (Jaeger 1+)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen acuity (distance) using Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Erythema and mild edema of the right lower eyelid medially; puncta present and patent; no lash abnormalities.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Mild conjunctival injection.
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Right lower lid medial erythema and tenderness; mucoid discharge expressed from punctum; puncta patent; reflux through upper and lower puncta on irrigation; distal nasolacrimal duct obstruction (complete).
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea, no epithelial defect, no infiltrate, no corneal scarring or edema.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal.
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open/normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lens bilaterally


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, and reactive to light (no abnormality noted)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: briskly consensual
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: Pupils equal, round, and reactive to light (no afferent pupillary defect)
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact (no restriction)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Conjunctiva mildly injected; puncta present and patent; erythema and mild edema of right lower lid medially with tenderness; whitish mucoid discharge expressed from punctum; remainder of ophthalmic examination completely normal.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal appearing optic nerve
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: WNL


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Dacryocystitis, right side, secondary to disrupted nasolacrimal duct from prior nasal surgery (lethal midline granuloma with septal resection). Presented with chronic right-sided epiphora and recent medial lower eyelid erythema, tenderness, mucous discharge; exam showed mucoid reflux from punctum, mild conjunctival injection, patent puncta, and complete distal nasolacrimal duct obstruction on irrigation. Responded to oral antibiotics; planned and subsequently underwent dacryocystorhinostomy with silicone tube placement with good resolution of epiphora.
Primary Diagnosis: Dacryocystitis, right side, secondary to disrupted nasolacrimal duct from prior nasal surgery
Secondary Diagnoses: Lethal midline granuloma (midfacial necrotizing lesion); prior nasal septum removal and frontoethmoidectomies resulting in disrupted nasolacrimal duct
Differential Diagnosis: Dacryocystitis secondary to nasolacrimal duct obstruction; Preseptal cellulitis; Lacrimal sac tumor (neoplasm)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right
Complications: recurrent dacryocystitis and potential lacrimal sac abscess; persistent epiphora until DCR; possible facial/nasal structural complications related to prior surgeries (nasal septum removal)


Plan
All Medications: oral antibiotics; antibiotic ophthalmic drops; silicone tubing
All Procedures: nasolacrimal irrigation; aspiration of lacrimal sac abscess (if present); dacryocystorhinostomy (DCR) with placement of silicone tubing; application of warm compresses
Follow-Up Instructions: Continue follow-up with ENT and oncologist; monitor for recurrence of epiphora or infection; postoperative visits for DCR and silicone tube care as scheduled.
Patient Education: Warm compresses to the affected area several times daily, take prescribed oral antibiotics as directed, expect possible surgical drainage if an abscess develops, avoid eye drops as they are usually unnecessary, and plan definitive treatment with dacryocystorhinostomy (DCR) after infection resolves; follow up with ophthalmology and ENT for postoperative care.
Referrals: ear, nose, and throat physician; oncologist
Emergency Instructions: Apply warm compresses frequently to the inflamed site; seek urgent care for worsening pain, increasing swelling, fever, spreading redness (preseptal/orbital cellulitis), or failure to improve on antibiotics.


CASE 92 — Idiopathic Orbital Inflammation
=========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Left eye pain and decreased vision for 2 days
DURATION: 2 days
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "resolution of his pain."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 63-year-old man with 2-day history of acute onset left eye pain and decreased vision. No prior ocular disease, surgery, or injury. Associated marked periorbital edema and erythema, 3 mm left globe proptosis, increased resistance to retropulsion, painful diffuse motility limitation, 3+ conjunctival injection with diffuse chemosis, and vision reduced to 20/400 OS with a 2+ relative afferent pupillary defect. Afebrile, normal WBC.
History Narrative: A 63-year-old man presented with a 2-day history of left eye pain and decreased vision. He had no prior ophthalmic disease, surgery, or injury. Past medical history was notable for poorly controlled hypertension. He denied a history of rheumatoid disease, collagen vascular disease, diabetes mellitus, sinus disease, malignancy, immunosuppressive therapy, or recent trauma. Review of systems revealed no thyroid symptoms. On exam, best-corrected visual acuity was 20/20 OD and 20/400 OS with a 2+ relative afferent pupillary defect in the left pupil. There was marked left periorbital edema and erythema, 3 mm of left globe proptosis, increased resistance to retropulsion, significant painful motility deficit in all directions, 3+ conjunctival injection with diffuse chemosis, and diffuse choroidal thickening on dilated fundus exam. He was afebrile and had a normal white blood cell count.
Symptom Onset: 2-day history
Symptom Duration: 2 days
Progression: Improved with rapid response to high‑dose IV steroids; vision and inflammatory signs resolved after treatment and remained well on tapered oral prednisone.
Characteristics: Acute onset painful diffuse anterior orbital inflammation with periorbital edema, erythema, proptosis, motility restriction with pain, conjunctival injection and chemosis, associated scleritis and choroidal thickening/effusion, steroid-responsive.


Subjective (cont.)
Alleviating Factors: High‑dose systemic corticosteroids (IV methylprednisolone followed by oral prednisone)
Associated Symptoms: Eye pain; decreased vision; periorbital edema and erythema; chemosis; motility pain/ophthalmoplegia; proptosis; resistance to retropulsion; relative afferent pupillary defect
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes of orbital inflammation reported
Prior Treatments: None prior; treated acutely with intravenous methylprednisolone (250 mg q6h) then converted to oral prednisone.
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — significant transient impairment (left eye vision reduced to 20/400, pain, motility limitation and proptosis) that rapidly improved with treatment and returned to normal with steroid taper.
Systemic Symptoms: no history of rheumatoid disease or collagen vascular disease; no history of diabetes mellitus; afebrile; no renal or pulmonary disease reported; no thyroid symptoms (heat/cold intolerance, weight change, hair/voice changes)
Risk Factors: Poorly controlled hypertension; no diabetes; no history of sinus disease, malignancy, immunosuppression, trauma, rheumatoid or collagen vascular disease; age 63 (older age)

Assessment Narrative: 92-year-old? N/A
Primary Diagnosis: Idiopathic orbital inflammation
Secondary Diagnoses: Hypertension (poorly controlled)
Differential Diagnosis: Orbital cellulitis; Thyroid-related orbitopathy (thyroid eye disease); Orbital lymphoma/neoplastic infiltration; Systemic vasculitis (Wegener’s granulomatosis)
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Left
Complications: scleritis and secondary choroidal effusion


Plan
All Medications: methylprednisolone (IV 250 mg every 6 hours), oral prednisone
All Procedures: MRI of the orbits with and without gadolinium; Intravenous methylprednisolone then conversion to oral prednisone; Dilated fundus examination
Follow-Up Instructions: Referred for systemic evaluation for collagen‑vascular disease and lymphoma; slow taper of oral prednisone with clinical follow‑up and repeat MRI to ensure resolution and exclude underlying neoplasm.
Patient Education: Idiopathic orbital inflammation (orbital pseudotumor) is an inflammatory condition causing eye pain, swelling, redness, and sometimes vision change. It is not caused by cancer in this case and often responds rapidly to high‑dose corticosteroids. Key points for you: - Cause and prognosis: The exact cause is unknown (idiopathic). With prompt steroid treatment most patients improve quickly and recover normal vision, as happened here. - Treatment plan: You received intravenous steroids followed by an oral prednisone taper. It is important to finish the taper as directed to prevent recurrence and withdrawal symptoms. - Symptoms to watch for: Return of eye pain, increasing redness, new or worsening vision loss, fever, or signs of infection—report these immediately. - Medication precautions: Take prednisone exactly as prescribed. Do not stop suddenly. Expect possible side effects (increased appetite, fluid retention, mood changes, elevated blood pressure, blood sugar changes). Because you have hypertension, monitor your blood pressure closely and inform your primary doctor; adjustments to blood‑pressure medications may be needed. - Follow‑up and testing: Keep scheduled ophthalmology follow‑ups and the recommended systemic workup (blood tests/imaging) to exclude systemic disease (e.g., autoimmune disorders or lymphoma). A repeat MRI was done and was normal; further imaging or biopsy would be considered only if symptoms recur or do not respond to treatment. - Eye care measures: Use cool compresses for comfort and avoid rubbing the eye. If prescribed topical drops, use as directed. - When to seek urgent care: sudden worsening vision, severe increasing pain, fever, or signs of steroid complications (severe weakness, chest pain, severe mood changes). If you have questions about your prednisone dose, side effects, or blood‑pressure management while on steroids, contact your treating physician.
Referrals: Systemic evaluation for collagen-vascular disease or lymphoma


CASE 93 — Orbital Cellulitis
============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Right-sided eyelid swelling, pain, and fever for 4 days
DURATION: 4 days
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "somewhat lethargic and continued to complain of pain around the right eye."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 7-year-old boy with 4 days of right-sided eyelid swelling, pain, and fever. Initially diagnosed as preseptal cellulitis and started on oral antibiotics; symptoms progressed over 2 days with worsening right upper lid edema causing complete ptosis, lethargy, persistent periorbital pain, and fever (100 °F). On presentation had marked periorbital edema and erythema, conjunctival injection, decreased right globe motility in all directions, mild proptosis, and enlarged preauricular and submandibular lymph nodes; visual acuity 20/25 OD and 20/20 OS, pupils reactive without RAPD.
History Narrative: A 7-year-old boy presented with a 4-day history of right-sided eyelid swelling, pain, and fever. Initially diagnosed with preseptal cellulitis and treated with oral antibiotics, his symptoms progressed over two days with worsening right upper lid edema leading to complete ptosis. On arrival he was somewhat lethargic, complained of periorbital pain, and had a temperature of 100 °F. Examination revealed marked periorbital edema and erythema with complete right upper eyelid ptosis, enlarged preauricular and submandibular lymph nodes, diffuse conjunctival injection, decreased right ocular motility in all directions, mild proptosis (measurement precluded by edema), intact pupillary reactions without relative afferent pupillary defect, quiet anterior segments, and a normal dilated fundus exam. Visual acuity with the eyelid gently lifted was 20/25 OD and 20/20 OS.
Symptom Onset: 4 days prior to presentation
Symptom Duration: 4 days
Progression: Worsening of right upper lid edema leading to complete ptosis despite oral antibiotics
Characteristics: Periorbital edema and erythema, chemosis, pain, fever, decreased ocular motility, mild proptosis, complete ptosis of the eyelid, conjunctival injection, enlarged preauricular and submandibular lymph nodes


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: eyelid swelling, pain around the right eye, fever, lethargy, periorbital edema and erythema, complete ptosis of the eyelid, conjunctival injection (chemosis), decreased motility of the right globe in all directions, mild proptosis, enlarged preauricular and submandibular lymph nodes
Previous Episodes: None reported
Prior Treatments: Oral antibiotics (for presumed preseptal cellulitis)
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant temporary impairment (limited by pain, eyelid ptosis preventing eye opening and restricted eye movements) but resolved after treatment.
Systemic Symptoms: Fever and lethargy
Risk Factors: Ethmoid sinusitis; eyelid trauma; pre-existing preseptal cellulitis; dacryocystitis; dental infection; recent sinusitis (ethmoid); pediatric age (child)


Objective Exam

Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20

Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg

External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Marked periorbital edema and erythema with complete right upper‑eyelid ptosis; no conjunctival discharge; mild proptosis; enlarged preauricular and submandibular lymph nodes.
