External Exam – Orbit: Right: marked periorbital edema and erythema with complete upper‑lid ptosis, diffuse conjunctival injection and chemosis, mild proptosis, and markedly decreased extraocular motility in all directions; left: normal.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Quiet, no corneal abnormality detected
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Quiet, no significant abnormalities noted
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: reactive, no relative afferent defect (normal pupils)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: reactive normally
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Normal
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: No relative afferent pupillary defect

Slit Lamp Exam Description: Anterior segments were quiet on slit‑lamp examination.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: No significant abnormalities
Fundus Exam – Macula: no significant abnormalities
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normalappearing retinal vessels
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No significant abnormalities


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Orbital cellulitis of the right orbit with superomedial subperiosteal abscess secondary to ethmoid sinusitis; failed initial oral antibiotic therapy with progression to complete upper eyelid ptosis, decreased extraocular motility, mild proptosis, conjunctival injection and fever. Imaging (CT) confirmed large right superomedial subperiosteal abscess and ethmoid sinus mucosal thickening. Treated with urgent surgical drainage of subperiosteal abscess and ethmoidectomy plus IV antibiotics (ceftriaxone and clindamycin) with marked clinical improvement and eventual complete resolution of swelling and motility deficit.
Primary Diagnosis: Orbital cellulitis
Secondary Diagnoses: ethmoid sinusitis; subperiosteal orbital abscess; fever; enlarged preauricular and submandibular lymphadenopathy
Differential Diagnosis: Preseptal cellulitis; Idiopathic orbital inflammation; Orbital infiltration by malignancy (leukemia/retinoblastoma)
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Right
Complications: orbital apex syndrome; blindness; brain abscess; cavernous sinus thrombosis; death


Plan
All Medications: oral antibiotics intravenous ceftriaxone clindamycin oxacillin (mentioned as example for preseptal cellulitis) penicillinase-resistant penicillin (general class)
All Procedures: Drainage of large right superomedial subperiosteal orbital abscess; right ethmoidectomy; intraoperative purulent culture collection; preoperative blood cultures.
Follow-Up Instructions: Subsequent follow-up examination to confirm resolution of periorbital swelling and motility restriction.
Patient Education: Orbital cellulitis is a serious infection behind the eyelid that can cause pain, swelling, fever, limited eye movement, and occasionally vision loss. It most often follows sinus infection or eyelid trauma. Because it can progress rapidly and cause complications (abscess, vision loss, brain infection), prompt medical care is essential. Key points and instructions: - This condition requires urgent evaluation and treatment in the hospital, often with intravenous (IV) antibiotics and sometimes surgical drainage of an abscess. - Follow all hospital and ENT/surgery recommendations; if surgery is recommended (e.g., drainage or sinus surgery), it is important to proceed to prevent vision- or life-threatening complications. - Take all prescribed antibiotics exactly as directed. Do not stop antibiotics early even if symptoms improve. - Report immediately to the emergency department or your surgeon if you notice: worsening vision, new double vision, increasing pain, increased swelling or redness, high fever, severe headache, confusion, or seizures. - Keep follow-up appointments with ophthalmology and ENT to monitor healing and eye function. Visual acuity and eye movement will be rechecked. - Pain can be managed with acetaminophen or ibuprofen as advised by the team; avoid NSAIDs only if specifically instructed otherwise. - At-home care after discharge: rest, keep the head elevated to reduce swelling, apply cool compresses if recommended, and finish the full course of oral antibiotics. - If cultures identified a specific organism, take any additional precautions or medication adjustments given by the infectious disease team. If you have questions about medications, wound care, activity restrictions, or signs of recurrence, contact the treating team or return to the emergency department immediately.
Referrals: ENT surgeon; pediatric infectious disease consult


CASE 94 — Dacryoadenitis
========================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: 1-week history of swelling of the left upper eyelid with redness and fullness of the temporal aspect (left upper lid)
DURATION: 1 week
LATERALITY: Left
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 12-year-old boy with a 1-week history of swelling of the left upper eyelid with redness and fullness of the temporal aspect producing an S-shaped deformity; associated tenderness over the enlarged lacrimal gland, lateral bulbar conjunctival injection and chemosis, mild regional lymph node enlargement, otherwise healthy with normal motility and no intraocular inflammation.
History Narrative: A 12-year-old boy presented with a 1-week history of swelling of the left upper eyelid. He was otherwise healthy without known chronic medical disease. On exam there was redness and fullness of the temporal aspect of the left upper lid producing an “S-shaped” deformity. Manual elevation of the left upper eyelid revealed marked injection and chemosis of the lateral bulbar conjunctiva with enlargement and erythema of the palpebral lobe of the lacrimal gland. There was moderate tenderness over the enlarged lacrimal gland and mild enlargement of regional lymph nodes. The remainder of the ocular examination, including motility, was normal with no evidence of intraocular inflammation.
Symptom Onset: 1 week
Symptom Duration: 1 week
Progression: Acute, self-limited with resolution on follow-up
Characteristics: Inflammation and enlargement of the lacrimal gland causing redness, tenderness, upper‑outer eyelid fullness with an "S‑shaped" eyelid deformity, conjunctival injection and chemosis temporally, often acute onset (rapid enlargement), may have regional lymphadenopathy; may be infectious (e.g., EBV, bacterial), idiopathic, or neoplastic.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Eye pain and fullness of the upper outer eyelid worsened by palpation or manual elevation of the eyelid (pressure on the enlarged lacrimal gland)
Associated Symptoms: eye pain, fullness of the upper outer eyelid, redness, eyelid swelling with “S-shaped” deformity, conjunctival injection and chemosis, tenderness over lacrimal gland, tearing
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes reported
Impact on Daily Activities: Minimal to none — patient otherwise healthy; symptoms limited to localized eyelid swelling and tenderness with normal ocular motility and no intraocular inflammation, allowing normal daily activities.
Systemic Symptoms: No systemic symptoms reported; otherwise healthy (no chronic medical disease).
Recent Travel: No recent travel reported
Risk Factors: Recent Epstein–Barr virus (EBV) infection; prior viral infections (e.g., mumps historically); bacterial infections (Staphylococcus, Streptococcus, Neisseria gonorrhoeae); idiopathic orbital inflammation; age (pediatric)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 118/72 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 88 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 18 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen 20/20 (measured unaided)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: 15 mmHg — Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Left upper eyelid temporal fullness and erythema with S-shaped deformity; enlargement and erythema of palpebral lobe of lacrimal gland; moderate tenderness; mild regional lymphadenopathy; remaining lids/lashes otherwise normal.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Chemosis and marked injection of the temporal bulbar conjunctiva; otherwise no conjunctival scarring or scleral injection noted.
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Enlarged, erythematous, and tender palpebral lobe of the left lacrimal gland with associated temporal upper eyelid fullness (S-shaped deformity), lateral bulbar conjunctival injection and chemosis; mild regional lymphadenopathy.
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis; normal motility; left superotemporal eyelid fullness with S-shaped deformity, erythema and tenderness over enlarged lacrimal gland; lateral bulbar conjunctival injection and chemosis.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: No corneal involvement; cornea clear and normal.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear, no cataract detected


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal and reactive, normal size and round
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Pupils equal and reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Normal
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: PERRL without afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion OU; motility normal
Slit Lamp Exam Description: There was marked injection and chemosis of the lateral bulbar conjunctiva with enlargement and erythema of the palpebral lobe of the lacrimal gland; remainder of the ocular exam including motility was normal and there was no intraocular inflammation.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal peripheral retina, no abnormalities detected


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 12-year-old boy with 1-week history of left upper eyelid swelling and tenderness. Exam: redness and fullness of the temporal left upper lid with “S-shaped” deformity, marked injection and chemosis of the lateral bulbar conjunctiva, enlargement and erythema of the palpebral lobe of the lacrimal gland, moderate tenderness, and mild regional lymphadenopathy. No motility deficit or intraocular inflammation. Serology: positive anti-EBV viral capsid antigen antibodies and negative anti-EBV nuclear antigen antibodies. Impression: acute dacryoadenitis of the left eye, most consistent with recent EBV infection. Management: conservative/medical therapy (consider topical or oral corticosteroids if inflammatory; antibiotics only if bacterial infection suspected), with follow-up for resolution.
Primary Diagnosis: Acute dacryoadenitis
Secondary Diagnoses: Epstein–Barr virus infection
Differential Diagnosis: Acute infectious dacryoadenitis (viral—EBV/mumps; bacterial—Staphylococcus/Streptococcus/Gonococcus); Idiopathic orbital inflammatory disease (orbital pseudotumor) involving lacrimal gland; Lacrimal gland neoplasm (primary epithelial/nonepithelial tumor or metastatic involvement)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Left


Plan
All Medications: topical corticosteroid; oral corticosteroid; empiric topical antibiotic; empiric systemic antibiotic
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up for resolution of symptoms.
Patient Education: Acute dacryoadenitis is inflammation of the lacrimal (tear) gland, usually causing pain, swelling, redness and a fullness of the outer upper eyelid often with tearing. In many children it is caused by a recent viral infection (commonly EBV) and often improves on its own over days to weeks. What to expect - Eyelid swelling and tenderness that should gradually lessen over days to weeks. - No surgery is usually needed. When caused by a virus, antibiotics are not helpful unless a bacterial infection is suspected. Home care - Apply warm compresses to the affected eyelid for 10–15 minutes 3–4 times daily to reduce discomfort. - Avoid pressing or massaging the area. - Use over‑the‑counter pain relievers (acetaminophen or ibuprofen) as needed and as directed for age/weight. When treatment is used - If bacterial infection is suspected, your child may receive topical and/or oral antibiotics. - If inflammation is pronounced and not improving, a short course of oral corticosteroids may be considered by the doctor. Follow‑up and warning signs - Return for follow‑up as scheduled to ensure resolution. - Seek immediate care if your child develops worsening pain, fever, decreased vision, double vision, increasing eyelid redness/swelling, inability to move the eye, or any new neurological symptoms.


CASE 95 — Orbital Tumors of Childhood
=====================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Mass over the right eye, present since shortly after birth with slow enlargement
DURATION: 8 months
LATERALITY: Right
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: An 8-month-old healthy boy whose parents noted a mass over the right eye present since shortly after birth with slow continual enlargement; no change in color or size with crying or Valsalva; otherwise healthy, full‑term birth after uncomplicated pregnancy. Examination showed a moderately firm, fixed superotemporal right eyelid mass (≈20×10×5 mm) with intact overlying skin, no measurable proptosis, central fixation and following in each eye.
History Narrative: An 8-month-old healthy boy presented for evaluation of a mass over the right eye. The mass had been present since shortly after birth, and the parents reported a slow continual enlargement of the mass. The child was born at full-term after an uncomplicated pregnancy. He was otherwise healthy with no known medical problems. There was no associated change in color or size of the mass with crying or Valsalva’s maneuvers. Examination showed central fixation with following in each eye. The rest of his ophthalmic exam was only notable for a moderately firm mass over the superotemporal right eyelid. The mass measured approximately 20 mm × 10 mm × 5 mm and was fixed to the underlying tissues. The overlying skin was intact without discoloration or ulceration. Careful palpation of the superior orbital rim revealed no discernable defect. There was no measurable proptosis, and both orbits were normal to retropulsion. No other masses were palpated.
Symptom Onset: Shortly after birth
Symptom Duration: Approximately 8 months (present since shortly after birth)
Progression: Slow continual enlargement since shortly after birth
Characteristics: Moderately firm, well-circumscribed, fixed to underlying tissues, 20 mm × 10 mm × 5 mm, overlying skin intact without discoloration or ulceration, located superotemporal at the orbital rim.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: None—no change in color or size with crying or Valsalva maneuvers
Associated Symptoms: No associated change in color or size with crying or Valsalva; overlying skin intact without discoloration or ulceration; no measurable proptosis; normal retropulsion; central fixation and following in each eye; no other masses palpated.
Previous Episodes: Present since shortly after birth, with slow continual enlargement (no prior separate episodes)
Prior Treatments: None
Impact on Daily Activities: None — no impact on daily activities.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Congenital choristoma (developmental inclusion at suture lines) — presence since birth; location at zygomaticofrontal suture (superotemporal orbital rim) — female/male not specified; slow growth; risk of rupture with minor trauma leading to granulomatous inflammation.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Pulse: 120 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.4 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 28 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: central fixation with following in each eye
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: Central fixation with following in each eye
Visual Acuity – Method: Central fixation with following
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Not measured (unable to obtain)


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Moderately firm, well-circumscribed mass over the superotemporal right eyelid; overlying skin intact without discoloration or ulceration; no ptosis; lashes normal.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal.
External Exam – Lacrimal System: normal
External Exam – Orbit: Moderately firm mass over the superotemporal right eyelid, well-circumscribed 20 mm × 10 mm × 5 mm, fixed to underlying tissues; overlying skin intact without discoloration or ulceration; no measurable proptosis; orbits normal to retropulsion.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear, normal cornea.
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: aphakic


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils equal, round, reactive to light (no abnormal size or shape noted)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk and symmetric to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Brisk and symmetric
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: Pupils equal and reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: central fixation with following in each eye
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 8-month-old boy with a congenital, slowly enlarging, firm, well‑circumscribed superotemporal right orbital mass measuring approximately 20 × 10 × 5 mm, fixed to underlying tissues with intact overlying skin, no proptosis or bone defect on palpation, central fixation and following in both eyes. Clinical presentation and location at the zygomaticofrontal suture consistent with a dermoid cyst of the right orbit. Recommended and performed surgical excision via lid-crease approach; complete removal achieved without complication.
Primary Diagnosis: Dermoid cyst of the right orbit
Differential Diagnosis: Dermoid cyst; Epidermoid cyst; Capillary hemangioma; Rhabdomyosarcoma
Severity: Mild
Laterality: Right
Complications: None reported


Plan
All Procedures: Excision of right orbital dermoid cyst via lid crease incision
Follow-Up Instructions: Routine postoperative follow-up with ophthalmology to monitor wound healing and eyelid function; return for suture removal and wound check in ~1 week; routine long-term follow-up as needed for cosmetic outcome and to monitor for recurrence.
Patient Education: This mass is a dermoid cyst — a benign, congenital (present from birth) cystic lesion commonly found at the superotemporal orbital rim. It grows slowly and can rupture if injured, which may cause significant inflammation. Because of this risk, surgical removal is recommended; the operation is typically done through a lid-crease incision for a concealed scar. There is no effective medical (non‑surgical) treatment. Surgery is usually postponed until the infant is at least 6 months old when possible to lower anesthesia risk. After complete excision recurrence is unlikely and prognosis is excellent. Follow-up exams will check healing and eye function; seek prompt care if you notice redness, swelling, discharge, sudden increase in size, or changes in the child’s vision or eye movements.


CASE 96 — Orbital Tumors in Adults
==================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Increasing painless prominence of the left eye (progressive left eye proptosis)
DURATION: increasing painless prominence (progressive proptosis) for ~1 year
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "I've had increasing painless prominence of the left eye without any double vision."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 44-year-old man with progressive, painless prominence (proptosis) of the left eye over months, no diplopia, no prior medical history; noted changing left-eye refractive error (glasses changed twice in past year).
History Narrative: A 44-year-old man was referred for evaluation of progressive, painless proptosis of the left eye. He had no prior medical history. He reported increasing prominence of the left eye without diplopia and had two changes in his glasses prescription over the past year. Visual acuity was 20/20 OU with manifest refraction −2.25 D OD and +1.00 D OS. Motility and pupils were normal. The left palpebral fissure was widened with 4 mm relative proptosis OS and increased resistance to retropulsion. No palpable bony defects or masses, no regional lymphadenopathy, and no orbital bruits were noted. The anterior segment and bulbar conjunctiva were unremarkable. Dilated fundus exam of the left eye showed choroidal striae at the posterior pole; the optic nerve was normal without hemorrhage or exudate. The right eye exam was normal.
Symptom Onset: Increasing over past year
Symptom Duration: increasing over the past year
Progression: Progressive proptosis
Characteristics: Well-circumscribed, retrobulbar, encapsulated, dark-red vascular lesion causing painless progressive proptosis with marginal contrast enhancement and increased resistance to retropulsion


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: Painless progressive prominence of the left eye; no diplopia; change in glasses prescription (decrease in myopia); possible visual distortion from choroidal striae.
Previous Episodes: None
Prior Treatments: None
Impact on Daily Activities: Minimal impact — patient remained 20/20 vision OU, no diplopia, only progressive proptosis and refractive change; able to undergo surgery and recovered with stabilization of refraction and resolution of choroidal striae.
Systemic Symptoms: No systemic symptoms reported
Risk Factors: Middle-aged adulthood (middle-aged man), no prior medical history, typical presentation of cavernous hemangioma (age-related risk)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Palpebral fissure widened on the left; otherwise lids and lashes unremarkable.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: unremarkable without evidence of abnormal vascularity
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Proptosis OS with 4 mm relative left-sided proptosis; widened left palpebral fissure; increased resistance to retropulsion OS; no palpable bony defects or definite mass; no conjunctival chemosis or abnormal vascularity.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Quiet
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear crystalline lens


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Within normal limits
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk and symmetric
Pupils – Reaction to Near: briskly reactive to near
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: Within normal limits
Extraocular Movements: Within normal limits
Slit Lamp Exam Description: The anterior segment was quiet by slit-lamp examination; the bulbar conjunctiva was unremarkable without evidence of abnormal vascularity.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Deferred/Not documented


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 44-year-old man with painless progressive left proptosis, 4 mm relative proptosis OS, increased resistance to retropulsion, change in refractive error, CT showing a well-circumscribed retrobulbar enhancing mass without bony changes, consistent with a left orbital cavernous hemangioma; underwent lateral orbitotomy with en bloc excision and histopathologic confirmation of cavernous hemangioma.
Primary Diagnosis: Probable cavernous hemangioma of the left orbit
Differential Diagnosis: Cavernous hemangioma; Hemangiopericytoma; Orbital lymphoma (lymphoproliferative lesion); Lacrimal gland epithelial tumor (pleomorphic adenoma)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Left
Complications: None


Plan
All Procedures: Lateral orbitotomy; surgical excision (removal in toto) of left orbital cavernous hemangioma
Follow-Up Instructions: Patient did well postoperatively; monitor visual acuity, refraction, and resolution of choroidal striae with routine postoperative visits; observe for recurrence with periodic orbital exam and imaging as indicated.
Patient Education: This is most likely a benign cavernous hemangioma of the orbit — a slow-growing, encapsulated vascular tumor that commonly causes painless proptosis. Imaging (CT or MRI) helps define its size and location. Because yours caused progressive bulging and a change in vision, surgical removal via a lateral orbitotomy was recommended and performed; the tumor was removed intact and pathology confirmed the diagnosis. What to expect after surgery - Recovery: Mild pain, swelling, bruising, and temporary bruising around the eye are common and improve over days to weeks. Keep head elevated and use cold packs in the first 48 hours as instructed. - Vision: Vision typically returns to baseline; your left-eye prescription stabilized after surgery (to approximately −2.00 D). Report any sudden vision decrease, new double vision, severe pain, or fever immediately. - Eye movements: Temporary limitation or discomfort with eye movement may occur but usually improves. Persistent diplopia is uncommon with uncomplicated removal. - Wound care: Keep the incision clean and dry; follow wound-care instructions and avoid strenuous activity or heavy lifting until cleared. - Medications: Take prescribed pain medication and any antibiotic or steroid drops/systemic medications as directed. Finish the full course if given. - Follow-up: Attend scheduled postoperative visits to monitor healing, vision, and intraorbital status. Imaging is generally not required after complete removal unless symptoms recur. - Recurrence: Cavernous hemangiomas are usually cured by complete excision and rarely recur; report any new progressive proptosis or visual change. - When to seek care: sudden vision loss, increasing eye pain, severe swelling, signs of infection (fever, increased redness, drainage), or new/progressive bulging. Lifestyle and long-term outlook - No specific activity restrictions long-term; gradual return to normal activities as advised. - Prognosis is excellent after complete removal; most patients have resolution of proptosis and stabilization of refractive change.


CASE 97 — Benign Tumors of the Eyelid
=====================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Left upper eyelid lesion present for several years, slowly enlarging over the last several months
DURATION: several years
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "I had a lesion on my left upper eyelid for several years that has been slowly enlarging for the last several months. It hasn't been red or bleeding, and I don't have any history of skin disease or skin cancer."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 32-year-old man with a left upper eyelid lesion present for several years, slowly enlarging over the last several months, without redness or bleeding; otherwise healthy, no prior skin disease or skin cancer, no medications. External exam notable for a 10×8×5 mm smooth dome-shaped amelanotic lesion of the preseptal skin of the left upper lid with intact overlying skin, no ulceration or telangiectasia, and no regional lymphadenopathy or other head/neck skin lesions.
History Narrative: A 32-year-old man presented for evaluation of a left upper eyelid lesion that had been present for several years and had been slowly enlarging over the last several months. He reported no associated redness or bleeding from the lesion and had no history of previous skin disease or skin cancer. He was in good health and took no medications. Ophthalmic examination was within normal limits except for the external exam, which showed a 10 mm × 8 mm × 5 mm smooth dome-shaped skin lesion involving the preseptal skin of the left upper lid. The tumor was amelanotic with intact overlying skin without ulceration or telangiectasia. There was no regional lymphadenopathy and no other skin lesions on the head and neck.
Symptom Onset: several years
Symptom Duration: several years
Progression: Slow growth over many years, with recent slow enlargement over the last several months.
Characteristics: Very slow growth over many years; lack of skin ulceration, scaling, madarosis, or telangiectasia; smooth domeshaped, amelanotic skin lesion with intact overlying skin; 10×8×5 mm preseptal upper eyelid lesion; benign (intradermal nevus).


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: No associated redness or bleeding
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes reported
Prior Treatments: None
Impact on Daily Activities: No impact on daily activities.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: Sun exposure (actinic damage), history of nevus/pigmented lesions (often present from birth), age-related changes; no personal skin cancer or skin disease reported.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 122/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J+1
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: 10 mm × 8 mm × 5 mm smooth dome-shaped, amelanotic skin lesion involving the preseptal left upper eyelid skin; overlying skin intact without ulceration, telangiectasia, or madarosis; no eyelid margin distortion.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: within normal limits
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Within normal limits
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Within normal limits
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear crystalline lens


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils equal, round, and reactive to light (no anisocoria)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive to light
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: Equal and reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full motility in all directions of gaze without limitation or diplopia.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Within normal limits except for external exam showing a 10 × 8 × 5 mm smooth dome-shaped, amelanotic skin lesion of the left upper preseptal eyelid with intact overlying skin (no ulceration or telangiectasia).
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal appearing optic nerve
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Benign eyelid tumor of the left upper eyelid, most consistent with an intradermal nevus: a slow‑growing 10×8×5 mm smooth, dome‑shaped, amelanotic preseptal skin lesion without ulceration, telangiectasia, madarosis, or regional lymphadenopathy. Excisional biopsy performed; histopathology confirmed intradermal nevus. No evidence of recurrence on follow‑up.
Primary Diagnosis: Benign eyelid tumor, most likely intradermal nevus
Differential Diagnosis: Intradermal nevus; Squamous papilloma; Seborrheic keratosis; Hidrocystoma
Severity: Mild
Laterality: Left
Complications: None


Plan
All Procedures: Excisional biopsy of left upper eyelid lesion (office procedure under local anesthesia) with primary repair/allowed to heal by secondary intention as appropriate; specimen sent for histopathologic examination
Follow-Up Instructions: Patient has done well without evidence of recurrence; routine postoperative follow-up with wound check and ophthalmic exam as needed.
Patient Education: This lesion is a benign intradermal nevus—slow‑growing and not cancerous. No medical therapy is needed. Options include simple observation or surgical removal; removal is often done in the office under local anesthesia and the small wound can be closed directly or left to heal on its own. Because excision is performed for diagnosis and cosmesis, the tissue is sent to pathology to confirm the diagnosis. After excision expect mild pain, swelling, bruising, and a small scar; keep the wound clean, follow wound care instructions, and avoid rubbing or strenuous activity that stresses the eyelid. Report increasing pain, spreading redness, drainage, fever, or any rapid regrowth of the lesion. Routine follow‑up is advised to review pathology and monitor for recurrence.


CASE 98 — Malignant Tumors of the Eyelid
========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Enlarging skin nodule of the right upper eyelid (present ~8 months)
DURATION: Approximately 8 months
LATERALITY: right
PATIENT QUOTES: "I had picked at the lesion."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 55-year-old man with an 8-month history of an enlarging, fairly rapidly growing nonpigmented nodule of the right upper eyelid near the medial canthus, with one episode of bleeding after picking, extensive sun exposure history, and no prior skin cancer.
History Narrative: A 55-year-old man presented for evaluation of an enlarging skin nodule of his right upper eyelid. The lesion had been present for approximately 8 months and had grown fairly rapidly. The patient recalled one episode of bleeding from the area after he had picked at the lesion. While there was no previous history of skin cancer, the patient reported an extensive sun-exposure history. His ophthalmic examination was within normal limits except for the external examination. There was a large elevated nonpigmented nodule involving the skin of the right upper eyelid near the medial canthal region. The nodule measured 15 mm × 12 mm × 7 mm and showed numerous small telangiectatic vessels on its surface. The edges of the tumor had a thickened pearly appearance. There was no ulceration present. The skin underlying the lesion was freely moveable, and the tumor did not appear fixed to the underlying deep structures. There was no regional lymphadenopathy. The skin of the patient’s face showed diffuse sun-exposure changes consisting of fine wrinkles and telangiectatic vessels and scattered areas of crusting.
Symptom Onset: approximately 8 months
Symptom Duration: 8 months
Progression: Enlarging over ~8 months, fairly rapid growth
Characteristics: Enlarging, rapidly growing 15×12×7 mm elevated nonpigmented nodule on right upper eyelid (medial canthal region) with numerous surface telangiectatic vessels, thickened pearly edges, freely mobile over underlying tissue, no ulceration, occasional bleeding, associated facial sun-damage.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Extensive sun exposure
Associated Symptoms: enlarging skin nodule of right upper eyelid, one episode of bleeding, rapid growth, crusting on lesion surface
Previous Episodes: One episode of bleeding after picking at the lesion
Impact on Daily Activities: No impact on daily activities.
Systemic Symptoms: No systemic symptoms reported
Risk Factors: fair skin; northern European ancestry; extensive sun exposure; history of previous basal cell carcinoma; family history of skin cancer; age over 50


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 76 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mm Hg


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Large elevated nonpigmented nodule of right upper eyelid near the medial canthal region with numerous surface telangiectatic vessels; edges thickened pearly; no ulceration; skin underlying freely movable; no madarosis noted.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Within normal limits
External Exam – Lacrimal System: No epiphora; lacrimal drainage system not involved (no signs of lacrimal obstruction or lacrimal sac involvement).
External Exam – Orbit: Within normal limits
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Within normal limits
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Within normal limits
Anterior Segment – Iris: Within normal limits
Anterior Segment – Lens: No cataract; lens within normal limits (phakic)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, reactive to light (no abnormality noted)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk and equal
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Brisk
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: PERRL
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions; extraocular movements intact (no restriction)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Within normal limits except for external exam showing a large elevated nonpigmented nodule of the right upper eyelid near the medial canthus; otherwise slit-lamp exam normal.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: disc normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Lesion of the right upper eyelid (medial canthal region); probable basal cell carcinoma. Evidence: 8-month history of a rapidly enlarging, nonpigmented elevated 15 × 12 × 7 mm nodule with telangiectasias, pearly thickened edges, one episode of bleeding, freely mobile over deep structures, no regional lymphadenopathy. Clinical impression most consistent with nodular basal cell carcinoma; tissue diagnosis required (biopsy recommended).
Primary Diagnosis: Lesion of the right upper eyelid (medial canthal region); probable basal cell carcinoma
Secondary Diagnoses: Extensive sun-exposure history; diffuse facial actinic changes (solar elastosis/telangiectasias)
Differential Diagnosis: Basal cell carcinoma; Squamous cell carcinoma; Sebaceous gland carcinoma; Malignant melanoma
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: right
Complications: None reported


Plan
All Medications: Imiquimod; 5-fluorouracil; Vismodegib; Sonidegib
All Procedures: Excisional biopsy of right upper eyelid lesion with intraoperative frozen section analysis confirming basal cell carcinoma and full-thickness skin graft repair of the resultant defect.
Follow-Up Instructions: Educated about increased risk for new skin malignancies and placed under dermatologist care for continued surveillance.
Patient Education: Educated about his increased risk for the development of new skin malignancies and placed under the care of a dermatologist for continued surveillance.
Referrals: Dermatology and oncology (for complete metastatic evaluation if sebaceous carcinoma or melanoma suspected)
